The Shia Argument from Consensus for Ali's Imamate
Razi presents the Shia claim and its two supporting premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first Shia argument for Ali's imamate, which relies on consensus and the infallibility requirement.

Core Idea
If consensus limits the imam to three, and two are unfit, the third must be Ali.

This lesson examines the first argument the Shia present for Ali's imamate. They claim that the ummah unanimously agrees that the rightful imam after the Prophet is one of three: Ali, Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas. Then they argue that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas cannot be imams because they lack infallibility, leaving Ali as the only candidate. The force of this argument depends on two premises: that consensus is a valid proof, and that the imam must be infallible.

Guided Path
1
Overview of the Shia claim
Read the entire passage and identify the overall structure: the Shia assert that consensus limits the imamate to three individuals, then they eliminate two, leaving Ali.
2
First premise: Consensus as proof
Focus on how the Shia justify consensus as a valid proof. They argue that the age cannot be without an infallible person, and that consensus includes that infallible's statement.
3
Second premise: Unsuitability of Abu Bakr and al-Abbas
Examine the reasoning: reason requires the imam to be infallible, and consensus affirms that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not infallible, so they are not suitable for imamate.
This premise relies on the first premise about consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشيعة فقد احتجوا على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم علي بن أبي طالب لوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : - وهي التي عليها يعولون وبها يصولون - أن قالوا : أجمعت الأمة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله إما علي، وإما أبو بكر وإما العباس ولا يجوز أن يكون الإمام هو أبو بكر أو العباس فتعين أن يكون الإمام هو علي فيفتقر في تقدير هذا الدليل إلى مقدمتين : المقدمة الأولى : أن الإجماع حجة قالوا والدليل عليه أنا قد دللنا على أن زمان التكليف لا يخلو من معصوم البتة، وقول المعصوم حجة، فإذا حصل الإجماع كان ذلك الإجماع مشتملا على قول ذلك المعصوم، فوجب أن يكون الإجماع حجة. المقدمة الثانية : أن أبا بكر والعباس لم يكونا صالحين للإمامة، قالوا : لأنه ثبت بالعقل أن الإمام لا بد وأن يكون واجب العصمة وثبت بإجماع الأمة أنهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة، وإذا ثبت أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وثبت أنهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة ثبت أنهما ما كانا صالحين للإمامة، ولما بطلت إمامتهما وجب القطع بإمامة علي حتى لا يخرج الحق عن قول كل الأمة. فهذا هو العمدة الكبرى للإثنى عشرية في هذه المسألة.
As for the Shia, they have argued that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, is Ali ibn Abi Talib, by several arguments. The first specious argument – which is the one they rely on and with which they attack – is that they said: The ummah is unanimous that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of God is either Ali, or Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas, and it is not permissible for the Imam to be Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, so it is determined that the Imam is Ali. This argument requires two premises: The first premise: that consensus is a proof. They said, and the evidence for it is that we have demonstrated that the time of obligation is never devoid of an infallible one at all, and the statement of the infallible is a proof. So when consensus occurs, that consensus includes the statement of that infallible one, so consensus must be a proof. The second premise: that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not suitable for the imamate. They said because it is established by reason that the imam must necessarily be infallible, and it is established by consensus of the ummah that they were not necessarily infallible. And when it is established that the imam must be infallible, and it is established that they were not infallible, it is established that they were not suitable for the imamate. And when their imamate is invalidated, the definitive judgment of Ali's imamate is required, so that the truth is not excluded from the statement of the entire ummah. This is the greatest foundation of the Twelver Shia in this issue.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the first Shia argument (shubha) for Ali's imamate, which Razi will later refute. The argument is based on consensus (ijma'). The Shia claim that the ummah agrees that the rightful Imam after the Prophet is one of three: Ali, Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas. They then argue that since Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not suitable (because the imam must be infallible, and these two are not considered infallible by consensus), the only remaining candidate is Ali. This argument requires two premises. The first premise is that consensus is a valid proof. They justify this by noting that there must always be an infallible person in every age; consensus necessarily includes the opinion of that infallible person, so consensus is authoritative. The second premise is that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not fit for imamate because reason requires the imam to be infallible, and the ummah agrees that these two were not infallible. Therefore, their imamate is invalid, and Ali's imamate is established. Razi notes that this is the main argument of the Twelver Shia on this issue.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة
معصوم
عصمة
إمامة
صالح للإمامة
واجب العصمة
إثنى عشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia argument assumes that the entire ummah agreed that the imam is one of three, but this consensus is not historically accepted.
Correction: In the Shia argument, they assert a consensus among the ummah, but Sunni scholars reject that there was such a consensus. The argument is based on a claimed consensus that Razi will later critique.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise in the Shia argument from consensus?
A) Consensus is a proof.
B) The imam must be from Quraysh.
C) Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not suitable for imamate because they are not infallible.
D) Ali was the most deserving.

Key Takeaway
The Shia argue that the imam must be infallible, and since only Ali among the three candidates was infallible, he is the rightful imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shia claim that there must be an infallible person in every age. Do you have someone in your life whom you treat as if they are infallible—a leader, scholar, or even yourself? What would it mean to hold that person to the same critical standard Razi uses here? Will you examine that assumption today?
?
This argument relies on a 'consensus' that limits candidates to three. When have you accepted a conclusion because 'everyone says so' without checking the evidence? What comfort or fear keeps you from questioning that consensus?
?
The Shia premise about infallibility leads them to a specific conclusion about Ali. Is there a belief you hold that feels logically airtight but is actually based on a premise you have never truly tested? What would it cost you to examine that premise honestly?

The Shia Argument Against Abu Bakr's Imamate
Razi presents the Shia argument that Abu Bakr's imamate cannot be proven by text or bay'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia argument that Abu Bakr's imamate cannot be established by either divine text or bay'ah, and the specific reason against text.

Core Idea
The Shia argue that imamate must come from text or bay'ah, and both fail for Abu Bakr.

This lesson presents one of the Shia arguments against the imamate of Abu Bakr. Razi quotes the Shia claim that imamate can only be proven by direct divine text (nass) or by the people's oath of allegiance (bay'ah). They argue that both are impossible in Abu Bakr's case, and thus his imamate is invalid. We will examine the argument against the text-based proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Note the two proposed proofs for imamate mentioned.
The passage cuts off; the argument against bay'ah is not fully given.
2
Identify the two proofs
The Shia claim that imamate can only be established by either text (nass) or bay'ah. Find in the passage where this division is made.
3
Understand the argument against text
The Shia argue that if Abu Bakr had a text from the Prophet appointing him, his seeking bay'ah at Saqifa would be a great sin. Think about why that would be a contradiction.
This is the key point: a text would make the request for bay'ah redundant and sinful.
4
Reflect on the incomplete argument
The passage ends without giving the argument against bay'ah. Consider what that argument might be based on your knowledge of Shia theology.
Sunni scholars respond that the Shia assumption of only two proofs is false; shura and election are also valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن أبا بكر والعباس لم يكونا صالحين للإمامة، قالوا: لأنه ثبت بالعقل انه لو كان أبو بكر إماما لكانت إمامته إما أن تثبت بالنص أو بالبيعة، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بإمامة أبي بكر أما الحصر فلأن كل من قال بإمامته لم يثبتها إلا بأحد هذين الطريقين، وإنما قلنا إنه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنص لأنه لو كان منصوصا عليه بالإمامة لكان توقيفه يوم السقيفة إمامة نفسه على البيعة من أعظم المعاصي، وذلك
The second specious argument: that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not suitable for the imamate. They said: because it is established by reason that if Abu Bakr were imam, his imamate would have to be established either by text or by bay'ah, and both divisions are invalid, so the statement of Abu Bakr's imamate is invalid. As for the [exclusive] division, it is because everyone who affirmed his imamate did not affirm it except by one of these two ways. And we said that it cannot be established by text because if he were appointed by text to the imamate, then his requesting bay'ah for himself on the day of Saqifa would be among the greatest sins. And that...
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shia argument against Abu Bakr's imamate, though it is cut off at the end of the page. The Shia argue that Abu Bakr's imamate must be proven either by explicit divine text (nass) or by the people's oath of allegiance (bay'ah). They claim both are impossible. The text-based proof is impossible because if Abu Bakr had been explicitly appointed by the Prophet, his actions at Saqifa (where he sought bay'ah for himself) would be a grave sin, contradicting his status. The bay'ah-based proof is invalid for reasons not fully given in this passage. Therefore, Abu Bakr's imamate is invalid. The argument then presumably concludes that Ali's imamate is the only remaining option. Razi presents this as a Shia argument he will later refute.

Key Terms
نص
بيعة
إمامة
سقيفة
معصية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The only two proofs for imamate are nass and bay'ah.
Correction: Sunni scholars also consider shura (consultation) and election as valid methods for establishing imamate. The Shia have narrowed the possibilities to exclude these.

Checkpoint
According to the Shia argument, why can Abu Bakr's imamate not be proven by text?
A) No text exists for Abu Bakr.
B) If a text existed, his seeking bay'ah would be a great sin.
C) The text was lost.
D) The text was ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
The Shia claim that Abu Bakr's imamate cannot be proven by text because that would make his actions at Saqifa sinful, and cannot be proven by bay'ah for reasons not fully stated in this passage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a companion at Saqifa and heard this argument, would you have been persuaded? Why or why not? What evidence matters most to you when deciding whom to follow as a leader?
?
The Shia say that if there were a divine text appointing Abu Bakr, his request for bay'ah would be a sin. Can you think of a scenario where a divinely appointed leader might still need to seek human confirmation? What does this reveal about your own understanding of divine authority and human participation?
?
This argument reduces the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate to a logical contradiction. When you defend your own beliefs about early Islamic history, do you rely more on reason or transmitted reports? Which one truly anchors your conviction?

First Shia Argument: Consensus Proves Ali's Imama
Imam Razi presents the first Shia argument for Ali's imama based on consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first Shia argument for Ali's imama, which relies on consensus and two key premises.

Core Idea
The Shia argue that consensus, combined with the infallibility requirement, uniquely identifies Ali as the rightful Imam.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of Shia objections by presenting their strongest argument: the claim that consensus of the community proves Ali's imama. This lesson examines the logical structure of that argument, focusing on its two premises. By understanding how the Shia build this case, you will be better prepared to evaluate the subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Imam Razi frames this as the first of several Shia arguments.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument hinges on two premises. Find the first premise about consensus being a proof, and the second premise about Abu Bakr and al-Abbas not being fit for imama.
The Shia justify the first premise by appealing to the existence of an infallible in every age.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
See how from these two premises the Shia conclude that Ali must be the Imam. Ask yourself: what would need to be true for this argument to be sound?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشيعة فقد احتجوا على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم علي بن أبي طالب لوجوه : الشبهة الأولى : - وهي التي عليها يعولون وبها يصولون - أن قالوا : أجمعت الأمة على أن الإمام الحق بعد رسول الله إما علي، وإما أبو بكر وإما العباس ليالينا ولا يجوز أن يكون الإمام هو أبو بكر أو العباس فتعين أن يكون الإمام هو فيفتقر في تقدير هذا الدليل إلى مقدمتين : المقدمة الأولى : أن الإجماع حجة قالوا والدليل عليه أنا قد دللنا على أن زمان التكليف لا يخلو من معصوم البتة، وقول المعصوم حجة، فإذا حصل الإجماع كان ذلك الإجماع مشتملا على قول ذلك المعصوم، فوجب أن يكون الإجماع حجة. المقدمة الثانية : أن أبا بكر والعباس لم يكونا صالحين للإمامة، قالوا : لأنه ثبت بالعقل أن الإمام لا بد وأن يكون واجب العصمة وثبت بإجماع الأمة أنهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة، وإذا ثبت أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وثبت أنهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة ثبت أنهما ما كانا صالحين للإمامة، ولما بطلت إمامتهما وجب القطع بإمامة علي حتى لا يخرج الحق عن قول كل الأمة. فهذا هو العمدة الكبرى للإثنى عشرية في هذه المسألة.
As for the Shia, they have argued that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is Ali ibn Abi Talib, by means of several points. The first argument – which is the one they rely on and with which they attack – is that they said: The community (umma) has agreed that the rightful Imam after the Messenger of Allah is either Ali, or Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas, lest we... and it is not permissible that the Imam be Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, so it is determined that the Imam is [Ali]. This argument depends on two premises: The first premise: That consensus (ijma) is a proof (hujja). They said the evidence for it is that we have shown that the time of religious obligation is never devoid of an infallible one (ma'sum) at all, and the statement of the infallible is a proof. So when consensus occurs, that consensus includes the statement of that infallible, so consensus must be a proof. The second premise: That Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not fit for the imama. They said: because it is established by reason that the Imam must necessarily be infallible (wajib al-isma), and it is established by consensus of the community that they were not infallible. And when it is established that the Imam must be infallible, and it is established that they were not infallible, it is established that they were not fit for the imama. And when their imama is invalidated, it becomes necessary to definitively affirm the imama of Ali, so that the truth does not depart from the statement of the entire community. This is the greatest foundation for the Twelvers in this matter.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the first Shia argument (shubha) for the imama of Ali. It is an argument based on consensus (ijma). The author, Imam Razi, is presenting the view of the opposition before refuting it. The Shia claim that the community agrees that the rightful Imam after the Prophet is either Ali, Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas. Since—they argue—Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not fit, Ali must be the Imam. This argument rests on two premises. The first premise is that consensus is a proof. Their justification is that the age of obligation always contains an infallible person (ma'sum) whose statement is authoritative; consensus must include that infallible's opinion, so consensus is authoritative. The second premise is that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not fit for imama because reason requires the Imam to be infallible, and the community agrees that they were not infallible. Therefore, only Ali remains, and his imama is certain. The passage concludes by noting that this is the main argument of the Twelver Shia on this issue.

Key Terms
إجماع
حجة
معصوم
عصمة
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Consensus is automatically a valid proof for any theological claim.
Correction: The Shia argue consensus is proof because it includes an infallible, but this premise is contested by Sunnis who hold that consensus is valid only when based on revelation.

Checkpoint
According to the first Shia argument, what two premises are needed to prove Ali's imama?
A) Consensus is a proof; Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not infallible.
B) Ali is infallible; Abu Bakr is not.
C) Consensus is not a proof; Ali was explicitly appointed.
D) The Prophet appointed Ali; the community accepted him.

Key Takeaway
The Shia base their claim for Ali's imama on the twin premises that consensus is a proof and that only an infallible can be Imam, thereby excluding Abu Bakr and al-Abbas.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that the Shia require an infallible leader for the umma, do you see a similar longing in your own heart for a perfect guide? How does that longing shape your expectations of those in authority over you? Are you willing to follow imperfect leaders for the sake of unity?
?
The Shia claim that consensus must include an infallible, implying that truth is always present in the community. Do you live as if you believe that truth is accessible today? In what specific area of your life are you settling for what is easy rather than seeking the full truth of Allah's guidance?
?
If you had been among the companions at the Saqifa, would you have argued for an infallible leader? What does your answer reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom in allowing the umma to choose a leader without that explicit condition? How does this challenge your reliance on human perfection over divine providence?

Second Shia Argument: The Dilemma of Nass and Bay'a
Imam Razi presents the second Shia argument against Abu Bakr's imama.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia logical dilemma that attempts to invalidate Abu Bakr's imama by arguing it cannot be proven by either explicit designation (nass) or popular election (bay'a).

Core Idea
Without nass or valid bay'a, Abu Bakr's imama collapses.

The Shia present a sophisticated argument that reduces the possible bases for Abu Bakr's imama to two: explicit designation (nass) or oath of allegiance (bay'a). They then attempt to disprove both, leaving no valid foundation. This lesson unpacks that argument and its logical structure, as recorded by Imam Razi.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the full argument. Notice the logical flow from the exhaustive division (hasr) to the refutation of each possibility.
Pay attention to the terms nass and bay'a.
2
Identify the exhaustive division
The Shia claim that Abu Bakr's imama must be proven either by nass or bay'a. Find where this division is stated and notice the phrase 'al-hasr' (حصري).
The division is presented as complete: no other possibilities exist.
3
Analyze the refutation of nass
The passage argues that if Abu Bakr had been designated by nass, his actions at Saqifa (waiting for bay'a) and his statement 'Relieve me' would be major sins. Understand why this would be contradictory.
This refutation uses the behavior of Abu Bakr himself.
4
Analyze the refutation of bay'a
The passage dismisses bay'a by referring to earlier arguments (from a previous chapter) that bay'a cannot establish imama. Note that the details of those arguments are not repeated here.
This step relies on prior knowledge of Shia objections to bay'a.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثانية : أن أبا بكر والعباس لم يكونا صالحين للإمامة، قالوا: لأنه ثبت بالعقل انه لو كان أبو بكر إماما لكانت إمامته إما أن تثبت بالنص أو بالبيعة، والقسمان باطلان، فبطل القول بإمامة أبي بكر. أما الحصر فلأن كل من قال بإمامته لم يثبتها إلا بأحد هذين الطريقين، وإنما قلنا إنه لا يمكن إثباتها بالنص لأنه لو كان منصوصًا عليه بالإمامة لكان توقيفه يوم السقيفة إمامة نفسه على البيعة من أعظم المعاصي، وذلك أنه لا يقدح في إمامته، ولكان قوله : "أقيلوني" من أعظم المعاصي، وإنما قلنا : إنه لا يمكن إثباتها بالبيعة للوجوه التي حكيناها في الفصل الثالث، حكاية عنهم، يجوز أن تكون البيعة طريقا إلى ثبوت الإمامة قالوا فثبت أنه لو كان إماماً لكانت إمامته بأحد هذين الطريقين وثبت فساد كل واحد منهما، فبطل القول بكونه إماما.
The second argument: That Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not fit for the imama. They said: because it is established by reason that if Abu Bakr were Imam, his imama would be established either by explicit designation (nass) or by oath of allegiance (bay'a). Both divisions are invalid, so the claim of Abu Bakr's imama is invalid. As for the exhaustive division (al-hasr), it is because everyone who asserts his imama only establishes it by one of these two ways. We say that it cannot be established by nass because if he had been explicitly designated for the imama, then his halting on the day of Saqifa of his own imama upon the bay'a would be one of the greatest sins, and that would not detract from his imama, and his saying 'Relieve me' would be one of the greatest sins. We say that it cannot be established by bay'a due to the reasons we narrated in the third chapter, quoting them: it is possible that bay'a is a way to establish the imama. They said: So it is established that if he were Imam, his imama would be by one of these two ways, and the corruption of each one is established, so the claim that he is Imam is invalid.
Source pages p. 431

Commentary
This passage presents the second Shia argument against the imama of Abu Bakr. The Shia assert that Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not fit for imama. Their reasoning is a dilemma: if Abu Bakr were Imam, his imama would have to be proven either by explicit textual designation (nass) or by popular election (bay'a). Both are impossible. The division is exhaustive because all supporters of Abu Bakr use one of these two proofs. As for nass, they argue that if Abu Bakr had been explicitly designated by the Prophet, his hesitation at Saqifa (where he waited for the bay'a before assuming leadership) would be a major sin, and his famous statement 'Relieve me' would also be sinful. As for bay'a, they refer to earlier arguments (from chapter three) that bay'a cannot be a valid method for establishing imama. Therefore, since both avenues are invalid, Abu Bakr cannot be the rightful Imam.

Key Terms
نص
بيعة
سقيفة
إمامة
حصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia claim that the only ways to establish imama are nass or bay'a, and that both are invalid for Abu Bakr.
Correction: Sunnis argue that imama can also be established by the collective agreement (ijma') of the community, and that the bay'a at Saqifa represented that consensus, not a mere election.

Checkpoint
According to the second Shia argument, what are the two possible ways to establish Abu Bakr's imama?
A) Nass and bay'a
B) Inheritance and election
C) Appointment by the previous Imam and consultation
D) Conquest and treaty

Key Takeaway
The second Shia argument attempts to destroy the foundation of Abu Bakr's imama by showing that neither explicit designation nor popular election can validly establish it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you follow a leader—whether in religion, politics, or community—do you require explicit designation or popular acceptance? Could you be following someone without valid basis?
?
If the Shia argument were valid, how would that reshape your view of early Islamic history? Have you ever accepted a claim about the past without examining its foundation?
?
The Shia use reason (العقل) to argue. Do you use reason to test your own beliefs, or do you accept them uncritically? What would it take to question a deeply held conviction?

The Shia Tawatur Argument for Ali's Imamate
Presents the Shia claim of a mutawatir text designating Ali as Imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the Shia argument that a mutawatir report designates Ali as Imam, including the three conditions for tawatur and their application.

Core Idea
The Shia claim certainty for Ali's imamate through a clear text reported by massive transmission (tawatur).

This passage presents the third Shia argument for the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib. The argument asserts that there exists a clear textual designation (nass jali) from the Prophet Muhammad, and this designation is known through tawatur (massive transmission) which yields certainty. Before we examine the counter-arguments, we must first understand how the Shia establish the existence of this text through the conditions of tawatur.

Guided Path
1
Read the Shia claim
Start by reading the opening lines: the Shia assert a clear text (nass jali) designating Ali as Imam, which is not open to interpretation.
Notice the claim is presented as an argument, not a fact.
2
Identify the three conditions for tawatur
The passage lists three conditions for a mutawatir report to yield knowledge: number/dispersion, sensory nature of the reported matter, and continuity across generations. Find and understand each.
These conditions are standard in kalam theory of testimony.
3
Examine the two proofs for the third condition
The Shia provide two proofs to show that the transmission chain has remained at tawatur level across generations. Read both carefully.
The first proof is based on current Shia reporting about their predecessors; the second is an argument from lack of contrary information.
4
Trace the conclusion
The passage concludes that tawatur is established, and therefore the imamate of Ali is certain. Recognize that this is the argument's final claim.
The author's own refutation is not included here; this lesson only covers the presentation of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة : قالوا : إنه نص على إمامة علي بن أبي طالب ا نصا جليا لا يقبل التأويل، فوجب القطع بكونه إمامًا، والذي يدل على وجود هذا النص الجلي : : أن هذا النص ثبت بخبر التواتر وخبر التواتر يفيد العلم. وإنما قلنا : إنه ثبت بخبر التواتر لأن خبر التواتر عن الأمور الماضية يعتبر في كونه مفيدا للعلم بثلاث شرائط : أولها : أن يكون المخبرون بلغوا في الكثرة والتفرق في البلاد إلى حيث يمتنع عقلا اتفاقهم على الكذب وهذا الشرط حاصل ههنا، وذلك لأن الشيعة مع كثرتهم وتفرقهم في مشارق الأرض ومغاربها يخبرون عن أن وجود هذا النص حاصل. وثانيها : أن يكون المخبرون إنما يخبرون عن أمر محسوس، وهذا الشرط حاصل ههنا، وذلك لأن المخبر عنه تنصيص محمد على إمامة علي، وذات محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وذات وعلي، وتكلم محمدصلى الله عليه وسلم بهذا الكلام أمر محسوس، وهذا الشرط ههنا حاصل. وثالثها : ان يكون حال المخبرين في كثرتهم وامتناع اتفاقهم على الكذب في الأزمنة مثل ما في هذا الزمان. والذي يدل على حصول هذا الشرط في هذه الواقعة وجهان: الأول : أن هؤلاء الشيعة مع كثرتهم وامتناع اجتماعهم على الكذب يخبرون أنه أخبرهم قوم شأنهم كذلك، وكذلك الطبقة الثانية والثالثة، وهكذا جميع الطبقات إلى زمان محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وعلي ال كانوا في الكثرة وامتناع اجتماعهم على الكذب على ما هم عليه في هذا اليوم ولما كان أهل زماننا بالغين إلى التواتر وهؤلاء قد أخبروا أن كل واحد مكن الطبقات الماضية كانوا هكذا حصل العلم بأن الطبقات الماضية كانوا على هذه الصفة. الثاني : لو فرضنا أن بعض الطبقات الماضية كانوا أقل من عدد التواتر لوجب أن يشتهر ذلك، وأن يصل هذا الخبر إلينا، ولما لم يصل هذا الخبر إلينا علمنا أن المخبرين عن هذا الخبر في جميع الطبقات كانوا موصوفين بالصفات المعتبرة في التواتر . فثبت بما ذكرنا حصول التواتر في نص رسول الله لا على إمامة علي بن أبي طالب، وخبر التواتر يفيد العلم، وذلك يفيد المطلوب.
The third objection: They said: There is a clear text (nass jaliy) designating the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib, not open to interpretation, so it is necessary to be certain that he is the Imam. That which indicates the existence of this clear text is that this text is established by mutawatir report, and mutawatir report yields knowledge. We say that it is established by mutawatir report because mutawatir report about past events is considered to yield knowledge subject to three conditions: First: The reporters must attain such number and dispersion across lands that it is rationally impossible for them to agree upon a lie. This condition is fulfilled here, because the Shia, with their numbers and dispersion in the East and West of the earth, report that this text exists. Second: The reporters must report about a sensory matter. This condition is fulfilled here, because the reported matter—Muhammad's designation of Ali as Imam, the bodies of Muhammad and Ali, and Muhammad's utterance of this speech—are sensory matters. This condition is fulfilled here. Third: The condition of the reporters in their numbers and impossibility of agreeing upon a lie across eras must be like that of the present time. That which indicates the fulfillment of this condition in this incident are two aspects: First: These Shia, with their numbers and impossibility of agreeing upon a lie, report that they were informed by people whose condition was similar, and likewise the second and third layers, and so all layers up to the time of Muhammad and Ali, were in numbers and impossibility of agreeing upon a lie similar to what they are today. Since the people of our time have reached tawatur, and these have reported that each of the past layers were like this, knowledge is obtained that the past layers were in this state. Second: If we suppose that some past layers were less than the number of tawatur, that would necessarily become well-known and reach us. Since this report has not reached us, we know that the reporters of this report in all layers were characterized by the conditions considered in tawatur. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that tawatur exists for the text of the Messenger of God designating Ali ibn Abi Talib as Imam, and mutawatir report yields knowledge, and that yields what is sought.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage presents the third Shia argument for the imamate of Ali, namely that there exists a clear textual designation (nass jali) from the Prophet Muhammad for Ali's imamate, and this designation is known through tawatur (massive transmission) which yields certainty. The author first states the claim: there is a clear text designating Ali, so his imamate is certain. To prove this, they argue that the text is established by mutawatir report, which yields knowledge. They then detail three conditions for tawatur regarding past events: (1) The reporters must be so numerous and dispersed that it is rationally impossible for them to agree on a lie; they claim this is true of the Shia across the world. (2) The reporters must report about a sensory matter; here the designation is sensory because it involves the Prophet's speech and the persons of Muhammad and Ali. (3) The continuous chain of reporters across generations must maintain the same characteristics of number and impossibility of collusion. They provide two proofs for the third condition: first, the current Shia report that their predecessors were similar in these qualities, and since we accept tawatur now, we must accept it for earlier generations; second, if any generation had fallen below tawatur, that would have become known, and since no such report reached us, the tawatur is intact. They conclude that tawatur is established for the text, and since mutawatir reports give certainty, the imamate of Ali is certain. This is a purely presentational passage of the Shia argument; no refutation is included in this segment.

Key Terms
نص جلي
تواتر
خبر التواتر
شرائط التواتر
إمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the passage is presenting the author's own view or that the Shia argument is accepted as true.
Correction: The passage is presenting the Shia argument for the purpose of refutation. The author does not endorse it; rather, it is an objection that will be answered later.

Checkpoint
What is the main basis for the Shia claim that the text designating Ali is certain?
A) It was written in the Quran
B) It is reported through tawatur (massive transmission)
C) It was witnessed by all companions
D) It was agreed upon by the community

Key Takeaway
The Shia argue that a clear text (nass jali) designating Ali as Imam is proven by mutawatir report, which satisfies three conditions and yields certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What would it cost you to seriously entertain the possibility that the Shia argument for Ali's imamate might be logically sound? Does fear of being wrong or of community disapproval prevent you from honestly evaluating such arguments? Examine your heart: is your certainty based on evidence or on inherited opinion?
?
The Shia claim certainty (al-‘ilm) from mutawatir report. What matters in your own faith do you hold with similar certainty, and how did you arrive at that certainty? Have you ever examined the basis of your deeply held beliefs as critically as this argument examines the designation of Ali?
?
After reading this passage, do you find the Shia argument compelling? If not, which of the three conditions do you consider not properly fulfilled? This lesson shows that the Shia have a structured case. Have you ever taken the time to understand arguments that oppose your own views, or do you typically dismiss them without examination?

The Fourth Shia Argument: Prophet's Necessity to Appoint a Successor
Presents the Shia argument that the Prophet must have appointed a successor using three reasons.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shia argument that the Prophet must have appointed a successor, based on three rational proofs derived from his practice and compassion.

Core Idea
The Prophet's consistent deputization and compassion demand he appointed a leader for after his death.

This passage presents the fourth Shia argument for the imamate of Ali. The author first clarifies that this argument differs from the second: here they accept that bay'a might be valid, but insist that the Prophet must have designated someone. They then provide three reasons to prove that the Prophet did designate a successor, focusing on his practice of appointing deputies and his compassion for the community.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the fourth argument
Read the opening lines where the author distinguishes this argument from the second. Note that here the possibility of bay'a is granted, but the focus is on the necessity of designation (nass).
The second argument denied bay'a as a valid method; this one accepts it but says designation overrides it.
2
Examine reason one: Deputization during absences
The Prophet always appointed a deputy when leaving Medina even for short periods. Since death is the greatest absence, he would certainly appoint a successor.
This is an argument from consistency and greater priority.
3
Examine reasons two and three: Parental compassion and extreme care
The Prophet compared himself to a parent who cares for children after death; leaving the community without a leader would cause them to be lost. Also, his teaching of even minor etiquettes shows his extreme care, so he would have addressed leadership.
The third reason is incomplete in the text but conveys the idea of extreme concern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : أن الإمام منصوص عليه من قبل رسول الله، ومتى كان الأمر أن يكون الإمام هو علي بن أبي طالب والنا. وجب والفرق بين هذه الشبهة والشبهة الثانية أنهم في الشبهة الثانية بينوا أنه لا يجوز أن تكون البيعة طريقاً إلى ثبوت الإمامة، وأما ههنا فإنهم لا يقولون: البيعة لا يجوز أن تكون طريقا إلى الإمامة بل قالوا هب أن البيعة يجوز أن تكون طريقا للإمامة إلا أنه صحت الدلالة على أن رسول الله ما نص على إمامة من يكون إماما بعده، ثم يستنتج من هذا أن ذلك المنصوص عليه لا بد وأن يكون هو علي. وإنما قلنا : إنه نص على إمامة من كان بعده لوجوه : الأول: أن النبي الا الله لم يخرج عن المدينة قط إلا قد استخلف عليها رجلًا، وإنما كان يفعل ذلك كيلا يختل أمر الرعية، وهذا المعنى بعد الموت أهم؛ لأن رعاية المصالح وقت الغيبة وإن كانت شاقة إلا أنها كانت ممكنة أما رعايتها بعد الموت فغير ممكنة، فلما لم يترك الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم الاستخلاف وقت الغيبة القليلة فكيف يجوز أن يتركه وقت الغيبة العظمى، وهي الموت؟! الثاني : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «إنما أنا لكم مثل الوالد لولده، فإذا ذهب أحدكم إلى الغائط فلا يستقبل القبلة ولا يستديرها الحديث فكما أنه يجب على الوالد المشفق رعاية مصالح أولاده حال حياته فكذا يجب عليه رعاية مصالحهم بعد مماته، لئلا يضيعوا ومعلوم أنه لو لم يستخلف ولم ينص على أحد لضاعوا في دينهم ودنياهم، فوجب القطع بأنه نص على من يكون إماما بعده. الثالث : أن النبي الله الله بالغ في الشفقة على الأمة وفي إرشادهم إلى الأصلح أن علمهم في كيفية الاستنجاء ثلاثين أدبا، ومن المعلوم أن المصالح المتعلقة
The fourth objection: That the Imam is designated by the Messenger of God, and when the matter is that the Imam is Ali ibn Abi Talib and the people... It is necessary. And the difference between this objection and the second objection is that in the second objection they explained that the pledge of allegiance (bay'a) cannot be a way to establish the imamate, but here they do not say that the bay'a cannot be a way to the imamate; rather they say, suppose the bay'a can be a way to the imamate, but it is established that the Messenger of God did not designate the imamate of anyone to be Imam after him, then from this it is concluded that the one who was designated must be Ali. We say that he designated the imamate of the one after him for reasons: First: The Prophet never left Medina without appointing a deputy over it, and he only did that so that the affairs of the subjects would not be disordered, and this meaning after death is more important, because taking care of interests during absence, though difficult, was possible, but taking care of them after death is impossible. Since the Prophet did not leave off appointing a deputy during a short absence, how is it permissible that he leaves it off during the great absence, which is death? Second: The Prophet said, 'I am to you like a parent to his child; when one of you goes to the toilet, do not face the qibla nor turn your back...' So just as a compassionate parent must take care of the interests of his children during his life, likewise he must take care of their interests after his death, lest they be lost. And it is known that if he did not appoint a deputy and did not designate anyone, they would be lost in their religion and their worldly affairs. Therefore it is necessary to conclude that he designated someone to be Imam after him. Third: The Prophet was extreme in compassion for the community and in guiding them to what is best; he taught them thirty etiquettes about how to clean themselves after relieving themselves, and it is known that the interests related to...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Shia argument for the imamate of Ali. The author first clarifies the difference between this argument and the second: the second argued that bay'a (pledge of allegiance) cannot establish the imamate, while this argument accepts that bay'a might be valid but insists that the Prophet must have designated someone, and since he did not designate anyone openly (according to Sunni view), the designated one must be Ali (as per Shia claim of a hidden designation). The argument then provides three reasons to prove that the Prophet did designate an Imam after him: (1) The Prophet consistently appointed a deputy whenever he left Medina even for a short time; since death is a greater absence, he would surely appoint a deputy for it. (2) The Prophet compared himself to a compassionate parent who cares for children both in life and after death; leaving the community without a leader would cause them to be lost in religion and worldly matters. (3) The Prophet taught even minor etiquettes like the proper way to clean after relieving oneself, showing his extreme care; thus he would have surely addressed the major issue of leadership. The third reason is cut off. This is a presentational passage of the Shia argument; no refutation is included in this segment.

Key Terms
الشبهة الرابعة
البيعة
الاستخلاف
النص
الشفقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage represents the author's own view and the Sunni position.
Correction: This passage is presenting the Shia argument; the author is an Ash'ari theologian who will refute it later. The text is a presentation of the opponent's view.

Checkpoint
The fourth Shia argument claims that the Prophet must have appointed a successor because:
A) The Quran commands it explicitly
B) He always appointed deputies when leaving Medina, so he would do so for the greater absence of death
C) Ali asked him to do so
D) The community voted for Ali

Key Takeaway
The Shia argue that the Prophet's actions and teachings necessitate his appointment of a successor, with Ali being the designated one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Shia argue that a compassionate leader would not leave his community without guidance after death. If you truly believe the Prophet was the most compassionate, how confident are you that you follow all the guidance he left? What are you doing to ensure you do not stray after your current teachers are gone?
?
The Prophet taught thirty etiquettes about cleaning himself, yet according to this argument, he might have neglected the most important issue of succession. Does this make you reconsider the completeness of the Prophet's message? How do you personally reconcile the Shia claim with your own belief in the Prophet's perfection?
?
If you knew you were leaving your family tomorrow, would you leave without appointing someone to care for them? The Shia use this analogy for the Prophet. Does this argument make you feel a sense of responsibility for how you prepare those you lead? What steps are you taking to ensure your legacy of guidance continues?

The Fourth Shubha: Prophet Must Designate a Successor
Razi presents the fourth Shia argument with four proofs for prophetic designation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure and logic of the fourth Shia argument for the imamate of Ali, based on the claim of explicit prophetic designation.

Core Idea
The Shia argue that the Prophet's practice and compassion necessitate explicit designation of a successor.

This passage presents the fourth of ten Shia objections discussed by Imam Razi. It argues that the Prophet must have explicitly designated a successor, and that successor can only be Ali. The Shia offer four proofs from the Prophet's conduct and teachings to support this claim. We will examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction from the second shubha
Note how the Shia differentiate this argument from the second one: here they concede bay'a could be valid, but insist the Prophet's designation overrides it.
This shows a strategic concession to strengthen their case.
2
Examine the four proofs
Read each proof carefully: (1) appointment of deputies when leaving Medina, (2) parental compassion, (3) detailed teaching on minor matters, (4) completion of religion.
Each proof uses an analogy from the Prophet's known behavior.
3
Follow the logical conclusion
After the proofs, the argument concludes that the designated person cannot be Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, so it must be Ali.
The exclusion is based on the claim that if it were Abu Bakr, his reliance on bay'a would be a sin.
4
Note the rhetorical structure
Remember that Razi is presenting the Shia view to refute it later. This is not his own position.
This is crucial for understanding the broader context of the book.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة : أن الإمام منصوص عليه من قبل رسول الله، ومتى كان الأمر أن يكون الإمام هو علي بن أبي طالب والنا. وجب والفرق بين هذه الشبهة والشبهة الثانية أنهم في الشبهة الثانية بينوا أنه لا يجوز أن تكون البيعة طريقاً إلى ثبوت الإمامة، وأما ههنا فإنهم لا يقولون: البيعة لا يجوز أن تكون طريقا إلى الإمامة بل قالوا هب أن البيعة يجوز أن تكون طريقا للإمامة إلا أنه صحت الدلالة على أن رسول الله ما نص على إمامة من يكون إماما بعده، ثم يستنتج من هذا أن ذلك المنصوص عليه لا بد وأن يكون هو علي. وإنما قلنا : إنه نص على إمامة من كان بعده لوجوه : الأول: أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يخرج عن المدينة قط إلا قد استخلف عليها رجلًا، وإنما كان يفعل ذلك كيلا يختل أمر الرعية، وهذا المعنى بعد الموت أهم؛ لأن رعاية المصالح وقت الغيبة وإن كانت شاقة إلا أنها كانت ممكنة أما رعايتها بعد الموت فغير ممكنة، فلما لم يترك الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم الاستخلاف وقت الغيبة القليلة فكيف يجوز أن يتركه وقت الغيبة العظمى، وهي الموت؟! الثاني : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : «إنما أنا لكم مثل الوالد لولده، فإذا ذهب أحدكم إلى الغائط فلا يستقبل القبلة ولا يستديرها الحديث فكما أنه يجب على الوالد المشفق رعاية مصالح أولاده حال حياته فكذا يجب عليه رعاية مصالحهم بعد مماته، لئلا يضيعوا ومعلوم أنه لو لم يستخلف ولم ينص على أحد لضاعوا في دينهم ودنياهم، فوجب القطع بأنه نص على من يكون إماما بعده. الثالث : أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بالغ في الشفقة على الأمة وفي إرشادهم إلى الأصلح أن علمهم في كيفية الاستنجاء ثلاثين أدبا، ومن المعلوم أن المصالح المتعلقة بالإمامة التي هي أعظم المصالح في الدين والدنيا بعد الرسالة، أعظم من سائر المصالح، فلما علَّم في أخس المصالح وأدونها رتبة - وهو الاستنجاء - ثلاثين أدبا فكيف يليق به أن يرسل أمر الإمامة بالكلية مع أنها أعظم المناصب في الدين والدنيا ؟ الرابع : أنه لم يخرج من الدنيا حتى صار أمر الدين كاملا، كما قال تعالى : اليَوْمَ أَكْمَلْتُ لَكُمْ دِينَكُمْ وَأَتْمَمْتُ عَلَيْكُمْ نِعْمَتِي ﴾ [المائدة: ٣] والإمامة أعظم أركان الدين، فهذا يقتضي أن أمر الإمامة قد تم قبل وفاته. وهذا المعنى إنما يحصل إذا قلنا : إنه نص على إمامة شخص معين، فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أنه نص حال حياته على إمامة من يكون إمامًا بعده, ونقول : ذلك الشخص لا يجوز أن يكون هو أبا بكر، إذ لو كان كذلك لكان توقيفه الأمر على البيعة من أعظم المعاصي، وذلك يقدح في إمامته، ولما ثبت أنه ليس أبا بكر ولا العباس ثبت أنه علي لئلا يخرج الحق عن قول كل الأئمة.
The fourth objection: That the imam is explicitly designated (manṣūṣ ʿalayh) by the Messenger of Allah, and when the matter is that the imam is ʿAlī ibn Abī Ṭālib... it is necessary. And the difference between this objection and the second objection is that they in the second objection explained that it is not permissible for the pledge of allegiance (bayʿa) to be a path to the establishment of the imamate. But here they do not say that the pledge of allegiance is not permissible as a path to the imamate; rather they said: Suppose the pledge of allegiance is permissible as a path to the imamate, but the evidence has been established that the Messenger of Allah did not explicitly designate (naṣṣ) the imamate of whoever would be imam after him. Then from this it is concluded that that designated person must be ʿAlī. And we say: He did explicitly designate the imamate of whoever was after him, for reasons: First: That the Prophet (peace be upon him) never left Medina except that he appointed a deputy over it, and he only did that so that the affairs of the subjects would not become disordered. And this meaning after death is more important; because tending to interests during absence, though difficult, was possible, but tending to them after death is not possible. So when the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not leave delegation during a short absence, how could it be permissible for him to leave it during the great absence, which is death? Second: That the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'I am like a father to you. When one of you goes to the lavatory, let him not face the qibla nor turn his back to it...' So just as a compassionate father must attend to the interests of his children during his life, so must he attend to their interests after his death, lest they be lost. And it is known that if he did not delegate nor designate anyone, they would be lost in their religion and their world. So it is necessary to be certain that he designated whoever would be imam after him. Third: That the Prophet (peace be upon him) was extreme in compassion for the nation and in guiding them to what is most beneficial, to the point that he taught them thirty etiquettes concerning the method of cleaning after relieving oneself. And it is known that the interests related to the imamate, which are the greatest interests in religion and world after prophethood, are greater than other interests. So when he taught thirty etiquettes concerning the lowest and meanest of interests, namely cleaning after relieving oneself, how could it befit him to completely neglect the matter of the imamate, despite its being the greatest station in religion and world? Fourth: That he did not leave this world until the matter of religion became complete, as Allah says: 'This day I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you' (al-Māʾida 5:3). And the imamate is the greatest pillar of religion. This requires that the matter of the imamate was completed before his death. And this meaning only obtains if we say: He explicitly designated the imamate of a specific person. So it is established by the totality of what we have mentioned that he explicitly designated during his life the imamate of whoever would be imam after him. And we say: That person cannot be Abū Bakr, because if it were so, his leaving the matter to the pledge of allegiance would be one of the greatest sins, and that would impugn his imamate. And since it is established that it is not Abū Bakr nor al-ʿAbbās, it is established that it is ʿAlī, so that the truth is not outside the saying of all the imams.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth Shia objection (shubha) regarding the imamate of ʿAlī. It begins by stating the core claim: the imam is explicitly designated (manṣūṣ ʿalayh) by the Prophet, and since that designation must point to ʿAlī, it is necessary that he is the imam. The text then distinguishes this objection from the second one: in the second objection, they argued that the pledge of allegiance (bayʿa) cannot be a valid path to establish the imamate. Here, they concede that even if bayʿa were valid, the Prophet's explicit designation would override it, but they assert that the Prophet did not designate anyone else—thus the designation must be for ʿAlī. The Shia then provide four proofs (wujūh) that the Prophet must have designated a successor: (1) The Prophet always appointed a deputy when leaving Medina, so he would not neglect it at death; (2) His fatherly compassion requires he appoint a successor to prevent the community's ruin; (3) He taught even minor etiquettes like istinjāʾ, so he would not neglect the greatest matter of imamate; (4) The completion of religion demands that imamate was settled before his death. After these proofs, they conclude that the designated person cannot be Abū Bakr (because if it were, his reliance on bayʿa would be a sin) nor al-ʿAbbās, so it must be ʿAlī. The logic is directed to force the conclusion that only ʿAlī fits the prophetic designation.

Key Terms
نص (naṣṣ)
إمامة (imāma)
بيعة (bayʿa)
منصوص عليه (manṣūṣ ʿalayh)
توقيف (tawqīf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage represents Razi's own view.
Correction: Razi is presenting the Shia argument to refute it later in the book. This is not his own position.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth shubha, why does the Shia argue that the designated person cannot be Abu Bakr?
A) Abu Bakr was not qualified for the imamate.
B) Abu Bakr's reliance on the pledge of allegiance would be a sin if the Prophet had designated him.
C) The Prophet explicitly said Abu Bakr is not the imam.
D) The Prophet designated Ali directly.

Key Takeaway
The fourth Shia argument relies on the necessity of prophetic designation and concludes that Ali is the designated imam, using four analogical proofs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe the Prophet was the most compassionate of all people, how do you reconcile the possibility that he might have left the greatest matter of leadership unsettled? What does your answer reveal about your trust in his wisdom and your own assumptions about what is 'necessary'?
?
The Shia argue that the Prophet taught thirty etiquettes for istinjāʾ but allegedly didn't designate a successor. Where in your own life do you focus on minor details while neglecting major responsibilities? What concrete step will you take today to realign your priorities with what truly matters?
?
The completion of religion verse is used here to argue that imamate was settled before the Prophet's death. Do you live as if your religion is complete and sufficient, or do you feel something is missing? What would change in your daily practice if you fully internalized that Allah has perfected His favor upon you?

The Fifth Shubha: The Best Must Be Imam
Razi presents the fifth Shia argument that Ali must be imam because he is best.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fifth Shia objection for ʿAlī’s imamate and the rational analogy used to support the premise that the best must be the imam.

Core Idea
If the best can be made a follower, then reason is offended.

In this passage, Razi presents the fifth Shia argument for ʿAlī’s imamate. The argument rests on two claims: that ʿAlī is the best of creation after the Prophet, and that the best person must be the imam. The second claim is defended with an analogy that appeals to what is rationally repugnant—making a superior person subordinate to an inferior one.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of the fifth shubha
The argument has two premises and a conclusion. Identify each part in the passage.
The first premise is deferred; focus on the second premise and analogy.
2
Analyze the rational analogy
Read the school analogy carefully. Why is it repugnant to make al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa sit under a mediocre teacher? How does this support the second premise?
This analogy is a qiyās (analogical reasoning).
3
Connect premises to conclusion
See how if both premises are true, the conclusion that ʿAlī is the imam follows necessarily. Consider what assumptions are being made.
The Sunni response will challenge the first premise and the universality of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : أن عليّا أفضل الخلق بعد الرسول الله والأفضل يجب يكون إماما أما المقدمة الأولى : فسيجيء تفسيرها بعد ذلك، وأما المقدمة الثانية: فالدليل على صحتها أن من جعل إمامًا لغيره فقد جعل متبوعا لذلك الغير، وجعل الأكمل تبعًا للأنقص قبيح في العقول؛ ألا ترى أنه من بنى مدرسة فجعل واحدًا من أوساط الفقهاء مدرسا فيها، ثم أمر الشافعي وأبا حنيفة أن يجلسا في درسه، وأن يكونا من أتباعه وأشياعه فإن كل واحد يذمه عليه ويلومه ويقول: إنك جعلت الكامل تبعا للناقص وهذا قبيح في العقول، وإذا ثبت أن عليا أفضل الخلق وثبت أن الأفضل هو الإمام ثبت أن عليا هو الإمام بعد رسول الله الله.
The fifth objection: That ʿAlī is the best of creation after the Messenger of Allah, and the best must be imam. As for the first premise, its explanation will come later. As for the second premise, the proof of its validity is that whoever appoints an imam over others has made him followed by that other, and making the more perfect a follower of the less perfect is repugnant in the intellect. Do you not see that whoever builds a school and makes one of the mediocre jurists a teacher in it, then orders al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa to sit in his lesson and be among his followers and partisans—everyone would blame him and reproach him and say: 'You have made the perfect a follower of the imperfect, and this is repugnant in the intellect.' And if it is established that ʿAlī is the best of creation and it is established that the best is the imam, then it is established that ʿAlī is the imam after the Messenger of Allah.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth Shia objection. It is built on two premises: first, that ʿAlī is the best of all people after the Prophet (the explanation of which is deferred); second, that the best person must be the imam. The proof for the second premise uses a rational analogy (qiyās): if someone builds a school and appoints a mediocre jurist as teacher, then orders the great scholars al-Shāfiʿī and Abū Ḥanīfa to be his students, this would be universally condemned as making the superior follow the inferior—an act repugnant to reason. Similarly, if ʿAlī is the best, he cannot be made to follow a lesser imam; therefore, he must be the imam himself. The conclusion is that ʿAlī is the imam after the Prophet.

Key Terms
أفضل (afḍal)
إمام (imām)
متبوع (matbūʿ)
تابع (tābiʿ)
قبيح (qabīḥ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if someone is the best, they automatically should be the imam in every respect.
Correction: The Sunni response distinguishes between merit and actual appointment; the best may not be the imam if other considerations (like consultation or community choice) prevail.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the fifth shubha use to prove that the best must be the imam?
A) A king appointing a minister.
B) A school where a mediocre teacher instructs superior scholars.
C) A father teaching his son.
D) A judge ruling over a case.

Key Takeaway
The fifth shubha argues that since ʿAlī is the best after the Prophet, he must be the imam, because making the perfect follow the imperfect is rationally repugnant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt resentment toward a leader or teacher you believed was less capable than you? This passage declares such a situation 'repugnant to the intellect.' How does your emotional reaction align with or contradict this rational judgment? What does your response reveal about your own pride and your need to submit to Allah's order even when it seems imperfect?
?
The argument here depends on the premise that ʿAlī is the best. But what if you were to apply this logic to your own life? Who is the 'best' person you know? Would you be willing to follow them completely? Why or why not? Does your hesitation show that the argument is more complex than it seems?
?
If you were to take this argument seriously, you would have to conclude that the imam must be the most perfect person. But who decides that? Is it your own judgment or Allah's designation? This passage challenges you to examine: Are you more comfortable trusting your own assessment of who is best, or are you willing to accept Allah's choice even if it seems to make the less perfect a leader? Which approach leads to true submission?

The Sixth Shubha: Ali's Superior Qualities as Proof of Imamate
Razi presents the sixth Shia argument for Ali's imamate based on his superior qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the sixth Shia argument for Ali's imamate, which relies on his superior knowledge, courage, and closeness to the Prophet.

Core Idea
Three superior qualities make Ali the most qualified to be the imam.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents the sixth Shia objection (shubha) regarding the imamate of Ali. The argument claims that Ali's superior knowledge, courage, and closeness to the Prophet make him the rightful successor. This lesson breaks down the argument's premises and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three claims
Read the passage and note the three qualities attributed to Ali: being the most knowledgeable (aʿlam), the bravest (ashjaʿ), and the most attached to the Prophet (ashadd al-tisāqā).
The first two are claimed but their proof is deferred to later.
2
Understand the evidence for closeness
See how the argument supports closeness through lineage (nasab) and marriage (ṣihriyya), which are presented as obvious.
3
Grasp the logical link to imamate
The argument states that the imam is the one who is followed and imitated (yuʾtammu bih, yaqtadī bih), and this requires knowledge and courage. Thus Ali, having these in the highest degree, is most worthy.
This step connects the qualities to the definition of imam.
4
Recognize the underlying principle
The shubha assumes that the most qualified person should lead. Later responses will challenge the uniqueness of these qualities to Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : قالوا : إن عليّا أعلم الصحابة وأشجعهم وأشدهم التصاقا برسول الله ، فوجب أن يكون هو القائم مقامه أما أنه أعلم وأشجع فسيأتي الدليل عليه وأما أنه أشدهم التصاقا برسول الله فهو ظاهر أما من حيث النسب فلا شك فيه، وأما من حيث الصهرية فكذلك وإذا ثبت هذا وجب يكون هو الإمام ؛ لأن الإمام هو الذي يؤتم به ويقتدي به وهذا لا يتأتى إلا بسبب العلم والشجاعة، وإذا كان علمه وشجاعته أكثر كان الاقتداء به والاهتداء بهداه أولى.
The sixth objection: They said: ʿAlī is the most knowledgeable of the Companions, the bravest, and the most attached to the Messenger of Allah. So he must be the one to succeed him. As for his being most knowledgeable and bravest, the proof will come later. As for his being most attached to the Messenger of Allah, it is apparent: as for lineage, there is no doubt; and as for affinity by marriage, likewise. And if this is established, then he must be the imam, because the imam is the one who is followed and imitated, and this is only attained through knowledge and courage. And when his knowledge and courage are greater, following him and being guided by his guidance are more appropriate.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth Shia objection. It argues that ʿAlī possesses three superior qualities: knowledge, courage, and closeness to the Prophet (through lineage and marriage). The first two are claimed but proof is deferred. The third is said to be obvious. Because the imam is the one whom the community follows and takes as a model—something that requires knowledge and courage—and ʿAlī has these in the highest degree, he is the most deserving to be the imam after the Prophet. The argument relies on the principle that the most qualified should lead.

Key Terms
أعلم (aʿlam)
أشجع (ashjaʿ)
أشد التصاقا (ashadd al-tisāqā)
يؤتم به (yuʾtammu bih)
يقتدي به (yaqtadī bih)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that this argument is an exhaustive proof that Ali is the rightful imam, assuming that the qualities are unique to him.
Correction: The argument merely asserts these qualities without proof for knowledge and courage, and the uniqueness is assumed. The Sunni response will challenge whether these qualities are indeed exclusive to Ali.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth shubha, why does closeness to the Prophet support Ali's imamate?
A) Because only a relative can succeed the Prophet.
B) Because the imam must be closely related to the Prophet to understand his teachings.
C) Because lineage and marriage are mentioned as evidence of his attachment, which together with knowledge and courage make him the best model.
D) Because the Prophet explicitly said so.

Key Takeaway
The sixth shubha argues that Ali's superior knowledge, courage, and closeness to the Prophet make him the most qualified imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you choose a leader—whether in your community, workplace, or personal life—do you weight knowledge and courage more heavily than personal connection or shared background? What does your actual behavior reveal about your priorities? This lesson forces you to ask: are your criteria for following someone based on merit or comfort?
?
Imagine you are in 7th-century Medina, and you must decide who should lead the Muslims after the Prophet. Would you be swayed more by a person’s closeness to the Prophet (by blood or marriage) or by their demonstrated knowledge and bravery? Could your own biases toward those you feel 'attached' to blind you to a more qualified candidate? How does this lesson expose the tension between loyalty to kin and loyalty to merit?

The Istinja' Argument: Completeness of Guidance
Argues from divine thoroughness on minor acts to necessity of specifying imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that God's detailed guidance on even minor acts like istinja' implies He must have explicitly designated an imam.

Core Idea
God's teaching of thirty etiquettes for cleaning shows He would not neglect the imamate.

This lesson examines a Shi'i proof from the completeness of divine guidance. The argument draws an analogy between the detailed instructions for istinja' and the supposed silence on imamate, concluding that silence is inconceivable. We'll explore the logic and its presuppositions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author uses an analogy between a lowly act and a high matter.
2
Identify the analogy
Identify the two things being compared: the detailed teaching for istinja' and the supposed lack of designation for imamate. Note the rhetorical question that concludes the argument.
The analogy is based on contrast: if God taught so much for a minor act, how could He leave the major act unspecified?
3
Evaluate the logical structure
Consider the implicit premises: (1) God teaches what is necessary, (2) imamate is more important than istinja', so it must be taught. Ask if this reasoning is sound.
4
Reflect on the argument's context
Understand that this is presented as a Shi'i proof that the Prophet must have designated an imam. It assumes that imamate is a religious matter that requires divine guidance.
Later in the text, this argument will be refuted from a Sunni perspective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون بالإمامة التي هي أعظم المصالح في الدين والدنيا بعد الرسالة، أعظم من سائر المصالح، فلما علَّم في أخس المصالح وأدونها رتبة - وهو الاستنجاء - ثلاثين أدبا فكيف يليق به أن يرسل أمر الإمامة بالكلية مع أنها أعظم المناصب في الدين والدنيا ؟ الرابع : أنه لم يخرج من الدنيا حتى صار أمر الدين كاملا، كما قال تعالى : الْيَوْمَ أَكْمَلْتُ لَكُمْ دِينَكُمْ وَأَتْمَمْتُ عَلَيْكُمْ نِعْمَتِي [المائدة: ٣] والإمامة أعظم أركان الدين، فهذا يقتضي أن أمر الإمامة قد تم قبل وفاته. وهذا المعنى إنما يحصل إذا قلنا : إنه نص على إمامة شخص معين، فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أنه نص حال حياته على إمامة من يكون إمامًا بعده, ونقول : ذلك الشخص لا يجوز أن يكون هو أبا بكر، إذ لو كان كذلك لكان توقيفه الأمر على البيعة من أعظم المعاصي، وذلك يقدح في إمامته، ولما ثبت أنه ليس أبا بكر ولا العباس ثبت أنه علي لئلا يخرج الحق عن قول كل الأئمة.
The thirty-ninth issue: By the imamate which is the greatest of interests in religion and this world after prophethood, greater than all other interests. So when He taught concerning the basest of interests and the lowest rank – namely istinja' – thirty etiquettes, how could it be fitting for Him to leave the matter of imamate entirely, while it is the greatest of positions in religion and this world? Fourth: That he (the Prophet) did not depart from this world until the matter of religion became complete, as He said: 'This day I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you' (Al-Ma'idah 5:3). And the imamate is the greatest pillar of religion, so this necessitates that the matter of imamate was completed before his death. And this meaning is only obtained if we say that he explicitly designated a specific person for imamate. So it is established by all that we have mentioned that he explicitly designated during his life the imamate of the one who would be imam after him. And we say: that person cannot be Abu Bakr, because if it were so, his leaving the matter to a pledge of allegiance would be among the greatest sins, and that would detract from his imamate. And since it is established that it is not Abu Bakr nor al-Abbas, it is established that it is Ali, lest the truth exit from the saying of all the imams.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents a Shi'i proof for the necessity of explicit designation (nass) of the imam by the Prophet. The argument is structured as a fourth proof in a series. It begins by noting that God taught even the lowly act of istinja' with thirty etiquettes, so it is inconceivable that He would neglect to specify the imamate, which is the greatest interest after prophethood. This is an argument from the completeness of divine guidance. Then it presents a fourth sub-argument: the Prophet did not die until religion was perfected (Quran 5:3). Since imamate is the greatest pillar, its specification must have been completed before his death, implying explicit designation. It then concludes that the designated person cannot be Abu Bakr because he left the matter to election (bay'ah), which would be a sin, nor al-Abbas. Therefore it must be Ali. The author is presenting this as a Shi'i argument that will later be refuted.

Key Terms
الإمامة
النص
الاستنجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why does the detailed teaching of istinja' imply that the imamate must have been explicitly designated?
A) Because istinja' and imamate are equally important.
B) Because God taught many etiquettes for a lowly act, so He would not neglect the greatest matter.
C) Because the Prophet himself taught istinja' but not imamate.
D) Because the Quran mentions istinja' but not imamate.

Key Takeaway
God's detailed guidance on minor acts like istinja' implies that He must have also provided clear guidance on the imamate, the greatest interest after prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God has provided detailed guidance for the basest of acts (istinja'), how can you justify ignoring or being indifferent to the question of who should lead the Muslim community after the Prophet? Does your complacency reflect a lack of trust in God's complete guidance?
?
Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and being asked about the matter of imamate. Will you say that you followed what was clear to you, or will you admit that you never seriously investigated it because it seemed less important than your daily routines? What will your answer be?
?
The passage argues that God's completeness in teaching minor etiquettes implies His completeness in specifying major matters. How does your own attitude toward learning about the imamate compare to your eagerness to learn the details of istinja'? Are you consistent in seeking divine guidance for all aspects of life?

The Perfection of Religion and the Conclusion that Imam is Ali
Uses perfection of religion verse to argue for explicit designation of imam, concluding it is Ali.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that the perfection of religion before the Prophet's death necessitates explicit designation of an imam, and why the author concludes it must be Ali.

Core Idea
Religion was perfected, imamate is its greatest pillar, so the imam was designated — and it is Ali.

This lesson continues the fourth proof, now using the Quranic verse on the perfection of religion. The argument links the completion of religion to the necessity of specifying the imam, then eliminates other candidates to conclude it is Ali. We'll trace the logical steps and the conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice how the argument builds from a Quranic verse to a specific conclusion.
The verse cited is Quran 5:3, often used in Islamic theology to indicate the completion of revelation.
2
Identify the key premises
Identify the two main premises: (1) religion was perfected before the Prophet's death, (2) imamate is the greatest pillar of religion. Then see how they lead to the necessity of explicit designation.
3
Analyze the elimination process
The author eliminates Abu Bakr and al-Abbas as possible designees. Understand the reasoning: if Abu Bakr were designated, his leaving the matter to election would be a sin; since it is not so, he is not the designated one. Similarly for al-Abbas.
This elimination leads to the conclusion that it must be Ali.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Notice that this is a deductive argument: perfection + imamate's importance → designation; then elimination of other candidates → Ali. Consider the assumptions about what constitutes designation.
This is a Shi'i argument; in Sunni theology, the perfection of religion is interpreted differently.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون بالإمامة التي هي أعظم المصالح في الدين والدنيا بعد الرسالة، أعظم من سائر المصالح، فلما علَّم في أخس المصالح وأدونها رتبة - وهو الاستنجاء - ثلاثين أدبا فكيف يليق به أن يرسل أمر الإمامة بالكلية مع أنها أعظم المناصب في الدين والدنيا ؟ الرابع : أنه لم يخرج من الدنيا حتى صار أمر الدين كاملا، كما قال تعالى : الْيَوْمَ أَكْمَلْتُ لَكُمْ دِينَكُمْ وَأَتْمَمْتُ عَلَيْكُمْ نِعْمَتِي [المائدة: ٣] والإمامة أعظم أركان الدين، فهذا يقتضي أن أمر الإمامة قد تم قبل وفاته. وهذا المعنى إنما يحصل إذا قلنا : إنه نص على إمامة شخص معين، فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أنه نص حال حياته على إمامة من يكون إمامًا بعده, ونقول : ذلك الشخص لا يجوز أن يكون هو أبا بكر، إذ لو كان كذلك لكان توقيفه الأمر على البيعة من أعظم المعاصي، وذلك يقدح في إمامته، ولما ثبت أنه ليس أبا بكر ولا العباس ثبت أنه علي لئلا يخرج الحق عن قول كل الأئمة.
The thirty-ninth issue: By the imamate which is the greatest of interests in religion and this world after prophethood, greater than all other interests. So when He taught concerning the basest of interests and the lowest rank – namely istinja' – thirty etiquettes, how could it be fitting for Him to leave the matter of imamate entirely, while it is the greatest of positions in religion and this world? Fourth: That he (the Prophet) did not depart from this world until the matter of religion became complete, as He said: 'This day I have perfected for you your religion and completed My favor upon you' (Al-Ma'idah 5:3). And the imamate is the greatest pillar of religion, so this necessitates that the matter of imamate was completed before his death. And this meaning is only obtained if we say that he explicitly designated a specific person for imamate. So it is established by all that we have mentioned that he explicitly designated during his life the imamate of the one who would be imam after him. And we say: that person cannot be Abu Bakr, because if it were so, his leaving the matter to a pledge of allegiance would be among the greatest sins, and that would detract from his imamate. And since it is established that it is not Abu Bakr nor al-Abbas, it is established that it is Ali, lest the truth exit from the saying of all the imams.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents a Shi'i proof for the necessity of explicit designation (nass) of the imam by the Prophet. The argument is structured as a fourth proof in a series. It begins by noting that God taught even the lowly act of istinja' with thirty etiquettes, so it is inconceivable that He would neglect to specify the imamate, which is the greatest interest after prophethood. This is an argument from the completeness of divine guidance. Then it presents a fourth sub-argument: the Prophet did not die until religion was perfected (Quran 5:3). Since imamate is the greatest pillar, its specification must have been completed before his death, implying explicit designation. It then concludes that the designated person cannot be Abu Bakr because he left the matter to election (bay'ah), which would be a sin, nor al-Abbas. Therefore it must be Ali. The author is presenting this as a Shi'i argument that will later be refuted.

Key Terms
الإمامة
النص
البيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the perfection of religion (Quran 5:3) refers only to legal rulings and does not imply anything about political leadership.
Correction: The Shi'i author argues that since imamate is the greatest pillar of religion, its specification must have been part of the perfection, hence the verse implies explicit designation.

Checkpoint
According to this argument, why can Abu Bakr not be the designated imam?
A) Because he was not present at the Prophet's death.
B) Because he left the matter to a pledge of allegiance, which would be a sin if he were already designated.
C) Because the Quran explicitly rejects him.
D) Because Ali was more knowledgeable.

Key Takeaway
The perfection of religion verse, combined with the status of imamate as the greatest pillar, is used to argue that the Prophet explicitly designated Ali as imam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah perfected this religion and completed His favor, then the matter of leadership must have been settled by divine command. Have you ever questioned whether your allegiance is based on personal preference or on a sincere search for the truth as presented in the Qur'an and Sunnah? Will you commit to re-examining your stance with an open heart, even if it means challenging long-held beliefs?
?
The author eliminates candidates until only Ali remains. This forces a decisive choice: are you ready to accept a position that may be unpopular or against the majority, if the evidence points to it? What is preventing you from seeking and following the truth regardless of the cost to your comfort or social standing?
