The Argument from Superior Virtue
Shi'i argument for Ali's imamate via two premises and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-premise argument that the most virtuous person must be the imam, and the rational analogy used to support the second premise.

Core Idea
Making a less virtuous person leader over a more virtuous one is rationally repugnant.

This passage presents a key Shi'i argument for the imamate of Ali based on his superior virtue. It is structured as a syllogism: Ali is the most virtuous, and the most virtuous must be the imam. The proof for the second premise relies on a compelling analogy that resonates with common sense. Let us examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the formal structure of a shubah (doubt/argument).
The passage is dense but logically layered.
2
Identify the two premises
Find where the author states 'As for the first premise' and 'As for the second premise.' Confirm each premise's content.
The first premise is deferred to a later explanation.
3
Analyze the analogy for premise two
Examine the school analogy: a mediocre jurist made teacher over al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa. Why is this 'repugnant in intellects'?
The analogy is meant to show that making the less perfect a leader over the more perfect is irrational.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Combine the two premises to derive the conclusion: since Ali is most virtuous, he must be imam after the Prophet.
5
Reflect on the underlying assumption
Consider what this argument assumes about the criteria for imamate. Is virtue the only factor?
This reflection helps understand the sectarian different.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الخامسة : أن عليّا أفضل الخلق بعد الرسول الله والأفضل يجب يكون إماما. أما المقدمة الأولى : فسيجيء تفسيرها بعد ذلك، وأما المقدمة الثانية: فالدليل على صحتها أن من جعل إمامًا لغيره فقد جعل متبوعا لذلك الغير، وجعل الأكمل تبعًا للأنقص قبيح في العقول؛ ألا ترى أنه من بنى مدرسة فجعل واحدًا من أوساط الفقهاء مدرسا فيها، ثم أمر الشافعي وأبا حنيفة أن يجلسا في درسه، وأن يكونا من أتباعه وأشياعه فإن كل واحد يذمه عليه ويلومه ويقول: إنك جعلت الكامل تبعا للناقص وهذا قبيح في العقول، وإذا ثبت أن عليا أفضل الخلق وثبت أن الأفضل هو الإمام ثبت أن عليا هو الإمام بعد رسول الله الله.
The fifth doubt: That Ali is the most virtuous of creation after the Messenger of God, and the most virtuous must be imam. As for the first premise, its explanation will come later. As for the second premise, the proof of its validity is that whoever appoints an imam over others has made someone followed by those others, and making the more perfect a follower of the less perfect is repugnant in intellects. Do you not see that if someone builds a school and makes one of the mediocre jurists a teacher in it, then orders al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa to sit in his lesson and to be his followers and partisans, then every person would blame him and censure him and say: 'You have made the perfect a follower of the deficient, and this is repugnant in intellects.' And when it is established that Ali is the most virtuous of creation, and it is established that the most virtuous is the imam, then it is established that Ali is the imam after the Messenger of God.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth shubah (doubt/argument) from the Shi'i perspective. It is structured as a two-premise syllogism. Premise one: Ali is the most virtuous after the Prophet. Premise two: The most virtuous must be the imam. The proof for premise two uses an illustrative analogy: if a school founder appoints a mediocre jurist as teacher and orders eminent scholars like al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa to be his followers, that is universally deemed repugnant. Similarly, making a less virtuous person imam over a more virtuous one is rationally repugnant. Therefore, since Ali is the most virtuous, he must be the imam. The first premise is said to be proven later. The argument aims to establish imamate based on superiority in virtue.

Key Terms
الأفضل
الإمامة
المتبوع
التابع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the most virtuous person must necessarily be the imam in every context, with no other criteria.
Correction: This argument assumes virtue is the sole criterion for imamate. Sunnis hold that other factors such as Qurayshi lineage and leadership ability also matter, and the most virtuous is not always the imam.

Checkpoint
What analogy does the fifth shubah use to argue that the most virtuous must be imam?
A) A king appointing a vizier.
B) A school founder making a mediocre jurist teacher over al-Shafi'i and Abu Hanifa.
C) A father choosing a guardian for his children.
D) A general leading an army.

Key Takeaway
The fifth shubah argues that because Ali is the most virtuous, he must be the imam, using an analogy that making the perfect follower of the deficient is rationally repugnant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you genuinely believe that the most virtuous person must lead, who would you choose as your imam today? What does that choice reveal about your own virtue or lack thereof?
?
Think of a leader you follow in your life (religious, political, or personal). Is that person more virtuous than you? If not, what excuses do you make for following them? Will this argument change your standards?
?
If you reject this argument, you must provide a counter-reason. Is your rejection based on scripture, reason, or blind tradition? Are you willing to put your rejection to the test of rational scrutiny as the author did?

The Sixth Shubah: Ali's Superiority and Imamate
Presents a shubah arguing Ali's three superior qualities necessitate his imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument of the sixth shubah, which claims that Ali's superior knowledge, bravery, and closeness to the Prophet make him the rightful imam.

Core Idea
Ali's knowledge, bravery, and closeness to the Prophet make him the most worthy of being followed.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents the sixth doubt (shubah) raised by those who argue for Ali's immediate succession. The argument is based on three qualities: knowledge, bravery, and attachment to the Prophet. Razi leaves the proof for knowledge and bravery for later, focusing here on the logical conclusion that the one with these qualities must be the imam because the imam is the one who is followed and emulated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument is structured: first three qualities are claimed, then the conclusion is drawn.
The passage is the sixth shubah – a doubt or argument from the opponent.
2
Identify the three qualities attributed to Ali
Find and list the three qualities: being the most knowledgeable (أعلم), the bravest (أشجع), and the most attached to the Prophet (أشدهم التصاقا). Note how attachment is further justified by lineage and marriage.
The proof for knowledge and bravery is deferred to later.
3
Trace the logical flow to the conclusion
The passage argues: because the imam is the one who is followed (يؤتم به), and following requires knowledge and bravery, and Ali has them in the highest degree, then he is the most deserving of being imam.
This is a straightforward deduction: if imamate means being followed, the best follower is the most knowledgeable and brave.
4
Reflect on the strength of this shubah
Consider how this argument might be answered. Does it prove that knowledge and bravery alone qualify for imamate? Are there other necessary qualities? This prepares you for the reply that follows in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السادسة : قالوا : إن عليّا أعلم الصحابة وأشجعهم وأشدهم التصاقا برسول الله ، فوجب أن يكون هو القائم مقامه. أما أنه أعلم وأشجع فسيأتي الدليل عليه. وأما أنه أشدهم التصاقا برسول الله فهو ظاهر أما من حيث النسب فلا شك فيه، وأما من حيث الصهرية فكذلك. وإذا ثبت هذا وجب يكون هو الإمام ؛ لأن الإمام هو الذي يؤتم به ويقتدي به وهذا لا يتأتى إلا بسبب العلم والشجاعة، وإذا كان علمه وشجاعته أكثر كان الاقتداء به والاهتداء بهدي أولى.
The sixth doubt: They said: Indeed Ali is the most knowledgeable of the Companions and the bravest and the most attached to the Messenger of God, so it is necessary that he be the one who stands in his place. As for him being the most knowledgeable and bravest, the proof will come later. And as for him being the most attached to the Messenger of God, it is apparent; as for lineage, there is no doubt about it, and as for marriage relationship, likewise. And when this is established, it is necessary that he be the imam, because the imam is the one who is followed and emulated, and this is only achieved through knowledge and bravery. And when his knowledge and bravery are greater, then emulating him and being guided by his guidance are more appropriate.
Source pages p. 434

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth shubah, arguing that Ali's superior qualities necessitate his imamate. It claims three qualities for Ali: most knowledgeable, bravest, and most attached to the Prophet. The proof for knowledge and bravery is deferred. The attachment is argued to be evident in lineage (cousin) and marriage (son-in-law). The conclusion is that he must be the imam because the imam is the one to be followed, and following requires knowledge and bravery. Greater knowledge and bravery make him more worthy of being followed.

Key Terms
أعلم
أشجع
الاقتداء
الصهرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can be a good imam even without superior knowledge and bravery; piety alone suffices.
Correction: The passage argues that the imam is the one who is followed, and following requires knowledge and bravery. Without these, people cannot properly emulate the imam. So these qualities are essential.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth shubah, what two qualities are essential for the imam to be followed?
A) Wealth and lineage.
B) Knowledge and bravery.
C) Piety and age.
D) Eloquence and strength.

Key Takeaway
The sixth shubah argues that Ali's superior knowledge, bravery, and closeness to the Prophet make him the most deserving of being the imam because the imam is the one who is followed and such following requires those qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Companion hearing this argument, would your personal admiration for Ali's knowledge and bravery have swayed you to accept his immediate imamate? What does that reveal about how easily your emotions can cloud your judgment in matters of truth?
?
Have you ever followed someone simply because they appeared more knowledgeable or courageous, without critically examining whether they truly deserved your allegiance? What was the outcome, and what would you do differently now after understanding this shubah?
?
This shubah presents a compelling case, yet it is a doubt that needs an answer. When you encounter arguments that appeal to your loyalties—whether to a person, group, or idea—how do you ensure you do not accept them uncritically? Write down one practical step you will take to guard against being misled by seemingly strong but incomplete arguments.

The Argument from Obedience for Imam's Infallibility
Argument for imam's infallibility from Quran 4:59

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the command to obey 'ulu al-amr' in Quran 4:59 is used to argue for the infallibility of the imam, and the logical steps of this argument.

Core Idea
Absolute obedience to imams demands their infallibility.

This lesson unpacks a key Shi'a proof for the imam's infallibility rooted in the Quran. Imam Razi presents the seventh doubt (shubah) which reasons that since Allah commands absolute obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr), it would be contradictory for them to ever command disobedience. Thus, the imam must be infallible. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to understand the argument's flow.
Notice the verse citation (4:59) and the logical phrase 'if it were permissible...'.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts from the premise that God commands absolute obedience to 'those in authority' (uli al-amr). This is the foundation of the reasoning.
The phrase 'أمراً جازماً' indicates a decisive command.
3
Understand the logical implication
If uli al-amr could command disobedience, then God would be commanding us to disobey Him, which is impossible. Therefore, uli al-amr must only command obedience.
This is a modus tollens argument: if P then Q, not Q, so not P.
4
Conclude the necessity of infallibility
Since uli al-amr only command obedience, they must be infallible (ma'sum). The verse thus implies that the imam—the highest authority—must be infallible.
The passage ends by noting that those who hold this view (Shi'a) identify the infallible imam as 'Ali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله : أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم [النساء : ٥٩] أمر بطاعة أولي الأمر أمراً جازماً، فوجب على أولي الأمر أن لا يأمروا إلا بالطاعة، إذ لو جاز أن يأمروا بالمعصية مع أن الله تعالى أمرنا بطاعتهم مطلقا لكان قد أمرنا - على هذا التقدير - بالمعصية، وذلك باطل. إذا ثبت هذا علمنا أن أولي الأمر الذين أمرنا الله بطاعتهم لا يأمرون إلا بالطاعة، وهذا يقتضي أن المراد بأولي الأمر في هذه الآية شخص واجب العصمة، فثبت أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وكل من قال بذلك قال إنه علي والنا[؟].
The seventh doubt: His saying: 'Obey God and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (An-Nisa 4:59). He commanded obedience to those in authority (uli al-amr) with a decisive command. So it is necessary that those in authority do not command except obedience, because if it were permissible for them to command disobedience while God Almighty commanded us to obey them absolutely, then it would have been – on this assumption – that He commanded us to disobedience, and that is false. When this is established, we know that those in authority whom God commanded us to obey do not command except obedience. And this necessitates that what is meant by 'those in authority' in this verse is a person who is necessarily infallible (wajib al-isma). So it is established that the imam must be necessarily infallible, and everyone who said that said that he is Ali and [؟].
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh shubah, which argues for the imam's infallibility (isma) from the Quranic verse of obedience (4:59). The reasoning is: God commands absolute obedience to uli al-amr. If they could command disobedience, then God would be commanding us to disobey Him, which is impossible. Therefore, uli al-amr must only command obedience, meaning they are infallible. Hence the imam must be infallible, and according to the Shi'a, this applies to Ali.

Key Terms
أولي الأمر
الطاعة
العصمة
واجب العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse refers to any ruler, and obedience to them is unconditional.
Correction: Sunnis generally hold that obedience to rulers is conditional on their commands not contradicting God's commands; the verse is interpreted as referring to those who are just.

Checkpoint
Why does the seventh shubah argue that uli al-amr must be infallible?
A) Because they are prophets.
B) Because absolute obedience to them implies they cannot command sin.
C) Because the verse mentions them after the Prophet.
D) Because they are from the Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
The command to obey 'those in authority' in Quran 4:59, if taken absolutely, logically requires that those authorities be infallible, which Shi'a theology applies to Imam 'Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to follow anyone with absolute obedience, would that person need to be infallible? Who in your life comes closest to that? How does this verse make you feel about the trust you place in your leaders?
?
Do you ever excuse a leader's sin because you feel obligated to obey? How does this argument challenge you to either demand purity from your leaders or question your own understanding of obedience?
?
Is there any human whom you would obey even if they commanded something against God's law? If not, then how do you reconcile the absolute command of this verse with your own conscience?

Eighth Shubah: Permanent Disqualification from Imamate
Argues that prior disbelief permanently disqualifies from imamate using Quran 2:124.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that a single moment of disbelief permanently disqualifies a person from the imamate, based on Quran 2:124 and the linguistic scope of negation.

Core Idea
A single moment of disbelief permanently bars one from the imamate.

This lesson examines the eighth shubah, which argues that Abu Bakr, Umar, and al-Abbas were disqualified from the caliphate because they were once disbelievers. The argument hinges on the interpretation of God's covenant in the Quran and the scope of its negation for wrongdoers. We will trace the logical steps that lead to the conclusion that Ali alone was fit for the imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full literal translation. Let the argument's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Note that the argument includes Umar even though the title only mentions Abu Bakr and al-Abbas.
2
Identify the key Quranic verse
Locate Quran 2:124 in the passage and understand how it is used: God's covenant (interpreted as imamate) does not reach the wrongdoers.
The argument interprets 'covenant' as the imamate.
3
Trace the reasoning from disbelief to disqualification
Follow the steps: disbeliever = wrongdoer (Quran 31:13, 2:254) → wrongdoers excluded from covenant → exclusion applies at the moment of wrongdoing and extends to all future times.
Linguistic rule: exception (e.g., 'not ... except') implies the negation covers all times unless specified.
4
Consider the common misunderstanding
Reflect on the Sunni counterargument: that the covenant only excludes wrongdoers at the time of the covenant, not permanently. This is the misconception this passage guards against.
The passage argues that 'does not reach' is a permanent state.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : أن أبا بكر والعباس ما كانا صالحين للإمامة، فتعين أن يكون الإمام هو علي. إنما قلنا إنهما ما كانا صالحين وذلك لأنهما كانا كافرين في أول الأمر، وكل من كفر بالله طرفة عين لم يصلح للإمامة، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: وَإذِ ابْتَلَى إِبْرَاهِيمَ رَبُّهُ بِكَلِمَاتٍ فَأَتَمَّهُنَّ قَالَ إِنِّي جَاعِلُكَ لِلنَّاسِ إِمَامًا قَالَ وَمِن ذُرِّيَّتِي قَالَ لا يَنَالُ عَهْدِي الظَّالِمِينَ [البقرة : ١٢٤]. وجه الاستدلال به أن لفظ العهد مطلق، وذكر الإمامة قد جرى قبل ذلك، فوجب أن يكون المراد من هذا العهد هو الإمامة. إذا ثبت هذا فتقول: كل من كان كافرًا فهو ظالم، لقوله تعالى : إِنَّ الشِّرْكَ لَظُلْمٌ عَظِيمٌ [لقمان: ۱۳] ولقوله تعالى: وَالْكَافِرُونَ هُمُ الظَّالِمُونَ [البقرة : ٢٥٤]. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كل شخص اتصف بالكفر فهو في تلك الحالة يتصف بالظلم، فكان في ذلك الوقت موصوفًا بأنه ظالم، فوجب أن يصدق عليه في ذلك الوقت أنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة، وقولنا بأنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة كلام يتناول الوقت الخاص الحاضر، وجملة الأوقات المستقبلة، بدليل صحة استثناء جميع الأوقات منه، فيقال: إن هذا الظالم، والكافر لا ينال عهد الإمامة لا بعد كفره ولا بعد ظلمه، والاستثناء يخرج من الكلام ما لولاه لدخل تحت اللفظ فثبت أن الكافر حال ما كان موصوفا بكفره صدق عليه أنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة البتة في شيء من أوقات وجوده لا حال كفره، ولا بعد كفره . وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أن كل من كفر بالله طرفة عين فإنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة بمقتضى هذه الآية، وثبت أن أبا بكر وعمر والعباس كانوا قبل ظهور دين محمد على الكفر، فوجب أن يخرجوا عن صلاحية الإمامة، وإذا خرجوا عن هذه الصلاحية تعينت علي للإمامة.
The eighth doubt: That Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were not fit for the imamate, so it is determined that the imam is Ali. We only say that they were not fit because they were disbelievers at the beginning, and whoever disbelieved in God for the blink of an eye is not fit for the imamate. The proof is His saying: 'And when his Lord tested Ibrahim with words and he fulfilled them, He said: I am making you a leader (imam) for the people. He said: And from my offspring? He said: My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' (Al-Baqarah 2:124). The point of inference is that the word 'covenant' is absolute, and the mention of imamate preceded it, so it must be that what is meant by this covenant is the imamate. When this is established, we say: Everyone who was a disbeliever is a wrongdoer, due to His saying: 'Indeed, shirk is great wrongdoing' (Luqman 31:13) and His saying: 'And the disbelievers are the wrongdoers' (Al-Baqarah 2:254). And when this is established, we say: Every person who is described by disbelief is in that state described by wrongdoing, so at that time he was characterized as a wrongdoer, so it must be validly said of him at that time that he does not attain the covenant of imamate. And our saying that he does not attain the covenant of imamate is a statement that encompasses the specific present time and all future times, due to the validity of excepting all times from it. So it is said: This wrongdoer and disbeliever does not attain the covenant of imamate neither after his disbelief nor after his wrongdoing. And the exception removes from the speech what would otherwise be included under the wording. So it is established that the disbeliever, at the moment he is described by his disbelief, it is true of him that he does not attain the covenant of imamate at all in any of the times of his existence, neither during his disbelief nor after his disbelief. And when this is established, it is established that whoever disbelieves in God for the blink of an eye does not attain the covenant of imamate according to this verse. And it is established that Abu Bakr, Umar, and al-Abbas were before the emergence of Muhammad's religion upon disbelief, so it is necessary that they exit from fitness for imamate, and when they exit from this fitness, it is determined for Ali for the imamate.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth shubah, a lengthy argument disqualifying Abu Bakr, Umar, and al-Abbas from the imamate based on their prior disbelief. The key proof is Quran 2:124, where God tells Ibrahim that His covenant (interpreted as imamate) does not reach the wrongdoers. The argument proceeds: 1) The covenant is imamate. 2) Disbelievers are wrongdoers (Quran 31:13, 2:254). 3) A disbeliever at any moment is a wrongdoer at that moment. 4) The denial of the covenant applies to the specific time of wrongdoing and extends to all future times because exceptions cannot be made for later times. Thus, if someone was ever a disbeliever, even for an instant, they are permanently disqualified from the imamate. Since Abu Bakr, Umar, and al-Abbas were disbelievers before Islam, they are disqualified, leaving Ali as the only candidate. Note that Umar is included in this argument, though earlier only Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were mentioned.

Key Terms
عهد
ظالم
كافر
طرفة عين
صلاحية الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some Sunnis think that past disbelief does not permanently disqualify one from imamate because the covenant only excludes wrongdoers at the time of the covenant, not later after repentance.
Correction: The passage argues that the negation in 'does not reach' applies to all times, not just the moment of wrongdoing, based on the linguistic rule that exceptions from a negated statement remove the negation for the excepted time, implying the negation covers all times.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth shubah, why does a previous state of disbelief permanently disqualify someone from the imamate?
A) Because wrongdoers are never forgiven.
B) Because the covenant of imamate is denied to wrongdoers, and the denial covers all times, not just the moment of wrongdoing.
C) Because the Prophet said so.
D) Because Abu Bakr and Umar were not from the Quraysh.

Key Takeaway
A single instant of disbelief, according to this argument, permanently disqualifies a person from the imamate based on Quran 2:124 and the linguistic scope of negation.

?

?
How does the interpretation of 'covenant' in this passage challenge you to examine your own biases when interpreting religious texts? In what areas of your life might you be applying an absolute standard to others that you do not apply to yourself?
?
If this argument were true, what would it mean for your own standing before God? Are you willing to be judged by the same standard of permanent disqualification for any past sin? How does that make you feel about God's mercy?

Seventh Shubuha: Argument from Obedience to Ulu al-Amr
Presents the seventh Shi'a argument for the infallible imam using Quran 4:59.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the seventh Shi'a argument for an infallible imam based on Quran 4:59.

Core Idea
Absolute command to obey implies those commanded must be infallible.

Imam Razi presents the seventh objection from Shi'a theology. This argument uses the Quranic verse commanding obedience to 'those in authority' to argue that the imam must be infallible. We will examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice the logical flow from the Quranic command to the conclusion about infallibility.
2
Identify the premise
The verse commands obedience to 'those in authority' (ulu al-amr) with an absolute, unqualified command (amr jazim).
3
Examine the implication
If those in authority could command disobedience, then the absolute command to obey them would entail that God commands disobedience, which is impossible.
4
Understand the conclusion
Therefore, those in authority must be infallible (ma'sum). The Shi'a identify this infallible figure as Ali and his descendants.
This conclusion is the core of the shubuha.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة السابعة : قوله : أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكر [النساء : ٥٩]، أمر بطاعة أولي الأمر أمرًا جازماً، فوجب على أولي الأمر أن لا يأمروا إلا بالطاعة، إذ لو جاز أن يأمروا بالمعصية مع أن الله تعالى أمرنا بطاعتهم مطلقا لكان قد أمرنا - على هذا التقدير - بالمعصية، وذلك باطل. إذا ثبت هذا علمنا أن أولي الأمر الذين أمرنا الله بطاعتهم لا يأمرون إلا بالطاعة، وهذا يقتضي أن المراد بأولي الأمر في هذه الآية شخص واجب العصمة، فثبت أن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة، وكل من قال بذلك قال إنه علي والنا.
The seventh doubt: His saying: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' [Quran 4:59]. He commanded obedience to those in authority with a decisive command. So it necessarily follows that those in authority should not command except obedience. For if it were permissible for them to command disobedience while Allah the Exalted has commanded us to obey them absolutely, then, on this assumption, He would have commanded us to disobedience, and that is invalid. Once this is established, we know that those in authority whom Allah has commanded us to obey only command obedience, and this requires that what is meant by 'those in authority' in this verse is a person who is infallible. So it is established that the imam must be infallible, and everyone who said that said that it is Ali and [?].
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh Shi'a argument (shubuha) for the imamate. It is an argument from the Quranic verse 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' (4:59). The argument proceeds as follows: The command to obey those in authority (ulu al-amr) is absolute and unqualified. Therefore, those in authority must only command obedience; if they could command disobedience, then the absolute command to obey them would entail that God sometimes commands disobedience, which is impossible. Hence, those in authority must be infallible. Since the Shi'a hold that only Ali and his descendants are infallible, they conclude that the intended imam is Ali. The author, Imam Razi, presumably will refute this later, but here he presents the argument in full.

Key Terms
الشبهة
أولي الأمر
واجب العصمة
الإمام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is simply about obeying any ruler.
Correction: The argument specifically hinges on the absolute nature of the command, which forces the conclusion of infallibility.

Checkpoint
What is the key conclusion of the seventh shubuha regarding the imam?
A) The imam must be a descendant of the Prophet.
B) The imam must be infallible.
C) The imam must be from the Quraysh.
D) The imam must be chosen by consultation.

Key Takeaway
The seventh shubuha argues that the absolute command to obey 'those in authority' necessitates their infallibility, thus the imam must be infallible, identified by Shi'a as Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever given absolute obedience to a leader, teacher, or ideology, only to realize they were fallible? How does this argument challenge you to scrutinize the authorities you follow in your life? What concrete step can you take this week to ensure your ultimate loyalty remains with Allah and His Messenger?
?
This passage claims that absolute obedience implies the one obeyed must be infallible. Think of someone you obey nearly unquestioningly—perhaps a parent, spouse, or spiritual guide. Are they infallible? If not, what does this lesson demand you change about how you relate to them? Write down one specific boundary you will set.
?
The Shi'a used this verse to identify Ali as the infallible imam. Are you prepared to accept that only Allah and His Messenger deserve absolute, unconditional obedience? What does that mean for your allegiance to any human authority, including your own desires? Commit to one practical example where you will prioritize divine command over human expectation.

The Eighth Shubuha: Disqualification for Past Kufr
The eighth Shi'a argument using Quran 2:124 to disqualify former disbelievers from the imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth Shi'a argument uses Quran 2:124 to argue that anyone who was ever a disbeliever is permanently disqualified from the imamate.

Core Idea
A single moment of past kufr forever bars one from the imamate, according to this argument.

This lesson presents the eighth doubt (shubuha) raised by the Shi'a against the caliphates of Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and al-'Abbas. The argument hinges on interpreting the 'covenant' in Quran 2:124 as the imamate and applying it to all wrongdoers, including those who were once disbelievers. Understanding this argument is crucial for analysing the Sunni response that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, a Qur'anic proof, and a chain of reasoning.
The argument is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the core claim and proof
Find the main claim: Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were unfit because they were once disbelievers. Then locate the Qur'anic proof: verse 2:124.
The verse is about Abraham and the covenant.
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace how the argument moves from 'wrongdoer' to 'disbeliever' and then to the temporal scope of the denial. Note the use of 'absolute' wording and exclusion.
The key move is that the denial applies to all times, including after repentance.
4
Consider the conclusion
The conclusion is that only 'Ali, who never disbelieved, can be imam. Reflect on how this challenges Sunni positions.
The refutation comes later; for now, understand the argument as presented.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثامنة : أن أبا بكر والعباس ما كانا صالحين للإمامة، فتعين أن يكون الإمام هو علي، إنما قلنا إنهما ما كانا صالحين وذلك لأنهما كانا كافرين في أول الأمر، وكل من كفر بالله طرفة عين لم يصلح للإمامة، والدليل عليه قوله تعالى: وإذ ابتلى إبراهيم ربه بكلمات فأتمهن قال إني جاعلك للناس إماما قال ومن ذريتي قال لا ينال عهدي الظالمين [البقرة : ١٢٤] وجه الاستدلال به أن لفظ العهد مطلق، وذكر الإمامة قد جرى قبل ذلك، فوجب أن يكون المراد من هذا العهد هو الإمامة. إذا ثبت هذا فتقول: كل من كان كافرًا فهو ظالم، لقوله تعالى : إن الشرك لظلم عظيم [لقمان: ۱۳] ولقوله تعالى: والكافرون هم الظالمون [البقرة : ٢٥٤] وإذا هذا فنقول : كل شخص اتصف بالكفر فهو في تلك الحالة يتصف بالظلم، فكان في ذلك الوقت موصوفًا بأنه ظالم، فوجب أن يصدق عليه في ذلك الوقت أنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة، وقولنا بأنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة كلام يتناول الوقت الخاص الحاضر، وجملة الأوقات المستقبلة، بدليل صحة استثناء جميع الأوقات منه، فيقال: إن هذا الظالم، والكافر لا ينال عهد الإمامة لا بعد كفره ولا بعد ظلمه، والاستثناء يخرج من الكلام ما لولاه لدخل تحت اللفظ فثبت أن الكافر حال ما كان موصوفا بكفره صدق عليه أنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة البتة في شيء من أوقات وجوده لا حال كفره، ولا بعد كفره . وإذا ثبت هذا ثبت أن كل من كفر بالله طرفة عين فإنه لا ينال عهد الإمامة بمقتضى هذه الآية، وثبت أن أبا بكر وعمر والعباس الله كانوا قبل ظهور دين محمد على الكفر، فوجب أن يخرجوا عن صلاحية الإمامة، وإذا خرجوا عن هذه الصلاحية تعين عليها للإمامة.
The eighth doubt: That Abu Bakr and al-‘Abbas were not fit for the imamate, so it is determined that the imam is ‘Ali. We only say that they were not fit because they were disbelievers in the beginning, and whoever disbelieves in Allah for the blink of an eye is not fit for the imamate. The proof for that is His saying: 'And when Abraham was tested by his Lord with words and he fulfilled them, He said: I will make you an imam for mankind. He said: And of my offspring? He said: My covenant does not include the wrongdoers' [Quran 2:124]. The aspect of inference from it is that the word 'covenant' is absolute, and mention of the imamate had preceded it, so it is necessary that what is meant by this covenant is the imamate. If this is established, then we say: Everyone who is a disbeliever is a wrongdoer, due to His saying: 'Indeed, polytheism is a great wrongdoing' [Quran 31:13] and His saying: 'And the disbelievers are the wrongdoers' [Quran 2:254]. And if this is so, we say: Every person who is described with disbelief is in that state described with wrongdoing, so he was at that time described as a wrongdoer. So it must be true of him at that time that he does not attain the covenant of the imamate. And our saying that he does not attain the covenant of the imamate is a statement that includes the specific present time and all future times, as evidenced by the validity of excluding all times from it. So it is said: This wrongdoer and disbeliever does not attain the covenant of the imamate neither after his disbelief nor after his wrongdoing. And exclusion removes from the statement what would otherwise be included under the wording. So it is established that the disbeliever, while he is described with his disbelief, it is true of him that he does not attain the covenant of the imamate at all in any of the times of his existence, neither during his disbelief nor after his disbelief. And if this is established, it is established that whoever disbelieves in Allah for the blink of an eye does not attain the covenant of the imamate by virtue of this verse. And it is established that Abu Bakr, ‘Umar, and al-‘Abbas - may Allah [be pleased with them?] - were before the emergence of the religion of Muhammad upon disbelief. So it is necessary that they are excluded from the suitability for the imamate. And if they are excluded from this suitability, then ‘Ali is determined for the imamate.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth Shi'a argument, which uses Quran 2:124 to argue that those who were ever disbelievers cannot become imams. The argument is: The verse declares that God's covenant (interpreted as the imamate) does not include wrongdoers (zalimin). Since disbelief is a form of wrongdoing, a one-time disbeliever is a wrongdoer at that time and thus excluded from the covenant forever. Therefore, anyone who was ever a disbeliever (like Abu Bakr, 'Umar, and al-'Abbas before their conversion) is permanently disqualified. Only 'Ali, whom Shi'a believe never disbelieved, is fit for the imamate. The author, Imam Razi, will likely refute this by arguing that the covenant refers to the covenant of prophethood or that forgiveness removes the status of wrongdoing, but here he merely presents the argument.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الكفر
الظلم
عهد الإمامة
طرفة عين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse only denies the imamate to those who are currently wrongdoers or will become wrongdoers, not to those who repented.
Correction: The argument insists the verse denies the covenant absolutely to anyone who was ever a wrongdoer, regardless of later repentance, based on the 'absolute' scope of the denial and the fact that exclusion of all times is valid.

Checkpoint
According to the eighth shubuha, who is disqualified from the imamate?
A) Only those who are currently disbelievers.
B) Anyone who has ever committed polytheism, even if after repentance.
C) Anyone who has ever been a disbeliever, even for a moment.
D) Only those who die as disbelievers.

Key Takeaway
The eighth shubuha argues that past kufr, even for an instant, permanently disqualifies a person from the imamate by invoking the absolute wording of Quran 2:124.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed someone's potential for good because of their past sins? How does this lesson's argument—and its underlying assumption about permanent disqualification—challenge you to reconsider your own judgments about others? What would it take for you to truly believe that God's mercy can erase past wrongs?
?
Imagine if every mistake you made in the past permanently disqualified you from ever being trusted again. How would that feel? Yet this is the logic of the eighth shubuha. Does this make you more grateful for God's forgiveness, or more cautious about how you view those who have repented? What change in your heart does this reflection demand?
?
The argument uses a verse of the Quran as a proof for an absolute, unforgiving principle. When have you used religious texts to justify a harsh stance toward someone? Are you willing to examine your own interpretations with the same critical eye this lesson applies to the shubuha? What would it mean to be both truthful to the text and compassionate to people?

The Inconsistency of the Eighth Shubuha
Razi points out the self-defeating implications of the eighth shubuha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi exposes a logical inconsistency in the eighth shubuha regarding Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The eighth shubuha would invalidate the consensus on the caliphs if applied consistently.

In this brief but dense passage, Imam Razi turns the eighth shubuha on its head. He shows that the very logic used to disqualify Abu Bakr and al-‘Abbas would also disqualify ‘Ali if he had ever disbelieved, which would undermine the Sunni consensus on the first caliph. This forces a reevaluation of the interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
Pay attention to how Razi uses the same verse to derive two opposite implications.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Trace the logical steps: (1) The verse indicates Ali is imam. (2) It therefore implies he never disbelieved. (3) If he had disbelieved, he would be disqualified. (4) The same condition disqualifies Abu Bakr and al-Abbas. (5) Then all three are disqualified, breaking consensus.
3
Reflect on the role of consensus
Razi treats consensus (ijma') as an infallible authority. Because attacking consensus is invalid, the conclusion that Ali never disbelieved is necessary to salvage the consensus.
This shows how kalam arguments often appeal to established Sunni principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن هذه الآية كما دلت على أن عليا هو الإمام بعد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فكذلك تدل على أنه لم يكفر طرفة عين بالله ؛ لأنه لو كان قد كفر بالله في أول عمره لزم بحكم هذه الآية أن لا يكون أهلا للإمامة، فثبت أيضًا أن أبا بكر والعباس ليسا أهلا للإمامة بمقتضى هذه الآية فيلزم خروج أبي بكر والعباس وعلي عن أهلية الإمامة، ويكون إجماع الأمة على أن الإمام بعد رسول الله أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة باطلاً، ولما كان الطعن في الإجماع فاسدًا وثبت أن أبا بكر والعباس كانا قد كفرا بالله قبل ظهور دين محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ثبت أن عليا انه لم يكفر بالله قط، حتى لا يلزم الطعن في الإجماع.
And know that this verse, just as it indicates that ‘Ali is the imam after the Prophet (peace be upon him), likewise indicates that he never disbelieved in Allah for the blink of an eye. For if he had disbelieved in Allah in his early life, it would be necessary by the ruling of this verse that he not be fit for the imamate. So it is also established that Abu Bakr and al-‘Abbas are not fit for the imamate by virtue of this verse. Then it would necessitate the exclusion of Abu Bakr, al-‘Abbas, and ‘Ali from the fitness for the imamate, and the consensus of the ummah that the imam after the Messenger of Allah is one of these three would be invalid. And since attacking consensus is corrupt, and it is established that Abu Bakr and al-‘Abbas had disbelieved in Allah before the emergence of the religion of Muhammad (peace be upon him), it is established that ‘Ali never disbelieved in Allah at all, so that attacking consensus is not necessitated.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage is a commentary by Imam Razi on the eighth shubuha. He points out a logical consequence: If the verse (2:124) is used to disqualify Abu Bakr and al-'Abbas due to past kufr, then it would also disqualify 'Ali if he had ever been a disbeliever. But since the Sunni consensus (ijma') holds that the imam after the Prophet was one of those three (Abu Bakr, 'Umar, 'Uthman, or 'Ali?), if the verse leads to all three being disqualified, that would contradict the valid consensus. To preserve the consensus, we must accept that the verse does not apply in that way, or that 'Ali never disbelieved. But the Shi'a claim that 'Ali never disbelieved, so for them, the verse supports 'Ali and rejects the others. However, Razi is showing that the argument is self-defeating if 'Ali also had a past of disbelief (which Sunnis do not claim). Actually, Razi seems to be using the argument to show that the verse does not disqualify those who later become believers, because otherwise 'Ali would also be disqualified if he had ever been an idolater before Islam. Since the consensus of the ummah is that the imamate after the Prophet was held by Abu Bakr, etc., the interpretation must be that the verse refers to wrongdoers who persist, not those who repent. The passage is a brief refutation within the presentation.

Key Terms
إجماع الأمة
أهلية الإمامة
طعن في الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this passage is part of the eighth shubuha itself, presenting a Shi‘a argument.
Correction: In fact, Razi is offering his own commentary to show that the eighth shubuha leads to an unacceptable consequence, thereby refuting it.

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi argue would happen if the eighth shubuha's interpretation of the verse were applied to both ‘Ali and Abu Bakr?
A) Both would be qualified for imamate.
B) The consensus on the early caliphs would be invalidated.
C) The verse would be abrogated.
D) ‘Ali would be the only eligible imam.

Key Takeaway
The eighth shubuha, if applied strictly, would contradict the consensus on the early caliphs, revealing its invalidity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you defend a belief, are you willing to follow its logic even where it leads to uncomfortable conclusions? Have you ever held a position that, if applied consistently, would contradict other truths you accept? What did you do?
?
Consensus (ijma') is a pillar of Sunni theology. In your family, community, or workplace, do you prioritize unity over winning an argument? What would it cost you to yield for the sake of harmony?
?
Razi exposes a flaw in reasoning by showing its self-destructive outcome. Is there an area of your life—your habits, assumptions, or relationships—where you refuse to think through the full consequences because doing so would demand change? Will you examine it today?

Ninth Shubuha: The Argument from 'Being with the Truthful'
Presents the ninth Shi'a argument using Quran 9:119 to require an infallible imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a argument from Quran 9:119 for the necessity of an infallible imam, and how it identifies the imam as Ali.

Core Idea
A command to be with the truthful implies there must be a known infallible guide.

This lesson presents the ninth doubt (shubuha) raised by Shi'a theologians. They invoke Quran 9:119, which commands believers to 'be with the truthful,' arguing that this order requires the existence of someone who is certainly truthful and therefore infallible. The passage then concludes that this infallible person is Ali, based on earlier arguments. Understanding this argument is crucial for engaging with Shi'a theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the original Arabic and its literal translation to grasp the structure of the argument. Notice the logical flow: verse → command → condition → conclusion.
The passage is concise but dense.
2
Identify the scriptural basis
The argument is based on Quran 9:119, which commands believers to fear Allah and be with the truthful. Identify how the Shi'a interpret 'the truthful' as referring to a specific infallible individual.
The key term is 'الصادقين' (the truthful).
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the argument: First, the verse commands being with the truthful. Second, this command is only meaningful if there is someone known to be truthful with certainty. Third, such certainty requires infallibility. Fourth, therefore, an infallible imam is necessary.
This is a conditional-deductive argument.
4
Consider the conclusion linking to Ali
The passage briefly states that the imam is Ali, referencing earlier arguments. Think about why the Shi'a specifically identify Ali as the infallible truthful person.
This conclusion is asserted without proof here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة التاسعة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : يا أيها الذين آمنوا اتقوا الله وكونوا مع الصادقين [التوبة : ۱۱۹] أمرنا بالكون مع الصادقين، والأمر بالكون مع الصادقين مشروط بوجود من يعلم قطعا أنه من الصادقين هو الذي يعلم منه كونه واجب العصمة، فثبت أن الخلق مأمورون بمتابعة شخص واحد واجب العصمة، وإذا ثبت ذلك ثبت أن الإمام وهو علي بالوجه الذي قررناه بالطرق المتقدمة.
The ninth doubt: Adherence to His saying: 'O you who have believed, fear Allah and be with the truthful' [Quran 9:119]. He commanded us to be with the truthful, and the command to be with the truthful is conditional upon the existence of one who is known with certainty to be among the truthful — that is, one from whom it is known that he is infallible. So it is established that the creation are commanded to follow a single person who is infallible. And if that is established, it is established that the imam is ‘Ali, in the manner we have set forth by the preceding methods.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth Shi'a argument. It uses Quran 9:119, which commands believers to 'be with the truthful' (al-sadiqin). The argument states that this command requires the existence of someone known with certainty to be truthful, which implies infallibility. Therefore, there must be an infallible imam for the community to follow. The Shi'a then claim that this infallible person is 'Ali, based on earlier arguments (which are not detailed here). The argument is presented as a proof for the necessity of an infallible imam, and that this imam is 'Ali.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الصادقين
واجب العصمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the verse simply encourages being in the company of righteous people generally, without necessitating a single infallible leader.
Correction: The Shi'a argument interprets 'the truthful' as a specific individual whose truthfulness is known with certainty, which they argue implies infallibility. The command would be impossible to fulfill if no such person existed.

Checkpoint
What condition does the ninth shubuha claim for the command to be with the truthful?
A) That there be many truthful people.
B) That there be someone known with certainty to be truthful and infallible.
C) That the truthful are the scholars.
D) That the truthful are the Companions collectively.

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a argue that Quran 9:119 necessitates an infallible imam, identified as Ali, by requiring believers to follow a person known with certainty to be truthful.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you follow any human leader—scholar, teacher, or guide—with the same certainty that this argument demands for ‘the truthful’? If not, what does that say about the strength of your conviction? If yes, how do you justify that certainty?
?
The argument says the command ‘be with the truthful’ is meaningless unless there is a person known for sure to be truthful. Who in your life do you know for sure is truthful? What would it take for you to trust someone that completely?
?
If you encountered a person who claimed to be the infallible guide like Ali, would you be willing to follow them unconditionally? What would you be afraid of losing if you did?

The Tenth Shi'a Argument: Priority of Kinship
Argument from Quran 8:75 for 'Ali's priority in imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shi'a use Quran 8:75 to argue for 'Ali's right to the imamate based on blood kinship.

Core Idea
Blood kinship is used to claim 'Ali's priority over Abu Bakr for leadership.

This lesson examines the tenth objection presented by Shi'a theologians in favor of 'Ali's imamate. It relies on a verse about kinship priority to argue that the imamate must go to the closest relative of the Prophet. Let's read the argument carefully and see how it unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the logical flow.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage argues that Quran 8:75 establishes an absolute priority of relatives, which must apply to all forms of nearness, including succession after death.
This includes the imamate.
3
Follow the exception reasoning
The argument uses exception logic: certain descriptions are excepted from the general rule, but imamate is not excepted.
Understand how the Shi'a apply verse to leadership.
4
Apply to Abu Bakr and 'Ali
Since Abu Bakr is not a blood relative while 'Ali is, 'Ali is more entitled. This is the conclusion of the argument.
Consider the strength of this argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة العاشرة: التمسك بقوله تعالى : ﴿وَأُولُوا الْأَرْحَامِ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلَى بِبَعْضٍ فِي كِتَابِ اللَّهِ﴾ [الأنفال: ٧٥] وهذه الأولوية مطلقة، فوجب حملها على الكل دفعا للإجمال، وأيضًا استثناء أي وصف أريد كقوله: ﴿وَأُولُوا الْأَرْحَامِ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلَى﴾ يصح بعض [الأنفال : ٧٥] إلا في كونه قائما مقامه بعد موته وإلا في التصرف في اتباعه، وحكم الاستثناء إخراج ما لولاه لدخل فثبت أن هذا اللفظ يتناول الإمامة. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لا شك أن أبا بكر ما كان من أولي الأرحام لمحمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وكان على كذلك، فكان علي انا أولى بالإمامة.
The tenth doubt: Adherence to His saying: 'And those of kinship are more entitled to one another in the Book of Allah' [Quran 8:75]. And this entitlement is absolute, so it must be applied to everything to remove ambiguity. Also, the exception of any description that is intended, such as His saying 'And those of kinship are more entitled' — it is valid to except some of it — except in his being his successor after his death and except in disposal over his followers. And the rule of exception is to remove what would otherwise be included. So it is established that this wording includes the imamate. If this is established, we say: There is no doubt that Abu Bakr was not among the kinsfolk of Muhammad (peace be upon him), while ‘Ali was. So ‘Ali is more entitled to the imamate.
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth Shi'a argument, which uses Quran 8:75. It claims that the phrase 'those of kinship are more entitled to one another' is absolute, covering all forms of entitlement, including succession after death and authority over followers. Therefore, the imamate is included among these entitlements. Since ‘Ali was a blood relative of the Prophet (his cousin and son-in-law) while Abu Bakr was not, ‘Ali is more entitled to the imamate. The argument assumes that the imamate is a form of nearness and inheritance of authority, which only a relative can rightfully claim.

Key Terms
الشبهة
أولو الأرحام
أولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse refers only to inheritance of property, but the argument extends it to political and religious leadership.
Correction: The Shi'a argument assumes the verse covers all forms of nearness, including succession and authority, not just inheritance.

Checkpoint
On what basis does the tenth shubuha argue that ‘Ali is more entitled to the imamate than Abu Bakr?
A) ‘Ali was older.
B) ‘Ali was a blood relative of the Prophet.
C) ‘Ali had more knowledge.
D) ‘Ali was appointed by the Prophet in writing.

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a argue that Quran 8:75 gives 'Ali priority over Abu Bakr for imamate based on blood kinship, assuming the verse covers all forms of nearness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever favored a family member for a position of responsibility, believing blood ties give them priority? How does this argument challenge you to examine whether your reasons are based on divine guidance or personal bias?
?
When you read Quranic verses like this one, do you interpret them with the full context or are you tempted to extract meanings that justify your own preferences? What would it cost you to read honestly?
?
If someone used a verse from the Quran to claim that your relative should lead instead of a non-relative, would you accept it or resist? Are you willing to submit to the Quran even when it contradicts your natural inclinations?

The Verse of Walaya: Proving Ali's Imamate
Presents the Shia argument from Quran 5:55 for Ali's Imamate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shia argument from Quran 5:55 uses linguistic analysis and consensus to prove Ali's Imamate.

Core Idea
The verse of walaya restricts leadership to Allah, the Prophet, and Ali, proving his Imamate.

This passage presents one of the most detailed Shia arguments for Ali's Imamate, based on Quran 5:55, known as the verse of walaya. The argument carefully analyzes the Arabic word 'wali' and uses the principle of restriction to show that the verse designates a specific person as ruler over the community. We will explore the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The verse indicates the Imamate of a specific person. Note that the argument aims to prove that this person is Ali.
2
Analyze the word 'wali'
Identify the two possible meanings: one who disposes (ruler) and friend/helper. See how the argument uses linguistic evidence.
The argument cites hadith and Quranic usage for each meaning.
3
Exclude the helper meaning
Notice the use of 'innama' (restriction) to argue that the helper meaning is not intended, because that meaning would be general for all believers.
The helper meaning is general in 9:71, but this verse is restricted.
4
Conclude the meaning as ruler
Since the helper meaning is invalid, 'wali' must mean ruler, making the verse say: 'Your ruler is only Allah, His Messenger, and specific believers.'
5
Identify Ali as that ruler
Two proofs: (a) no other candidate is claimed by the ummah, and (b) consensus of exegetes that the description refers to Ali.
The passage cuts off at the second proof, but the argument is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الحادية عشرة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ اللَّهُ وَرَسُولُهُ وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا الذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلَوَةَ وَيُؤْتُونَ الزَّكَوةَ وَهُمْ رَاكِعُونَ ) [المائدة : ٥٥] وجه الاستدلال بالآية أن نقول : هذه الآية تدل على إمامة شخص معين ومتى كان الأمر كذلك وجب أن يكون ذلك الشخص هو علي. أما بيان المقام الأول : فالدليل عليه أن لفظ الولي مستعمل في معنيين : أحدهما: المتصرف كقوله : أيما امرأة نكحت نفها بغير إذن وليها فنكاحا باطل. والثاني : المحب والناصر كقوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَاتُ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلِيَاءُ بَعْضٍ [التوبة : ٧١] و يجب ألا يكون حقيقة في معنى آخر تقليلا للاشتراك. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : الولي المذكور في هذه الآية ليس بمعنى الناصر، فوجب أن يكون بمعنى المتصرف وإنما قلنا إنه ليس بمعنى الناصر لأن الولي المذكور في هذه الآية غير عام في حق كل المؤمنين؛ لأنه تعالى ذكره بكلمة وإنما وهي للحصر، قال الشاعر : ولست بالأكثر منهم حصى وإنما العزة زة لـلــكــاثــر وأما الولاية بمعنى النصرة فهي عامة في حق كل المؤمنين، بدليل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَتُ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلِيَاءُ بَعْضٍ [التوبة : ۷۱] ويلزم من صحة هاتين المقدمتين القطع بأن الولاية المذكورة في هذه الآية ليس بمعنى النصرة، وإذا بطل هذا المعنى وجب أن يكون المراد من الولاية المذكورة في هذه الآية التصرف فصار معنى الآية : إنما المتصرف فيكم أيها الأمة هو الله ورسوله والمؤمنون الموصوفون بكذا وكذا، والمتصرف في كل الأمة هو الإمام، فثبت أن هذه الآية دالة على إمامة شخص معين. وجهان وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : وجب أن يكون ذلك الشخص هو عليه ويدل عليه وجهان الأول : أن الأمة في هذه الآية على قولين منهم من قال: إنها لا تدل على إمامة أحد منهم ومنهم من قال : إنها تدل على إمامة علي بن أبي طالب، وليس الأمة أحد يقول إنها تدل على إمامة غيره فلما ثبت دلالتها على أصل الإمامة ، وجب دلالتها على إمامة علي بن أبي طالب، إذ لو دلت على إمامة غيره كان ذلك قولا ثالثاً خارقا للإجماع وهو باطل. الثاني : أنه اتفق أئمة التفسير على أن المراد بقوله تعالى : الَّذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلَوَةَ وَيُؤْتُونَ الزَّكَوةَ وَهُمْ رَاكِعُونَ ﴾ [المائدة : ٥٥] هو علي بن أبي طالب، ولما دل قوله :
The eleventh objection: Clinging to His saying, the Most High: 'Your Wali is only Allah, His Messenger, and those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah while bowing' [Al-Ma'idah 5:55]. The way of argument from the verse is that we say: This verse indicates the Imamate of a specific person. And when it is so, that person must be Ali. As for the clarification of the first station: The evidence for it is that the word 'wali' is used in two meanings: One: the one who disposes (al-mutasarrif), as in his saying: 'Any woman who marries without the permission of her wali, her marriage is invalid.' The second: the friend and helper, as in His saying: 'The believing men and believing women are awliya' of one another' [At-Tawbah 9:71]. And it must not be a reality in another meaning, to reduce ambiguity. When this is established, we say: The 'wali' mentioned in this verse is not in the sense of helper, so it must be in the sense of the one who disposes. And we say it is not in the sense of helper because the 'wali' mentioned in this verse is not general for all believers; because the Most High mentioned it with the word 'innama', which is for restriction. The poet said: 'And I am not the most numerous of them in pebbles, but honor is for the many.' As for the guardianship in the sense of helping, it is general for all believers, as evidenced by His saying: 'The believing men and believing women are awliya' of one another.' It follows from the correctness of these two premises the certainty that the 'wilayah' mentioned in this verse is not in the sense of helping. And when this meaning is invalidated, it must be that the intended meaning of the 'wilayah' mentioned in this verse is disposition. So the meaning of the verse becomes: 'The one who disposes over you, O community, is only Allah, His Messenger, and the believers described as such-and-such.' And the one who disposes over the entire community is the Imam. So it is established that this verse indicates the Imamate of a specific person. Two aspects: And when this is established, we say: That person must be Ali. And two aspects indicate it: The first: The community concerning this verse are of two opinions: some say it does not indicate the Imamate of anyone, and some say it indicates the Imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib. And no one among the community says it indicates the Imamate of anyone other than him. So when its indication of the principle of Imamate is established, its indication of the Imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib is necessary. For if it indicated the Imamate of someone else, that would be a third opinion breaking the consensus, which is invalid. The second: That the leading scholars of interpretation have agreed that the intended meaning of His saying: 'who establish prayer and give zakah while bowing' [Al-Ma'idah 5:55] is Ali ibn Abi Talib. And when His saying indicates...
Source pages p. 436

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh and most detailed Shia argument for the Imamate of Ali, based on Quran 5:55, the 'verse of walaya.' The argument has three parts: (1) proving that the verse designates a specific person, (2) proving that the meaning of 'wali' here is 'ruler' (mutasarrif) not 'helper', and (3) proving that person is Ali. First, the word 'wali' has two meanings: one who disposes (guardian/ruler) and friend/helper. To avoid ambiguity, it cannot have another meaning. Then it argues that 'wali' in this verse cannot mean 'helper' because the verse is restricted (hasr) by 'innama' and is not general like the 'helper' usage in 9:71. So it must mean 'ruler'. Thus the verse means: Your ruler is only Allah, His Messenger, and certain believers—who are described as giving zakah while bowing. That ruler over the whole community is the Imam, so the verse indicates a specific Imam. Then two proofs identify him as Ali: (a) the ummah's opinions on the verse are only that it indicates no one specific or that it indicates Ali; no one says it indicates anyone else, so if it indicates an Imam, it must be Ali. (b) The consensus of exegetes is that the description in the verse refers to Ali. The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the argument is essentially complete in intent.

Key Terms
الولي
المتصرف
الناصر
الحصر
إنما
الإمامة
علي بن أبي طالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some interpret 'wali' in Quran 5:55 as simply 'friend' or 'helper', meaning all believers are friends of Allah and the Prophet.
Correction: The argument shows that the verse uses 'innama' for restriction, which is incompatible with a general meaning like 'helper'. Therefore, the intended meaning must be 'ruler' (one who disposes), which designates a specific person as Imam.

Checkpoint
Why does the argument conclude that 'wali' in Quran 5:55 means 'ruler' rather than 'helper'?
A) Because the verse uses 'innama' for restriction, unlike the general use in 9:71.
B) Because Ali was known as a ruler.
C) Because 'helper' is not a valid meaning.
D) Because the context is about inheritance.

Key Takeaway
Through linguistic analysis of 'wali' and the restrictive 'innama', the verse of walaya establishes that leadership is confined to Allah, the Prophet, and Ali, thereby proving his Imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does that challenge you to re-examine other verses you might have assumed a common meaning for? What verse will you re-read today with this kind of scrutiny?
?
The argument relies on a claim of consensus among exegetes that the descriptive phrase refers to Ali. But this consensus might not be universal. ?

First Argument from the Mawla Hadith: Priority and Imamate
Razi defines 'mawla' as priority and equates it with imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi argues that the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' implies priority (awlawiyya), which is the essence of imamate, thereby proving the imamate of Ali.

Core Idea
The Prophet's 'mawla' means 'more entitled' — and that entitlement is the very definition of imamate.

Imam al-Razi presents a tightly reasoned argument from the famous hadith of Ghadir. By analyzing the term 'mawla' and its implications, he demonstrates that the hadith establishes priority in authority for Ali. This priority, he argues, is exactly what imamate means. The passage is a model of theological reasoning grounded in linguistic principles.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic premise
Razi first establishes that 'mawla' in this hadith means 'awla' (more entitled). This is based on the validity of exception (istithna') and removal of ambiguity (ijmal).
These are established principles in usul al-fiqh.
2
Follow the argument from priority to imamate
Razi then argues that priority in all things (awlawiyya) means the Prophet's authority over the community is more deserving than their own self-authority. This is precisely the meaning of imamate.
3
Appreciate the conclusion
Razi concludes that the hadith thus proves Ali's imamate. Notice how he uses a single term to derive a comprehensive claim.
Consider the polemical context: this argues against those who limit 'mawla' to love or friendship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : لما ثبت أن قوله : من كنت مولاه معناه من كنت أولى به فعلي أولى به فنقول: هذا يدل على الإمامة ؛ لأن قوله : "من كنت وليا به فعلي أولى به وجب حمله على ثبوت الأولوية في جميع الأشياء، بدليل صحة الاستثناء، بدليل دفع الإجمال، ومعنى الأولوية : ان نفاذ حكمه فيكم أولى من نفاذ حكمهم في أنفسهم ولا معنى للإمامة إلا هذا فثبت بهذا دلالة هذا الخبر على إمامة علي بن أبي طالب.
The Third Introduction: When it is established that his saying 'whoever I am his mawla' means 'whoever I am more entitled to him' then Ali is more entitled. So we say: This indicates imamate; because his saying 'whoever I am a guardian over him, Ali is more entitled' must be interpreted as establishing priority in all things, due to the validity of exception (istithna') and due to removing ambiguity (ijmal). The meaning of priority is that his authority over you is more deserving than their authority over themselves. And there is no meaning of imamate except this. So it is established by this the indication of this report (khabar) on the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. Razi begins by stating that the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' means 'whoever I am mawla to him', and it has been established that mawla here means 'awla' (more entitled). Then he argues that the phrase 'ali awla bihi' must be taken in a general sense of priority in all things, because of two principles: the validity of exception (istithna') and the removal of ambiguity (ijmal). He then defines priority (awlawiyya) as the Prophet's authority over the believers being more deserving than their own authority over themselves. This is precisely the meaning of imamate. Therefore, the hadith proves Ali's imamate.

Key Terms
المولى
الأولوية
الإمامة
الاستثناء
الإجمال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' only implies love, support, or friendship, not authority.
Correction: Razi argues that linguistic principles force the meaning 'more entitled' (awla), which entails authority (wilaya) and thus imamate.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what does the term 'mawla' in the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu' mean?
A) friend
B) master
C) more entitled (awla)
D) cousin

Key Takeaway
The hadith of Ghadir, when properly understood, proves that Ali has priority in authority over the believers, which is the essence of imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi says that the Prophet's authority over you is more deserving than your own authority over yourself. Is there any area of your life where you still insist on your own judgment over the Prophet's command? What would it cost you to surrender that area completely?
?
If you truly believed that Ali had the same level of entitlement over you as the Prophet, how would your daily attitude towards him differ? Are you willing to ask Allah to show you any resistance in your heart to this truth?
?
The passage defines imamate as authority—not mere love or support. Do you treat the Prophet as having real authority over your choices, or only as a source of inspiration? Before you answer, consider your last major decision: did it prioritize the Prophet's guidance or your own preference?

Second Argument: Linguistic Elimination of Mawla's Meanings
Razi enumerates meanings of 'mawla' and eliminates all but 'mutasarrif'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses linguistic elimination to prove that 'mawla' in the hadith means 'one with authority' (mutasarrif), thereby establishing Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
Razi eliminates five meanings of 'mawla' to leave only 'mutasarrif', proving imamate.

Razi's second argument from the Mawla hadith is a careful linguistic elimination. He lists all possible meanings of the word 'mawla' and systematically eliminates each one, leaving only 'mutasarrif' (one with authority). This remaining meaning directly implies imamate. The reasoning is a classic example of how kalam uses language to establish doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: Razi first lists meanings, then eliminates them one by one.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries a logical step.
2
Identify the six meanings listed
Find the six meanings of 'mawla' that Razi mentions: manumitter, manumitted, cousin, ally, helper, and one with authority.
These cover the range of usage in Arabic.
3
Understand the elimination process
Note that Razi eliminates the first four by consensus (ijma), and the fifth (helper) by a reductio argument: it would make the statement trivial. Only 'mutasarrif' remains.
The elimination is systematic and complete.
4
Connect to imamate
Razi concludes that 'mutasarrif' means one with authority to dispose, and that this is precisely the meaning of imamate. Thus the hadith proves Ali's imamate.
This is the doctrinal payoff of the linguistic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني في بيان دلالة هذا الخبر على الإمامة : أن لفظ المولى في اللغة جاء بمعنى المعتق والمعتق وابن العم والحليف والناصر والمتصرف، ودل الإجماع على أنه ليس المراد المعتق والمعتق وابن العم والحليف، ولا يجوز أن يكون المراد الناصر ؛ لأنه يصير التقدير : من كنت ناصرا له فعلي ناصر له، وهذا غير جائز ؛ لأن هذا المعنى في غاية الظهور، فلا يليق بالرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم أن الجمع العظيم لشرح هذا المعنى. ولما بطل الكل لم يبق إلا أن يكون المراد المتصرف، فيصير المعنى: من كنت متصرفًا فيه كان علي متصرفًا فيه ولا معنى للإمامة إلا ذلك، فثبت أن هذا الخبر يدل على إمامته.
The second aspect in explaining the indication of this hadith on imamate: That the word 'mawla' in the language occurs with the meanings: the manumitter and the manumitted, the cousin, the ally, the helper, and the one with authority (mutasarrif). Consensus indicates that the intended meaning is not the manumitter, manumitted, cousin, or ally. And it is not permissible that the intended meaning is the helper, because then the estimation would be: 'Whoever I am a helper to, Ali is a helper to him.' This is not permissible because this meaning is extremely apparent, and it is not befitting of the Messenger (s) to gather a great assembly to explain this meaning. So when all (other meanings) are invalidated, nothing remains except that the intended meaning is the one with authority (mutasarrif). Thus the meaning becomes: 'Whoever I have authority over, Ali has authority over him.' And there is no meaning of imamate except that. So it is established that this hadith indicates his imamate.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a classification and elimination argument. Razi lists the possible meanings of 'mawla' in Arabic: manumitter, manumitted, cousin, ally, helper, and one with authority (mutasarrif). He then eliminates the first four by consensus (ijma). He eliminates 'helper' (nasir) because if it meant that, the statement would be trivial ('whoever I help, Ali helps'), and the Prophet would not gather the people for such an obvious meaning. Therefore, the only remaining meaning is 'mutasarrif'—the one who has authority to dispose. This defines imamate. Hence the hadith proves Ali's imamate.

Key Terms
المولى
المتصرف
الإجماع
الناصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'mawla' can have multiple meanings simultaneously in the hadith, or that the meaning is ambiguous.
Correction: Razi shows that through systematic elimination, only one meaning remains, so the hadith is clear and decisive for imamate.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the meaning of 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir)?
A) Because it is contradicted by consensus
B) Because it would make the hadith trivial and unworthy of the Prophet's gathering
C) Because it contradicts the context of Tabuk
D) Because it is not a linguistic meaning

Key Takeaway
The Mawla hadith proves Ali's imamate because 'mawla' can only mean 'mutasarrif' (one with authority) after eliminating all other meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu fa Aliun mawlahu', does your heart submit to the authority it establishes, or do you find yourself seeking alternative interpretations to avoid the clear implication? What is holding you back from full acceptance?
?
Razi argues that if 'mawla' meant helper, the statement would be too trivial for the Prophet to gather the community. How often do you reduce the Prophet's words to trivial matters, missing the profound implications for your own life and leadership?
?
The argument hinges on consensus eliminating some meanings. In your own life, when the community of believers is united on a matter, do you submit to that consensus, or do you cling to your own interpretations? How might that be affecting your relationship with Allah and His Messenger?
