The Manzila Hadith: The Argument for Ali's Imamate
Razi constructs a three-premise proof for Ali's imamate from the Manzila hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' to argue for Ali's imamate through three premises.

Core Idea
The Manzila hadith proves Ali's imamate by establishing that all of Aaron's stations except prophethood belong to Ali.

Razi presents a structured argument from the well-known hadith. He builds three premises: (1) Aaron's status as successor to Moses, (2) the nature of stations including potential ones, and (3) the generality of the hadith. The argument culminates in the conclusion that Ali inherits the station of succession, proving his imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice how Razi begins by stating the hadith's authenticity and then sets up three premises.
The passage is dense; reading both versions will help you follow the logic.
2
Identify the three premises
Look for the three numbered premises. The first concerns Aaron's succession, the second explains types of stations, and the third argues for the generality of the hadith.
Each premise is introduced with 'First:', 'Second:', 'Third:'.
3
Understand the analogy of son and father
In the second premise, Razi uses the example of a son and father regarding inheritance. Grasp this analogy: even if the father is alive, the son has the potential to inherit. Similarly, Aaron had the potential to succeed Moses.
This clarifies the concept of a 'potential station' (manzila bi al-quwwa).
4
Examine the objection and reply
After the conclusion, Razi addresses an objection: the hadith was said in the context of Tabuk. Note his reply: 'consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause.'
This is a key principle in hadith interpretation.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Re-read the conclusion: because Ali lived after Muhammad, and the hadith grants him all Aaron's stations (except prophethood), he becomes the actual successor.
This is the core claim: the hadith is an explicit text (nass) for Ali's imamate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الثالثة عشرة: التمسك بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى
أما بيان صحة هذا الخبر فكما تقدم ذكره في خبر المولى.
وأما الاستدلال به على الإمامة فهو مبني على مقدمات :
أولها أن هارون كان خليفة لموسى الا بعد موته، والدليل عليه أنه كان خليفة له حال حياته، فوجب أن يكون خليفة له بعد موته بتقدير أن يبقى، وإنما قلنا : إنه كان خليفة له حال حياته لقوله تعالى : وَقَالَ مُوسَى لِأَخِيهِ هَرُونَ اخْلُفْني في قومى [الأعراف : ١٤٢]
وإنما قلنا إنه لما كان خليفة له حال حياته وجب أن يكون خليفة له بعد موته؛ لأن هارون كان بحيث لو بقي بعد وفاة موسى لكان خليفة له؛ لأنه لو لم يكن كذلك لاقتضى ذلك انعزال هارون عن تلك الخلافة وهذا الانعزال مشتمل على الإهانة والإذلال، وذلك لا يليق بمنصب النبوة التي كانت خاصة لهارون.
وثانيها : أن المنازل قسمان منها ما حصل ومنها ما كونه بحيث لو بقي لحصل له مثاله للابن مع الأب حالتان إحداهما : إذا مات الأب أخذ الابن ميراثه، والثاني : ما إذا لم يمت الأب ففي هذه الحالة الابن وإن لم يأخذ ميراثه، لكنه حصل للابن في هذه الساعة كونه بحيث لو مات الأب لأخذ ميراثه، فكونه في هذه الحيثية وبهذه الحالة حكم حاصل في الحال.
وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : إن هارون لما توفي قبل موسى لم يصر خليفة له بعد وفاته ولكنه كان بحال لو بقي بعد موسى لكان خليفة له بعد موته، فكون هارون بهذه الحيثية وهذه الحالة صفة من صفات هارون ومنزلة من منازله، و هي منزلة متحققة حاصلة في الحال.
وثالثها: أن قوله : أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى يتناول جميع المنازل، ويدل عليه وجهان
الأول: لو كان المراد منه منزلة واحدة مع أنها غير مذكورة لصار هذا الحديث مجملاً ، والإجمال خلاف الأصل، فوجب حمل اللفظ على كل المنازل دفعا للإجمال.
والثاني : أنه قال في آخر هذا الحديث : إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي وهذا يدل على أن المراد من الخبر إثبات جميع المنازل سوى هذه المنزلة الواحدة.
وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمات الثلاث فنقول : الخبر دل على أن المنازل الحاصلة لهارون من موسى كانت حاصلة لعلي من محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وثبت أن من منازل هارون من موسى كون هارون بحيث لو عاش بعد موسى لكان خليفة له، فوجب أن يكون من جملة منازل علي من محمد أنه بحيث لو عاش بعد محمد لكان خليفة له، لكنه عاش بعد محمد فوجب أن يكون خليفة له.
فثبت أن هذا الخبر نص في إمامة علي بن أبي طالب
لا يقال هذا الخبر في واقعة طعن المنافقين في قصة تبوك ؛ لأنا نقول :
العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب.
The thirteenth doubt: Holding onto his saying (s) 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses'. As for the explanation of the authenticity of this hadith, it is as previously mentioned in the hadith of the mawla. As for using it as evidence for imamate, it is based on premises: First: That Aaron was a successor (khalifa) to Moses after his death. The evidence for that is that he was his successor during his lifetime, so it is necessary that he be his successor after his death, given that he remained. We only say he was his successor during his lifetime due to His statement: 'Moses said to his brother Aaron: Be my successor among my people' (7:142). We only say that since he was his successor during his lifetime, it is necessary that he be his successor after his death, because Aaron was such that if he remained after Moses' death he would be his successor. If it were not so, that would necessitate Aaron's removal from that succession, and that removal contains insult and humiliation, and that is not befitting for the station of prophethood which was specific to Aaron. Second: That stations (manazil) are of two types: some are attained, and some are such that if (the other) remained they would be attained. An example is the son with the father: two states: one, if the father dies the son inherits; the other, if the father does not die, then in this case the son does not inherit, but the son has in this moment the state of being such that if the father were to die he would inherit. So his being in this respect and this state is a ruling (hukm) that occurs immediately. And if this is established, we say: Aaron died before Moses, so he did not become successor after his death, but he was in a state such that if he remained after Moses he would be successor after his death. So Aaron's being in this respect and this state is a description of Aaron and a station of his stations, and it is a realized station that occurs immediately. Third: That his saying 'You are to me as Aaron was from Moses' encompasses all stations. Two aspects indicate this: First, if it meant a single station without it being mentioned, this hadith would be ambiguous, and ambiguity is contrary to the principle, so the word must be interpreted as all stations to remove ambiguity. Second, he said at the end of this hadith: 'except that there is no prophet after me', and this indicates that the intended meaning of the hadith is establishing all stations except this one station. When these three premises are established, we say: The hadith indicates that the stations that Aaron had from Moses were established for Ali from Muhammad. And it is established that among Aaron's stations from Moses is that Aaron was such that if he lived after Moses he would be his successor. Thus it is necessary that among Ali's stations from Muhammad is that he was such that if he lived after Muhammad he would be his successor. But he did live after Muhammad, so it is necessary that he be his successor. So it is established that this hadith is an explicit text (nass) on the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib. It should not be said that this hadith was said in the incident of the hypocrites' criticism in the expedition of Tabuk, because we say: Consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage is a chain of proof consisting of three premises. Razi first notes the authenticity of the hadith is already established. Then he argues: First premise: Aaron was a successor to Moses after his death. Evidence: Aaron was his successor during his lifetime (Quran 7:142), and if he remained after Moses' death he would be successor, otherwise it would be humiliating for a prophet. Second premise: There are two types of stations: those actually attained and those that are potential. Razi uses the analogy of a son and father regarding inheritance: even if the father is alive, the son has the status of being a potential heir. Similarly, Aaron had the station of being a potential successor even though he died before Moses. Third premise: The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was from Moses' includes all stations, because it would be ambiguous if it meant only one, and the exception 'no prophet after me' confirms that the only excluded station is prophethood. Conclusion: Since Ali had all the stations that Aaron had, and one of Aaron's stations was the potential to be successor after Moses, and Ali actually lived after Muhammad, he becomes the actual successor. Therefore, the hadith explicitly proves Ali's imamate. The objection that the hadith was said in a specific context (Tabuk) is rejected by the principle of general wording.

Key Terms
المنازل
الخلافة
الإمامة
عموم اللفظ
المنزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith was only about the Tabuk incident and does not apply generally to all Muslims.
Correction: Razi invokes the principle 'consideration is for the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause' to argue that the hadith has universal application, not limited to the context.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which station of Aaron was definitely excluded from Ali by the hadith 'except that there is no prophet after me'?
A) Succession
B) Prophethood
C) Brotherhood
D) Help

Key Takeaway
Through a careful chain of reasoning, Razi demonstrates that the Manzila hadith is an explicit text (nass) supporting Ali's imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After reading Razi's argument, ask yourself: Do I truly base my convictions about leadership in Islam on careful reasoning and evidence, or do I follow inherited opinions without scrutiny?
?
The Manzila hadith asserts that Ali held all of Aaron's stations except prophethood. If you were among the early Muslims, would you have accepted this as proof for Ali's authority? What might hold you back from submitting to evidence that challenges your biases?
?
Razi uses the principle that general wording applies universally. How often do you interpret religious texts with that same commitment to their full scope, rather than confining them to the situations you find comfortable?

The Three Premises of the Manzila Hadith Argument
Razi presents three premises and a conclusion from the Manzila hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses three logical premises from the Manzila hadith to establish Ali's imamate, including the distinction between actual and potential stations and the principle of general wording.

Core Idea
Potential stations are real, and the Manzila hadith proves Ali's caliphate.

This passage is the climax of Razi's argument from the famous hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses.' He unpacks the logic step by step: first, he clarifies what a 'station' means—including potential ones—then argues that the hadith covers all such stations except prophethood, and finally applies it to Ali to prove his imamate. Follow along as he builds a watertight case.

Guided Path
1
Read the complete passage
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the three distinct premises and the final conclusion.
The passage is dense but logically structured.
2
Identify the three premises
The author explicitly says 'the second of them,' 'the third of them,' and then 'when these three premises are established.' Find each premise and its supporting evidence.
Premise 1: distinction between actual and potential stations. Premise 2: generality of the hadith. Premise 3: application of the potential station concept to Aaron.
3
Examine the son-and-father analogy
Razi uses an example of a son inheriting from his father to illustrate a potential station. Understand that the son has the station of 'heir' even before the father's death.
This is key to understanding how Aaron could have a potential caliphate even though he died before Moses.
4
Analyze the generality argument
Notice the two proofs for why the hadith covers all stations: (1) ambiguity is avoided, (2) the exception 'except prophethood' implies all other stations are included.
This is a classic principle of usul al-fiqh: general wording unless specified otherwise.
5
Review the conclusion and objection
See how the premises combine to prove Ali's imamate, and note the rebuttal to the objection that the hadith was about the Tabuk expedition.
The principle 'consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not the specificity of the cause' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن المنازل قسمان منها ما حصل ومنها ما كونه بحيث لو بقي لحصل له مثاله للابن مع الأب حالتان إحداهما: إذا مات الأب أخذ الابن ميراثه، والثاني: ما إذا لم يمت الأب ففي هذه الحالة الابن وإن لم يأخذ ميراثه، لكنه حصل للابن في هذه الساعة كونه بحيث لو مات الأب لأخذ ميراثه، فكونه في هذه الحيثية وبهذه الحالة حكم حاصل في الحال. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن هارون لما توفي قبل موسى لم يصر خليفة له بعد وفاته ولكنه كان بحال لو بقي بعد موسى لكان خليفة له بعد موته، فكون هارون بهذه الحيثية وهذه الحالة صفة من صفات هارون ومنزلة من منازله، وهي منزلة متحققة حاصلة في الحال. وثالثها: أن قوله: «أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى» يتناول جميع المنازل، ويدل عليه وجهان: الأول: لو كان المراد منه منزلة واحدة مع أنها غير مذكورة لصار هذا الحديث مجملاً، والإجمال خلاف الأصل، فوجب حمل اللفظ على كل المنازل دفعاً للإجمال. والثاني: أنه قال في آخر هذا الحديث: «إلا أنه لا نبي بعدي» وهذا يدل على أن المراد من الخبر إثبات جميع المنازل سوى هذه المنزلة الواحدة. وإذا ثبتت هذه المقدمات الثلاث فنقول: الخبر دل على أن المنازل الحاصلة لهارون من موسى كانت حاصلة لعلي من محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وثبت أن من منازل هارون من موسى كون هارون بحيث لو عاش بعد موسى لكان خليفة له، فوجب أن يكون من جملة منازل علي من محمد أنه بحيث لو عاش بعد محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم لكان خليفة له، لكنه عاش بعد محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم فوجب أن يكون خليفة له. فثبت أن هذا الخبر نص في إمامة علي بن أبي طالب. لا يقال: هذا الخبر في واقعة طعن المنافقين في قصة تبوك؛ لأنا نقول: العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب.
And the second of them: that the stations are two types: those that are actualized and those that are such that if he had remained, an example of it would have been obtained for him. For the son with the father there are two states: one of them: when the father dies, the son takes his inheritance; and the second: when the father has not died, then in this state the son, even though he does not take his inheritance, yet there is obtained for the son at this moment his being such that if the father died, he would take his inheritance. Thus his being in this respect and in this state is a ruling actualized in the present. And when this is established, we say: indeed Aaron, when he died before Moses, did not become a caliph after his death, but he was in a state such that if he had remained after Moses, he would have been a caliph after his death. So Aaron's being in this respect and this state is a quality of Aaron and a station among his stations, and it is a station actualized and obtained in the present. And the third of them: that his statement, 'You are to me in the station of Aaron to Moses,' encompasses all the stations, and there are two proofs for it: First: if the intended meaning were one station while it is not mentioned, this hadith would become ambiguous, and ambiguity is contrary to the principle, so it is necessary to apply the wording to all stations to avoid ambiguity. Second: that he said at the end of this hadith, 'except that there is no prophet after me,' and this indicates that the intended meaning of the report is to establish all stations except this single station. And when these three premises are established, we say: the report indicates that the stations obtained for Aaron from Moses were obtained for Ali from Muhammad (peace be upon him). And it is established that among the stations of Aaron from Moses was Aaron's being such that if he had lived after Moses, he would have been a caliph for him. So it must be that among the stations of Ali from Muhammad is that he was such that if he had lived after Muhammad (peace be upon him), he would have been a caliph for him. But he lived after Muhammad (peace be upon him), so it must be that he was a caliph for him. Thus it is established that this report is a clear text regarding the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib. It cannot be said: this report concerns the incident of the hypocrites' criticism in the Tabuk expedition; because we say: consideration is given to the generality of the wording, not to the specificity of the cause.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage concludes the argument from the Manzila hadith. It is a logical proof structured as three premises leading to a conclusion. The author first distinguishes two types of stations (manazil): actual and potential. He illustrates potential station with the example of a son who is heir to his father: even if the father is alive, the son possesses the station of being the heir in a potential sense. Applying this to Aaron, though he died before Moses, he possessed the potential station of being caliph after Moses had he lived. The second premise argues that the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' is general, covering all stations except prophethood, because the exception 'except there is no prophet after me' excludes only prophethood. The third premise combines these: if the hadith establishes all stations of Aaron for Ali, and among Aaron's stations was the potential caliphate, then Ali had that potential. Since Ali actually outlived Muhammad, that potential became actual, making him caliph. Thus the hadith is a clear text (nass) for Ali's imamate. The author then preempts the objection that the hadith was about a specific event (Tabuk) by stating the principle of general wording, not specific cause.

Key Terms
المنازل
الخليفة
الإجمال
العبرة بعموم اللفظ لا بخصوص السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Manzila hadith only applies to the specific incident of the Tabuk expedition and has no general implication for succession.
Correction: Razi argues that the principle of general wording (al-ibra bi umum al-lafz) applies, so the hadith establishes all stations for Ali, including potential caliphate, which became actual when he outlived Muhammad.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the status of Aaron's potential caliphate after Moses?
A) It is non-existent because Aaron died before Moses.
B) It is an actualized station in the present, as a potentiality.
C) It is only metaphorical, not real.
D) It is a future state that was never realized.

Key Takeaway
The Manzila hadith, properly understood with the distinction between actual and potential stations and the principle of general wording, provides a clear textual proof for Ali's imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were a Companion at the time of the Tabuk expedition, would you have accepted the Manzila hadith as proof for Ali's caliphate? What personal biases or loyalties might have clouded your judgment? How can you guard against such biases today when evaluating religious evidence?
?
The principle of general wording demands that we look beyond the specific occasion of a text. In your own life, do you sometimes limit the application of Allah's commands to particular situations to avoid their full demand on you? What would change if you applied them universally without excuse?
?
Razi argues that potential stations are real—like the son's inheritance before the father's death. Think of a responsibility you have not yet fulfilled, such as caring for aging parents or fulfilling a duty you keep postponing. Is that potential duty a real station upon you now? What would it mean to treat it as an actual obligation today?

Two Objections Against the Early Caliphate
Razi presents two Shi'a objections against the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two objections raised against the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman: the continuity argument from Ali's deputyship and the argument from transmitted defects.

Core Idea
Two objections challenge the early caliphate: continuity of Ali's deputyship and incriminating narratives against the first three caliphs.

This lesson presents two arguments used by opponents of the first three caliphs. Razi outlines them without immediate refutation, setting the stage for his later responses. Understanding these objections is key to following the sectarian debate on imamate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the two objections. Then read the English translation. Notice how each objection is structured.
2
Analyze the first objection
Identify the logic: the Prophet appointed Ali as deputy over Medina during Tabuk and never removed him, so he should be caliph after the Prophet's death.
The argument relies on continuity and no distinction between deputyship over Medina and over the whole ummah.
3
Analyze the second objection
Identify the logic: narrated actions of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman impugn their imamate, so by elimination Ali's imamate is established.
The objector claims these criticisms are recorded in extensive books.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشبهة الرابعة عشرة: أن الأمة مجمعة على أنه استخلف علياً على المدينة في غزوة تبوك، ثم إنه ما عزله عنها، فوجب أن يبقى خليفة له على المدينة بعد موته، وإذا كان خليفة له على المدينة بعد موته كان خليفة له في كل الأمة؛ لأنه لا قائل بالفرق. الشبهة الخامسة عشرة: أنهم نقلوا عن أبي بكر وعمر وعثمان أفعالاً طاعنة في صحة الإمامة، وإذا بطلت إمامتهم بهذا الطريق ثبت القول بإمامة علي بن أبي طالب ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق، وتلك المطاعن مذكورة في الكتب المبسوطة.
The fourteenth objection: that the community is agreed that he (the Prophet) appointed Ali over Medina during the expedition of Tabuk, and then he did not remove him from it, so it must be that he remained his caliph over Medina after his death. And if he was his caliph over Medina after his death, he was his caliph over the entire ummah, because there is no one who distinguishes. The fifteenth objection: that they have narrated from Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman actions that are defamatory regarding the validity of the imamate. And if their imamate is invalidated by this path, the statement of the imamate of Ali ibn Abi Talib is established, by necessity because there is no one who distinguishes. Those criticisms are mentioned in extensive books.
Source pages p. 440

Commentary
This passage presents two objections raised by opponents (likely Shi'a) against the caliphate of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. The first objection (shubha 14) argues from continuity: the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy over Medina during Tabuk and never removed him, so he should still be caliph after the Prophet's death. If he was caliph over Medina, he must be caliph over all Muslims, as no distinction is made. The second objection (shubha 15) argues from transmitted defects: narrations report actions by Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman that impugn their imamate. If their imamate is invalidated, then by elimination (and since no one distinguishes between the three early caliphs and Ali), Ali's imamate is established. The objector claims these criticisms are recorded in extensive books. Razi does not answer here but will later.

المطاعن

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers may think that Razi accepts these objections as valid or that the passage contains his own view.
Correction: Razi is presenting the objections without endorsement; he will refute them later. The passage is descriptive of opposing arguments.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The fourteenth and fifteenth objections rely on the principles of continuity and elimination to argue for Ali's imamate.

First Proof: Quran 24:55 on Succession
Razi presents the first proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate from Quran 24:55.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 24:55 and the plural form to argue for the legitimacy of the four caliphs.

Core Idea
A plural promise in the Quran points to the caliphate of four.

Razi has presented the opponents' arguments. Now he begins his own case with a proof from the Quran. The verse promises succession to believers who do good deeds, using a plural form. Razi argues that the minimum plural is three, and since only the four caliphs' succession matches, their caliphate is divinely validated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of Razi's argument before analyzing it.
Notice the introductory phrase where Razi transitions from opponents' views to his own.
2
Analyze the Quranic citation
Identify the verse (24:55) and note how Razi focuses on the plural form 'الذين آمنوا' to derive a minimum of three individuals.
The argument hinges on the plural form indicating at least three.
3
Connect to historical fulfillment
Consider Razi's claim that the promise must be fulfilled and that the only succession observed was the caliphate of the four caliphs.
This step grounds the proof in historical reality.
4
Reflect on the decisiveness
Understand why Razi considers this proof to necessitate a decisive judgment on the validity of the four caliphs.
The conclusion is that the verse praises and validates their caliphate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنا لما أوردنا أكثر ما يتمسك القوم به في مذهبهم فلنذكر أيضًا بعض ما نتمسك به في إثبات إمامة أبي بكر لها ثم ترجع إلى الجواب عن شبهاتهم، فنقول : في المسألة وجوه أخر من الدلائل سوى ما ذكرناه : لنا الحجة الأولى التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَعَدَ اللهُ الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا مِنكُم وعملوا صلِحَاتِ لَيَسْتَخْلِفَتَهُمْ فِي الْأَرْضِ كَمَا اسْتَخلَفَ الذين من قبلهم [النور: ٥٥] فقوله : الَّذِينَ آمَنُوا مِنكُمْ وَعَمِلُوا الصَّالِحَت له [النور: ٥٥] صيغة جمع أقلها ثلاثة، فقد وعد الثلاثة فما فوقها من أصحاب محمد الله أن يستخلفهم في الأرض ويمكنهم من دينهم الذي ارتضى لهم وكل ما وعد الله به فقد فعله، ولم يوجد إلا خلافة الخلفاء الأربعة، فوجب القطع بأنها هي التي وعد الله به في هذه الآية، وأثنى عليها وعظمها، وهذا يوجب القطع بصحة خلافة هؤلاء الأربعة.
Know that when we have presented most of what the people (the opponents) cling to in their doctrine, let us also mention some of what we cling to in establishing the imamate of Abu Bakr, then return to answering their objections. So we say: In this matter there are other proofs besides what we mentioned. For us: The first proof: clinging to the saying of the Exalted: 'Allah has promised those of you who believe and do righteous deeds that He will surely make them successors in the land, just as He made successors those before them' [24:55]. His saying 'those of you who believe and do righteous deeds' is a plural form, the minimum of which is three. So He promised three or more of the Companions of Muhammad (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) that He will make them successors in the land and establish for them their religion which He has approved for them. And everything that Allah promised, He did. And no succession was found except the succession of the four caliphs. So it is necessary to decide that it is that which Allah promised in this verse, and He praised and magnified it. And this necessitates the decisive judgment of the validity of the caliphate of these four.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage begins with an introductory statement where Razi notes that after presenting the opponents' arguments, he will now present his own proofs. The first proof relies on Quran 24:55, which uses the plural form 'those who believe' (الذين آمنوا). Razi argues that the minimum plural is three, so Allah promised succession to at least three Companions. Since the promise must be fulfilled, and the only succession observed after the Prophet was the caliphate of the four caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, Ali), this verse confirms the validity of their caliphate. Razi emphasizes that this proof decisively establishes the legitimacy of the four caliphs.

Key Terms
الحجة
الخلفاء الأربعة
صيغة جمع
وعد الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the verse explicitly names the four caliphs.
Correction: The proof does not claim the verse explicitly names the four caliphs; it argues from the plural form and the historical fulfillment.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's first proof, why does the plural form in Quran 24:55 support the caliphate of the four caliphs?
A) It mentions their names explicitly
B) It promises succession to at least three, and the only succession of that time was the four caliphs
C) It specifically refers to the first three caliphs
D) It refers to all believers without distinction

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof uses the plural promise in Quran 24:55 and its historical fulfillment to decisively establish the legitimacy of the four caliphs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Second Proof: Quran 48:16 on the Caller
Razi eliminates possibilities for the caller in verse 48:16 to support the first three caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 48:16 to infer the legitimacy of the caliphates of Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman by eliminating other possible callers.

Core Idea
The caller in the verse must be one of the first three caliphs, not Ali or others.

Razi presents another scriptural argument for the caliphate of Abu Bakr and the first three caliphs. He takes a verse that speaks of a future caller, and through a process of elimination, he identifies who that caller must be. This lesson walks you through his reasoning and the sectarian implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's application
Read Quran 48:16 and then Razi's interpretation. Notice how he first identifies the verse as a prediction about a future call to fight.
The verse addresses bedouins who were left behind.
2
Identify the four candidates
Razi lists four possibilities for who the caller could be: the Prophet, one of the first three caliphs, Ali, or someone after Ali.
This sets up the elimination process.
3
Follow Razi's elimination of three candidates
Razi eliminates the Prophet (due to verse 48:15), Ali (because his fighting was for imamate, not Islam), and those after Ali (because they were in error or disbelief).
Each elimination is based on a specific reason from the verse or historical context.
4
Understand the conclusion and its extension
The only remaining possibility is one of the first three caliphs. Since their caliphates are interconnected, the verse validates all four caliphs (by extension to Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman, and Ali via the principle of no distinction).
This argument supports the Sunni position on the first four caliphs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : التمسك بقوله تعالى : قُل لِلْمُخَلَّفِينَ مِنَ الْأَعْرَابِ سَتَدْعَوْنَ إِلَى قَوْمٍ أولي بَأْسٍ شَدِيدٍ نُقَتِلُونَهُمْ أَو يُسْلِمُون فإن تُطِيعُوا يُؤْتِكُمُ اللهُ أَجْرًا حَسَنا وَإن تتولوا كما توليم [؟] تَوَلَّيْتُم مِن قَبْلُ يُعَذِّبَكُمْ عَذَابًا أَلِيمًا ﴾ [الفتح : ١٦] . وجه الاستدلال بالآية أن الداعي لهؤلاء الأعراب إما محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإما أحد الخلفاء الثلاثة - أعني أبا بكر وعمر وعثمان - وإما أن يكون الداعي هو علي وإما أن يكون الداعي من كان بعد علي. لا جائز أن يقال : الداعي هو محمد الا الله لقوله تعالى: ﴿سَيَقُولُ الْمُخَلَّفُونَ إِذَا انطَلَقْتُمْ إِلَى مَفَانِ [؟] لِتَأْخُذُوهَا ذَرُونَا نَتَّبِعْكُمْ يُريدون أن يبدلوا كلم الله قُل لَّن تَتَّبِعُونَا كَذلِكُمْ قَالَ اللهُ مِن قَبْلُ) [الفَتْح : ١٥] . كان ولا جائز أن يكون المراد هو علي ؛ لأنه تعالى قال : في صفة هذه الدعوة: نُقَتِلُونَهم أَوْ يُسْلِمُونَ ﴾ [الفَتْح : ١٦] ولم يتفق لعلي بعد النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قتال بسبب طلب الإسلام، بل كانت محاربته بسبب طلب الإمامة، ولا جائز أن يكون المراد من بعد علي، لأنهم عندنا كانوا على الخطأ، وعند الخصم كانوا على الكفر، وعلى التقديرين فلا يليق بهم قوله تعالى : فإن تُطِيعُوا يُؤْتِكُمُ اللهُ اجرا حسنا وإن تتولوا كما تَوَلَّيْتُم مِّن قَبْلُ يُعَذِبكُم عَذَابًا اليمام [؟] له [الفتح: ١٦] ولما بطلت هذه الأقسام لم يبق إلا أن يكون المراد به أحد الخلفاء الثلاثة أعني أبا بكر وعمر وعثمان - وعلى هذا التقدير تكون الآية دالة على صحة خلافة أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة، ومتى صحت خلافة أحدهم صحت خلافة الكل ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
The second proof: clinging to the saying of the Exalted: 'Say to the bedouins who were left behind: You will be called against a people of mighty prowess; you will fight them or they will submit. So if you obey, Allah will give you a good reward; but if you turn away as you turned away before, He will punish you with a painful punishment' [48:16]. The way of inference from the verse: The one who calls these bedouins is either Muhammad (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) or one of the three caliphs – I mean Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman – or the caller is Ali, or the caller is one after Ali. It is not possible that the caller is Muhammad (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) because of His saying: 'Those who were left behind will say, when you set out toward spoils to take them, "Let us follow you," wanting to change Allah's words. Say, "You will never follow us; thus Allah has said before"' [48:15]. And it is not possible that the intended is Ali; because He said in the description of this call: 'You will fight them or they will submit' [48:16], and no fighting occurred for Ali after the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) for the sake of Islam; rather his fighting was for the sake of the imamate. And it is not possible that the intended is after Ali, because according to us they were in error, and according to the opponent they were in disbelief; and on both estimates, His saying 'So if you obey, Allah will give you a good reward, but if you turn away as you turned away before, He will punish you with a painful punishment' [48:16] is not befitting them. When these divisions are invalidated, nothing remains except that the intended is one of the three caliphs, namely Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman. On this estimation, the verse indicates the validity of the caliphate of one of these three. And whenever the caliphate of one of them is valid, the caliphate of all is valid, necessarily, because there is no one who makes a distinction.
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
Razi's second proof is an elaborate inference from Quran 48:16. The verse addresses the bedouins who were left behind and says they will be called to fight a mighty people. Razi considers four possibilities for who the caller could be: the Prophet himself, one of the first three caliphs (Abu Bakr, Umar, Uthman), Ali, or someone after Ali. He eliminates the Prophet because the context of verse 48:15 shows that the bedouins were seeking to go out for spoils, not for a campaign led directly by the Prophet. He eliminates Ali because his battles were for the imamate, not for Islam. He eliminates those after Ali because they were either in error (Sunni view) or disbelief (Shi'a view), and such people would not be praised for obedience. Therefore, the only remaining possibility is one of the first three caliphs. Since the verse promises reward for obedience, it validates the caliphate of that caller. And because the caliphates of these three are interconnected (no distinction can be made), the proof supports all four caliphs by extension.

Key Terms
الداعي
الخلفاء الثلاثة
القتال بسبب الإمامة
لا قائل بالفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is arguing that Ali's caliphate is invalid or that his wars were wrong.
Correction: Razi does not attack Ali's legitimacy; he only argues that the verse describes fighting for Islam, not for political authority, so Ali's battles do not fit this particular verse.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi eliminate Ali as a candidate for the caller in verse 48:16?
A) Because Ali was not involved in any battles after the Prophet
B) Because Ali's battles were for the imamate, not for the sake of Islam
C) Because Ali was not among the bedouins
D) Because the verse was revealed before Ali's time

Key Takeaway
By eliminating other possible callers, Razi shows that the verse points to Abu Bakr, Umar, or Uthman, thus validating their caliphates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this elimination argument, do you find yourself hoping for a particular outcome because of your loyalty to a specific figure? How might that bias affect your acceptance of the truth?
?
The principle 'no one makes a distinction' among the caliphs is central here. In your heart, do you feel you make distinctions—perhaps valuing one companion over another? What does that reveal about your sincerity in following the guidance of the Salaf?
?
Verse 48:16 promises a good reward for obedience to the caller. Who is the caller you are obeying in your life right now—your desires, a leader, or the true authorities from the early community? Are you prepared to give up your own preferences to earn that reward?

Razi's Third Proof: Divine Praise Validates Caliphate
Razi presents a conditional proof from Quranic praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's conditional argument that God's praise of Abu Bakr in the Quran necessarily implies the validity of his caliphate.

Core Idea
Divine commendation makes caliphate valid.

Razi now turns to the Quran itself to argue that Abu Bakr's caliphate must be legitimate. The logic is simple: if his leadership were wrong, God would not have praised him in the Quran. Since the Quran clearly does praise Abu Bakr, his caliphate is validated. This lesson walks through Razi's conditional argument and the two key verses he uses to prove the premise that Abu Bakr is indeed praised by God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the overall structure: a conditional statement followed by two supporting proofs from the Quran.
Pay attention to the conditional phrase 'لو كانت... لما كان'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Razi's proof is a conditional: If the caliphate were invalid, then Abu Bakr would not be praised. But he is praised (proved by verses), so the caliphate is valid. Understand this logical form.
3
Examine the two Quranic proofs
Read carefully how Razi uses Surah al-Fath (48:18) and Surah al-Tawbah (9:100). For the second verse, note how he handles the objection about 'foremost first' to include Abu Bakr.
Razi clarifies that 'foremost' does not mean only the first believer; it includes all who had precedence in religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كانت خلافة أبي بكر باطلة لما كان ممدوحًا معظما عند الله تعالى، وقد كان كذلك، فوجب القطع بصحة خلافته. أما الملازمة فظاهرة والخصم موافق عليه. وإنما قلنا بأنه ممدوح من عند الله لوجوه: أحدها : قوله تعالى: ﴿لَقَدْ رَضِيَ الله عَنِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ إِذْ يُبَايِعُونَكَ تَحْتَ الشَّجَروا [الفتح: ١٨] وهو ممن كان بايع تحت الشجرة، فوجب أن يكون ممن رضي عنه الله. وثانيها: قوله تعالى: وَالسَّبِقُونَ الْأَوَّلُونَ مِنَ الْمُهَجِرِينَ وَالْأَنصَارِ وَالَّذِينَ اتَّبَعُوهُم بِإحْسَنِ رَضِيَ اللهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنْهُ ﴾ [التّوبَة : ۱۰۰] ولا شك أنه كان من السابقين الأولين، فإنا وإن اختلفنا في أنه هل كان إيمانه قبل إيمان الكل؟ إلا أن لفظ السابقين يفيد كل من كان له سبق في الدين ولولا أن المراد ذلك وإلا لما دخل فيه الأنصار، وإذا ثبت أنه من السابقين وجب أن يدخل تحت قوله : ورَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُمْ وَرَضُوا عَنه [التوبة: ١٠٠]
The third proof: If the caliphate of Abu Bakr were invalid, he would not be praised and magnified by Allah the Exalted. And he was indeed so. Therefore, it is necessary to decide the validity of his caliphate. As for the implication, it is apparent and the opponent agrees with it. And we only say that he is praised by Allah because of several aspects: First: His saying, 'Allah was pleased with the believers when they pledged allegiance to you under the tree' [48:18]. And he was among those who pledged allegiance under the tree, so it is necessary that he be among those with whom Allah is pleased. Second: His saying, 'And the foremost first among the Muhajirun and the Ansar and those who followed them in excellence, Allah is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him' [9:100]. There is no doubt that he was among the foremost first, for even if we differ as to whether his faith was before the faith of everyone, the term 'foremost' applies to everyone who had precedence in the religion. If not, the Ansar would not be included. So when it is established that he is among the foremost, it is necessary that he be included under His saying 'Allah is pleased with them and they are pleased with Him'.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
Razi's third proof is a conditional argument: if Abu Bakr's caliphate were invalid, Allah would not have praised him in the Quran; but Allah did praise him, therefore the caliphate is valid. Razi provides two Quranic proofs of this praise. First, Quran 48:18 states that Allah is pleased with the believers who pledged allegiance under the tree at Hudaybiyya; Abu Bakr was among those, so he is included in Allah's pleasure. Second, Quran 9:100 speaks of the 'foremost first' (السابقون الأولون) among the Muhajirun and Ansar with whom Allah is pleased. Abu Bakr is undoubtedly among them. Razi addresses a potential objection: even if one disputes whether Abu Bakr was the very first to believe, the term 'foremost' is general enough to include anyone with precedence in the religion. If it meant only the absolute first, the Ansar (who converted later) would be excluded, which is not the case. Thus, Abu Bakr is included, and his caliphate is validated by divine praise.

Key Terms
المدح
السابقون الأولون
البيعة تحت الشجرة
رضي الله عنهم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof requires that Abu Bakr was the very first person to embrace Islam.
Correction: Razi explicitly clarifies that 'foremost first' includes anyone with precedence in religion, not just the absolute first. The inclusion of the Ansar—who converted later—proves the term is general.

Checkpoint
How does Razi argue that Abu Bakr is included in Allah's pleasure from verse 9:100?
A) By stating that Abu Bakr was the first to believe
B) By showing that 'foremost first' includes all who had precedence in religion, such as Abu Bakr
C) By quoting a verse that specifically names Abu Bakr
D) By arguing that the Ansar were not included

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that if Abu Bakr's caliphate were invalid, Allah would not have praised him in the Quran; since the Quran does praise him, his caliphate must be valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah has praised Abu Bakr in the Quran, yet we often neglect reflecting on such proofs, what does that say about the depth of our conviction? Do we truly submit to divine evidence, or do we follow inherited opinions without engaging our minds?
?
Razi used logical reasoning to defend a core belief—are we willing to use our intellect to understand and strengthen our own faith, or do we shy away from thinking deeply about matters of creed? What is one area where you need to think more critically about your beliefs?
?
Abu Bakr's position is validated by seeking Allah's pleasure (رضا). In your daily life, are you more driven by seeking Allah's pleasure or by seeking approval from people? What one change can you make today to prioritize divine pleasure over human praise?

Identifying Abu Bakr in Quran 92:17-21
Razi identifies Abu Bakr as the subject of Quran 92:17-21.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quranic exegesis and logical reasoning to argue that Surah Al-Layl 92:17-21 specifically refers to Abu Bakr and not Ali.

Core Idea
A single Quranic phrase disqualifies Ali and points exclusively to Abu Bakr.

Razi presents a sophisticated argument to show that a key Quranic passage is about Abu Bakr. He begins with exegetical agreement, then uses a subtle linguistic point to eliminate the alternative candidate, Ali. This lesson walks through that careful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and Razi's claim
Begin by reading the Arabic of Quran 92:17-21 and Razi's initial statement that most exegetes agree it refers to Abu Bakr.
Notice that Razi is using both consensus and independent reasoning.
2
Trace the 'atqa' argument
Follow how Razi connects 'most righteous' to 'most noble' using Quran 49:13, then to 'best of creation.' Understand why the field narrows to Abu Bakr or Ali.
This step shows Razi's use of intra-Quranic cross-referencing.
3
Examine the exclusion of Ali
Focus on the phrase 'no requitable favor.' Razi argues this cannot apply to Ali because of his upbringing by the Prophet. See how a single Quranic restriction excludes Ali.
This is the core logical move in the argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The verse must refer to Abu Bakr. Consider why Razi needed this identification to establish his caliphate.
The identification is a necessary premise for the later arguments about superiority and divine pleasure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها: قوله تعالى: ﴿وَسَيُجَنَّبُهَا الْأَتْقَى ﴿١٧﴾ الَّذِي يُؤْتِي مَالَهُ يَتَزَكَّىٰ ﴿١٨﴾ وَمَا لِأَحَدٍ عِندَهُ مِن نِّعْمَةٍ تُجْزَىٰ ﴿١٩﴾ إِلَّا ابْتِغَاءَ وَجْهِ رَبِّهِ الْأَعْلَىٰ ﴿٢٠﴾ وَلَسَوْفَ يَرْضَىٰ ﴿٢١﴾ فنقول : اتفق الأكثرون من أهل التفسير على أن المراد من هذه الآية هو أبو بكر ونحن مع هذا نقيم الدلالة عليه. فنقول : إنه تعالى وصف الشخص المراد من هذه الآية بأنه أتقى، وإذا كان أتقى كان أكرم لقوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ أَكْرَمَكُمْ عِندَ اللهِ أَنْقَلَكُمْ [الحجرات : ١٣] والأكرم عند الله لا بد وأن يكون أفضل، فثبت أن المراد من هذه الآية شخص هو أفضل الخلق، وأجمعت الأمة على أن أفضل الخلق بعد الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم إما أبو بكر وإما علي، فإذن هذه الآية مختصة إما بأبي بكر وإما يعلي لا جائز أن تكون نازلة في حق علي ؛ لأن الشخص المراد من هذه الآية موصوف بوصف معين، وهو ليس لأحد عنده من نعمة تجزى، وعلي ما كان كذلك ؛ لأن عليا إنما نشأ في تربية محمد وطعامه عنده وشرابه وذلك نعمة تجزى. أما أبو بكر فإنه ما كان للنبي الا الله في حقه نعمة تجزى، بل كان له في حقه نعمة الإرشاد إلى الدين، إلا أن هذه النعمة لا تجزى بدليل أنه تعالى حكى عن الأنبياء - عليهم السلام - أنهم كانوا يقولون لأممهم : وَمَا أَسْلُكُمْ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ أَجْرٍ إِنْ أَجْرِيَ إِلَّا عَلَى رَبِّ [الشُّعَرَاء : ۱۰۹] ولم يقل تعالى : وما لأحد عنده من نعمة، بل قال وَمَا لِأَحَدٍ عِندَهُ مِن نِعْمَةٍ تُجرى ﴾ [الليل : ۱۹] فبهذا القيد خرج علي عن أن يكون مرادا بهذه الآية، فبقي أن يكون المراد بهذه الآية هو أبو بكر. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : دلت الآية أيضا على أنه أفضل الخلق، فإنه تعالى لما وصفه بأنه أتقى والأتقى أفضل وجب أن يكون هو أفضل الخلق، ودلت الآية أيضًا على أنه تعالى راضي عنه في الحال والاستقبال لأنه قال : ولسوف يرضى [الليل: ۲۱] وكلمة "سوف" مختصة بالاستقبال هذا يدفع سؤال من قال: لعله تعالى كان راضيا عنه في تلك الحالة ثم زال الرضاء عنه وقت اشتغاله بالخلافة ؛ لأنه تعالى بين بقوله : ﴿وَلَسَوْفَ يَرْضَى [الليل : ۲۱] بقاء ذلك الرضوان في المستقبل. فثبت بمجموع ما ذكرنا أنه لو كانت خلافته باطلة لما كان مرضيا عند الله في الحال والاستقبال، وثبت أنه مرضي عند الله في الحال والاستقبال، فوجب القطع بصحة خلافته.
And third: His statement, Exalted is He: 'But the most righteous will be removed from it, [17] who gives his wealth to purify himself [18] and not for anyone who has a favor to be requited [19] but only seeking the face of his Lord, Most High [20] and he will surely be satisfied [21].' So we say: Most of the exegetes agree that what is meant by this verse is Abu Bakr, and we, along with this, establish the proof for it. So we say: He, Exalted, described the person intended by this verse as 'most righteous' (atqa). And if he is most righteous, he is most noble (akram) because of His statement: 'Indeed, the most noble of you in the sight of Allah is the most righteous' (49:13). And the most noble in the sight of Allah must be the best (afdal). So it is established that the person intended by this verse is the best of creation. And the community (umma) agrees that the best of creation after the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, is either Abu Bakr or Ali. So this verse is specifically either concerning Abu Bakr or concerning Ali. It cannot have been revealed concerning Ali because the person intended by this verse is described with a specific attribute: 'and not for anyone who has a favor to be requited.' And Ali was not like that because Ali grew up under the upbringing of Muhammad, with his food and drink with him, and that is a favor that is requited. As for Abu Bakr, he had no favor from the Prophet [؟] that is requited; rather, he had from him the favor of guidance to the religion, but this favor is not requited, as indicated by His statement - reporting from the prophets, peace be upon them - that they said to their nations: 'And I do not ask you for it any payment; my payment is only from the Lord of the worlds' (26:109). And He did not say: 'And not for anyone who has a favor,' but rather said: 'And not for anyone who has a favor to be requited' (92:19). So by this restriction, Ali is excluded from being intended by this verse, leaving that the one intended by this verse is Abu Bakr. If this is established, then we say: The verse also indicates that he is the best of creation because He described him as most righteous, and the most righteous is best, so it is necessary that he be the best of creation. And the verse also indicates that He is pleased with him in the present and future, because He said: 'And he will surely be satisfied' (92:21), and the word 'sawfa' is specific to the future. This refutes the question of those who said: Perhaps He was pleased with him at that time, and then the pleasure ceased at the time of his involvement with the caliphate? Because He made clear by His saying: 'And he will surely be satisfied' (92:21) the continuance of that pleasure in the future. So by the totality of what we have mentioned, it is established that if his caliphate were invalid, he would not be pleasing to Allah in the present and future, and it is established that he is pleasing to Allah in the present and future, so the certainty of the validity of his caliphate is obligatory.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. Razi begins by quoting Quran 92:17-21 and states that most exegetes understand it to refer to Abu Bakr. He then proceeds to demonstrate this through a chain of reasoning. First, the verse describes a person as 'the most righteous' (al-atqa). Using Quran 49:13 ('the most noble is the most righteous'), Razi argues that this person is also the most noble and therefore the best of creation. The community agrees that after the Prophet, the best is either Abu Bakr or Ali. So the verse must apply to one of them. Then Razi examines a key phrase in the verse: 'and not for anyone who has a favor to be requited' (wa ma li-ahadin 'indahu min ni'matin tujza). He argues that this phrase cannot apply to Ali because Ali grew up under the Prophet's care, receiving food and drink, which is a favor that can be requited. In contrast, Abu Bakr had no such material favor from the Prophet; the only favor he had was the guidance to Islam, but this is not a requitable favor because prophets do not ask for payment. Razi supports this by quoting the prophets' statement 'I do not ask you for any payment' (26:109). Thus the restriction excludes Ali, leaving Abu Bakr as the intended person. Having established that the verse refers to Abu Bakr, Razi draws two conclusions: (1) Abu Bakr is the best of creation after the Prophet, since he is described as most righteous; (2) Allah is pleased with him in the present and future, because of the phrase 'and he will surely be satisfied' (92:21). The word 'sawfa' indicates future, so the pleasure continues, refuting any claim that Allah's pleasure might have ceased during his caliphate. The argument concludes that if Abu Bakr's caliphate were invalid, he would not be pleasing to Allah, but since he is pleasing, his caliphate must be valid.

Key Terms
أتقى
أفضل
خليفة
نعمة تجزى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the verse could apply to anyone who is righteous, or that it might refer to Ali because of his closeness to the Prophet.
Correction: Razi shows that the phrase 'no requitable favor' is key: Ali received material benefits from the Prophet, making him ineligible, while Abu Bakr did not.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does the Quranic phrase 'وَمَا لِأَحَدٍ عِندَهُ مِن نِّعْمَةٍ تُجْزَىٰ' exclude Ali from being the one described in Surah Al-Layl?
A) Because Ali had received many favors from the Prophet that could be requited.
B) Because Ali was not from the Quraysh.
C) Because the verse was revealed in Mecca and Ali was young.
D) Because the verse refers to Abu Bakr only.

Key Takeaway
Razi identifies Abu Bakr as the subject of Quran 92:17-21 by using the description 'most righteous' and the exclusionary phrase 'no requitable favor,' which uniquely fits Abu Bakr and not Ali.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi argues that the most righteous person (atqa) is the most noble. When you consider your own life, do you seek to be 'atqa' as your highest priority, or do you measure your worth by other standards like wealth or status? What concrete step can you take today to make righteousness your true honor?
?
The verse describes someone who gives wealth to purify himself, seeking only the face of Allah. Are there acts of charity or sacrifice in your life that you have done hoping for recognition or reward from others? How can you purify your intention to seek only Allah's face?
?
Ali had a requitable favor from the Prophet, yet Abu Bakr did not. Are there relationships where you feel entitled to reciprocation because of favors you have given or received? How can you break that cycle of expectation and give freely for the sake of Allah alone?

Superiority and Divine Pleasure Prove Caliphate
Razi concludes from the verse Abu Bakr's superiority and eternal pleasure, proving his caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses Quran 92:17-21 to argue that Abu Bakr is the best of creation after the Prophet and that Allah's ongoing pleasure with him validates his caliphate.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's caliphate is valid because he is the best and eternally pleasing to Allah.

Having established that the verse refers to Abu Bakr, Razi now draws two major conclusions: Abu Bakr's superiority over all other companions, and the certainty of Allah's pleasure with him in this life and the next. These conclusions lead directly to the indispensability of his caliphate.

Guided Path
1
Revisit the conclusion from Lesson 1
Assume that the verse has been shown to refer to Abu Bakr. Now Razi builds on that to prove Abu Bakr's superiority.
This step connects the two lessons.
2
Trace the superiority argument
The verse describes Abu Bakr as 'most righteous,' which implies he is the best of creation. Understand the logical chain from 'atqa' to 'afdal.'
Razi uses the same reasoning as before but now applies it to Abu Bakr specifically.
3
Analyze the divine pleasure argument
The verse ends with 'and he will surely be satisfied.' Razi explains that 'sawfa' indicates future and thus continuous pleasure, rebutting any claim that Allah's pleasure might have ceased later.
This is a response to potential objections about Abu Bakr's caliphate.
4
Combine to prove caliphate validity
If Abu Bakr is the best and always pleasing to Allah, then his caliphate must be valid. Follow the final conditional argument.
This is the culmination of the third proof.

Commentary
This passage is the third proof (al-hujja al-thalitha) for the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's caliphate. Razi begins by quoting Quran 92:17-21 and states that most exegetes understand it to refer to Abu Bakr. He then proceeds to demonstrate this through a chain of reasoning. First, the verse describes a person as 'the most righteous' (al-atqa). Using Quran 49:13 ('the most noble is the most righteous'), Razi argues that this person is also the most noble and therefore the best of creation. The community agrees that after the Prophet, the best is either Abu Bakr or Ali. So the verse must apply to one of them. Then Razi examines a key phrase in the verse: 'and not for anyone who has a favor to be requited' (wa ma li-ahadin 'indahu min ni'matin tujza). He argues that this phrase cannot apply to Ali because Ali grew up under the Prophet's care, receiving food and drink, which is a favor that can be requited. In contrast, Abu Bakr had no such material favor from the Prophet; the only favor he had was the guidance to Islam, but this is not a requitable favor because prophets do not ask for payment. Razi supports this by quoting the prophets' statement 'I do not ask you for any payment' (26:109). Thus the restriction excludes Ali, leaving Abu Bakr as the intended person. The word 'sawfa' indicates future, so the pleasure continues, refuting any claim that Allah's pleasure might have ceased during his caliphate. The argument concludes that if Abu Bakr's caliphate were invalid, he would not be pleasing to Allah, but since he is pleasing, his caliphate must be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Allah's pleasure with a person can change due to later actions, such as accepting the caliphate.
Correction: Razi uses the future tense 'sawfa yarda' to argue that Allah's pleasure is continuous and not subject to change, thus the caliphate is divinely approved.

Checkpoint
How does the word 'سوف' (sawfa) in Quran 92:21 contribute to Razi's argument for the validity of Abu Bakr's caliphate?
A) It indicates that Allah's pleasure is limited to the future, not the past.
B) It shows that Allah's pleasure with Abu Bakr continues indefinitely.
C) It implies that Abu Bakr will be pleased with Allah.
D) It refers to the Day of Judgment only.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves Abu Bakr's caliphate is valid because Quran 92:21 guarantees his superior status and Allah's continuous pleasure, which cannot coexist with an invalid caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that divine pleasure is the ultimate measure of a leader's legitimacy, would you be willing to support a leader even if they contradicted your personal opinions? What would that require from you?
?
When you think about your own future, do you have a 'sawfa' of hope that Allah's pleasure will continue with you, or do you rely on momentary acts of piety? What three concrete steps can you take today to align your life with that continuous pleasure?
?
Razi proves that Abu Bakr's caliphate is valid because invalid would displease Allah. Are there areas in your own life where you hold onto actions or beliefs that might displease Allah, even while claiming good intentions? What will you do about them this week?

Fourth Proof: The Companions' Testimony to Abu Bakr's Caliphate
Razi uses the unanimous address of Abu Bakr as caliph and the Quranic testimony to the companions' truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate, which combines the observable fact of the companions addressing him as caliph with the Quranic declaration of their truthfulness.

Core Idea
The truthful testimony of the companions confirms Abu Bakr as the rightful successor.

Imam Razi presents a logical argument grounded in historical observation and divine revelation. The opponents admit the fact that the companions called Abu Bakr caliph but claim it was due to dissimulation. Razi counters with the Quranic description of the companions as truthful, making their testimony reliable. This lesson examines the structure and strength of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow from observation to Quranic citation to conclusion.
Pay attention to how Razi presents the opponent's objection and immediately refutes it.
2
Identify the two premises
The argument has two main premises: (1) the companions, including Ali, addressed Abu Bakr as caliph, and (2) the Quran describes those companions as truthful. Understand how each premise is established.
The first premise is empirical; the second is scriptural.
3
Understand the opponent's objection
The opponent (al-khasm) does not deny the first premise but interprets it as taqiyya. Consider why this interpretation is problematic given the second premise.
Taqiyya would mean the companions were lying, contradicting the Quranic description.
4
See how the Quranic verse resolves the issue
Razi cites Quran 59:8 which calls the emigrant companions 'truthful'. This divine attestation overcomes the taqiyya objection because it would be inconsistent for Allah to call them truthful if they were engaging in dissimulation.
The verse applies to the same group of companions who addressed Abu Bakr as caliph.
5
Summarize the proof in your own words
Articulate the full argument: The companions called Abu Bakr caliph; Allah declared them truthful; therefore their testimony is reliable and Abu Bakr is truly the caliph.
Razi calls this argument 'close' (qarib), meaning it is straightforward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة قال بعضهم: رأينا الصحابة كانوا يقولون له : خليفة رسول الله وعلي بن أبي طالب كان يخاطبه بهذا الخطاب، والخصم يساعد عليه، إلا أنه يحمله على التقية، ثم رأينا أن الله تعالى وصف الصحابة بالصدق فقال : للفقراء الْمُهَاجِرِينَ الَّذِينَ أُخْرِجُوا مِن دِيَرِهِمْ وَأَمْوَالِهِمْ ﴾ [الحشر: ۸] إلى قوله تعالى : أُولَتِكَ هم الصَّادِقُونَ الله [الحشر: ۸] ولما ثبت أنهم خاطبوه بخليفة رسول الله وأخبر الله تعالى عن كونهم صادقين لزم الحكم بأنه كان خليفة رسول الله حقاً، وهذا الوجه قريب.
The fourth proof: Some said: We saw the Companions saying to him: 'Khalifat Rasul Allah' (Successor of the Messenger of Allah), and Ali ibn Abi Talib addressed him with this address, and the opponent agrees to it, except that he interprets it as taqiyya (dissimulation). Then we saw that Allah, Exalted, described the Companions as truthful, saying: 'For the poor emigrants who were expelled from their homes and their properties...' (59:8) up to His statement: 'Those are the truthful' (59:8). And when it is established that they addressed him as 'Khalifat Rasul Allah' and Allah informed about them that they are truthful, the judgment necessarily follows that he was truly the Successor of the Messenger of Allah. And this aspect is close.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof (al-hujja al-rabi'a) for Abu Bakr's caliphate. The argument is based on observable behavior of the Companions and Quranic verification. Razi notes that the Companions, including Ali ibn Abi Talib, addressed Abu Bakr as 'Khalifat Rasul Allah' (Successor of the Messenger of Allah). The opponent (the Shi'a) does not deny this fact but interprets it as taqiyya (dissimulation) — that is, Ali did so out of fear or precaution. Razi then invokes Quran 59:8, where Allah describes the emigrant Companions as 'truthful' (al-sadiqun). Since the Companions addressed Abu Bakr with this title and Allah attests to their truthfulness, their testimony must be accepted. Therefore, Abu Bakr was indeed the rightful successor. Razi remarks that this argument is straightforward ('qarib').

Key Terms

صدق
صحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shi'a objection that Ali's address to Abu Bakr as caliph was due to taqiyya is valid and undermines the proof.
Correction: This objection fails because the Quran describes the Companions, including Ali, as truthful (al-sadiqun). If they were lying about the caliphate, they would not be truthful, contradicting the Quranic description.

Checkpoint

A) That Abu Bakr himself claimed the caliphate.
B) That the companions called Abu Bakr 'Khalifat Rasul Allah' and God declared them truthful.
C) That Ali also accepted Abu Bakr's caliphate without any reservation.
D) That the Quran directly names Abu Bakr as caliph.

Key Takeaway
The truthful testimony of the Companions, affirmed by the Quran, establishes Abu Bakr's caliphate as a historical and theological fact.

Examine your own life: Are there situations where you have hidden your true beliefs out of fear or convenience? How does this lesson challenge you to be truthful even when it is difficult?
?

Have you ever dismissed a broadly accepted Islamic position because of a minority objection? How does this lesson call you to humility before the consensus of the Salaf?

Razi's Fifth Proof: The Dilemma of Ali's Rightful Claim
Razi presents a logical dilemma to disprove Ali's rightful claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a logical dilemma and a Quranic verse to argue that Abu Bakr's caliphate was correct.

Core Idea
A logical dilemma shows Ali's claim would make the ummah evil, contradicting the Quran.

This lesson presents Razi's fifth proof, which is a clever logical argument. It forces the opponent into a dilemma: either Ali failed to act, or the ummah abandoned him. Both outcomes are unacceptable, so the premise must be false.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the structure of the dilemma.
Notice the conditional 'if... then' structure.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Razi presents two possibilities: the ummah helped Ali or they did not. Spot these in the text.
3
Analyze the Quranic citation
See how verse 3:110 is used to refute the second possibility. Why does 'best ummah' conflict with abandoning Ali?
The verse emphasizes enjoining good and forbidding evil.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
Both horns of the dilemma lead to falsehood, so the original premise that Ali had a rightful claim must be false.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider the strength of this proof as a rational argument. What assumptions does it rely on?
It assumes that if Ali had a right, the ummah would either support or abandon him – a logical disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : لو كانت الخلافة حقا لعلي لكان إما أن يقال: الأمة أعانوه على طلب هذا الحق أو ما أعانوه فإن كان الأول وجب عليه أن يطلبه؛ لأنه إذا لم مع القدرة على الطلب كان ذلك التقصير لا محالة عليه، وإن قلنا: إنهم ما أعانوه بل خذلوه لزم أن يقال: إن هذه الأمة كانت شر الأمم ، لكنه تعالى وصف هذه الأمة بأنها خير الأمم، قال تعالى: كُنتُمْ خَيْرَ أُمَّةٍ أُخْرِجَتْ لِلنَّاسِ تَأْمُرُونَ بِالْمَعْرُوفِ وَتَنْهَوْنَ عَنِ الْمُنكَرِ ﴾ [آل عمران : ۱۱۰] فوصفهم بكونهم آمرين بكل معروف، ناهين عن كل منكر، فلو أنهم خذلوا عليا وما أعانوه على طلب حقه لكانوا شر أمة أخرجت للناس، ولما كانوا آمرين بالمعروف ولا ناهين عن المنكر، وكل ذلك باطل.
The fifth proof: If the caliphate were a right for Ali, then either it would be said: The ummah helped him seek this right, or they did not help him. If the first, then he was obliged to seek it, because if he did not [seek it] while having the ability, that failure would inevitably be upon him. And if we say: They did not help him, but rather abandoned him, then it would be necessary to say: This ummah was the worst of nations. But He, Exalted, described this ummah as the best of nations. He said: 'You are the best nation produced for mankind, enjoining what is right and forbidding what is wrong' (3:110). So He described them as enjoining every good and forbidding every evil. So if they abandoned Ali and did not help him seek his right, they would be the worst nation produced for mankind, and they would not be enjoiners of good nor forbidders of evil. All of that is false.
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth proof (al-hujja al-khamisa) for Abu Bakr's caliphate, structured as a dilemma. Razi posits a conditional: if the caliphate were rightfully Ali's, then there are two possibilities regarding the ummah's behavior. First possibility: the ummah helped Ali seek his right. In that case, Ali would have been obligated to pursue it. Since he did not pursue it, it would be his own failure, which is untenable given his virtue. Second possibility: the ummah did not help him but abandoned him. In that case, the ummah would be the worst of nations. However, Quran 3:110 explicitly states that this ummah is the best of nations, characterized by enjoining good and forbidding evil. For them to abandon a rightful claimant would contradict this description. Therefore, both possibilities are false, so the original premise (that Ali had a rightful claim) must be false. Thus, Abu Bakr's caliphate is correct.

Key Terms
خير أمة
أمر بالمعروف
نهي عن المنكر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that not supporting Ali was a collective failure, but that would imply the ummah is evil.
Correction: Razi uses Quran 3:110 to show that the ummah cannot be evil because Allah called them the best of nations.

Checkpoint
What is the second possibility in the dilemma presented in the fifth argument?
A) That the ummah supported Ali but he failed to act.
B) That the ummah did not support Ali and thus would be the worst of nations.
C) That Ali did not have a right to the caliphate.
D) That the ummah was divided and some supported Ali.

Key Takeaway
Razi's fifth proof uses a logical dilemma to show that Ali's claim would either blame Ali or condemn the ummah, both impossible, thus confirming Abu Bakr's caliphate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you had been alive at that moment, would you have recognized Ali's right or followed the majority? What does that reveal about your priorities between truth and social conformity?
?
The Quran calls this ummah the best for enjoining good and forbidding evil. But are you personally part of that description? Identify one good you have neglected to enjoin or one evil you have failed to speak against—what will you do about it today?
?
Razi argues that abandoning a rightful claim makes a community the worst. Is there a truth in your life—a right you know is true—that you are ignoring because it is unpopular or costly? What would it cost you to stand for it, and are you willing to pay that price?

Sixth Proof: Hadith of Abu Bakr and Umar with Shi'a Objections
Razi cites proof and lists two Shi'a objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for Abu Bakr's caliphate based on the hadith 'Follow Abu Bakr and Umar' and the two main Shi'a objections raised against it.

Core Idea
A command to follow implies correctness; Shi'a object on grounds of single narration and textuality.

This lesson presents the sixth proof from Razi's work, relying on the prophetic command to follow Abu Bakr and Umar. Razi argues that since the command comes from the Prophet, following them must be permissible and thus they cannot be in error. He then presents two Shi'a objections that challenge the authority of this hadith. Note that the text cuts off before Razi's response is given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice that it presents a proof and then lists objections.
The passage ends abruptly; the response to the second objection is missing.
2
Understand the proof structure
Razi cites the hadith as a command (sigha amr). He argues that a command indicates permissibility of following, and if the leaders were in error, following would not be permissible.
3
Identify the two Shi'a objections
First: the hadith is khabar wahid (solitary report), not authoritative. Second: if authentic, it would be an explicit text (nass) for imamate and should have been acted upon immediately.
These are standard arguments against relying on this hadith for caliphate.
4
Reflect on the incomplete text
Note that Razi's refutation of the objections is not included here. Think about how a Sunni scholar might respond.
This highlights the importance of context in kalam works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : التمسك بقوله الله : «اقتدوا بالذين من بعدي أبي بكر وعمر وقوله : «اقتدوا صيغة ،أمر، وهي إما للوجوب أو للندب، وعلى التقديرين فإنه يدل على جواز الاقتداء بهما في الأحكام، ولو كانا على الخطأ والضلال لما جاز ذلك، والشيعة طعنوا فيه من وجوه : أحدها: أنه خبر واحد، فلا يكون حجة. وثانيها : أن هذا لو صح لكان نصًا في ثبوت إمامته، فكان يجب عليه يوم
The sixth proof: Adherence to his statement, peace be upon him: 'Follow the two after me: Abu Bakr and Umar.' And his statement: 'Follow' is a command form, and it is either for obligation or recommendation, and in both cases it indicates the permissibility of following them in rulings. And if they were upon error and misguidance, that would not be permissible. And the Shi'a have criticized it in several ways: One: that it is a solitary report (khabar wahid), so it is not an authority. And second: that if this were authentic, it would be an explicit text (nass) for the establishment of his imamate, so it would have been necessary for him on the day...
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof (al-hujja al-sadisa) for Abu Bakr's caliphate, citing the hadith 'Iqtadu bi alladheena min ba'di: Abi Bakr wa 'Umar' (Follow those after me: Abu Bakr and Umar). Razi explains that the command 'iqtadu' (follow) is an imperative, which can indicate either obligation or recommendation. In either case, it shows that following them in religious rulings is permissible. If they were in error, following them would not be permissible. Therefore, they must be correct, implying the legitimacy of Abu Bakr's leadership. Then Razi lists two Shi'a objections to this hadith. First objection: the hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and therefore not authoritative in matters of such importance. Second objection: if the hadith were authentic, it would constitute an explicit text (nass) for Abu Bakr's imamate, which should have been acted upon immediately. The text cuts off before Razi's response to the second objection is given.

Key Terms
اقتدوا
خبر واحد
نص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the hadith is universally accepted as proof without objections.
Correction: The passage shows that even though Razi uses it as a proof, the Shi'a have raised two specific objections, indicating it is contested.

Checkpoint

B) That it is a solitary report (khabar wahid) and that it would be an explicit text (nass) for imamate.
C) That it only applies to religious matters and not political leadership.
D) That it was abrogated and that Umar was not meant.

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof uses a command from the Prophet to argue for the correctness of Abu Bakr and Umar, but it faces two significant objections from Shi'a theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Examine your own stance on the succession: is it based on what you have been taught, or have you truly wrestled with the evidences as presented in this lesson? Commit to seeking the truth even if it unsettles your current beliefs.
?
The Shi'a objection that the hadith is a solitary report raises a critical question: How do you determine what is authoritative in matters of religion? Are you following a religion based on your own preferences or on established proofs? Let this lesson push you to verify the foundations of your beliefs.

Imagine you were present at the Saqifah or after the Prophet's death. Would you have followed Abu Bakr and Umar based on this hadith? Or would you have demanded clearer proof? This lesson challenges you to think about what kind of proof you require for leadership. Is your criterion consistent with the criteria of the early Muslims? Let this prompt you to purify your intention: are you seeking truth or just confirmation of your own group's position?
