Eighth Proof: Abu Bakr's Superiority Establishes His Imamate
Razi presents three proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority and concludes he must be imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's eighth proof for Abu Bakr's imamate, which argues that Abu Bakr is the best of creation and therefore must be the imam.

Core Idea
The best person must be imam, and Abu Bakr is proven best by Quran and hadith.

In this lesson, we examine the eighth proof presented by Imam al-Razi for the imamate of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. Razi builds his argument on a simple premise: the best person is most deserving of leadership. He then provides three pieces of evidence—a Quranic verse and two prophetic reports—to establish that Abu Bakr is indeed the best of all people after the prophets. This proof is particularly effective because even the opponent agrees with the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the structure of the proof become clear.
Notice that Razi states his premise first, then gives evidence, then concludes.
2
Identify the three pieces of evidence
Razi uses three distinct proofs for Abu Bakr's superiority. Find each one and note the type: one Quranic verse and two hadiths.
The hadiths are described as 'well-known' (khabar mashhur) and a direct statement from the Prophet.
3
Understand the conclusion
After establishing superiority, Razi concludes that Abu Bakr must be imam. Note that he mentions the opponent agrees with this premise.
This makes the proof stronger, as it uses a point of agreement.
4
Reflect on the argument's structure
Consider why Razi presents this proof and how it fits into the broader debate on imamate. Think about how the opponent might respond.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : أن أبا بكر أفضل الخلق والأفضل هو الإمام، إنما قلنا إنه هو الأفضل لوجوه.
أحدها : التمسك بقوله تعالى : وسيجنبها الأنقى الأنقى [الليل : ١٧] وقد مر تقريره
وثانيها : الخبر المشهور، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : والله ما طلعت الشمس ولا غربت على أحد بعد النبيين أفضل من أبي بكر
وثالثها : قوله لأبي بكر وعمر: هذا سيدا كهول أهل الجنة ما خلا النبيين والمرسلين
وإذا ثبت أنه أفضل وجب أن يكون هو الإمام، للوجه الذي تمسك به الخصم وأيضًا فالخصم موافق في هذه المقدمة.
The eighth proof: That Abu Bakr is the best of creation, and the best is the imam. We only say that he is the best because of several aspects:
First: Adherence to the saying of the Exalted: 'But the most righteous will be removed from it' [Surah al-Layl 92:17] – and its explanation has already passed.
Second: The well-known report (khabar mashhur), which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set upon anyone after the prophets better than Abu Bakr.'
Third: His saying to Abu Bakr and Umar: 'These two are the masters of the elderly of Paradise, except for the prophets and messengers.'
If it is established that he is the best, then he must be the imam, due to the aspect with which the opponent argued, and also the opponent agrees with this premise.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth proof (al-hujja al-thamina) for Abu Bakr's imamate. Razi states the premise: Abu Bakr is the best of all people (afdal al-khalq), and the best person deserves to be imam. He then provides three pieces of evidence for Abu Bakr's superiority. The first is a Qur'anic verse: 'But the most righteous will be removed from it' (92:17), which he says has been explained earlier. The second is a well-known hadith (khabar mashhur) in which the Prophet swears that no one after the prophets is better than Abu Bakr. The third is another hadith where the Prophet calls Abu Bakr and Umar the masters of the elderly of Paradise, excluding prophets and messengers. From these, Razi concludes that since Abu Bakr is the best, he must be the imam. He adds that even the opponent (the Shi'a) accepts the premise that the best person should be the imam, so this proof is conceded.

Key Terms
أفضل الخلق
الأفضل هو الإمام
الخبر المشهور
سيدا كهول أهل الجنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent (Shi'a) would reject the entire proof because they don't accept Abu Bakr's superiority.
Correction: Razi notes that the opponent actually agrees with the premise that the best person should be imam; the disagreement is about who is best.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three proofs Razi uses to show Abu Bakr's superiority?
A) Quranic verse from Surah al-Layl
B) A well-known hadith that no one after the prophets is better than Abu Bakr
C) The hadith that Abu Bakr and Umar are masters of the elderly of Paradise
D) The fact that Abu Bakr was the first to accept Islam

Key Takeaway
Razi's proof for Abu Bakr's imamate rests on establishing his superiority through Quran and hadith, and then applying the agreed-upon principle that the best person leads.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own beliefs about the companions, do you truly hold Abu Bakr as the best after the prophets in your heart, or do you merely pay lip service? In what practical ways does your daily life reflect that conviction?
?
Razi uses three proofs to establish Abu Bakr's superiority. Which of these proofs impacts you most personally? Have you ever sincerely pondered the meaning of the hadith that no one after the prophets is better than Abu Bakr, and what does that demand of your own loyalty to the Sunnah?
?
The opponent supposedly agrees that the best should be imam. But do you live as if the best of this ummah are those who followed the Prophet most closely? Whom do you truly admire and try to emulate in your life—worldly heroes or the righteous predecessors?

The Ninth Proof: Appointment to Prayer Implies General Caliphate
Razi argues that appointment to prayer proves general caliphate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer serves as a rational proof for his overall caliphate, and how Imam Ali's statement supports this inference.

Core Idea
Leading prayer leads to leading the Ummah.

Imam Razi presents one of the key proofs for Abu Bakr's caliphate: the Prophet's choice of him to lead prayer. This proof is based on the principle that religious deputyship extends to worldly authority, as endorsed by Imam Ali himself. Read on to see the logical steps and the response to a common objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Get a feel for Razi's concise argumentative style.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا قائل بالفرق' — no one makes a distinction.
2
Identify the logical chain
Trace the inference: appointment to prayer → caliph in prayer → caliph in all affairs. Notice the principle 'no one distinguishes'.
3
Examine Imam Ali's testimony
Read Ali's reported statement. See how it reinforces the proof by linking religious and worldly leadership.
This quote is presented as support from the highest authority after the Prophet.
4
Consider the objection and response
Razi anticipates doubt about the historical record. He counters by citing authentic books like Sahih al-Bukhari.
Note the rhetorical question: 'How can it be refuted merely by caprice?'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أنه استخلفه على الصلاة أيام مرض موته، وما عزله عن ذلك فوجب أن يبقى بعد موته خليفة له في الصلاة وإذا ثبتت خلافته في الصلاة ثبتت خلافته في سائر الأمور، ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق وهذا الوجه الذي تمسك به أمير المؤمنين علي عليه السلام في إثبات إمامة الصديق حيث قال : " لا نقيلك ولا نستقيلك، قدمك رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لديننا، أفلا نقدمك في أمور دنيانا؟".
فإن قالوا : لم يثبت أنه استخلفه في أمر الصلاة مدة مرضه قلنا: هذه القضية لا يمكن التوصل إليها إلا بالروايات والكتب الصحيحة، والأخبار ناطقة بذلك، مثل صحيح البخاري وغيره، فكيف يمكن مدافعته بمجرد التشهي.
The ninth proof: That he [the Prophet] appointed him [Abu Bakr] over prayer during the days of his terminal illness, and he did not remove him from that. Therefore, it must be that after his death he remained his caliph in prayer. If his caliphate in prayer is established, then his caliphate in all other matters is established, necessarily because there is no one who makes a distinction [between prayer and other matters]. This is the aspect with which the Commander of the Faithful, Ali (peace be upon him), argued in establishing the imamate of al-Siddiq, when he said: 'We do not dismiss you nor do we ask to be dismissed. The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, advanced you in our religion, so shall we not advance you in our worldly affairs?'
If they say: It is not established that he appointed him in the matter of prayer during his illness, we say: This issue cannot be reached except through reliable narrations and authentic books, and reports speak of it, such as Sahih al-Bukhari and others. So how can it be refuted merely by caprice?
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof (al-hujja al-tasi'a) for Abu Bakr's caliphate. Razi argues that the Prophet appointed Abu Bakr to lead the prayer during his final illness and never revoked that appointment. Therefore, after the Prophet's death, Abu Bakr remained his deputy (khalifa) in prayer, and by extension in all matters, because no one distinguishes between religious and worldly authority in this context. Razi supports this by quoting Caliph Ali (alayhi al-salam) himself, who reportedly said to Abu Bakr: 'We do not dismiss you nor ask to be dismissed; the Messenger advanced you in our religion, so shall we not advance you in our worldly affairs?' This shows that even Ali accepted this logic. Razi then anticipates an objection: that the appointment to prayer is not historically proven. He counters by citing authentic books like Sahih al-Bukhari, and argues that such well-attested reports cannot be rejected out of mere whim.

Key Terms
استخلفه
خليفة
الصلاة
الخلافة
لا قائل بالفرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the appointment to lead prayer is only about prayer and does not indicate general leadership.
Correction: Razi argues that no one distinguishes between prayer and other matters, so if Abu Bakr is caliph in prayer, he is caliph in all affairs.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the significance of the Prophet appointing Abu Bakr to lead prayer?
A) It shows Abu Bakr was the most knowledgeable in prayer.
B) It implies Abu Bakr is the general caliph in all affairs.
C) It was only a temporary arrangement and not binding.
D) It was done against the Prophet's will.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer is a strong indication of his overall caliphate, as affirmed by Imam Ali and defended by authentic narrations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you separated your religious duties from your everyday decisions? If the Prophet's appointment of a prayer leader set the example for all leadership, why do you still compartmentalize your faith? Commit today to letting your faith guide one worldly decision you have been avoiding.
?
Ali himself acknowledged Abu Bakr's precedence based on the prayer argument. Is there a truth you have resisted because of pride or sectarian bias, even when the evidence is clear? What step can you take this week to submit to truth, even if it challenges your comfort zone?
?
The proof relies on authentic hadith. Are you willing to accept revelation-based reasoning even when your intellect initially finds it difficult? What personal preference or assumption are you clinging to that might contradict a well-established teaching? Surrender it now.

Tenth Proof: Imamate by Choice Necessitates Abu Bakr's Imamate
Razi's tenth proof: imamate by choice necessitates Abu Bakr's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's logical argument that the imamate must be established by choice, and that this necessarily leads to the recognition of Abu Bakr as the rightful imam.

Core Idea
If imamate is by choice, and choice points to Abu Bakr, his imamate is certain.

In this passage, Razi presents a concise but powerful argument for the imamate of Abu Bakr. He assumes that there are only two ways to establish imamate: explicit divine text (nass) or community choice (ikhtiyar). After dismissing the possibility of nass (to be proven later), he concludes that choice is the only valid method. Then he notes that all who accept choice agree that Abu Bakr is the imam, thereby establishing certainty about his position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice how Razi structures his argument in two conditional steps.
The argument is highly compressed; each phrase carries weight.
2
Identify the two candidates for the method of imamate
Razi states that the imamate is attained either by nass (text) or ikhtiyar (choice). Understand what these terms mean in this context.
Nass here refers to explicit divine appointment, a view attributed to the Shia.
3
Trace the elimination of nass
Razi says that the claim of nass is invalid, and he will prove it later. For now, he assumes its invalidity, leaving only choice as the method.
This is a strategic move: the burden of proof is shifted to the proponents of nass.
4
Connect the method to the person
Razi then asserts that all who accept choice agree that Abu Bakr is the imam. This implies a consensus among those who hold the correct method. Therefore, Abu Bakr's imamate is necessarily true.
The phrase 'no one makes a distinction' means that no one accepts choice but rejects Abu Bakr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : أن طريق حصول الإمامة إما النص أو الاختيار، وبطل القول بالنص - على ما ستأتي دلالته - فبقي القول بالاختيار، وكل من قال : طريق الإمامة هو الاختيار قال : الإمام هو أبو بكر، فوجب القطع بصحة إمامته ضرورة أنه لا قائل بالفرق.
The tenth proof: That the way of attaining the imamate is either by definitive text (nass) or by choice (ikhtiyar). And the claim of definitive text is invalid – as its proof will come later – so the claim of choice remains. And everyone who says that the way of imamate is choice says that the imam is Abu Bakr. Therefore, it is necessary to be certain of the correctness of his imamate, necessarily because there is no one who makes a distinction [between the method and the result].
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth proof (al-hujja al-ashira). Razi summarizes that the imamate can be established either by explicit divine text (nass) or by the community's choice (ikhtiyar). He asserts that the argument for nass (referring to the Shi'a claim of explicit appointment of Ali) is invalid, as he will demonstrate later. Therefore, the only valid method is choice. Then he notes that all those who accept choice (i.e., the Sunni community) agree that the imam is Abu Bakr. Thus, it is certain that Abu Bakr's imamate is correct, because no one distinguishes between accepting the method of choice and accepting its result. This is a concise logical argument: if the premisses are granted (that nass is false and that choice leads to Abu Bakr), the conclusion follows necessarily.

Key Terms
النص
الاختيار
طريق الإمامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that even if nass is false, choice could lead to another candidate besides Abu Bakr, so the argument is weak.
Correction: Razi asserts that all who accept choice agree on Abu Bakr, meaning there is a consensus among the proponents of choice. If there were disagreement, the argument would not hold, but Razi presents it as a fact. The strength of the argument depends on the truth of that consensus.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's tenth proof, what two possible ways of attaining imamate does he consider?
A) Election and conquest
B) Text (nass) and choice (ikhtiyar)
C) Inheritance and appointment
D) Consensus and force

Key Takeaway
Razi's tenth proof reduces the imamate to a simple syllogism: only two methods exist, one is invalid, the other leads to Abu Bakr, so his imamate is certain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you willing to follow an argument to its logical conclusion even when it leads to a result you might not personally prefer? Razi's logic leads to Abu Bakr—do you accept it because it is logically necessary, or do you resist due to bias?
?
This proof hinges on the consensus of those who accept choice. Do you trust the collective agreement of scholars on matters of faith, or do you require personal verification for every point? How does your answer affect your spiritual certainty?
?
The source says 'no one makes a distinction.' In your own life, do you ever separate a principle you claim to accept from its practical application when it becomes inconvenient? Will you commit to consistency between what you profess and how you act?

Razi's Refutation of Ijma-Based Argument for Ali's Imamate
Razi refutes the objection that ijma limits imamate to three candidates by questioning the validity of ijma as proof and invoking taqiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two-fold refutation of the argument that consensus determines Ali as the exclusive imam: first by challenging the necessity of an infallible imam in every age, and second by invoking the possibility of taqiyya.

Core Idea
Consensus is not proof if the infallible imam may have agreed out of fear.

Razi takes on a central Shi'a proof for Ali's imamate: that the community agreed the imam is one of three, and since only Ali was infallible, he must be the imam. Razi's response cuts deep—he questions whether consensus is even a valid proof, especially if the infallible imam could have dissimulated. This lesson walks through his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first paragraph of the Arabic passage, which presents the Shi'a objection. Note the key claim: ijma limits imamate to three candidates, and only Ali qualifies.
The objection relies on the necessity of an infallible imam.
2
Identify Razi's first challenge
Razi begins by questioning the basis of ijma itself: 'Why do you say that ijma is a proof?' This shifts the burden to the opponent.
3
Understand the opponent's justification
The opponents argue that ijma is proof because the age must contain an infallible imam, and his inclusion makes the consensus true.
This is a key premise for their argument.
4
Analyze Razi's two-pronged rebuttal
First, Razi denies that the age must contain an infallible imam. Second, even if granted, the infallible imam might have agreed out of taqiyya and fear, not conviction.
This undermines the reliability of the supposed consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشبهة الأولى، وهي قولهم : أجمعت الأمة على أن الإمام إما علي أو أبو بكر أو العباس وأبو بكر والعباس لا يصلحان للإمامة؛ لأن الإمام يجب أن يكون واجب العصمة وهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة، فلم يصلحا للإمامة، فتعين علي عليه السلام للإمامة.
فالجواب: هب أن الأمة أجمعت على أن الإمام أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة، فلم قلتم إن الإجماع حجة قالوا لأنا دللنا على أن الزمان لا يخلو عن وجود المعصوم، وإذا أجمعت الأمة اشتمل إجماعهم على قوله، وقوله حق، والمشتمل على الحق حق، فكان إجماع الأمة حقا من هذا الوجه.
قلنا : لا نسلم أن الزمان لا يخلو عن وجود معصوم، وقد بينا ضعف دليلكم فيه، سلمنا، ذلك لكن لا يلزم من هذا أن الإجماع حجة لاحتمال أن الإمام خاف القوم فوافقهم على باطلهم على سبيل التقية والخوف وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من هذا القدر أن الإجماع حجة.
As for the first doubt, which is their saying: 'The community (umma) has unanimously agreed that the imam is either Ali or Abu Bakr or al-Abbas. But Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not fit for the imamate because the imam must be infallible (wajib al-'isma), and they were not infallible. Therefore, they are not fit for the imamate, so Ali (peace be upon him) is specifically designated for the imamate.'
The answer: Suppose the community agreed that the imam is one of these three. Why do you say that consensus (ijma') is a proof? They say: Because we have demonstrated that time cannot be devoid of the existence of an infallible one. When the community agrees, their consensus includes his saying, and his saying is truth, and what contains truth is true. So the consensus of the community is true from this aspect.
We say: We do not accept that time cannot be devoid of the existence of an infallible one, and we have already shown the weakness of your proof on this point. Even if we grant that, it does not follow from this that consensus is a proof, because it is possible that the imam feared the people and agreed with them on their falsehood out of dissimulation (taqiyya) and fear. If this is the case, then it does not follow from this that consensus is a proof.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage addresses the first objection (al-shubha al-ula) raised by the opponents (likely Shi'a). The objection is that the community has consensus (ijma') that the imam is one of three: Ali, Abu Bakr, or al-Abbas. Since Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not infallible (ma'sum), and the imam must be infallible, they are disqualified, leaving Ali as the only candidate. Therefore, Ali is the rightful imam. Razi responds by first challenging the validity of the supposed consensus. He asks why they consider consensus a proof. The opponents argue that consensus is proof because the age must contain an infallible person (the imam). Their consensus includes that infallible person's opinion, which is true, so the consensus is true. Razi rejects this by denying that the age must contain an infallible imam—he claims they have already shown the weakness of that argument. Even if it were granted, Razi says it does not prove consensus is a proof, because the infallible imam might have agreed with the majority out of taqiyya (dissimulation) and fear, not because the majority opinion is correct. Thus, the consensus argument fails.

Key Terms
الشبهة
الإجماع
واجب العصمة
التقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If an infallible imam exists and his opinion is part of a consensus, that consensus must be true and authoritative.
Correction: Razi argues that even if an infallible imam exists, he might have outwardly agreed with the majority out of fear or dissimulation (taqiyya), so the consensus does not necessarily reflect truth.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the opponent's claim that consensus is a proof in this context?
A) Because consensus can never be a proof in religious matters.
B) Because the infallible imam might have agreed out of taqiyya, not conviction.
C) Because the consensus was not properly documented.
D) Because Abu Bakr and al-Abbas were actually infallible.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Shi'a argument from consensus by denying the necessity of an infallible imam in every age and by positing that the imam might have dissimulated, thus undermining consensus as a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a situation where the majority holds a view you believe is false, do you have the courage to dissent, or do you remain silent out of fear? How does this lesson's discussion of taqiyya challenge your own tendency to conform?
?
Razi questions the authority of consensus because the infallible imam might have dissimulated. Have you ever outwardly agreed with something you knew was wrong to avoid conflict? What does that say about your own integrity in matters of truth?
?
This lesson shows that even a widely accepted consensus can be invalid if based on fear. Are there beliefs you hold simply because 'everyone believes them'? Will you examine them with the same scrutiny Razi applied to the Shi'a argument?

Razi's Response on Imamate by Bay'a
Razi responds to the objection that Abu Bakr's imamate must be by text or bay'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi defends Abu Bakr's imamate as established by bay'a and why the objection based on text versus bay'a is already answered.

Core Idea
Bay'a is a valid basis for imamate, and previous arguments already refute the doubts against it.

In this passage, Razi addresses the second objection raised against Abu Bakr's imamate. The opponents argue that imamate must come either by explicit text (nass) or by bay'a, and they reject both options. Razi simply affirms that bay'a is sufficient and points out that earlier discussions have already answered the criticisms of bay'a. This shows that the objection is not new and does not require a fresh refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the concise structure: objection followed by response.
2
Identify the objection
The opponents present a dichotomy: if Abu Bakr is imam, his imamate must come either by nass (text) or bay'a (allegiance). They assume both are invalid.
This is the 'second doubt'.
3
Understand Razi's response
Razi states that Abu Bakr's imamate was indeed established by bay'a. He then refers to earlier refutations of doubts against bay'a.
He does not repeat the arguments here.
4
Reflect on the argument
Notice that Razi does not introduce new evidence; he relies on previous discussions. This shows that the objection is not novel.
This is a common method in kalam: referring to earlier answers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : لو كان أبو بكر إماما لكانت إمامته إما أن تثبت بالنص أو بالبيعة قلنا : حصلت إمامته بالبيعة والشبهات التي ذكرتموها في إبطال البيعة قد سبق الجواب عنها.
As for the second doubt: which is their saying: 'If Abu Bakr were imam, his imamate would have to be established either by explicit text (nass) or by allegiance (bay'a).' We say: His imamate was established by allegiance, and the doubts you mentioned in invalidating allegiance (bay'a) have already been answered earlier.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection (al-shubha al-thaniya). The opponents argue that if Abu Bakr were truly the imam, his imamate must have come either through a clear textual designation (nass) or through a valid pledge of allegiance (bay'a). Since they reject both, they conclude Abu Bakr cannot be imam. Razi's response is straightforward: he affirms that Abu Bakr's imamate was indeed established by bay'a. He then points out that the various objections the opponents have raised against the validity of bay'a have already been refuted in previous discussions. Thus, this objection presents nothing new and is already answered.

Key Terms
الشبهة الثانية
النص
البيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Bay'a cannot establish imamate unless there is explicit text.
Correction: Razi shows that bay'a is indeed a valid method for establishing imamate, and earlier arguments have already defended it against objections.

Checkpoint
How does Razi say Abu Bakr's imamate was established?
A) By explicit text (nass)
B) By allegiance (bay'a)
C) By inheritance
D) By conquest

Key Takeaway
Razi affirms that Abu Bakr's imamate was established by bay'a, and earlier answers already refute any objections against bay'a.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The opponents insisted on a rigid either-or framework for establishing leadership. In your own life, are there areas where you limit God's ways of establishing truth or authority to only a narrow set, while rejecting valid alternatives that have been practiced? Will you commit to being open to all legitimate ways God has ordained, even when they challenge your assumptions?
?
Razi dismisses the objection by noting that the doubts against bay'a have already been answered. Do you tend to repeat objections that have already been resolved, simply because you haven't studied the refutations? What will you do to ensure you are not raising outdated or already-answered doubts?

Refuting the Claim of Explicit Text: The Transmission Argument
Razi challenges the transmission of the alleged explicit text and counters the fame objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's first line of argument against the Shi'a claim of an explicit text for Ali, which challenges the transmission chain and demonstrates that widespread fame is not a reliable indicator of truth.

Core Idea
Widespread fame does not guarantee truth—false rumors spread too.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi confronts one of the main Shi'a arguments for Ali's immediate succession: the claim that there exists an explicit, clear text designating him. Razi's initial response does not address the content of the alleged text but rather its transmission. He argues that the report never reached the level of tawatur, and he counters the objection that it would have become famous by noting that false rumors also become widespread.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation to grasp the two-part argument.
Notice the structure: claim, refutation, counter, answer.
2
Identify the two claims
Razi first challenges the transmission (tawatur) and then counters the fame objection. See how each part builds on the previous.
3
Understand the counterargument
The opponent says the text would be famous if true. Razi responds with an example of false rumors that become famous.
This is a key logical move: fame ≠ truth.
4
Reflect on the principle
Think about why this argument is important in theological debates: it separates transmission reliability from popularity.
This undermines the opponent's appeal to widespread acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي ادعاء النص الجلي فجوابها أنا لا نسلم أن الرواة كانوا في جميع الأعصار بالغين إلى حد الكثرة المعتبرة في التواتر. قوله : ولو كانوا في حد الآحاد في بعض الأعصار لاشتهر الآن وليس الأمر كذلك، قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين :
الأول : لا نسلم أنه يجب أن يشتهر ذلك والدليل عليه أن كثيرًا من الأراجيف الكاذبة قد اشتهرت الآن في الشرق والغرب، ولا نعلم أن زمان ذلك
As for the third doubt: which is the claim of explicit text (nass jali), its answer is that we do not accept that the transmitters reached at all times the degree of multiplicity considered necessary for tawatur (mass transmission). His statement: 'Even if they were at the level of solitary reports in some eras, it would have become famous now—but that is not the case.' We say: The answer to it is from two aspects:
First: We do not accept that it must be famous. The proof of that is that many false rumors have become famous now in the East and West, and we do not know the time of that...
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage begins the response to the third objection (al-shubha al-thalitha), which is the Shi'a claim that there is an explicit text (nass jali) designating Ali as the Prophet's successor. Razi's first line of defense is to challenge the transmission of this alleged text. He states that the narrators of this report did not, in every era, reach the number required for tawatur (mass transmission), so the report is not certain. He then paraphrases the opponent's counterargument: if the report was transmitted by solitary chains in some periods, it would have become widely famous by now—but it is not famous, so its authenticity is questionable. Razi responds with two points. Only the first is given: that it is not necessary for a true report to become widespread, because many false rumors (arajiif kadhiba) have spread across the East and West without a known basis. This shows that wide fame is not a reliable indicator of truth. The passage cuts off, but the argument continues on the next page.

Key Terms
النص الجلي
التواتر
الآحاد
الأراجيف الكاذبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A report that becomes widely famous must be true.
Correction: Razi points out that many false rumors become famous, so fame is not a reliable indicator of truth.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's first response to the claim that the text for Ali would have become famous if it were true?
A) He agrees that it would be famous.
B) He says that many false rumors have become famous, so fame is not a proof of truth.
C) He argues the text is actually in the Quran.
D) He says the text is famous but suppressed.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the claim of an explicit text for Ali fails because its transmission is not mass-transmitted, and widespread fame is not a proof of truth since false rumors also spread widely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted something about your religion simply because it was widely repeated, without verifying its source? How will you change that after learning that fame alone does not prove truth?
?
Razi uses the example of false rumors spreading widely. Are there any popular teachings or practices you follow that you have not critically examined through authentic transmission? What is one step you can take this week to verify a commonly held belief?
?
This lesson shows that the chain of transmission matters for religious knowledge. How diligently have you checked the basis of your own core beliefs? Commitment: name one belief you will investigate starting today.

Razi's Refutation of the First Shubha: Ijma' and Infallibility
Razi answers the Shia argument that ijma' and infallibility prove Ali's imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the Shia argument for Ali's imamate based on ijma' and infallibility, by questioning the proof of ijma' and raising the possibility of taqiyya.

Core Idea
Even if consensus existed, fear (taqiyya) can render it unreliable.

Razi now tackles a key Shia proof: the claim that the community agreed that the imam must be one of three, and since only Ali is infallible, he is the imam. In his response, Razi does not simply reject the consensus; he first grants it for argument and then challenges the underlying assumptions about the binding nature of ijma' and the possibility of dissimulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: a Shia assertion followed by Razi's two-part refutation.
The passage is a classic example of munazara (disputation).
2
Identify the Shia argument
The Shia claim that ijma' points to Ali. Identify the premises: the imam is one of three, Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not infallible, so Ali must be imam.
3
Understand Razi's first response
Razi grants the premise but asks why ijma' is a proof. The Shia respond by arguing that an infallible imam exists in every age, so his statement is included in the consensus, making it true.
Razi does not accept this premise; he challenges it.
4
Understand Razi's second response
Razi denies the necessity of an infallible imam at all times. Even if accepted, he raises the possibility of taqiyya: the imam might have agreed out of fear, making the consensus unreliable.
This is the decisive refutation.
5
Reflect on the method
Notice how Razi uses the method of munazara: first granting the opponent's premise for argument, then attacking the underlying proof and raising a counter-possibility.
This method is common in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشبهة الأولى، وهي قولهم : أجمعت الأمة على أن الإمام إما علي أو أبو بكر أو العباس وأبو بكر والعباس لا يصلحان للإمامة؛ لأن الإمام يجب يكون واجب العصمة وهما ما كانا واجبي العصمة، فلم يصلحا للإمامة، فتعين علي للإمامة.
فالجواب: هب أن الأمة أجمعت على أن الإمام أحد هؤلاء الثلاثة، فلم قلتم إن الإجماع حجة قالوا لأنا دللنا على أن الزمان لا يخلو عن وجود المعصوم، وإذا أجمعت الأمة اشتمل إجماعهم على قوله، وقوله حق، والمشتمل على الحق حق، فكان إجماع الأمة حقا من هذا الوجه.
قلنا : لا نسلم أن الزمان لا يخلو عن وجود معصوم، وقد بينا ضعف دليلكم فيه، سلمنا، ذلك لكن لا يلزم من هذا أن الإجماع حجة لاحتمال أن الإمام خاف القوم فوافقهم على باطلهم على سبيل التقية والخوف وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم من هذا القدر أن الإجماع حجة.
As for the first shubha (doubt), which is their saying: The community (umma) has agreed (ijma'at) that the imam is either Ali or Abu Bakr or al-Abbas, and Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not suitable for the imamate because the imam must be infallible (wajib al-isma) and they were not infallible, so they are not suitable for the imamate; therefore Ali is specified for the imamate.
The answer: Grant (hab) that the community agreed that the imam is one of these three, but why do you say that ijma' (consensus) is a proof? They said: Because we have proved that time is not free from the existence of an infallible one (al-masum), and when the community agrees, their consensus includes his statement, and his statement is true, and what includes truth is true; so the consensus of the community is true from this aspect.
We said: We do not accept (la nusallim) that time is not free from the existence of an infallible one, and we have shown the weakness of your proof concerning it. Even if we accepted that, it does not follow from this that ijma' is a proof, because of the possibility that the imam feared the people and agreed with them on their falsehood out of dissimulation (taqiyya) and fear. If this is the case, it does not follow from this degree that ijma' is a proof.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The Shia objection argues, on the basis of ijma' (consensus), that the imam must be one of three individuals, and since Abu Bakr and al-Abbas are not infallible, Ali must be the imam. Razi's answer proceeds in two steps: First, he grants the premise for argument (hab) but questions why ijma' is considered a proof. The Shia respond by claiming that their proof of an infallible imam at all times ensures that the consensus includes his statement, making it true. Second, Razi rejects this by denying the premise that time cannot lack an infallible imam, and even if it were accepted, he argues that ijma' still does not constitute a proof because of the possibility of taqiyya—that the imam might have consented out of fear. Thus, the Shia argument collapses. The passage demonstrates the method of disputation (munazara) by first granting the opponent's premise for argument and then refuting the underlying principles.

Key Terms
الإجماع
العصمة
التقية
المعصوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Razi rejects the concept of ijma' entirely.
Correction: Razi only questions the applicability of ijma' in this specific case due to the possibility of taqiyya, not the general authority of ijma'.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Razi gives for rejecting the Shia argument from ijma' even if one accepts the existence of an infallible imam at all times?
A) The infallible imam might not have been present at the time of the alleged ijma'.
B) The imam might have dissembled out of fear (taqiyya), making the consensus unreliable.
C) The reports of the ijma' are not mutawatir.
D) The Shia themselves disagree on who the infallible imam is.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Shia argument by showing that the alleged consensus does not constitute a proof due to the possibility of dissimulation (taqiyya).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief as 'certain' simply because many people around you agreed with it? This lesson shows that even a consensus can be unreliable due to hidden motives like fear. What is one belief you hold today that you have not examined critically because it is widely accepted among your community? Will you commit to re-examining it with the same rigor Razi applied to the Shia argument?
?
Razi points out that an infallible imam might practice dissimulation out of fear. How often do you silence your own conscience by outwardly agreeing with something you know is false, simply because you fear the reaction of others? What situation in your life right now is tempting you to hide your true conviction? Will you ask Allah for courage to speak the truth, even if it costs you?
?
The Shia used the concept of infallibility to argue for Ali's imamate, but Razi dismantled their proof by showing a logical gap. In your own faith journey, are there arguments you hold on to emotionally rather than with sound reasoning? Can you identify one belief you find hard to defend with clear proof? This lesson challenges you to examine your intellectual foundations. Are you willing to submit even your cherished opinions to the test of truth?

The Transmission and Fame of the Alleged Nass Jali
Razi answers the second shubha (bay'a) and begins refuting the third (nass jali) by attacking its transmission and fame.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's arguments that the alleged explicit text (nass jali) for Ali's imamate was not transmitted in a way that ensures certainty, and that its fame is not evidence of authenticity.

Core Idea
Fame does not prove truth; false rumors spread widely, and the nass jali claim originates from known liars.

Razi begins his refutation of the Shia claim of an explicit textual designation for Ali by questioning its transmission. He argues that the transmitters never reached the level of tawatur (mass transmission) required for certainty, and even if they did in some ages, the report should have become universally famous; yet it did not. He counters the fame objection with examples of false rumors and points out that the doctrine's originators were notorious liars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two shubhas
Read the passage and note that Razi first addresses the second shubha (bay'a) briefly, then moves to the third (nass jali). The second is dismissed by reference to previous answers.
The second shubha is very short; focus on the third.
2
Understand the denial of tawatur
Razi states that the transmitters of the alleged nass jali never reached the threshold of tawatur in any age. This means the report cannot be considered certain.
3
Examine the two responses to the fame argument
The Shia object that even if it were ahad in some ages, it should have become famous. Razi gives two rebuttals: (a) false rumors become famous without known origin, (b) the report simply did not reach the opponents.
Consider the example of widespread lies.
4
Trace the doctrine's origin
Razi identifies Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu Isa al-Warraq as the originators of the nass jali claim, both known for lying. This discredits the tradition.
This is an ad hominem but historically significant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : لو كان أبو بكر إماما لكانت إمامته إما أن تثبت بالنص أو بالبيعة قلنا : حصلت إمامته بالبيعة والشبهات التي ذكرتموها في إبطال البيعة قد سبق الجواب عنها.
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي ادعاء النص الجلي فجوابها أنا لا نسلم أن الرواة كانوا في جميع الأعصار بالغين إلى حد الكثرة المعتبرة في التواتر. قوله : ولو كانوا في حد الآحاد في بعض الأعصار لاشتهر الآن وليس الأمر كذلك، قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين :
الأول : لا نسلم أنه يجب أن يشتهر ذلك والدليل عليه أن كثيرًا من الأراجيف الكاذبة قد اشتهرت الآن في الشرق والغرب، ولا نعلم أن زمان ذلك الوضع، أي زمان كان ؟ ولا أن ذلك الواضع من كان؟.
وأيضًا فإن هذا النص الجلي لم يصل إلى المخالفين خبره ، حتى أنا نحلف بالله وبالأيمان التي لا مخارج عنها، أن خبر هذا النص لم يؤثر في قلوبنا ولم يفد ظن الصحة، فضلا عن القطع، فإذا جاز وقوع هذا النص مع عظم مرتبته، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن رواة هذا الخبر كانوا في حد الأحاد إلا أن هذا الخبر لم يصل إليكم ؟
الثاني: هب يجب ان يشتهر لكنه مشهور عند أهل العلم أن واضع هذا المذهب - أعني ادعاء النص الجلي - هو ابن الراوندي، وأبو عيسى الوراق وأمثالهما، من المشهورين بالكذب، ثم إن هؤلاء الروافض لشدة شغفهم بتقرير مذهبهم، قبلوا تلك الأحاديث رواها الأسلاف للأخلاف.
ثم الذي يدل على أنه كذب محض وجوه :
الأول : أن هذا النص الجلي الذي لا يحتمل التأويل لو حصل لكان إما أن يقال : أنه أوصله إلى أهل التواتر أو ما أوصله إليهم، فإن كان قد أوصله إليهم لكان قد شاع واستفاض ووصل إلى جمهور الأمة، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع على الأعداء إخفاء مثل هذا النص، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع إطباق الخلق مع شدة للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ومبالغتهم في تعظيم أوامره ونواهيه على ظلم علي بن أبي طالب، ومنعه عن حقه، فإن طالب الإمامة هب انه ينكر هذا النص إلا أن من لم يكن طالبًا للإمامة لا ينكره فما الذي يحمله على إنكار هذا النص الجلي، وعلى إلقاء النفس العذاب الأليم من غير غرض يرجع إليه في الدنيا والآخرة ؟ وأما إن قلنا إنه ما أوصل هذا النص الجلي إلى أهل التواتر، فحينئذ لا يكون مثل هذا الخبر حجة قاطعة، ويسقط هذا الكلام بالكلية.
الثاني : أنه لم ينقل عن علي أنه ذكر هذا النص الجلي في شيء من خطبه ومناشداته، مع أنه ذكر خبر المولى وخبر المنزلة، وتمسك بجميع الوجوه، فلو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا لكان أعظم من سائر الوجوه، وكيف يليق بالعاقل أن يترك التمسك بالحجة القاطعة ويعول على الوجوه الخفية المحتملة ؟
As for the second shubha (doubt): which is their saying: If Abu Bakr had been imam, his imamate would have to be established either by explicit designation (nass) or by the oath of allegiance (bay'a). We said: His imamate was established by bay'a, and the shubahat (doubts) you mentioned concerning the invalidation of bay'a have already been answered.
As for the third shubha: which is the claim of the explicit text (al-nass al-jali). Its answer is that we do not accept that the transmitters in all ages reached the limit of multiplicity considered in tawatur (mass transmission). His statement: 'Even if they were at the level of ahad (single isolated reports) in some ages, it would have become famous now, and it is not so.' We said: The answer is in two respects:
First: We do not accept that it must have become famous; the proof of this is that many false rumors have become famous now in the East and the West, and we do not know the time of that fabrication, nor who that fabricator was. Also, the report of this explicit text did not reach the opponents, so that we swear by God and by oaths from which there is no escape, that the report of this text did not affect our hearts and did not produce even a presumption of truth, let alone certainty. So if it is possible for this text to occur despite its great rank, yet its report did not reach us, why is it not possible that the transmitters of this report were at the level of ahad, except that this report did not reach you?
Second: Grant that it must have become famous; but it is well-known among the people of knowledge that the originator of this doctrine—I mean the claim of explicit text—is Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu Isa al-Warraq and their likes, who are well-known for lying. Then these Rawafid, due to their intense passion for establishing their doctrine, accepted those hadiths, transmitted by the predecessors to the successors.
Then what indicates that it is pure falsehood are several aspects (wujuh):
First: This explicit text which does not admit of interpretation, if it had occurred, one would say that either he delivered it to the people of tawatur or he did not deliver it to them. If he delivered it to them, it would have spread and become widespread and reached the masses of the umma; and if it were so, it would have been impossible for the opponents to conceal such a text; and if it were so, it would have been impossible for the creatures, despite their intense love for the Messenger (peace be upon him) and their exaggeration in magnifying his commands and prohibitions, to unite in oppressing Ali ibn Abi Talib and preventing him from his right. For the seeker of the imamate, even if he denies this text, yet one who is not a seeker of the imamate does not deny it; so what compels him to deny this explicit text and to cast himself into painful torment with no worldly or otherworldly benefit? And if we say that he did not deliver this explicit text to the people of tawatur, then such a report is not a decisive proof, and this speech falls entirely.
Second: It is not transmitted from Ali that he mentioned this explicit text in any of his sermons or appeals, although he mentioned the hadith of al-Mawla and the hadith of al-Manila, and he clung to all ways. If this explicit text existed, it would be greater than all other ways. How fitting is it for a rational person to abandon clinging to a decisive proof and rely on hidden and ambiguous ways?
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage answers two Shia objections in sequence. The second shubha is brief: the Shia argue that if Abu Bakr were imam, his imamate must be proven either by nass (explicit designation) or by bay'a (oath of allegiance); they imply bay'a is invalid. Razi dismisses it by stating that bay'a indeed established the imamate, and previous answers have refuted their objections to bay'a. The third shubha is the major claim: that there is an explicit text (nass jali) designating Ali as imam. Razi's refutation proceeds in several steps:
1. He first denies that the transmitters of this alleged text ever reached tawatur (mass transmission) in any period; without tawatur, the report is not conclusive. The Shia counter that if it were at the level of ahad in some periods, it would still have become famous by now. Razi gives two responses: (a) He refutes the necessity of fame by pointing out that many false rumors become famous without known origin; and (b) he argues that the report did not reach the opponents, so it could be that the transmitters were ahad but the report simply did not reach them. Even if fame were conceded, he traces the doctrine's origin to known liars like Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu Isa al-Warraq.
2. He then provides two decisive proofs that the claim is false: (i) If the text were genuine and transmitted widely, it would have been impossible for the community to ignore it and for Ali to be deprived of his right; the very fact that opposition occurred shows the text is spurious. (ii) Ali himself never cited this text in any of his known sermons or arguments, though he used other hadiths like the hadith of al-Mawla. If the nass jali existed, it would be the strongest argument, yet he did not use it—an absurdity for a rational person. Thus the claim collapses.

Key Terms
النص الجلي
التواتر
الآحاد
البيعة
الروافض
ابن الراوندي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a report becomes famous, it must be true.
Correction: Razi points out that many false rumors become famous without known origin, so fame does not guarantee truth.

Checkpoint
What is Razi's first response to the Shia claim that the nass jali should have become famous?
A) He argues that fame is a proof of authenticity.
B) He argues that many false rumors become famous without known origin.
C) He argues that the companions deliberately hid the text.
D) He argues that the text is too sacred to be transmitted.

Key Takeaway
Razi undermines the nass jali claim by showing its transmission is unreliable and its fame is not proof of authenticity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever embraced a claim about your faith simply because it was repeated often by people around you? What would it take for you to subject that claim to the same scrutiny Razi applies here—checking its origin and transmission?
?
When you encounter a familiar religious narrative that secretly pleases you (like favoring your group or leader), do you notice a reluctance to examine its historical chain? Are you willing to trace it back, even if it leads to a known fabricator?
?
The report of nass jali 'did not affect the hearts' of the opponents. Has any religious claim affected your heart without you verifying its truth? How can you ensure your heart's convictions are based on certainty, not just familiarity?

5 min
The Logical and Historical Impossibility of the Nass Jali
Razi provides two definitive proofs that the nass jali claim is false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's two decisive proofs that the alleged explicit text for Ali is a fabrication: its concealment would be impossible if it were widely known, and Ali himself never cited it.

Core Idea
If the nass jali were real, it would be undeniable and Ali would have used it; his silence proves it false.

Razi presents two powerful arguments against the authenticity of the nass jali. First, if the text had been delivered to the masses, it would have been impossible to conceal and impossible for the community to ignore it and oppress Ali. Second, if it existed, Ali would have cited it in his sermons instead of weaker arguments. The fact that he did not use it is conclusive evidence that it is a fabrication.

Guided Path
1
Read the two wujuh (proofs)
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to grasp the two main arguments Razi presents.
These are presented as 'وجوه' but the passage lists two distinct proofs.
2
Analyze the first proof: the transmission dilemma
Razi presents a dilemma: either the text was delivered to tawatur or not. If delivered, it would have spread and could not be concealed; if not, it is not a decisive proof. The historical reality shows it was not widely known, so the claim fails.
Focus on the 'impossible to conceal' and 'impossible for the community to oppress Ali' parts.
3
Analyze the second proof: Ali's silence
Razi argues that Ali never mentioned this text in any known sermon or appeal, even though he used other hadiths. This is irrational if the text existed and was stronger.
Consider the hadiths Ali actually used: al-Mawla and al-Manila.
4
Reflect on the rationality of the argument
Think about why this argument is considered persuasive in kalam: it uses common sense and historical observation to show the claim is self-contradictory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية : وهي قولهم : لو كان أبو بكر إماما لكانت إمامته إما أن تثبت بالنص أو بالبيعة قلنا : حصلت إمامته بالبيعة والشبهات التي ذكرتموها في إبطال البيعة قد سبق الجواب عنها.
وأما الشبهة الثالثة : وهي ادعاء النص الجلي فجوابها أنا لا نسلم أن الرواة كانوا في جميع الأعصار بالغين إلى حد الكثرة المعتبرة في التواتر. قوله : ولو كانوا في حد الآحاد في بعض الأعصار لاشتهر الآن وليس الأمر كذلك، قلنا : الجواب عنه من وجهين :
الأول : لا نسلم أنه يجب أن يشتهر ذلك والدليل عليه أن كثيرًا من الأراجيف الكاذبة قد اشتهرت الآن في الشرق والغرب، ولا نعلم أن زمان ذلك الوضع، أي زمان كان ؟ ولا أن ذلك الواضع من كان؟.
وأيضًا فإن هذا النص الجلي لم يصل إلى المخالفين خبره ، حتى أنا نحلف بالله وبالأيمان التي لا مخارج عنها، أن خبر هذا النص لم يؤثر في قلوبنا ولم يفد ظن الصحة، فضلا عن القطع، فإذا جاز وقوع هذا النص مع عظم مرتبته، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن رواة هذا الخبر كانوا في حد الأحاد إلا أن هذا الخبر لم يصل إليكم ؟
الثاني: هب يجب ان يشتهر لكنه مشهور عند أهل العلم أن واضع هذا المذهب - أعني ادعاء النص الجلي - هو ابن الراوندي، وأبو عيسى الوراق وأمثالهما، من المشهورين بالكذب، ثم إن هؤلاء الروافض لشدة شغفهم بتقرير مذهبهم، قبلوا تلك الأحاديث رواها الأسلاف للأخلاف.
ثم الذي يدل على أنه كذب محض وجوه :
الأول : أن هذا النص الجلي الذي لا يحتمل التأويل لو حصل لكان إما أن يقال : أنه أوصله إلى أهل التواتر أو ما أوصله إليهم، فإن كان قد أوصله إليهم لكان قد شاع واستفاض ووصل إلى جمهور الأمة، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع على الأعداء إخفاء مثل هذا النص، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع إطباق الخلق مع شدة للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ومبالغتهم في تعظيم أوامره ونواهيه على ظلم علي بن أبي طالب، ومنعه عن حقه، فإن طالب الإمامة هب انه ينكر هذا النص إلا أن من لم يكن طالبًا للإمامة لا ينكره فما الذي يحمله على إنكار هذا النص الجلي، وعلى إلقاء النفس العذاب الأليم من غير غرض يرجع إليه في الدنيا والآخرة ؟ وأما إن قلنا إنه ما أوصل هذا النص الجلي إلى أهل التواتر، فحينئذ لا يكون مثل هذا الخبر حجة قاطعة، ويسقط هذا الكلام بالكلية.
الثاني : أنه لم ينقل عن علي أنه ذكر هذا النص الجلي في شيء من خطبه ومناشداته، مع أنه ذكر خبر المولى وخبر المنزلة، وتمسك بجميع الوجوه، فلو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا لكان أعظم من سائر الوجوه، وكيف يليق بالعاقل أن يترك التمسك بالحجة القاطعة ويعول على الوجوه الخفية المحتملة ؟
As for the second shubha (doubt): which is their saying: If Abu Bakr had been imam, his imamate would have to be established either by explicit designation (nass) or by the oath of allegiance (bay'a). We said: His imamate was established by bay'a, and the shubahat (doubts) you mentioned concerning the invalidation of bay'a have already been answered.
As for the third shubha: which is the claim of the explicit text (al-nass al-jali). Its answer is that we do not accept that the transmitters in all ages reached the limit of multiplicity considered in tawatur (mass transmission). His statement: 'Even if they were at the level of ahad (single isolated reports) in some ages, it would have become famous now, and it is not so.' We said: The answer is in two respects:
First: We do not accept that it must have become famous; the proof of this is that many false rumors have become famous now in the East and the West, and we do not know the time of that fabrication, nor who that fabricator was. Also, the report of this explicit text did not reach the opponents, so that we swear by God and by oaths from which there is no escape, that the report of this text did not affect our hearts and did not produce even a presumption of truth, let alone certainty. So if it is possible for this text to occur despite its great rank, yet its report did not reach us, why is it not possible that the transmitters of this report were at the level of ahad, except that this report did not reach you?
Second: Grant that it must have become famous; but it is well-known among the people of knowledge that the originator of this doctrine—I mean the claim of explicit text—is Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu Isa al-Warraq and their likes, who are well-known for lying. Then these Rawafid, due to their intense passion for establishing their doctrine, accepted those hadiths, transmitted by the predecessors to the successors.
Then what indicates that it is pure falsehood are several aspects (wujuh):
First: This explicit text which does not admit of interpretation, if it had occurred, one would say that either he delivered it to the people of tawatur or he did not deliver it to them. If he delivered it to them, it would have spread and become widespread and reached the masses of the umma; and if it were so, it would have been impossible for the opponents to conceal such a text; and if it were so, it would have been impossible for the creatures, despite their intense love for the Messenger (peace be upon him) and their exaggeration in magnifying his commands and prohibitions, to unite in oppressing Ali ibn Abi Talib and preventing him from his right. For the seeker of the imamate, even if he denies this text, yet one who is not a seeker of the imamate does not deny it; so what compels him to deny this explicit text and to cast himself into painful torment with no worldly or otherworldly benefit? And if we say that he did not deliver this explicit text to the people of tawatur, then such a report is not a decisive proof, and this speech falls entirely.
Second: It is not transmitted from Ali that he mentioned this explicit text in any of his sermons or appeals, although he mentioned the hadith of al-Mawla and the hadith of al-Manila, and he clung to all ways. If this explicit text existed, it would be greater than all other ways. How fitting is it for a rational person to abandon clinging to a decisive proof and rely on hidden and ambiguous ways?
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage answers two Shia objections in sequence. The second shubha is brief: the Shia argue that if Abu Bakr were imam, his imamate must be proven either by nass (explicit designation) or by bay'a (oath of allegiance); they imply bay'a is invalid. Razi dismisses it by stating that bay'a indeed established the imamate, and previous answers have refuted their objections to bay'a. The third shubha is the major claim: that there is an explicit text (nass jali) designating Ali as imam. Razi's refutation proceeds in several steps:
1. He first denies that the transmitters of this alleged text ever reached tawatur (mass transmission) in any period; without tawatur, the report is not conclusive. The Shia counter that if it were at the level of ahad in some periods, it would still have become famous by now. Razi gives two responses: (a) He refutes the necessity of fame by pointing out that many false rumors become famous without known origin; and (b) he argues that the report did not reach the opponents, so it could be that the transmitters were ahad but the report simply did not reach them. Even if fame were conceded, he traces the doctrine's origin to known liars like Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu Isa al-Warraq.
2. He then provides two decisive proofs that the claim is false: (i) If the text were genuine and transmitted widely, it would have been impossible for the community to ignore it and for Ali to be deprived of his right; the very fact that opposition occurred shows the text is spurious. (ii) Ali himself never cited this text in any of his known sermons or arguments, though he used other hadiths like the hadith of al-Mawla. If the nass jali existed, it would be the strongest argument, yet he did not use it—an absurdity for a rational person. Thus the claim collapses.

Key Terms
النص الجلي
التواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Shia claim that the nass jali was hidden or suppressed by the enemies of Ali.
Correction: Razi argues that if it were widely known, concealment is impossible; and if it were not widely known, it is not a proof.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the second proof that the nass jali is false?
A) The opponents would have concealed it.
B) Ali never mentioned it in his sermons.
C) The text has multiple interpretations.
D) The transmitters were unknown.

Key Takeaway
The nass jali claim is logically impossible because it would have required universal knowledge which is absent, and Ali himself would have used it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that you assumed was undeniable, only to realize you never checked whether your own leaders or evidence actually supported it? Which belief are you most afraid to test against the truth?
?
If the strongest proof for something you believe in was never used by those who would have benefited most from it, would you be willing to abandon that belief? What is stopping you from doing that today?
?
Razi shows that a rational person does not abandon a decisive proof for weaker ones. What 'strong proof' in your life are you neglecting because of comfort, habit, or fear? Will you re-examine it now?

The Clear Text Did Not Reach Even Its Opponents
Razi argues that the nass jali did not reach even opponents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument against the Shi'a claim of a clear text (nass jali) by showing that the news of this text did not reach even its opponents, thus undermining its reliability.

Core Idea
A proof that never reaches its audience cannot be authoritative.

Razi presents a pointed rhetorical argument against the Shi'a claim that a clear text (nass jali) explicitly designated Ali as Imam. He begins by asserting that this allegedly weighty text never reached the opponents — including himself. This passage invites us to consider the implications of a text that, despite its supposed importance, fails to transmit to its intended audience. What does that say about its authenticity?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the strong language: Razi swears by Allah that the news did not affect their hearts.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the key assertion
Razi's main point is that the clear text's news did not even reach them, let alone convince them. Ask: Why is this significant?
3
Analyze the conditional argument
Razi constructs a conditional: If a text of such status could exist and yet not reach them, then its narrators might be like isolated transmitters (ahad) whose report never reached the Shi'a. Understand the logic.
This exposes the weakness of relying on an untransmitted text.
4
Connect to the broader debate
Reflect on how this argument fits into Sunni-Shi'a polemics over the imamate. The lack of widespread transmission (tawatur) is used to reject the claim.
See how Razi turns the Shi'a claim against itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضًا فإن هذا النص الجلي لم يصل إلى المخالفين خبره ، حتى أنا نحلف بالله وبالأيمان التي لا مخارج عنها، أن خبر هذا النص لم يؤثر في قلوبنا ولم يفد ظن الصحة، فضلا عن القطع، فإذا جاز وقوع هذا النص مع عظم مرتبته، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن رواة هذا الخبر كانوا في حد الأحاد إلا أن هذا الخبر لم يصل إليكم ؟
And also, the news of this clear text did not reach the opponents, so that we swear by Allah and by oaths from which there is no escape, that the news of this text did not affect our hearts and did not yield even a presumption of truth, let alone certainty. So if it is possible that this text occurred despite its great status, and its news did not reach us, then why may it not be said: The narrators of this report were in the category of isolated reports, except that this report did not reach you?
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage presents a rhetorical argument against the Shi'a claim of a clear text (nass jali) designating Ali as Imam. Razi begins by asserting that even the opponents of the claim have not received any report of such a text. He swears by Allah that the report of this text has not affected their hearts nor produced even a weak presumption of its truth, let alone certainty. Then he constructs a conditional: if such a text could have occurred with its great significance and yet not reach them, then why can it not be said that the narrators were in the category of isolated transmitters (ahad) but the report never reached the Shi'a? The point is to expose the weakness of the Shi'a reliance on an alleged textual proof that lacks widespread transmission.

Key Terms
نص جلي
أحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is denying the possibility of any hidden text or authoritative designation.
Correction: Razi is not denying that a text could exist; he is arguing that the claimed clear text lacks any credible transmission, so it cannot be used as proof.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim about the news of the clear text reaching the opponents?
A) It reached them but they rejected it.
B) It did not reach them.
C) It reached them and they accepted it.
D) It reached them partially.

Key Takeaway
Razi argues that the alleged clear text for Ali's imamate fails as proof because its news never even reached its opponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Imagine you have a belief about Islam that you hold strongly, but you discover that its proof never reached early authorities. Would that shake your confidence? How can you ensure that your most important beliefs are rooted in proofs that have a clear chain of transmission?

The Nass Jali Doctrine Invented by Liars
Razi claims the nass jali doctrine was invented by known liars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the doctrine of the clear text (nass jali) was invented by known liars and why this undermines its credibility.

Core Idea
The originators of the clear-text doctrine were notorious liars.

Razi addresses the objection that if the clear-text had been true, it would have been widely known. He counters by asserting that the doctrine's originators—Ibn al-Rawandi and his ilk—were known for dishonesty, and the Rawafid accepted their fabrications out of sectarian zeal. This passage attacks the very pedigree of the claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the core argument.
Notice how Razi begins with 'the second'—this is a continuation of a larger argument.
2
Identify the key figures
Identify Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu 'Isa al-Warraq as the alleged originators. Note the label 'well-known for lying'.
These figures are known in Islamic heresiography for their controversial views.
3
Evaluate the rhetorical move
Reflect on how Razi attacks the credibility of the claim without addressing its content. Why is this effective?
This is a common polemical strategy: discrediting the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنه الثاني: هب يجب ان يشتهر لكنه مشهور عند أهل العلم أن واضع هذا المذهب - أعني ادعاء النص الجلي - هو ابن الراوندي، وأبو عيسى الوراق وأمثالهما، من المشهورين بالكذب، ثم إن هؤلاء الروافض لشدة شغفهم بتقرير مذهبهم، قبلوا تلك الأحاديث رواها الأسلاف للأخلاف.
The second: Granted that it must be well-known, but it is well-known among scholars that the establisher of this doctrine—I mean the claim of the clear text—is Ibn al-Rawandi, and Abu 'Isa al-Warraq and their likes, who are well-known for lying. Moreover, these Rawafid, due to the intensity of their passion for establishing their doctrine, accepted those reports, transmitted by predecessors to successors.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
Here Razi counters the assumption that the nass jali must have been widespread if true. He says it is well-known among scholars that the originators of this doctrine are individuals like Ibn al-Rawandi and Abu 'Isa al-Warraq, who are notorious for lying. Then, the Shi'a (Rawafid) accepted these fabricated reports because of their eagerness to support their sect, and they transmitted them from generation to generation. This argument attacks the credibility of the source of the claim rather than its content.

Key Terms
ابن الراوندي
أبو عيسى الوراق
روافض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi is accusing all Shia of being liars.
Correction: Razi specifically names the originators of the doctrine (Ibn al-Rawandi, Abu 'Isa al-Warraq) as known liars, and only then says the Rawafid accepted their fabrications out of sectarian passion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Razi discredits the nass jali doctrine by tracing its origin to individuals with a reputation for lying, thereby undermining its claim to widespread acceptance.

?

First Proof: The Widespread Transmission Argument
Razi presents the first proof that the clear text is false via a logical disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if a clear text about Ali's succession existed, it would have been widely transmitted and impossible to conceal, and why this invalidates the Shia claim.

Core Idea
A true decisive text must be widely known; otherwise it proves nothing.

Razi begins his critique of Shia claims by presenting a logical dilemma. Either the alleged clear text was mass-transmitted or not. In this lesson, we explore the first horn of this dilemma and its implications for the reliability of the Shia claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp Razi's argument structure.
Notice the conditional reasoning and the either/or structure.
2
Identify the dichotomy
Razi presents two possibilities: the text was conveyed to the level of tawatur or not. Understand both branches.
Tawatur means widespread transmission by many reliable chains.
3
Analyze the first branch
If transmitted to tawatur, the text would become widespread and impossible to conceal. Razi then argues that given the people's love for the Prophet, they would not have agreed to wrong Ali.
Consider why Razi thinks concealment is impossible in this scenario.
4
Reflect on the second branch
If not transmitted to tawatur, the text cannot be a decisive proof. Thus, the Shia argument collapses.
This completes the logical dilemma, showing the claim fails either way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يدل على أنه كذب محض وجوه : الأول : أن هذا النص الجلي الذي لا يحتمل التأويل لو حصل لكان إما أن يقال : أنه أوصله إلى أهل التواتر أو ما أوصله إليهم، فإن كان قد أوصله إليهم لكان قد شاع واستفاض ووصل إلى جمهور الأمة، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع على الأعداء إخفاء مثل هذا النص، ولو كان كذلك لامتنع إطباق الخلق مع شدة للرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ومبالغتهم في تعظيم أوامره ونواهيه على ظلم علي بن أبي طالب، ومنعه عن حقه، فإن طالب الإمامة هب انه ينكر هذا النص إلا أن من لم يكن طالبًا للإمامة لا ينكره فما الذي يحمله على إنكار هذا النص الجلي، وعلى إلقاء النفس العذاب الأليم من غير غرض يرجع إليه في الدنيا والآخرة ؟ وأما إن قلنا إنه ما أوصل هذا النص الجلي إلى أهل التواتر، فحينئذ لا يكون مثل هذا الخبر حجة قاطعة، ويسقط هذا الكلام بالكلية.
Then what indicates that it is pure falsehood are aspects: The first: That this clear text which does not admit interpretation, if it occurred, then either it was conveyed to the people of tawatur or not. If it was conveyed to them, then it would have become widespread and reached the general body of the community. And if that were the case, it would have been impossible for the enemies to conceal such a text. And if that were the case, it would have been impossible for the people, despite their intense love for the Messenger (peace be upon him) and their exaggeration in glorifying his commands and prohibitions, to agree upon oppressing Ali ibn Abi Talib and preventing him from his right. For the seeker of the imamate, even if he denies this text, yet he who is not a seeker of the imamate does not deny it—so what compels him to deny this clear text and to cast himself into painful punishment without any worldly or otherworldly motive? But if we say that he did not convey this clear text to the people of tawatur, then such a report is not a decisive proof, and this statement falls entirely.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is the first of three proofs that the nass jali is a fabrication. Razi presents a dichotomy: either the text was transmitted to the mutawatir (mass-transmitted) level or not. If it was, it would have become widely known and reached the entire community. In that case, it would be impossible for enemies to hide it, and impossible for all people (given their love for the Prophet and respect for his commands) to agree on wronging Ali by denying him his right. Moreover, those who deny it without a worldly motive would have no reason to risk punishment by denying a clear text. If, on the other hand, the text was not transmitted to the level of tawatur, then it cannot serve as a decisive proof, and the Shi'a argument collapses. This proof uses a logical disjunction to show that either way, the claim fails.

Key Terms
تواتر
نص جلي
حجة قاطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what would happen if the clear text had been transmitted to the level of tawatur?
A) It would have been hidden by enemies.
B) It would have become widespread and impossible to conceal.
C) People would have accepted it immediately.
D) It would have been lost over time.

Key Takeaway
Razi's first proof refutes the Shia claim by showing that a clear text, if genuine, would have been mass-transmitted and undeniable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that you considered certain, yet you later realized it was based on something that wasn't widely known or confirmed? How does Razi's argument challenge you to examine the evidence for your most foundational convictions?
?
Razi argues that if a truth were genuinely clear and widely transmitted, it would be impossible to hide. Are there truths in your own life—about your character, your intentions, or your relationship with Allah—that you assume are hidden but actually should be evident to those around you? What would it take to let them become clear?
?
The argument here forces a choice: either accept that the supposed text was never conveyed reliably, or admit that it lacks decisive authority. In your spiritual journey, what is one claim you need to either fully verify or let go of because you cannot verify it? What is holding you back from making that decision?
