Second Proof: Ali Never Used the Text
Razi argues that Ali himself never cited the clear text

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument from silence: that Ali's failure to cite the alleged clear text (nass jali) in his public discourse demonstrates its non-existence.

Core Idea
If Ali had a clear text, he would have used it as his strongest argument.

Razi presents a powerful argument from historical practice. He observes that Ali ibn Abi Talib, in his many speeches and disputes, never once invoked a clear textual appointment from the Prophet. Yet Ali did use other hadiths. What does this silence tell us? This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice Razi's logical structure: he states the absence, gives examples of what Ali did use, then draws the conclusion.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify Key Terms
Identify the roles of 'nass jali' (clear text), 'hadith al-Mawla' and 'hadith al-Manzila' (other proofs Ali used), and 'hujja qati'a' (decisive proof).
These terms are central to the argument.
3
Analyze the Logic
Razi uses a reductio: if the clear text existed, it would be the strongest proof; abandoning it for weaker ones is irrational; Ali was rational, so the text must not exist. Confirm each step.
This is an argument from silence (dalil al-samt).
4
Consider the Objection
Some might think Ali used the text in secret. Razi's argument implies it would have been used publicly in sermons and pleadings. Discuss why.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.
5
Reflect on Implications
Why does this matter for the debate on leadership? This proof undermines claims of explicit appointment.
Connect to broader theological significance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لم ينقل عن علي أنه ذكر هذا النص الجلي في شيء من خطبه ومناشداته، مع أنه ذكر خبر المولى وخبر المنزلة، وتمسك بجميع الوجوه، فلو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا لكان أعظم من سائر الوجوه، وكيف يليق بالعاقل أن يترك التمسك بالحجة القاطعة ويعول على الوجوه الخفية المحتملة ؟
The second: That it has not been transmitted from Ali that he mentioned this clear text in any of his sermons or pleadings, despite his having mentioned the hadith of the Mawla and the hadith of the position (manzila), and having clung to all aspects. So if this clear text had existed, it would have been greater than all other aspects. And how is it fitting for a rational person to abandon clinging to the decisive proof and rely on subtle and probable aspects?
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is the second proof that the nass jali is false. Razi points out that historical records show Ali himself never used this alleged clear text in any of his speeches or when he was pleading his case. Ali did use other arguments, such as the hadith of Ghadir (Mawla) and the hadith of position (Manzila), but he never once cited a clear, explicit text. If such a text had existed, it would be the strongest possible evidence, and a rational person would not abandon the strongest proof in favor of weaker, more ambiguous arguments. Therefore, its absence from Ali's own discourse shows it never existed.

Key Terms
خبر المولى
خبر المنزلة
حجة قاطعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ali might have used the clear text in private, but it was not recorded.
Correction: Razi argues that Ali would have used the text publicly, as he did with other hadiths, because it would be the strongest proof for his claim.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider Ali's failure to mention the clear text significant?
A) Ali was shy.
B) The text was lost.
C) A rational person would use the strongest proof.
D) He was ordered not to use it.

Key Takeaway
Ali's silence in using the alleged clear text is strong evidence that no such text existed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says it is unfitting for a rational person to abandon a decisive proof for subtle and probable ones. Examine your own decision-making: do you rely on clear evidence from the Quran and Sunnah, or do you prefer subtle justifications that suit your desires? What step will you take today to prioritize the decisive proof in your faith?

The Proof from Absence of Knowledge
Razi presents the proof and its entailment leading to chaos.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if a divinely appointed text for imamah existed, it would have been widely known, and the chaotic consequences of assuming otherwise.

Core Idea
If a crucial text existed, we would know it; assuming its unknown existence leads to absurdity.

Razi presents a powerful logical argument: if the supposed clear text from the Prophet appointing an imam had been real, its fame would have reached everyone. Since we do not know it, it must not exist. He then shows the dangerous implications of denying this.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation for the first part of the proof. Notice how Razi states the conditional argument clearly.
The passage begins with 'الثالث' and ends at 'بطلانه'.
2
Understand the logical entailment
Razi explains that if we allow a text to exist unknown, we could similarly allow that the Quran was challenged or fasting abrogated without our knowledge. This would undermine the entire Shari'a.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the consequence (total confusion of the Shari'a) is false, the original premise—that the clear text exists—must also be false. Therefore, the text does not exist.
Ask yourself: Why does the argument rely on the public nature of the text?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا، لعرفناه ونحن لا نعرفه، فهو غير موجود. بيان الملازمة أنه لو جاز وجوده مع أنه لم يصل خبره إلينا لجاز أن يقال: القرآن قد عورض ولم يصل خبره إلينا ، وأن الله نسخ صوم رمضان والتوجه على الكعبة، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، وهذا يفضي إلى تشويش الشريعة بالكلية ولا شك في بطلانه. لا يقال: الفرق بين الصورتين أنه كان للقوم في إخفاء هذا النص غرض، أن يكونوا هم الملوك والأمراء، وليس لهم في إخفاء ما ذكرتموه غرض. لأنا نقول: إنه لا يلزم من عدم غرض معين عدم سائر الأغراض، فعليكم أن تبينوا أنه لم يوجد في هذه الصورة شيء من الأغراض الأخر.
The third: If this clear text had existed, we would have known it, but we do not know it, so it does not exist. Explanation of the entailment: If its existence were permissible while its news did not reach us, then it would be permissible to say: The Quran has been challenged (by a rival) and its news did not reach us, and that God abrogated the fast of Ramadan and facing the Ka'ba, and its news did not reach us. This leads to total confusion of the Shari'a, and there is no doubt in its falsity. Let it not be said: The difference between the two cases is that the people had a motive in hiding this text—so that they would be kings and rulers—but they had no motive in hiding what you mentioned. For we say: The absence of a specific motive does not necessitate the absence of all other motives. So you must demonstrate that in this case there is no other motive.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This is the third proof. Razi argues that if the nass jali existed but we do not know it, then it is reasonable to conclude it does not exist. He explains the logical entailment: if we accept that a very important text could exist without reaching us, then we would also have to accept that the Quran could have been challenged successfully, or fasting during Ramadan could have been abrogated, without our knowledge. This would lead to complete chaos in the sacred law, which is absurd. Then Razi anticipates an objection: that the companions had a motive to hide the text (to become rulers) but no motive to hide the other claims. He responds that the absence of one specific motive does not mean there are no other motives; the opponent must prove that in the case of the clear text there were no other motives. This shifts the burden of proof back to the Shi'a.

Key Terms
نص جلي
تشويش الشريعة
ملازمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the absence of knowledge about a text does not prove its non-existence, especially for hidden texts.
Correction: Razi argues that for a text of such importance (appointing a universal leader), it would necessarily be widely known. The assumption that it could be hidden leads to absurdity.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim would happen if we accept that a clear text could exist without reaching us?
A) The Quran would be considered incomplete.
B) It would lead to chaos in the Shari'a.
C) Imamah would be invalid.
D) The Prophet would be accused of lying.

Key Takeaway
Razi's third proof argues that the clear text about imamah cannot exist because if it did, we would know it; otherwise, the entire Shari'a would be unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you ever assume that because you haven't heard of a certain obligation or ruling, it must not exist? How does Razi's argument force you to reconsider the reliability of what you know about your faith?
?
Razi argues that if a crucial text existed but was unknown, the entire Shari'a would be unreliable. Do you sometimes treat parts of your deen as uncertain because they haven't been personally verified by you? What does that imply about your trust in the transmitted knowledge?
?
The proof shows that we can confidently affirm that certain things are not part of the religion because we would know if they were. Are there beliefs or practices you are tempted to add to Islam based on weak evidence? How does this argument protect you from innovation?

Objection and Response on Motive
Razi addresses the objection about motive and shifts the burden.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Shi'a objection that the Companions had a motive to hide the text and Razi's response that shifts the burden of proof.

Core Idea
Absence of one motive does not prove absence of all; the opponent must demonstrate no other motives existed.

Razi anticipates an objection: perhaps the Companions had a motive to hide the text (to become rulers) but no such motive for hiding other hypothetical abrogations. He responds by pointing out that the absence of one specific motive does not rule out other possible motives, and the burden is on the opponent to prove there were no other motives at all.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection starting from 'لا يقال'. Note that the opponent concedes the text could have been hidden due to a desire for leadership.
The objection tries to break the parallel between the clear text and other hypothetical events.
2
Understand Razi's response
Razi argues that the absence of a specific motive does not mean other motives are absent. The opponent cannot assume that only one motive exists.
This is a logical point: lack of evidence for one motive is not evidence against all motives.
3
Identify the burden shift
Razi places the burden on the opponent to demonstrate that in the case of the clear text, there were no other motives whatsoever. If they cannot, the objection fails.
This is a common dialectical move: the objector must prove the absence of all possible motives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا، لعرفناه ونحن لا نعرفه، فهو غير موجود. بيان الملازمة أنه لو جاز وجوده مع أنه لم يصل خبره إلينا لجاز أن يقال: القرآن قد عورض ولم يصل خبره إلينا ، وأن الله نسخ صوم رمضان والتوجه على الكعبة، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، وهذا يفضي إلى تشويش الشريعة بالكلية ولا شك في بطلانه. لا يقال: الفرق بين الصورتين أنه كان للقوم في إخفاء هذا النص غرض، أن يكونوا هم الملوك والأمراء، وليس لهم في إخفاء ما ذكرتموه غرض. لأنا نقول: إنه لا يلزم من عدم غرض معين عدم سائر الأغراض، فعليكم أن تبينوا أنه لم يوجد في هذه الصورة شيء من الأغراض الأخر.
The third: If this clear text had existed, we would have known it, but we do not know it, so it does not exist. Explanation of the entailment: If its existence were permissible while its news did not reach us, then it would be permissible to say: The Quran has been challenged (by a rival) and its news did not reach us, and that God abrogated the fast of Ramadan and facing the Ka'ba, and its news did not reach us. This leads to total confusion of the Shari'a, and there is no doubt in its falsity. Let it not be said: The difference between the two cases is that the people had a motive in hiding this text—so that they would be kings and rulers—but they had no motive in hiding what you mentioned. For we say: The absence of a specific motive does not necessitate the absence of all other motives. So you must demonstrate that in this case there is no other motive.

This is the third proof. Razi argues that if the nass jali existed but we do not know it, then it is reasonable to conclude it does not exist. He explains the logical entailment: if we accept that a very important text could exist without reaching us, then we would also have to accept that the Quran could have been challenged successfully, or fasting during Ramadan could have been abrogated, without our knowledge. This would lead to complete chaos in the sacred law, which is absurd. He responds that the absence of one specific motive does not mean there are no other motives; the opponent must prove that in the case of the clear text there were no other motives. This shifts the burden of proof back to the Shi'a.

Razi's response shows that the objection about motive fails because it does not exclude other possible motives, and the burden is on the objector to prove otherwise.

?

?

?
Razi shifts the burden of proof to the objector. In your own life, do you demand that others prove their claims completely before you accept them, or do you sometimes reject a claim too quickly because one piece of evidence is missing? What step will you take to be more fair in your judgments?

Razi's Refutation of the Text for Ali
Razi refutes the claim that a text exists for a specific person, Ali

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's refutation of the claim that there is an explicit text designating Ali as imam, and the theological principle that God's actions are governed by what is most beneficial for creation.

Core Idea
God's wisdom may prevent explicit designation if it leads to greater harm.

In this passage, Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi responds to the fourth Shi'a argument for the imamate of Ali: that there is an explicit text (nass) naming him. Razi challenges both the existence of such a text and its theological necessity. He argues that even if such a text were possible, God's knowledge of human rebellion would make it wiser to leave the choice to the community. This lesson unpacks Razi's reasoning and its implications for understanding divine wisdom in political leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the structure: denial first, then a hypothetical.
2
Identify the two main arguments
Razi first denies the existence of any text. Then he presents a hypothetical counter-argument: even if a text existed, God's knowledge of rebellion would make specification counterproductive.
3
Understand the purpose of imamate
Razi states that the purpose is to safeguard welfare (maslaha). If specification leads to strife, it is better not to specify.
This is a key principle in Islamic political theology.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this argument undermines the Shi'a claim by showing that the absence of a text does not imply divine neglect but divine wisdom.
The Rawafid themselves admit rebellion occurred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة وهي قولهم: إنه وجد النص على إمامة شخص معين، ومتى كان كذلك كان هذا الشخص هو علي. فنقول: لا نسلم أنه وجد النص على إمامة شخص بعينه، والوجوه التي ذكرتموها معارضة بوجه واحد وهو أنه يحتمل أن يقال: إن الله تعالى علم أنه لو نص على شخص معين لاستنكفوا عن طاعته ولتمردوا، وكيف يبعد ذلك والروافض يقولون : إنه تعالى لما نص على إمامة علي تمرد القوم وأبوا إطاعته وأظهروا منازعته ومخالته ؟ وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المقصود من نصب الإمام رعاية مصلحة الخلق، ولما علم الله تعالى أن التنصيص يفضي إلى الفتنة وإثارة المفسدة، كان الأصلح ترك التنصيص وتفويض الأمر إلى اختيارهم، وبهذا التقرير يسقط كل ما ذكرتموه.
As for the fourth doubt—which is their saying: There is a text for the imamate of a specific person, and whenever that is the case, that person is Ali. We say: We do not grant that there is a text for the imamate of a specific person. The aspects you have mentioned are countered by one aspect: that it is possible to say: Allah Almighty knew that if He specified a person, they would disdain obedience to him and rebel. And how far-fetched is that, while the Rawafid themselves say: When He specified the imamate of Ali, the people rebelled and refused to obey him and showed opposition and hostility? And if this is established, we say: The purpose of appointing an imam is to safeguard the welfare of the people. Since Allah Almighty knew that specification leads to tribulation and causing corruption, it was more appropriate to leave specification and delegate the matter to their choice. With this explanation, all that you have mentioned falls away.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage addresses the fourth Shi'a argument: that there is a clear text designating a specific person (Ali) as imam. Razi's response is twofold. First, he denies that any such text exists. Second, he provides a hypothetical counter-argument: even if such a text were possible, God's wisdom might prevent it because He knew that specifying a particular person would cause defiance and rebellion. Razi points out that the Shi'a themselves admit that when God allegedly specified Ali, the people rebelled. Therefore, it would be contrary to the purpose of the imamate (which is the welfare of the people) to specify a leader if that leads to strife. Hence, it was better not to specify and to leave the choice to the community. This reasoning undermines the necessity of a clear text.

Key Terms
تنصيص
فتنة
مصلحة الخلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Razi is saying God changed His mind or was unable to specify.
Correction: Razi argues that God's wisdom and knowledge of consequences led Him to choose not to specify because it would defeat the purpose of the imamate.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the main purpose of appointing an imam?
A) To enforce the law.
B) To safeguard the welfare of the people.
C) To continue the prophethood.
D) To maintain the lineage of the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the necessity of an explicit text for Ali by arguing that God's wisdom may withhold specification to prevent rebellion, as the purpose of the imamate is communal welfare.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's choice to withhold explicit guidance in some matters to prevent greater harm, do you trust His hidden wisdom, or do you find yourself demanding clear signs? What would it take for you to surrender your need for certainty and rely on His overarching plan?
?
The Rawafid admitted that even after a clear text for Ali, people still rebelled. In your own life, where do you resist clear truths from the Quran and Sunnah because of pride, comfort, or convenience? What is holding you back from complete submission?
?
If the purpose of leadership is the welfare of the people, how do you respond to leaders in your community or family who make decisions that seem unclear or against your preference? Can you obey them for the sake of collective benefit, even when you struggle to see the wisdom? What would it look like for you to genuinely prioritize the community's welfare over your own opinion?

The Superiority Argument: Does Merit Determine the Imam?
Razi counters the claim that Ali must be imam because he is superior.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Razi rejects the claim that superiority (afdal) necessarily determines imamate, and why the imamate of a less superior person is permissible.

Core Idea
Superiority does not automatically entitle one to the imamate.

Razi concludes his responses to the Shi'a objections with a final challenge: the argument from superiority. He shows that even if one were to concede that Ali is superior, it does not force the conclusion that he must be the imam. This passage introduces a key debate in Sunni theology about the relationship between merit and leadership.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note that this is the fifth objection.
Pay attention to the two parts of Razi's response.
2
Identify Razi's two responses
First, Razi mentions that Sunni companions counter by claiming Abu Bakr is superior, but he postpones the discussion. Second, he questions the premise that the superior must be the imam.
The second response is the logical core: even if the premise is granted, the conclusion doesn't follow.
3
Consider why Razi postpones the tafdil discussion
The issue of superiority (tafdil) is complex and will be addressed later. This shows Razi is not conceding or rejecting it here, but focusing on the logical link.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Razi challenges the necessity: why must the superior be imam? Why is it not permissible to have a less superior person as imam when a superior exists? This opens the door for Sunni political theology.
This is a classic form of argument: conceding the premise (superiority) but denying the conclusion (imamate).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الخامسة وهي أن عليا أفضل، والأفضل هو الإمام فنقول : إن أصحابنا عارضوا هذا بأن أبا بكر أفضل والأفضل هو الإمام وبالجملة فستجيء مسألة التفضيل إن شاء الله تعالى. سلمنا أن عليا كان أفضل فلم قلتم : إن الأفضل هو الإمام؟ ولم لا يجوز إمامة المفضول مع وجود الفاضل ؟
As for the fifth doubt—that Ali is superior (afdal), and the superior is the imam. We say: Our companions countered this by saying that Abu Bakr is superior, and the superior is the imam. In short, the issue of preference will come later, God willing. Even if we grant that Ali was superior, why do you say that the superior is the imam? And why is it not permissible to have the imamate of the less superior (mafdul) in the presence of the more superior?
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This is the fifth and final Shi'a objection: that Ali is superior to others, and therefore he should be the imam. Razi gives two responses. First, he points out that Sunni scholars (ashabuna) argue the opposite—that Abu Bakr is superior, so by the same logic he should be imam. But he postpones the detailed discussion of superiority (tafdil) to a later section. Second, even if one concedes that Ali is superior, Razi challenges the premise that the superior must be the imam. He asks why it is not permissible to have a less superior person as imam when a superior one exists. This challenges the necessity of the claim, opening the door to the Sunni position that virtue is not the sole criterion for imamate.

Key Terms
أفضل
مفضول
تفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi denies Ali's superiority outright.
Correction: Razi does not deny Ali's superiority in this passage; he challenges the logical link between superiority and imamate.

Checkpoint
What does Razi question in his response to the fifth doubt?
A) Whether Ali is superior.
B) Whether the superior must be the imam.
C) Whether Abu Bakr is superior.
D) Whether the imamate is necessary.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that even if one accepts Ali's superiority, it does not prove his right to the imamate, because the imamate of a less superior person remains permissible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone more qualified or virtuous than a current leader, do you feel that they must be in charge? How does Razi's challenge to that assumption affect your own expectations of leadership in your community?
?
Have you ever felt entitled to a position because you believed you were better or more deserving than others? How does this passage challenge that feeling?
?
If virtue alone does not determine who should lead, what other factors should matter? Take a moment to reflect on a current leader in your life—does your judgment of them align with this broader view, or do you still assume that the most virtuous should always lead?

The Third Shubha: The Supposed Explicit Text and Its Refutation
Imam Razi refutes the claim that an explicit text existed for Ali's imamate by showing it would lead to total confusion of Shari'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical refutation of the claim that an explicit text existed for Ali's imamate, and why accepting hidden texts would undermine the entire Shari'a.

Core Idea
If an explicit text existed, we would know it; its absence disproves its existence.

This lesson examines the third Shia objection concerning an explicit text (nass jali) designating Ali as imam. Imam Razi presents the objection, then refutes it with a logical entailment argument. He shows that if such a hidden text were possible, it would open the door to undermining the entire Shari'a. Then he addresses a potential distinction and dismisses it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its entailment
Read the first part of the passage where Imam Razi states the objection and explains the logical consequence. Note the structure: if it existed, we would know; we don't know, so it doesn't exist. Then he explains why this entailment is necessary by drawing an analogy.
The analogy with possible counterfeiting of the Qur'an or abrogation of rulings is a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Examine the opponent's distinction
The opponent tries to differentiate by saying there was a motive to hide the nass (to keep power) but no motive to hide the other hypothetical texts. See if you can anticipate Imam Razi's response before reading it.
The distinction is based on motive.
3
Understand Imam Razi's refutation
Imam Razi refutes quickly by stating that absence of a specific motive does not guarantee absence of all motives. The burden of proof is on the opponent to show no other motive existed.
This is a classic logical move: the opponent's distinction does not fully block the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو كان هذا النص الجلي موجودًا، لعرفناه ونحن لا نعرفه، فهو غير موجود. بيان الملازمة أنه لو جاز وجوده مع أنه لم يصل خبره إلينا لجاز أن يقال: القرآن قد عورض ولم يصل خبره إلينا، وأنه الله نسخ صوم رمضان والتوجه على الكعبة، ولم يصل خبره إلينا، وهذا يفضي إلى تشويش الشريعة بالكلية ولا شك في بطلانه. لا يقال: الفرق بين الصورتين أنه كان للقوم في إخفاء هذا النص غرض، أن يكونوا هم الملوك والأمراء، وليس لهم في إخفاء ما ذكرتموه غرض. لأنا نقول: إنه لا يلزم من عدم غرض معين عدم سائر الأغراض، فعليكم أن تبينوا أنه لم يوجد في هذه الصورة شيء من الأغراض الأخر.
The third: If this explicit text (al-nass al-jali) existed, we would know it, and we do not know it, so it does not exist. Explanation of the entailment: If it were possible for it to exist while its report did not reach us, then it could be said that the Qur'an has been opposed [by a similar text] and its report did not reach us, and that God abrogated the fast of Ramadan and facing the Ka'ba, and its report did not reach us, and this leads to total confusion of the Shari'a, and there is no doubt about its invalidity. It cannot be said: The difference between the two cases is that the people had a motive in concealing this text, namely that they themselves be kings and rulers, and they have no motive in concealing what you mentioned. For we say: The absence of a specific motive does not entail the absence of all other motives; you must show that in this case there is none of the other motives.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third Shia objection (shubha) concerning the existence of an explicit text (nass jali) designating Ali as the immediate successor. The objection states: if such a text existed, we would know it; since we do not know it, it does not exist. Imam Razi then explains the logical entailment (mulazama): if it were possible for the text to exist without reaching us, then one could also claim that the Qur'an was counterfeited or that rulings were abrogated without our knowledge, which would undermine the entire Shari'a. This reductio ad absurdum shows the impossibility of such a scenario. The opponent then attempts a distinction (fa'l-farq): there was a motive to conceal the nass (to keep power for the Quraysh), but no such motive for concealing counter-narratives. The refutation is swift: it is not proven that all other motives are absent; the opponent must show that no other motive exists in this case.

Key Terms
النص الجلي
الملازمة
تشويش الشريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is simply that we don't know about the text, so it doesn't exist.
Correction: The argument is a logical reductio: if the text could exist without reaching us, it would open the door to claiming other hidden texts that would undermine the Shari'a, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the third shubha regarding the nass?
A) The nass existed but was hidden
B) If the nass existed we would know it; we don't, so it doesn't exist
C) The nass was revealed but later lost
D) The nass is known only to the Ahl al-Bayt

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the claim of an explicit text by showing that accepting hidden texts would lead to total confusion of the Shari'a, a consequence that is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly convinced that not a single verse or ruling of the Shari'a has been lost or concealed from us? If you entertain even a sliver of doubt, how does that affect your obedience to what you know? Will you commit today to trusting the complete preservation of Allah's religion and acting upon it fully?
?
The opponent in this passage had a worldly motive for concealing the truth. Reflect on your own life: are there areas where you suppress a truth from yourself because it would demand change? Will you face that truth today, or continue to live the contradiction?

intermediate
6 min
The Refutation of a Designated Imam Text
Imam Razi refutes the claim of a designated text for a specific imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that a specific person (Ali) was designated as imam by explicit text, using the argument that divine wisdom avoids designation to prevent rebellion.

Core Idea
God's wisdom may prevent explicit designation to avoid greater harm.

Imam Razi now addresses the fourth Shia objection: that a specific person was designated as imam by divine text. This claim is central to the Shia position. Razi refutes it not by denying the possibility of designation, but by arguing that God, in His wisdom, chose not to designate to prevent rebellion and discord. This lesson presents that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Razi structures his response: he first denies the claim, then gives a single counter-argument.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the counter-argument
Razi argues that God knew designation would cause rebellion. He uses the Shia's own admission that rebellion occurred after Ali's designation as evidence.
This is a polemical move — using the opponent's claim against them.
3
Understand the principle of maslaha
The purpose of appointing an imam is the welfare (maslaha) of the community. Since designation would lead to corruption, the most beneficial (aslah) course was to leave it to choice.
This connects to broader Sunni theology of the imamate.
4
Apply to the debate
Reflect on how this argument collapses the Shia objection. It does not deny texts entirely, but shows that divine wisdom may withhold them for a greater good.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة وهي قولهم: إنه وجد النص على إمامة شخص معين، ومتى كان كذلك كان هذا الشخص هو علي. فنقول: لا نسلم أنه وجد النص على إمامة شخص بعينه، والوجوه التي ذكرتموها معارضة بوجه واحد وهو أنه يحتمل أن يقال: إن الله تعالى علم أنه لو نص على شخص معين لاستنكفوا عن طاعته ولتمردوا، وكيف يبعد ذلك والروافض يقولون : إنه تعالى لما نص على إمامة علي تمرد القوم وأبوا إطاعته وأظهروا منازعته ومخالته ؟ وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : المقصود من نصب الإمام رعاية مصلحة الخلق، ولما علم الله تعالى أن التنصيص يفضي إلى الفتنة وإثارة المفسدة، كان الأصلح ترك التنصيص وتفويض الأمر إلى اختيارهم، وبهذا التقرير يسقط كل ما ذكرتموه.
As for the fourth doubt (al-shubha al-rabi'a) which is their statement: 'The text existed for the imamate of a specific person, and when that is the case, that person is 'Ali.' So we say: We do not accept that a text has been established for the imamate of a specific person. The points you have mentioned are countered by a single point: it is possible to say: Almighty God knew that if He designated a specific person, they would disdain obedience to him and rebel. How could that be unlikely, when the Rawafid say that when God designated 'Ali for the imamate, the people rebelled, refused to obey him, and showed opposition and hostility? And when this is established, we say: The purpose of appointing an imam is the welfare of creation. Since God knew that designation leads to discord and corruption, the most beneficial course was to leave designation and delegate the matter to their choice. By this reasoning, everything you have mentioned collapses.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourth Shia objection which asserts that there was a text designating a specific person (i.e. Ali) as imam. The response begins by denying the existence of such a text. Imam Razi then presents a single counter-argument: God knew that explicit designation would cause people to rebel and refuse obedience, as even the Shia claim happened when Ali was designated. Therefore, the purpose of appointing an imam (maslaha) would be undermined by designation. Hence, the most beneficial (aslah) course was to leave it to the community's choice. This argument appeals both to divine wisdom and to historical facts acknowledged by the opponents.

Key Terms

التنصيص
المصلحة
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Sunni position denies any divine guidance in the imamate.
Correction: Razi does not deny divine guidance; he argues that God's wisdom sometimes prefers non-designation to prevent greater harm.

What is the main reason given in the refutation for why God did not explicitly designate an imam?
A) God was unable to choose
B) The people would have rebelled against the designated person
C) The Prophet forgot to announce it
D) The text was lost

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the claim of explicit designation by arguing that God's wisdom avoids designation to prevent rebellion, preserving communal welfare.

?

When you see divisions among Muslims today, do you trust that God's silence on certain leadership details is part of His mercy? Or do you find yourself wishing for clearer texts? How does your heart respond to that tension?
?
Imam Razi uses the admission of the Rawafid themselves to counter their argument. Are you willing to accept evidence from those you disagree with when it challenges your own position? Can you recall a time you had to reconsider a belief because of an unexpected source?

Responding to the Fifth Shubha: Counter-Claim and Deferral
Imam Razi presents the fifth doubt and the initial Sunni responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two Sunni responses to the Shia claim that Ali's superiority necessitates his imamate: the counter-assertion that Abu Bakr is superior and the deferral of the superiority debate.

Core Idea
The superiority claim is met with a counter-claim and deferred for later discussion.

The fifth doubt asserts that Ali is superior and therefore must be the imam. Sunni scholars initially respond by countering that Abu Bakr is superior, but they also defer the entire issue of superiority (tafdil) to a later discussion. This lesson covers these initial moves.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the Arabic passage that states the doubt and the immediate Sunni counter. Notice how the response begins with 'Our companions countered...' and then defers the topic.
The deferral shows that the full discussion of superiority is extensive.
2
Understand the counter-claim
The response asserts that Abu Bakr is superior. This directly challenges the premise that Ali is superior. Reflect on why this is a valid rhetorical move even if not fully argued here.
This counter is not fully developed; it is presented as a parallel assertion.
3
Note the deferral to later discussion
The phrase 'the issue of superiority will come later' indicates that the author chooses not to delve into proofs here. Understand that this is a common scholarly technique to avoid digression.
Deferral is not an evasion but a structural choice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الخامسة وهي أن عليا أفضل، والأفضل هو الإمام فنقول : إن أصحابنا عارضوا هذا بأن أبا بكر أفضل والأفضل هو الإمام وبالجملة فستجيء مسألة التفضيل إن شاء الله تعالى. سلمنا أن عليا كان أفضل فلم قلتم : إن الأفضل هو الإمام؟ ولم لا يجوز إمامة المفضول مع وجود الفاضل ؟ بيانه: أن المفضول إذا كان موصوفًا بالصفات المعتبرة في الإمامة لكنه كان أقل درجة في تلك الفضائل من غيره ونعلم أن الإمامة لو فوضت إلى ذلك الأفضل لحصل التشويش والاضطراب ولو فوضت إلى هذا المفضول لاستقامت الأمور وانتظمت المصالح فالعقل يقتضي تفويض الإمامة إلى ذلك المفضول ؛ لأن المقصود من نصب الإمام رعاية المصالح، وإذا كانت رعاية المصالح لا تحصل إلا بتفويض الإمامة إلى هذا المفضول لكان ذلك واجبًا، وكيف لا نقول ذلك، والروافض يقولون : إن أكثر الناس كانوا يبغضون علياً ؛ لأنه قتل أقاربهم، ولهذا السبب أنكروا النص عليه، ومنعوه عن حقه، وإذا كان كذلك فهم قد اعترفوا بان تفويض الإمامة إليه منشأ الفتن، وحينئذ يظهر ما ذكرناه - وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الشبهة السادسة.
As for the fifth doubt (al-shubha al-khamisa), which is that 'Ali is superior (afdal), and the superior is the imam.' So we say: Our companions countered this by saying that Abu Bakr is superior, and the superior is the imam. In summary, the issue of superiority (tafdil) will come later, God willing. We grant that 'Ali was superior, but why do you say that the superior must be the imam? Why is it not permissible for the less excellent (mafdul) to be imam in the presence of the more excellent (afdal)? Its explanation (bayanuhu): When the less excellent possesses the qualities considered in the imamate but is of a lower degree in those virtues than another, and we know that if the imamate were entrusted to that more excellent person, disorder and disturbance would occur, whereas if entrusted to this less excellent, affairs would be set right and interests (masalih) organized, then reason requires entrusting the imamate to the less excellent, because the purpose of appointing an imam is the welfare (ri'aya) of the interests. When the welfare of interests is only achieved by entrusting the imamate to this less excellent, that becomes obligatory. How can we not say this, when the Rawafid say that most people hated 'Ali because he killed their relatives, and for this reason they denied the text regarding him and prevented him from his right. If so, they have admitted that entrusting the imamate to him is a source of fitna. Hence what we have mentioned becomes clear. This is precisely the answer to the sixth doubt (al-shubha al-sadisa).
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage addresses the fifth Shia objection that Ali was superior (afdal) and therefore must be the imam. Imam Razi raises two responses. First, the Sunni position counters that Abu Bakr is superior, but this is deferred to a later discussion. Second, even if we concede that Ali was superior, the premise 'the superior must be imam' is denied. The explanation (bayan) provides a detailed argument: if the less excellent possesses the required qualities but is inferior in degree, yet his appointment would lead to order while the appointment of the more excellent would cause chaos, reason dictates that the less excellent should be imam because the primary goal is public welfare (masalih). Since the opponents themselves admit that appointing Ali would lead to fitna (as they claim people hated him), this supports the argument. The passage ends by noting that this same reasoning answers the sixth objection.

Key Terms
الأفضل
المفضول
التفضيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first response given by the Sunni scholars to the fifth doubt?
A) They deny that Ali was superior
B) They say that Abu Bakr is superior and defer the discussion
C) They admit Ali's superiority but deny the premise
D) They ignore the argument

Key Takeaway
The initial Sunni response to the claim that the superior must be imam is to counter with a claim of Abu Bakr's superiority and defer the broader debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about someone's superiority, do you examine the criteria you use? The Shia used 'superiority' to argue for Ali, and the Sunni countered with the same criterion for Abu Bakr. What criteria do you use to judge people's worth, and are they consistent?
?
The Sunni scholars deferred the detailed discussion of tafdil to a later time. Is there a theological or personal question you have been avoiding because it is difficult? How can you commit to returning to it with the intention of seeking truth, rather than just postponing it indefinitely?

The Maslaha Argument: Superiority Subordinated to Public Welfare
Imam Razi argues that public welfare justifies the imamate of the less excellent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed reasoning behind the Sunni position that a less excellent person can be imam if his leadership better serves public welfare, even if a more excellent person exists.

Core Idea
Public welfare (maslaha) overrides personal superiority in choosing an imam.

Imam Razi proceeds to concede the Shia premise that Ali is superior, but then questions why the superior must be imam. He provides a detailed argument based on the purpose of imamate: achieving public welfare. If the less excellent (mafdul) would bring more stability, reason requires his appointment. This reasoning is supported by the opponents' own admission that appointing Ali would cause fitna.

Guided Path
1
Read the concession and key question
Read the passage from 'We grant that 'Ali was superior' to the explanation. Note the shift: the superiority is granted, but the logical link to imamate is questioned.
This is a common dialectical move: accepting a premise for the sake of argument.
2
Examine the explanation (bayan)
The bayan presents a scenario where the less excellent has the required qualities but his appointment leads to order, while the more excellent causes chaos. Understand that the criterion is not superiority but the overall good.
The reasoning is based on the purpose of the imamate: ri'ayat al-masalih.
3
Connect to opponents' admission
Razi uses the Rawafid's own claim that people hated Ali to argue that his appointment would cause fitna. This reinforces the point that the less excellent may be preferable.
This is a powerful argument using the opponent's own premise.
4
See the application to the sixth doubt
The passage ends by noting that this same reasoning answers the sixth doubt. Reflect on how the principle of maslaha is generalizable.
This shows the coherence of the Sunni argument.

Refuting the Seventh Shubha: The Verse of Obedience
Imam Razi refutes the claim that the verse 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority' mandates obedience to an infallible imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that verse 4:59 proves an infallible imam, using the principles of apparent meaning (zahir) and implied insertion (idmar).

Core Idea
A verse that is not explicit cannot prove a hidden doctrine.

In this passage, Imam Razi tackles a key Shia proof-text: the Qur'anic command to obey 'those in authority.' He shows that the verse does not clearly refer to an infallible imam, and that attempts to add hidden conditions are arbitrary. This lesson will walk through his concise but powerful refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the full argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the refutation. Notice how Razi first states the doubt, then refutes it, then answers an objection.
2
Understand the first part: apparent meaning
Razi argues that if the verse meant an infallible imam, it would be apparent (zahir) that obedience is conditional on access. Since it's not apparent, the claim fails.
Focus on the logic: if the intended meaning were X, it would be clear; it is not clear, therefore not X.
3
Understand the second part: arbitrary insertion
The opponent says the verse implies 'obey when he appears.' Razi responds that if we allow such insertion, the Sunni insertion ('obey when he commands obedience') is equally valid, so the argument is inconclusive.
This is a key dialectical move: parity of reasoning.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Ask yourself: why is this argument effective in Sunni-Shia debates about the imamate? What does it reveal about the use of ambiguous verses as proofs?
Connect to the principle that theological proofs must be explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة السابعة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى : أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم [النساء : ٥٩] فنقول : لو كان المراد من أولي الأمر هو المعصوم لكان ظاهراً ؛ لأن الأمر بطاعته مشروط بالقدرة على الوصول إليه لكنه غير ظاهر، فعلمنا أن قوله : أَطِيعُوا اللَّهَ وَأَطِيعُوا الرَّسُولَ وَأُولى الأمر منكم [النساء : ٥٩] ليس أمرا بطاعة المعصوم. لا يقال : نضمر في الآية شيئًا، والتقدير : أطيعوا إذا ظهر. لأنا نقول : إذا فتحتم باب الإضمار فليس إضماركم أولى من إضمارنا، فإنا نقول : التقدير : أطيعوه إذا أمركم بالطاعة.
As for the seventh doubt (al-shubha al-sabi'a), which is clinging to the saying of the Exalted: 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' (4:59). So we say: If the intended meaning of 'those in authority' (ulu al-amr) were the infallible (al-ma'sum), it would be apparent, because the command to obey him is conditional upon the ability to reach him, but it is not apparent. Thus we know that His saying 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' is not a command to obey the infallible. One cannot say: We insert something into the verse, the implication being 'obey when he appears.' For we say: If you open the door of implied insertion (idmar), your insertion is not more warranted than our insertion, for we say: The implied meaning is 'Obey him when he commands obedience.'
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage refutes the seventh Shia objection which uses the Qur'anic verse 4:59 ('Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you') to argue for obedience to an infallible imam. Imam Razi responds that the verse does not specify an infallible imam, because if the intended meaning were an infallible imam, it would be apparent (zahir) that the command to obey is conditional on the ability to reach him, which is not the case. Furthermore, the opponent might argue that the verse implies 'obey when he appears' (i.e., when the infallible imam manifests). The refutation is that if arbitrary implied insertions are allowed, then the Sunni insertion is equally valid, namely 'obey him when he commands obedience' (i.e., obey him in what is right, which applies to any just ruler).

Key Terms
أولي الأمر
المعصوم
الإضمار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Verse 4:59 explicitly commands obedience to an infallible imam who is divinely appointed and hidden, and must be obeyed unconditionally.
Correction: Razi shows that the verse does not specify an infallible imam; its wording is general, and any implied condition (like 'when he appears') is not more warranted than a Sunni condition ('when he commands obedience'), so the verse cannot prove the Shia doctrine.

Checkpoint
What is the main Sunni response to the use of verse 4:59 to prove an infallible imam?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) The verse does not specify an infallible imam, and any inserted condition is arbitrary
C) The verse refers to the Prophet only
D) The verse is about scholars

Key Takeaway
A verse that is not explicit (zahir) cannot be used as a definitive proof for a hidden theological doctrine like the infallible imamate.

Refuting the Eighth Shubha: The Verse 'My Covenant Does Not Reach the Wrongdoers'
Imam Razi refutes the eighth Shia shubha from the verse by questioning its absolute scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Shia claim that the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' (2:124) proves the imam must be infallible, by showing that the verse may be temporally restricted and the term ambiguous.

Core Idea
A verse's general word does not guarantee absolute exclusion.

One of the most commonly cited proofs for the necessity of an infallible imam is the verse in which Allah says, 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.' The Shia argue that since the imam must receive God's covenant (imamate), he must never have been a wrongdoer—hence, he must be sinless. In this passage, Imam Razi challenges that reading by asking two simple but powerful questions about the scope of the prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the structure: the doubt is stated, then an answer is given with two points.
2
Identify the Shia claim being refuted
Understand what claim is being made from the verse. The Shia say the verse proves the imam cannot have ever committed oppression (zulm).
3
Analyze the first response: temporal restriction
Imam Razi suggests the verse might only deny the covenant to someone who is currently a wrongdoer, not to someone who repents.
This undermines the absolute nature of the Shia deduction.
4
Analyze the second response: division of the concept
Razi points out that the term 'zalimun' can be divided into two possibilities: does it mean the wrongdoer never attains the covenant, or only while doing wrong? The very possibility of division shows ambiguity.
The key is that the verse cannot be used as a decisive proof if it is open to multiple interpretations.
5
Reflect on the overall refutation
Consider how these two points together show that the verse does not unequivocally support the Shia position. The argument from the verse is not conclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثامنة : التمسك بقوله تعالى : ولا يَنَالُ عَهْدِى الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البَقَرَة : ١٢٤] فجوابه : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك مقصورًا على زمان حصول صفة الظلم؟ والاعتماد في العموم على دليل الاستثناء معارض بما أن هذا المفهوم يحتمل التقسيم ، فيقال : الظالم لا ينال عهد الإمامة في حال كونه ظالمًا، أو في جميع الأحوال؟ ولولا أن ذلك المفهوم مشترك بين هذين القسمين وإلا لم يصح تقسيمه إليهما.
As for the eighth doubt (al-shubha al-thamina): clinging to the saying of the Exalted: 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' (al-Baqara 2:124). Its answer: Why is it not permissible that this is restricted to the time when the attribute of wrongdoing (zulm) is present? And reliance on generality is opposed by the fact that this concept is susceptible to division; so it is said: The wrongdoer does not attain the covenant of imamate while being wrongdoer, or in all states? Were it not that the concept is common between these two divisions, otherwise its division into them would not be valid.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage refutes the eighth Shia objection based on Qur'an 2:124, where God says 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers.' The Shia argue that this proves the imam cannot have ever committed sin (zulm) and therefore must be infallible. Imam Razi responds by questioning whether the verse is restricted to the time when the person is actually committing wrongdoing (i.e., the covenant is not given to someone currently a wrongdoer, but could be given after repentance). He also points out that the concept (mafhum) of 'zalimun' is ambiguous: it could mean the wrongdoer does not attain the covenant while in that state, or never at all. The fact that this division is possible shows that the verse does not unequivocally support the Shia claim.

Key Terms
عهدي
الظالمين
العموم
الاستثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' proves that anyone who has ever committed a sin cannot be an imam.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that the verse may only deny the covenant during the act of wrongdoing, and the term 'zalimun' is ambiguous, so it does not conclusively prove infallibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Imam Razi's response to the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers'?
A) The verse is abrogated
B) The verse may be restricted to the time of the wrongdoing, and the term 'zalimun' is ambiguous
C) The verse refers to prophethood, not imamate
D) The verse was revealed about a specific person

Key Takeaway
The verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' does not prove that an imam must be infallible because it may be temporally restricted and the term occurs in an ambiguous context.

The Ninth Shubha: Refuting Infallible Imams and Proving Consensus
Imam Razi refutes the ninth shubha and proves consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that Quran 9:119 commands following infallible imams and instead uses it to establish the authority of consensus.

Core Idea
The verse 'Be with the truthful' proves consensus, not infallible imams.

Imam Razi addresses a Shia objection that the Qur'anic command to be with the truthful obligates following infallible imams. He argues that since these imams are not identifiable, the command must apply to the entire Muslim community, making the verse a proof for the authority of consensus (ijma'). This lesson breaks down his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Shia claim
The Shia argue that the verse 'Be with the truthful' (9:119) commands following infallible imams, whom they consider 'the truthful.' Identify this claim as the starting point of the objection.
2
Examine Razi's refutation
Razi rejects the Shia interpretation because the infallible imams are not apparent (zahir) – they are not publicly known in every age. This makes their identification impossible.
The concept of 'zuhur' (apparentness) is key.
3
See the conclusion for consensus
To avoid the verse being meaningless (ta'til), it must apply to something apparent – the entire Muslim community (al-umma). Thus the verse becomes evidence for the authority of consensus (ijma').
This is a classic Sunni argument for ijma'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة التاسعة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى : وَكُونُوا مَعَ الصَّادِقِينَ [التوبة : ١١٩] لا يمكن حمله على المعصومين ؛ لأنهم ليسوا ظاهرين، فوجب الأمة صونا للفظ عن التعطيل، فتصير هذه الآية دليلا على أن الإجماع حجة.
As for the ninth doubt (al-shubha al-tasi'a), which is clinging to His saying: 'And be with the truthful' (al-Tawba 9:119). It cannot be applied to the infallibles (al-ma'sumin) because they are not apparent (zahir). So it is necessary to apply it to the community (al-umma) in order to protect the word from being useless (ta'til). Thus this verse becomes evidence that consensus (ijma') is a proof.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage refutes the ninth Shia objection which uses the Qur'anic command 'Be with the truthful' (9:119) to argue that the community must follow infallible imams (the truthful). Imam Razi responds that this interpretation is impossible because the infallible imams are not apparent (i.e., not readily identifiable in every age). To avoid the verse being meaningless (ta'til), it must be applied to the entire community of Muslims, and thus it becomes a proof for the authority of consensus (ijma'). This is a classic Sunni argument that the verse obligates adhering to the majority of the community.

Key Terms
الصادقين
المعصومين
التعطيل
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Be with the truthful' commands following specific infallible imams.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that since infallible imams are not apparent, the verse must refer to the entire community, thus proving the authority of consensus.

Checkpoint

B) Because the infallible imams are not apparent (zahir)
C) Because the verse is abrogated
D) Because the truthful are the angels

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi uses the argument of apparentness (zuhur) to prove that the verse 'Be with the truthful' establishes the authority of consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How can you ensure your practice is based on clear, applicable teachings?

If you were to align yourself fully with the truthful, what relationships would you need to change, and what first step will you take today?

The Less Virtuous May Be Preferred for Order
Imam Razi explains why the less virtuous may be preferred for imamate to preserve order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the less virtuous candidate for imamate may be preferred to preserve public order, and how Imam Razi uses the Rawafid's own admission against them.

Core Idea
Public welfare can override the appointment of the most virtuous.

Imam Razi faces an objection: if the less virtuous is preferred as imam, that contradicts the principle of preferring the better. His answer is both rational and dialectical. He first establishes the principle that the purpose of imamate is the care of interests, so if the more virtuous causes disorder, the less virtuous is chosen. Then he turns the Rawafid's own historical claim about Ali (that his appointment caused strife) into evidence for the Sunni position.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection being answered
The objection claims that appointing a less virtuous imam violates the obligation to prefer the better. Keep this in mind as you read.
This is the sixth objection in the broader discussion on imamate.
2
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Imam Razi presents both a general rational principle and a response to the Rawafid's own claim.
3
Analyze the rational argument
Identify the premises: (1) the purpose of imamate is care of interests, (2) if the more virtuous causes disorder, interests are not served, (3) therefore the less virtuous may be preferred.
This argument is based on human benefit, not textual evidence alone.
4
See the dialectical turn against the Rawafid
Imam Razi points out that the Rawafid themselves claim most people hated Ali due to his killings, which led to strife. They thus admit that appointing Ali would cause disorder, which supports preferring another.
This turns their own historical narrative into a proof for the Sunni position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيانه: أن المفضول إذا كان موصوفًا بالصفات المعتبرة في الإمامة لكنه كان أقل درجة في تلك الفضائل من غيره ونعلم أن الإمامة لو فوضت إلى ذلك الأفضل لحصل التشويش والاضطراب ولو فوضت إلى هذا المفضول لاستقامت الأمور وانتظمت المصالح فالعقل يقتضي تفويض الإمامة إلى ذلك المفضول؛ لأن المقصود من نصب الإمام رعاية المصالح، وإذا كانت رعاية المصالح لا تحصل إلا بتفويض الإمامة إلى هذا المفضول لكان ذلك واجبًا، وكيف لا نقول ذلك، والروافض يقولون: إن أكثر الناس كانوا يبغضون علياً؛ لأنه قتل أقاربهم، ولهذا السبب أنكروا النص عليه، ومنعوه عن حقه، وإذا كان كذلك فهم قد اعترفوا بأن تفويض الإمامة إليه منشأ الفتن، وحينئذ يظهر ما ذكرناه - وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن الشبهة السادسة.
Its explanation: that the less virtuous, if he is described with the qualities considered in imamate but is of a lower degree in those virtues than another, and we know that if the imamate were entrusted to that more virtuous one, chaos and disorder would occur, and if entrusted to this less virtuous one, affairs would be straightened and interests arranged, then reason requires entrusting the imamate to that less virtuous one; because the purpose of appointing an imam is the care of interests, and if the care of interests is not achieved except by entrusting the imamate to this less virtuous one, then that is obligatory. How could we not say that? And the Rawafid say: most people hated Ali because he killed their relatives, and for this reason they denied the explicit designation (nass) for him and prevented him from his right. If that is so, then they have confessed that entrusting the imamate to him is a source of strife, and then what we mentioned becomes evident—and this is the very answer to the sixth objection.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the sixth objection. Imam Razi explains that the less virtuous candidate for imamate may be preferred if his appointment leads to better public order and welfare, which is the very purpose of imamate. He then turns the Rawafid's own admission against them: they claim that most people hated Ali due to his killing of their relatives, causing strife. Therefore, they themselves acknowledge that entrusting imamate to Ali would cause strife, justifying preferring someone else to preserve order. This is a direct refutation of their objection.

Key Terms
المفضول
الأفضل
رعاية المصالح
التشويش
الاضطراب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The most virtuous person in all qualities must always be appointed as imam, regardless of consequences.
Correction: This passage shows that if the more virtuous candidate would cause chaos and disorder, the less virtuous may be preferred because the primary purpose of imamate is to care for public welfare and maintain order.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what is the primary consideration in appointing an imam?
A) The imam must be the most virtuous in all qualities.
B) The imam must be from Quraysh.
C) The imam must be chosen by divine designation.
D) The imam must serve public welfare and maintain order.

Key Takeaway
The purpose of imamate is public welfare, and if the most virtuous candidate causes disorder, the less virtuous may be preferred to preserve order and benefit the community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, when have you insisted on getting the 'best' for yourself—whether in relationships, career, or religious practice—even though it caused tension or harm to those around you? What would it mean to choose the less virtuous path for the sake of peace and unity today?

Refuting the Shia Claim from the Verse of Obedience
Imam Razi refutes the Shia claim that the verse commands obedience to an infallible imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Shia argument that the verse 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' commands obedience to an infallible imam.

Core Idea
An unknown imam cannot be obeyed.

In the seventh objection, the Shia appeal to the Quranic command to obey 'those in authority' (uli al-amr) to argue that an infallible imam must exist and be obeyed. Imam Razi refutes this by pointing out a logical flaw: the command to obey someone is only meaningful if that person is accessible and known. He then counters the Shia attempt to reinterpret the verse with a principle of interpretive fairness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: objection, refutation, counterargument, rebuttal.
2
Identify the Shia claim and Razi's first argument
The Shia claim that 'those in authority' refers to an infallible imam. Razi says if that were the case, it would be evident (zahir), but the imam is not evident, so the command is impossible to fulfill.
Razi uses the principle that a command must be possible to obey.
3
Understand the counterargument about implication (idmar)
The Shia might argue that the verse implies 'obey when he appears'. Identify this in the text and then see Razi's response.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Razi's rebuttal is that if we allow implied interpretations, the Shia's implication is no more valid than the Sunni implication that 'obey when he commands you to obey'. Reflect on the principle of fairness in interpretation.
This highlights a key Sunni principle: the apparent meaning is preferred unless there is evidence for a different interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة السابعة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: {أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم} [النساء: ٥٩] فنقول: لو كان المراد من أولي الأمر هو المعصوم لكان ظاهراً؛ لأن الأمر بطاعته مشروط بالقدرة على الوصول إليه لكنه غير ظاهر، فعلمنا أن قوله: {أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولي الأمر منكم} ليس أمرا بطاعة المعصوم. لا يقال: نضمر في الآية شيئًا، والتقدير: أطيعوا إذا ظهر. لأنا نقول: إذا فتحتم باب الإضمار فليس إضماركم أولى من إضمارنا، فإنا نقول: التقدير: أطيعوه إذا أمركم بالطاعة.
And as for the seventh objection, which is the use as evidence of His statement: {Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you} [al-Nisa 4:59], we say: If what was meant by 'those in authority' is the infallible one, it would be evident, because the command to obey him is conditional on the ability to reach him, but he is not evident. Thus we know that His statement: {Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you} is not a command to obey the infallible. It cannot be said: we imply something in the verse, the implication being 'obey when he appears'. For we say: if you open the door of implication, then your implication is not more worthy than our implication, for we say: the implication is 'obey him when he commands you to obey'.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This is a refutation of the seventh objection, where the Shia use the verse 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' to argue for obedience to an infallible imam. Imam Razi argues that the verse cannot refer to an infallible imam because the command to obey is conditional on the ability to reach him, but the infallible imam is not manifest (i.e., not known to be present). Therefore, the verse does not command obedience to an infallible. He then rejects a potential counterargument that the verse implies 'obey when he appears' by stating that implied interpretations are not better than others; the Sunni interpretation could be 'obey him when he commands you to obey' (i.e., the ruler), which is more plausible.

Key Terms
أولي الأمر
المعصوم
الإضمار
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Obey Allah, obey the Messenger, and those in authority among you' proves the existence of an infallible imam whom Muslims must obey.
Correction: The verse cannot refer to an infallible imam because obedience requires the ability to reach him, and an infallible imam is not known or evident. This makes the command impossible, so the interpretation is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say the verse cannot refer to an infallible imam?
A) Because the imam is not from Quraysh.
B) Because the infallible imam is not manifestly known, making obedience impossible.
C) Because the verse is abrogated.
D) Because the command to obey is only for Allah and the Messenger.

Key Takeaway
The Shia interpretation of Quran 4:59 fails because it commands obedience to an unknown person, which is impossible; a sound interpretation must be consistent with possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever follow a religious leader or scholar without verifying that they are truly knowledgeable and trustworthy? How does this lesson's condition of 'ability to reach' challenge you to investigate before obeying?
?
Imam Razi shows that implying meanings can lead to a slippery slope. Are there commands of Allah that you have reinterpreted to suit your desires? What would it cost you to take them at their apparent meaning today?

If obedience requires a known authority, then how seriously do you take the responsibility of knowing your Prophet and his teachings? What will you do this week to remove ignorance about a specific command of Allah?

Refuting the Absolute Exclusion from Imamate
Imam Razi refutes the Shia use of the verse to exclude non-infallibles from imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's rational refutation of the Shia argument that the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' proves only infallibles can be imam.

Core Idea
A verse's meaning can be restricted to a specific condition, not absolute.

The Shia argue that the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' (2:124) proves that only those who have never committed wrongdoing can be imams. Imam Razi responds by questioning the absolute interpretation. He points out that the verse could be limited to the time when a person is actually a wrongdoer, and that the concept admits division into temporary versus permanent exclusion. This lesson explores this nuanced refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the concise style of Imam Razi's response.
2
Identify the Shia objection
The Shia use the verse to claim that only those who never did wrong can be imams. Recognize that this is an absolute interpretation.
3
Understand the two parts of Razi's rebuttal
First, he suggests the verse may be restricted to the time of wrongdoing. Second, he argues that the concept admits division into two possibilities.
These two points together show the verse is not decisive.
4
Reflect on the logical impact
Ask yourself: why does the ambiguity prevent using this verse as proof for perpetual exclusion? Think about how a valid proof must be unambiguous.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثامنة: التمسك بقوله تعالى: {ولا ينال عهدي الظالمين} [البقرة: ١٢٤] فجوابه: لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك مقصورًا على زمان حصول صفة الظلم؟ والاعتماد في العموم على دليل الاستثناء معارض بما أن هذا المفهوم يحتمل التقسيم، فيقال: الظالم لا ينال عهد الإمامة في حال كونه ظالمًا، أو في جميع الأحوال؟ ولولا أن ذلك المفهوم مشترك بين هذين القسمين وإلا لم يصح تقسيمه إليهما.
And as for the eighth objection, which is the use as evidence of His statement: {And My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers} [al-Baqara 2:124], the answer is: why is it not possible that this is restricted to the time of the occurrence of the attribute of wrongdoing? And relying on generality based on the evidence of exception is opposed by the fact that this concept admits division. So it is said: The wrongdoer does not attain the covenant of imamate while he is a wrongdoer, or in all circumstances? And were it not that that concept is common between these two divisions, then its division into them would not be valid.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage answers the eighth objection. The Shia argue that the verse 'My covenant does not reach the wrongdoers' proves that only the infallible (who never did wrong) can be imam. Imam Razi responds that the verse could be restricted to the time when wrongdoing occurs. The concept of not attaining the covenant is ambiguous: it could mean at the moment of wrongdoing (so after repentance, one can attain it) or permanently. Since the verse admits both interpretations, it cannot be used as decisive evidence for permanent exclusion.

Key Terms
الظالمين
عهدي
المفهوم
التقسيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse absolutely excludes anyone who has ever committed wrongdoing from ever being imam.
Correction: The verse may only exclude during the time of wrongdoing, and after repentance the person could be eligible.

Checkpoint
What is the key point in Imam Razi's answer to the eighth objection?
A) The verse is abrogated by another verse.
B) The verse only denies imamate to wrongdoers at the time they are wrongdoers, not forever.
C) The verse refers to covenant of prophethood, not imamate.
D) The verse was revealed about a specific person.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi shows that the verse is not decisive because it can be interpreted as temporary exclusion only, thus not proving perpetual disqualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

How does Imam Razi's logical division force you to be more careful in understanding divine speech and to avoid hasty conclusions about others or yourself?

The Verse 'Be with the Truthful' and Consensus
Razi refutes the Shia objection using logical necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Shia interpretation of Qur'an 9:119 and instead uses it to prove the authority of consensus (ijma').

Core Idea
The verse 'Be with the truthful' supports consensus, not a hidden imam.

Imam Razi here addresses one of the Shia proof-texts: the command to be with the truthful. The Shia claim it obligates following an infallible imam. Razi turns the argument on its head by showing that applying the verse to a hidden imam would render it meaningless, so it must refer to the community, whose truthfulness is expressed through consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of Razi's objection and response.
Notice the concise style of Razi's reasoning.
2
Identify the two premises
Razi gives two key points: 1) The verse cannot refer to infallibles because they are not manifest (ظاهرين). 2) If not applied to the community, the verse becomes inoperative (تعطيل).
The term تعطيل is crucial—it means rendering a text meaningless.
3
Understand the conclusion
To avoid تعطيل, the verse must refer to the whole community. That makes it a proof for consensus (إجماع). See how Razi turns a Shia proof into a Sunni one.
This shows the importance of logical consistency in tafsir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة التاسعة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: {وكونوا مع الصادقين} [التوبة: ١١٩] لا يمكن حمله على المعصومين؛ لأنهم ليسوا ظاهرين، فوجب حملة الأمة صونا للفظ عن التعطيل، فتصير هذه الآية دليلا على أن الإجماع حجة.
And as for the ninth objection, which is the use as evidence of His statement: {And be with the truthful} [al-Tawba 9:119], it cannot be interpreted as referring to the infallibles, because they are not manifest. So it must be interpreted as referring to the community (umma), to protect the wording from being rendered inoperative. Thus this verse becomes evidence that consensus (ijma') is a proof.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage addresses the ninth objection. The Shia use the verse 'Be with the truthful' to argue for following the infallible imam. Imam Razi responds that since the infallible imam is not manifest (i.e., not known or present), interpreting the verse that way would render it meaningless (تعطيل). Therefore, to give the verse meaning, it must refer to the entire community, and the truthfulness of the community is expressed through consensus (ijma'), thus the verse actually proves the authority of consensus.

Key Terms
الصادقين
المعصومين
التعطيل
الإجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse obligates following a specific infallible imam, such as the hidden imam of the Shia.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that applying the verse to a hidden imam would render it meaningless, so it must refer to the entire community, thus supporting consensus rather than a specific person.

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Razi say the verse 'Be with the truthful' cannot refer to infallible imams?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because the infallible imams are not manifest, causing the verse to be inoperative.
C) Because truthful means prophets only.
D) Because the verse was revealed about Abu Bakr.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi argues that the Qur'anic command to be with the truthful must refer to the consensus of the community, not a hidden infallible imam.

?
The Shia claim that only a hidden infallible leader deserves full loyalty. Razi says that would make the command meaningless.
