Refuting the Shia Claim from the Verse 'Those of Kin are Nearer'
Razi refutes the Shia claim by denying generality and invoking division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Shia argument from the verse 'Those of kin are nearer' by limiting its application to inheritance and denying its generality.

Core Idea
The verse about kin is specific to inheritance, not a general proof for imamate.

In this short passage, Imam Razi tackles the tenth objection raised by the Shia, who cite the Quranic verse 'And those of kin are nearer to one another' to argue that the Prophet's relatives (specifically Ali) are more deserving of imamate. Razi's response is concise but decisive: he points out that the verse does not convey generality—it applies only to inheritance rules, not to leadership. He also references the earlier argument about division to counter any reliance on exception. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source text once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the brevity and structure: the objection is stated, then answered with two points.
The Arabic uses legal terminology like 'عموم' (generality) and 'تقسيم' (division).
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The Shia objection is that the verse makes kin nearer, implying they deserve imamate. Razi's answer has two parts: (1) the phrase 'some of them are nearer to others' does not imply generality; (2) reliance on exception is opposed by the validity of division.
Think about what 'generality' means in Islamic legal theory.
3
Understand the concepts of generality and division
Razi says the verse does not have 'عموم' (generality), meaning it is not a universal rule applying to all contexts like imamate. The term 'تقسيم' (division) refers to the earlier argument that the concept of 'nearness' can be divided (e.g., into inheritance and other matters), so it cannot be used as an absolute proof.
This reflects a key principle in kalam: Quranic verses must be interpreted in their specific context.

وأما الشبهة العاشرة: وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: {وأولو الأرحام بعضهم أولى ببعض} [الأنفال: ٧٥] فجوابه أن قوله: {بعضهم أولى ببعض} لا يفيد العموم، والاعتماد على دليل الاستثناء معارض بما ذكرناه في صحة التقسيم.
And as for the tenth objection, which is the use as evidence of His statement: {And those of kin are nearer to one another} [al-Anfal 8:75], the answer is that His statement {some of them are nearer to others} does not convey generality, and reliance on the evidence of exception is opposed by what we mentioned regarding the validity of division.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage answers the tenth objection. The Shia argue that the verse 'Those of kin are nearer to one another' proves that the relatives of the Prophet (i.e., Ali) are more deserving of imamate. Imam Razi responds that the phrase 'some of them are nearer to others' does not imply generality (عموم) that all kin are nearer than non-kin in all matters. The verse is specific to inheritance rules, not imamate. Furthermore, as mentioned in previous objections, the concept admits division and cannot be used as a decisive general proof.

Refuting the Shia Interpretation of Verse 5:55
Imam Razi refutes the Shia claim that verse 5:55 designates Ali as imam by reinterpreting 'wilaya' as support and using grammatical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the Shia interpretation of verse 5:55 by showing that 'wilaya' means support and love, not authority, and that the plural grammar of the verse cannot refer to a single person.

Core Idea
The verse 'Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger' refers to love and support, not political authority.

The eleventh objection presented by the Shia is based on the verse 5:55, which they claim appoints Ali as imam because he gave zakah while bowing. Imam Razi provides a multi-layered refutation: first, he distinguishes between support and authority; second, he uses linguistic and logical arguments to show the verse cannot be about Ali alone. In this lesson, we will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the Shia claim and Razi's overall response.
2
Analyze the first interpretation: wilaya as support
Razi argues that if wilaya means support, it applies to all believers, not just Ali. Notice how he uses verse 9:71 to show believers are guardians of each other.
3
Examine the second argument: if wilaya means authority
Even if wilaya is taken as authority, Razi points out two problems: it would make Ali imam during the Prophet's lifetime, and the seven plural forms in the verse cannot refer to one person.
4
Conclude the refutation
Understand how Razi's arguments collectively invalidate the Shia claim. The premise that the verse designates imamate for Ali is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الحادية عشرة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: {إنما وليكم الله ورسوله} [المائدة: ٥٥] فجوابه: الولاية المذكورة في هذه الآية خاصة، والولاية بمعنى النصرة عامة. قلنا: الولاية بمعنى النصرة إذا أضيفت إلى من سوى علي كانت مخصوصة لا محالة، لأن الإنسان يستحيل أن يكون ناصرًا لنفسه، وأما إذا لم تكن مضافة إلى أقوام معينين كانت عامة. فقوله: {إنما وليكم الله ورسوله} والمؤمنون الموصوفون بالصفة المذكورة، وهذا خطاب مع كل الأمة سوى المؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة، فلا كانت الولاية بمعنى النصرة هذه خاصة بالمؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة. وأما قوله: {والمؤمنون والمؤمنات بعضهم أولياء بعض} [التوبة: ٧١] فنقول: ليست كانت خاصة بقوم معينين، فلا جرم الولاية المذكورة فيه مضافة إلى أقوام معينين، فثبت بما ذكرنا أنه لا يمتنع أن تكون الولاية المذكورة في قوله: {إنما وليكم الله} المفسرة بمعنى المحبة والنصرة. وإذا بطلت هذه المقدمة سقطت هذه الشبهة. ثم نقول: إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن الولاية المذكورة في الآية بمعنى التصرف فمعنا ما يبطل ذلك، وهو من وجهين: الأول: أنه يقتضي حصول الإمامة لعلي في زمان حياة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنه باطل. والثاني: أن قوله تعالى: {الذين آمنوا الذين يقيمون الصلاة ويؤتون الزكاة وهم راكعون} [المائدة: ٥٥] مشتمل على سبعة ألفاظ من صيغ الجموع، فحملها على الشخص الواحد خلاف الأصل.
And as for the eleventh objection, which is the use as evidence of His statement: {Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger} [al-Ma'ida 5:55], the answer is: the guardianship (wilaya) mentioned in this verse is specific, and guardianship in the sense of support (nusra) is general. We say: guardianship in the sense of support, when added to someone other than Ali, is necessarily specific because a person cannot be a supporter of himself. But when it is not added to specific groups, it is general. So His statement: {Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger} and the believers described with the mentioned attribute, and this is an address to all the community except the believers described with that attribute, so that guardianship in the sense of support is specific to the believers described with that attribute. And as for His statement: {And the believing men and believing women are guardians of one another} [al-Tawba 9:71], we say: it was not specific to specific groups, so inevitably the guardianship mentioned in it is added to specific groups. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that it is not impossible that the guardianship mentioned in His statement: {Your guardian is only Allah} is interpreted as love and support. And when this premise is invalidated, this objection falls. Then we say: if what you have mentioned indicates that the guardianship mentioned in the verse means authority (tasarruf), then we have that which invalidates it, and it is from two aspects: First, it would require that imamate be established for Ali during the lifetime of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and that is false. Second, that His statement: {Those who believe, who establish prayer and give zakah while bowing} [al-Ma'ida 5:55] contains seven plural forms, and applying them to a single person is contrary to the principle.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage refutes the eleventh objection at length. The Shia use verse 5:55, claiming it designates Ali as imam because he gave zakah while bowing. Imam Razi provides a multi-layered refutation. First, he distinguishes between 'guardianship' (wilaya) as love/support and as authority. The verse speaks of support, not authority. Second, he argues that if wilaya means support, it is specific to believers described (Ali and others), but support is general among believers as per verse 9:71. Third, even if one insists that wilaya means authority, that would imply Ali became imam during the Prophet's life, which is false. Fourth, the verse contains seven plural phrases (e.g., 'those who believe', 'they establish prayer') which grammatically cannot refer to a single person. Thus the Shia interpretation is invalid.

Key Terms
الولاية
النصرة
المحبة
التصرف
صيغ الجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger' proves that Ali is the imam because he gave zakah while bowing.
Correction: The word 'wilaya' in the verse means love and support, not political authority. Additionally, the verse contains plural forms that cannot refer to a single person.

Checkpoint
What is one of the two additional arguments Imam Razi gives if wilaya is interpreted as authority?
A) That would make Ali imam before the Prophet's death, which is false.
B) The verse is not authentic.
C) The verse was about Abu Bakr.
D) The word wilaya is ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi refutes the Shia claim by showing that 'wilaya' in verse 5:55 means love and support, not authority, and that the verse's plural grammar precludes it referring solely to Ali.

Razi argues that applying plural forms to a single person is contrary to principle. Are you willing to submit to the rules of Arabic and the principles of the Salaf, even when they contradict a belief you find emotionally satisfying? What is one area in your life where you have clung to a weak argument out of desire rather than evidence?

Refuting the Hadith of Ghadir: The Solitary Report Argument
Imam Razi begins his refutation of the hadith by identifying it as a solitary report.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi's first refutation of the Shia use of the hadith 'Whoever I am his master, Ali is his master' is that it is a solitary report (khabar wahid), which in theological matters does not provide certainty.

Core Idea
A solitary report cannot establish a matter of faith or political succession.

This passage begins the response to the famous hadith often cited by Shia to argue for Ali's immediate succession. Imam Razi opens with a fundamental point about the type of narration this hadith is. Before discussing meaning or context, he questions its epistemic status. He states it is a solitary report, implying that it cannot produce the certainty required for such a decisive claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to get the flow. Notice that it is cut off mid-sentence. The passage introduces the objection and the first aspect of the answer.
The text is incomplete due to high OCR risk.
2
Identify the objection and the answer
The Shia use the hadith as proof for Ali's imamate. Razi starts his answer with multiple aspects; we only see the first: that it is a solitary report.
The word 'mawla' can mean master, friend, or protector, but Razi's focus here is on the hadith's transmission.
3
Understand 'khabar wahid'
A solitary report is a hadith that does not reach the level of mass transmission (tawatur). In Islamic legal theory, it yields only probable knowledge (zann), not certainty (ilm). In matters of usul al-din (foundations of religion) and political leadership, certainty is required.
This is a key concept in hadith classification and kalam methodology.
4
Reflect on the community's agreement
Razi notes that the community agreed on the hadith's authenticity, meaning they accept it as a valid hadith, but that does not make it mutawatir or prove the Shia claim.
Authenticity does not imply that the hadith can be used for such a weighty claim.
5
Consider the implication
By classifying the hadith as a solitary report, Razi undercuts the argument at its epistemic root. Without certainty, the hadith cannot serve as definitive proof for imamate.

Source Text

And as for the twelfth objection, which is the use as evidence of his statement (peace be upon him): 'Whoever I am his master (mawla), then Ali is his master', the answer is from several aspects: First: it is a solitary report (khabar wahid). And the community [؟] agreed on its authenticity because some of them used it as evidence in

Commentary
This passage begins the refutation of the twelfth objection, the famous hadith 'Whoever I am his master, Ali is his master'. Imam Razi states that the answer has multiple aspects, and he starts with the first: that this hadith is a solitary report (khabar wahid), which in Islamic legal theory does not produce certainty (ilm) in matters of belief or crucial political authority. The text cuts off mid-sentence, so the complete argument is not given. He mentions that the community agreed on its authenticity, but that does not solve the interpretive problem.

Refutation of the Tenth Shubha: The Inheritance Verse
Razi refutes the tenth shubha using two arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the claim that the Qur'anic inheritance verse (Al-Anfal 75) proves Ali's imamate by showing the phrase does not indicate generality and the exception argument is invalid.

Core Idea
A general statement does not prove a specific claim.

Imam Razi continues his refutation of the tenth doubt raised by opponents who seek to prove Ali's imamate from the Qur'anic verse about kinship priority. In this concise passage, he dismantles their proof by demonstrating that the wording of the verse does not carry universal application, and their reliance on an exception argument is already undermined by a prior division.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the literal English translation to understand the basic argument.
Pay attention to the two parts of Razi's answer.
2
Identify Razi's two points
Razi gives two reasons: first, the phrase 'some are more deserving' does not imply generality; second, the exception argument is opposed by the validity of division.
These are the core of the refutation.
3
Reflect on the significance
Consider why Razi's refutation is effective: he shows that the verse cannot be used as a definitive proof for a specific claim because its wording is not universal.
This illustrates a key principle in theological argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة العاشرة: وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿ وَأَولُوا الْأَرْحَامِ بَعضُهم أولى بعض ﴾ [الأنفال: ٧٥] :فجوابه أن قوله : بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلَى بِبعض كم[؟] لله لا يفيد العموم، والاعتماد على دليل الاستثناء معارض بما ذكرناه في صحة التقسيم.
As for the tenth doubt: it is the adherence to His saying (the Most High): 'And the possessors of relationships, some of them are more deserving of others' (Al-Anfal 75). Its answer is that His saying 'some of them are more deserving of others in...' does not indicate generality, and the reliance on the evidence of the exception is opposed by what we mentioned regarding the validity of the division.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage refutes the tenth suspicion (shubha) raised by opponents who argue that the Qur'anic verse 'And those of kinship are more deserving of one another' (Al-Anfal 75) gives priority to relatives, implying Ali's imamate. Razi's answer has two points: first, the phrase 'some of them are more deserving of others' (ba'duhum awla bi ba'd) does not indicate universal applicability (la yufid al-'umum); it is a general statement but not absolute. Second, the opponents' reliance on the 'exception argument' (dalil al-istithna') is opposed by what Razi already established about the validity of the division (sihhat al-taqsim). The passage is a concise refutation of a specific proof-text used by the rival sect.

عموم
استثناء
تقسيم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic inheritance verse (Al-Anfal 75) is a general proof that Ali should be imam because he is a relative.
Correction: Razi argues that the verse's wording does not indicate universality, and the exception argument is invalid, so it cannot be used as a proof for Ali's imamate.

Refutation of the Verse of Wilaya: Interpreting Wilaya as Love and Support
Razi refutes the eleventh shubha by interpreting wilaya as love/support and providing two counterarguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi refutes the eleventh shubha by carefully analyzing the meaning of 'wilaya' in the verse and presenting two additional arguments against the Shia interpretation.

Core Idea
Wilaya in the verse can mean love and support, not authority; even if it did, it fails.

The eleventh doubt cited the verse 'Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger...' (5:55) to argue for Ali's immediate imamate. Imam Razi dismantles this claim by first clarifying that 'wilaya' in this context refers to love and support, not political authority. He then shows that even if it meant authority, it would entail absurd consequences. This lesson walks through his careful linguistic and logical argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a main response followed by two conditional refutations.
The Arabic has some OCR errors; refer to the translation for clarity.
2
Identify the main claim and Razi's response
The shubha claims the verse proves Ali's imamate. Razi's first response: the wilaya in the verse is specific to love/support, not authority.
3
Understand the specific vs general distinction
Razi explains that 'wilaya' meaning 'support' is general when not added to specific groups, but when added (as here to the believers described), it becomes specific. This defeats the shubha's claim of universal authority.
4
Examine the two refutations if wilaya means authority
Even if the opponent insists on 'authority', Razi gives two proofs: (1) it would require Ali being imam during the Prophet's life, false; (2) the verse has seven plural forms, so applying them to one person violates linguistic norms.
These are called 'two aspects' (wajhayn) in the passage.
5
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how Razi's layered response—first reinterpreting the key term, then providing alternative refutations—strengthens the Sunni position against this proof-text.
This is a model of detailed scriptural argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الحادية عشرة وهي التمسك بقوله تعالى : وإِنما وَلِيكُمُ الله : ورسولر ال [المائدة: ٥٥] فجوابه الولاية المذكورة في هذه الآية خاصة، والولاية بمعنى النصرة عامة. من الأمة قلنا : الولاية بمعنى النصرة إذا أضيفت إلى من سوى علي كانت مخصوصة لا محالة ،بعلي لأن الإنسان يستحيل أن يكون ناصرًا لنفسه، وأما إذا لم تكن مضافة إلى أقوام معينين كانت عامة فقوله : وإنها وليكم الله ورسول الله [المائدة : ٥٥] والمؤمنون الموصوفون بالصفة المذكورة وهذا خطاب مع كل الأمة، سوى المؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة، فلا كانت الولاية بمعنى النصرة هذه خاصة بالمؤمنين الموصوفين بالصفة المذكورة. جرم وأما قوله : ﴿وَالْمُؤْمِنُونَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَتُ بَعْضُهُمْ أَوْلِيَاءُ بَعْضٍ [التوبة: ٧١] فنقول: ليست كانت خاصة بقوم معينين، فلا جرم الولاية المذكورة فيه مضافة إلى أقوام معينين، فثبت بما ذكرنا أنه لا يمتنع أن تكون الولاية المذكورة في قوله : إِنَّمَا وَلِيُّكُمُ الله [المائدة: ٥٥] المفسرة بمعنى المحبة والنصرة وإذا بطلت هذه المقدمة سقطت هذه الشبهة ثم نقول : إن دل ما ذكرتم على أن الولاية المذكورة في الآية بمعنى التصرف فمعنا ما يبطل ذلك، وهو من وجهين : الأول : أنه يقتضي حصول الإمامة لعلي في زمان حياة محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم وإنه باطل. والثاني: أن قوله تعالى: وَالَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا الَّذِينَ يُقِيمُونَ الصَّلوةَ وَيُؤْتُونَ الزَّكَوةَ وَهُمْ راكفون الله [المائدة ٥٥] مشتمل على سبعة ألفاظ من صيغ الجموع، فحملها على الشخص الواحد خلاف الأصل.
As for the eleventh doubt: it is the adherence to His saying (the Most High): 'Your guardian (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger' (Al-Ma'idah 55). Its answer is that the wilaya mentioned in this verse is specific, and wilaya in the sense of support (nusra) is general. From the community, we said: wilaya in the sense of support, when added to anyone other than Ali, is necessarily specific, because it is impossible for a person to be a supporter of himself. But if it is not added to specific groups, it is general. So His saying 'Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger' (Al-Ma'idah 55) and the believers described with the mentioned description, and this address is to the entire community, except the believers described with the mentioned description. Thus, the wilaya in the sense of support was specific to the believers described with the mentioned description. And as for His saying: 'And the believing men and the believing women are guardians (awliya') of one another' (Al-Tawbah 71), we say: it was not specific to particular people, so the wilaya mentioned there is added to specific groups. It is established by what we mentioned that it is not impossible for the wilaya mentioned in His saying 'Your guardian is only Allah' (Al-Ma'idah 55) to be interpreted as love and support. When this premise is invalidated, this doubt falls. Then we say: If what you mentioned indicates that the wilaya mentioned in the verse means authority (tasarruf), then we have what invalidates that, namely two aspects: First: it requires that imamate be established for Ali during the lifetime of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and that is false. Second: His saying (the Most High): 'And those who believe, those who establish prayer and give zakah while they are bowing' (Al-Ma'idah 55) contains seven plural forms; applying them to a single person is contrary to the principle.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage refutes the eleventh shubha, which cites the verse 'Your guardian (wali) is only Allah and His Messenger' (Al-Ma'idah 55) to argue for Ali's imamate. Razi's answer is multi-layered. First, he distinguishes between 'wilaya' in the sense of love/support and 'wilaya' as authority. The verse's wilaya is specific to the believers described (those who give zakah while bowing), not general. He explains that if 'wilaya' means support (nusra), then when added to specific individuals like Ali, it becomes specific (makhsusa) because one cannot be a supporter of oneself. If not added to specific groups, it is general. The verse addresses the whole community except those believers described. He then addresses the apparent conflict with verse 9:71 ('Believers are guardians of one another') by noting that verse does not specify particular people, so it does not contradict. Having established that the verse's wilaya can be interpreted as love and support, the shubha collapses. However, Razi adds: even if one insists that the wilaya means authority (tasarruf), there are two refutations. First, it would entail Ali being imam during the Prophet's lifetime, which is false. Second, the verse (5:55) contains seven plural forms; taking them to refer to one person violates linguistic principle (khilaf al-asl). The passage thus carefully dismantles the proof-text on multiple levels, protecting the Sunni position that the verse does not prove immediate succession.

إمامة
جموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Your guardian is only Allah and His Messenger...' (5:55) proves that Ali was the immediate successor to the Prophet.
Correction: Razi shows that 'wilaya' here means love and support, not political authority, and even if it did, it would require imamate during the Prophet's life, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation Razi gives if 'wilaya' in the verse means authority?
A) It would require Ali to be imam during the Prophet's life, which is false.
B) The verse is abrogated.
C) The verse is not authentic.
D) Ali did not give zakah while bowing.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the eleventh shubha by demonstrating that the 'wilaya' in the verse is specific to love and support, and that even if interpreted as authority, it leads to absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 5:55, are you quick to interpret it in a way that supports your pre-existing beliefs, or do you hold yourself to the same rigorous standards Razi applies here? Ask yourself: What would it take for me to change my understanding of a verse if the evidence pointed elsewhere?
?
Razi dismantles this shubha with clear logic and linguistic analysis. Are you willing to subject your own cherished interpretations—especially on issues of leadership and authority—to the same scrutiny, even if it means surrendering a comfortable position?
?
The verse describes believers who give zakah while bowing. Razi points out the plural forms. Have you ever used a singular reference to a group to push a particular narrative? How would you feel if someone did that to you? Commit today to always consider the full context before drawing conclusions from the Quran.

The Hadith as a Single Narration and Its Certainty
Razi's first argument: the hadith is a single narration and its certainty is disputed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu' is a single narration (khabar wahid) and does not provide the certainty required for establishing imamate.

Core Idea
A single narration yields probability, not certainty—insufficient for foundational doctrine.

This passage from Imam Razi's Al-Arba'in opens the refutation of the twelfth doubt. The opponents cite the famous hadith to support the imamate of Ali. Razi's first move is to question the epistemological status of the hadith itself: is it definitive or merely probable? The answer determines whether it can be used as proof in foundational matters. In this lesson, you'll examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening objection
Understand the opponents' claim that the hadith is sound and accepted by the community.
Note the page reference which may be a textual insertion.
2
Identify Razi's distinction
Razi asks whether the acceptance is with certainty (qat') or probability (zann). Identify both possibilities.
3
Analyze the conclusion
If probability, it does not benefit the claim. If certainty, it is denied. See how this frames the debate.
This is a classic kalam move: disputing the epistemic value of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية عشرة وهي التمسك بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاء فجوابها من وجوه الأول : أنه خبر واحد . قوله : الأمة اتفقت على صحته لأن منهم من تمسك به في المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون ٤٥١ تفضيل علي، ومنهم من تمسك يه في إمامته قلنا : تدعي أن كل الأمة قبلوه قبول القطع أو قبول الظن ؟ الأول : ممنوع، وهو نفس المطلوب، والثاني: مسلم، وهو لا ينفعكم في مطلوبكم، سلمنا صحة الحديث لكن لا نسلم أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى. والاستدلال بقوله تعالى : والنَّارُ هِيَ مَولَكُمْ ﴾ [الحديد: ١٥] بمعنى هي أولى بكم معارض بما أنه لا يجوز إقامة كل واحد من هذين اللفظين مقام الآخر، فيقال : هذا أولى من ذلك، ولا يقال : هذا مولى من ذلك، ويقال : هذا مولى فلان، ولا يقال : هذا أولى فلان. وسلمنا أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى، ولكن لا نسلم أنه يجب حمل لفظ المولى في هذا الحديث على الأولى. أنه قوله : المولى مجمل والأولى يحتمل أن يكون بيانا له فوجب حمله عليه " قلنا : هذا دليل ظني فلا يقبل في القطعيات سلمنا أنه محمول على الأولى لكن لا نسلم يجب أن يكون أولى بهم في كل شيء، بل يجوز أن يكون أولى بهم في بعض الأشياء، وهو وجوب محبته وتعظيمه والقطع على سلامة باطنه، فإنه روي أنه إنما قال هذا الكلام عند منازعة جرت بين زيد وعلي، فقال علي لزيد : أنت مولاي، فقال زيد لست مولى لك، وإنما أنا مولى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال هذا الكلام عند هذه الواقعة فوجب صرف الأولوية إلى حكم هذه الواقعة، وهو أن هذه كنت أولى به في المحبة والتعظيم والقطع على سلامة الباطن فعلي أولى به به في الأحكام، ثم نقول : حمل اللفظ على ما ذكرناه أولى من حمله على الإمامة، وإلا لزم كونه إماما حال حياة محمد له و نافذ الحكم، متصرفا في الأمة، ولا شك في بطلان هذا الكلام.
As for the twelfth doubt: it is the adherence to his saying (peace be upon him): 'He whose mawla I am, Ali is his mawla.' Its answer is from several aspects. The first: it is a single narration (khabar wahid). His saying: the community agreed on its authenticity because among them are those who adhered to it in the thirty-ninth issue, page 451, preferring Ali, and among them are those who adhered to it in his imamate. We said: Do you claim that the entire community accepted it with certainty (qat') or with probability (zann)? The first is denied, and it is the very claim; the second is granted, but it does not benefit you in your claim. We grant the authenticity of the hadith, but we do not grant that the word 'mawla' bears the meaning of 'awla' (more deserving). And the inference with His saying (the Most High): 'And the Fire is your mawla' (Al-Hadid 15) meaning it is more worthy of you, is opposed by the fact that it is not permissible to substitute each of these two words for the other. It is said: 'this is awla from that', and it is not said: 'this is mawla from that'. And it is said: 'this is mawla of so-and-so', and it is not said: 'this is awla of so-and-so'. And we grant that the word 'mawla' bears the possibility of 'awla', but we do not grant that it is necessary to interpret the word 'mawla' in this hadith as 'awla'. That he said: 'mawla' is ambiguous (mujmal) and 'awla' may be an explanation for it, so it is necessary to interpret it accordingly. We said: This is a probabilistic proof (dalil zanni) and is not accepted in certain matters (qati'iyyat). We grant that it is interpreted as 'awla', but we do not grant that it must be that he is more deserving (awla) of them in everything. Rather, it is possible that he is more deserving of them in some things, which is the obligation of loving him, magnifying him, and affirming the soundness of his inner state. For it is narrated that he only said this statement at a dispute that occurred between Zayd and Ali. Ali said to Zayd: 'You are my mawla.' Zayd said: 'I am not your mawla; I am only the mawla of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him).' So he said this statement at this incident. Therefore, it is necessary to restrict the priority (awlawiyya) to the ruling of this incident, which is that you were more deserving of him in love, magnification, and affirming the soundness of the inner state. So Ali is more deserving of him in these judgments. Then we say: interpreting the word as we have mentioned is more appropriate than interpreting it as imamate. Otherwise, it would require that he be imam during the life of Muhammad (peace be upon him), executing judgments, acting with authority over the community, and there is no doubt about the falseness of this statement.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage presents the first face (wajh) of Razi's refutation of the twelfth shubha, which cites the famous hadith 'He whose mawla I am, 'Ali is his mawla.' Razi lists multiple points within this face. First, he notes the hadith is a khabar wahid (single narration), which only yields probability (zann), not certainty (qat'). The opponents claim the community agreed on its authenticity, but Razi counters: by what standard—certainty or probability? If they claim certainty, it is denied; if probability, it does not prove their claim (which requires certainty). Even if the hadith is authentic, the word 'mawla' may not mean 'first' (awla). They try to prove it from the Qur'anic verse 'the Fire is your mawla' (57:15) meaning 'more worthy of you', but Razi objects that one cannot substitute 'mawla' and 'awla' in usage. Even if 'mawla' can mean 'awla', it is not necessary to interpret it that way here. When they argue that 'mawla' is ambiguous and 'awla' clarifies it, Razi replies that this reasoning is probabilistic and not acceptable in definitive matters (qati'iyyat). Even granting that 'mawla' means 'awla' here, it does not imply priority in everything; it could be limited to love, respect, and affirming inner righteousness. Razi supports this by narrating a specific incident: a dispute between Zayd and Ali where Ali called Zayd 'mawla', and Zayd denied being Ali's mawla, claiming only to be the Prophet's mawla. The Prophet then said the hadith, indicating priority in love and respect, not general authority. Therefore, interpreting the hadith as love is more appropriate than imamate, because otherwise it would imply Ali's imamate during the Prophet's lifetime—which is false. Thus the first face dismantles the proof-text by questioning its certainty, the meaning of 'mawla', and limiting its application.

Key Terms
خبر واحد
قطعي
ظني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that because a hadith is widely accepted, it must be certain proof for any claim.
Correction: Razi shows that even if a hadith is authentic, it may only yield probability unless it meets the criteria for certainty (tawatur).

Checkpoint
What is Razi's first argument against the hadith 'Man kuntu mawlahu'?
A) The hadith is fabricated.
B) It is a single narration (khabar wahid) and does not yield certainty.
C) The word 'mawla' clearly means 'master'.
D) The hadith was revealed about someone else.

Key Takeaway
Razi undercuts the proof by showing the hadith is only a probable report, insufficient for establishing a definitive doctrine like imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about religion based on a single narration, without considering whether it yields certainty or mere probability? How would you feel if you discovered it was only probable?
?
If a core conviction of yours rests on a report that is only probable, would you be willing to re-examine its foundation? What would change if you did?
?
The opponents in this lesson assumed the hadith was conclusive. Are there any beliefs you treat as beyond question that actually rely on shaky evidence? What would it cost you to honestly assess them?

The Meaning of Mawla and Its Application
Razi's subsequent arguments: the meaning of 'mawla' and its limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's arguments that even if the hadith is authentic, 'mawla' does not necessarily mean 'awla' (more deserving), and if it does, it is limited to love and respect, not imamate.

Core Idea
Even if the hadith is sound, 'mawla' does not automatically imply political authority.

Having questioned the hadith's certainty, Razi now concedes its authenticity for the sake of argument and focuses on its interpretation. The term 'mawla' is ambiguous. The opponents claim it means 'more deserving' (awla) and thus implies authority. Razi dismantles this by showing linguistic usage, the need for a specific incident, and the absurd consequence of literal interpretation. This lesson walks through these nuanced points.

Guided Path
1
Grant the hadith's authenticity
Razi says 'We grant the authenticity...' to move the argument forward. Note this hypothetical concession.
2
Challenge the meaning of 'mawla'
Razi argues that 'mawla' does not bear the meaning of 'awla' univocally. He gives linguistic examples.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic verse and the counter-argument.
3
Even if it does, not in everything
Razi then argues that even if 'mawla' means 'awla', it is only in certain matters like love and respect, not general authority.
He cites the specific incident to support this.
4
Conclude the interpretation
Interpretation as love/respect is more appropriate, else it would imply Ali's imamate during the Prophet's lifetime, which is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثانية عشرة وهي التمسك بقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم من كنت مولاه فعلي مولاء فجوابها من وجوه الأول : أنه خبر واحد . قوله : الأمة اتفقت على صحته لأن منهم من تمسك به في المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون ٤٥١ تفضيل علي، ومنهم من تمسك يه في إمامته قلنا : تدعي أن كل الأمة قبلوه قبول القطع أو قبول الظن ؟ الأول : ممنوع، وهو نفس المطلوب، والثاني: مسلم، وهو لا ينفعكم في مطلوبكم، سلمنا صحة الحديث لكن لا نسلم أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى. والاستدلال بقوله تعالى : والنَّارُ هِيَ مَولَكُمْ ﴾ [الحديد: ١٥] بمعنى هي أولى بكم معارض بما أنه لا يجوز إقامة كل واحد من هذين اللفظين مقام الآخر، فيقال : هذا أولى من ذلك، ولا يقال : هذا مولى من ذلك، ويقال : هذا مولى فلان، ولا يقال : هذا أولى فلان. وسلمنا أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى، ولكن لا نسلم أنه يجب حمل لفظ المولى في هذا الحديث على الأولى. أنه قوله : المولى مجمل والأولى يحتمل أن يكون بيانا له فوجب حمله عليه " قلنا : هذا دليل ظني فلا يقبل في القطعيات سلمنا أنه محمول على الأولى لكن لا نسلم يجب أن يكون أولى بهم في كل شيء، بل يجوز أن يكون أولى بهم في بعض الأشياء، وهو وجوب محبته وتعظيمه والقطع على سلامة باطنه، فإنه روي أنه إنما قال هذا الكلام عند منازعة جرت بين زيد وعلي، فقال علي لزيد : أنت مولاي، فقال زيد لست مولى لك، وإنما أنا مولى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال هذا الكلام عند هذه الواقعة فوجب صرف الأولوية إلى حكم هذه الواقعة، وهو أن هذه كنت أولى به في المحبة والتعظيم والقطع على سلامة الباطن فعلي أولى به به في الأحكام، ثم نقول : حمل اللفظ على ما ذكرناه أولى من حمله على الإمامة، وإلا لزم كونه إماما حال حياة محمد له و نافذ الحكم، متصرفا في الأمة، ولا شك في بطلان هذا الكلام.
As for the twelfth doubt: it is the adherence to his saying (peace be upon him): 'He whose mawla I am, Ali is his mawla.' Its answer is from several aspects. The first: it is a single narration (khabar wahid). His saying: the community agreed on its authenticity because among them are those who adhered to it in the thirty-ninth issue, page 451, preferring Ali, and among them are those who adhered to it in his imamate. We said: Do you claim that the entire community accepted it with certainty (qat') or with probability (zann)? The first is denied, and it is the very claim; the second is granted, but it does not benefit you in your claim. We grant the authenticity of the hadith, but we do not grant that the word 'mawla' bears the meaning of 'awla' (more deserving). And the inference with His saying (the Most High): 'And the Fire is your mawla' (Al-Hadid 15) meaning it is more worthy of you, is opposed by the fact that it is not permissible to substitute each of these two words for the other. It is said: 'this is awla from that', and it is not said: 'this is mawla from that'. And it is said: 'this is mawla of so-and-so', and it is not said: 'this is awla of so-and-so'. And we grant that the word 'mawla' bears the possibility of 'awla', but we do not grant that it is necessary to interpret the word 'mawla' in this hadith as 'awla'. That he said: 'mawla' is ambiguous (mujmal) and 'awla' may be an explanation for it, so it is necessary to interpret it accordingly. We said: This is a probabilistic proof (dalil zanni) and is not accepted in certain matters (qati'iyyat). We grant that it is interpreted as 'awla', but we do not grant that it must be that he is more deserving (awla) of them in everything. Rather, it is possible that he is more deserving of them in some things, which is the obligation of loving him, magnifying him, and affirming the soundness of his inner state. For it is narrated that he only said this statement at a dispute that occurred between Zayd and Ali. Ali said to Zayd: 'You are my mawla.' Zayd said: 'I am not your mawla; I am only the mawla of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him).' So he said this statement at this incident. Therefore, it is necessary to restrict the priority (awlawiyya) to the ruling of this incident, which is that you were more deserving of him in love, magnification, and affirming the soundness of the inner state. So Ali is more deserving of him in these judgments. Then we say: interpreting the word as we have mentioned is more appropriate than interpreting it as imamate. Otherwise, it would require that he be imam during the life of Muhammad (peace be upon him), executing judgments, acting with authority over the community, and there is no doubt about the falseness of this statement.

Commentary
This passage presents the first face (wajh) of Razi's refutation of the twelfth shubha, which cites the famous hadith 'He whose mawla I am, 'Ali is his mawla.' Razi lists multiple points within this face. First, he notes the hadith is a khabar wahid (single narration), which only yields probability (zann), not certainty (qat'). The opponents claim the community agreed on its authenticity, but Razi counters: by what standard—certainty or probability? If they claim certainty, it is denied; if probability, it does not prove their claim (which requires certainty). Even if the hadith is authentic, the word 'mawla' may not mean 'first' (awla). They try to prove it from the Qur'anic verse 'the Fire is your mawla' (57:15) meaning 'more worthy of you', but Razi objects that one cannot substitute 'mawla' and 'awla' in usage. Even if 'mawla' can mean 'awla', it is not necessary to interpret it that way here. When they argue that 'mawla' is ambiguous and 'awla' clarifies it, Razi replies that this reasoning is probabilistic and not acceptable in definitive matters (qati'iyyat). Razi supports this by narrating a specific incident: a dispute between Zayd and Ali where Ali called Zayd 'mawla', and Zayd denied being Ali's mawla, claiming only to be the Prophet's mawla. The Prophet then said the hadith, indicating priority in love and respect, not general authority. Therefore, interpreting the hadith as love is more appropriate than imamate, because otherwise it would imply Ali's imamate during the Prophet's lifetime—which is false. Thus the first face dismantles the proof-text by questioning its certainty, the meaning of 'mawla', and limiting its application.

The Second Interpretation: 'Mawla' as Supporter or Master
Razi interprets 'mawla' as 'nasir' (supporter) or 'sayyid' (master), emphasizing Ali's high status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi interprets the word 'mawla' in the hadith as 'supporter' or 'master' to refute the sectarian claim for imamate, and the implications of this interpretation for Ali's status.

Core Idea
The word 'mawla' means supporter or master, not political authority.

In this passage, Razi offers a second interpretation of the crucial word 'mawla' from the famous hadith. Instead of using it to claim political succession, Razi shows that it can mean 'supporter' or 'master', affirming Ali's high spiritual status without implying imamate. This interpretation undermines the sectarian argument while honoring Ali.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the translation. Notice the key terms 'مولى', 'ناصر', and 'سيد'.
The passage is short, so read it slowly.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'mawla'
Razi gives two possible interpretations: 'al-nasir' (the supporter) and 'al-sayyid' (the master). Understand how each changes the meaning of the hadith.
3
Understand the theological implication
Razi argues that this interpretation proves the certainty (qat') of Ali's inner soundness from disbelief and sin, and that only those loved by Allah love him. This is a statement of honor, not political authority.
This is the key refutation of the sectarian claim.
4
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this interpretation undermines the claim that the hadith establishes imamate. It shows that the hadith can be understood in a way consistent with Sunni doctrine.
Contrast with the first face of the refutation (not in this passage).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني من الوجهين اللذين تمسكوا بهما من هذا الخبر فجوابه : أنا نحمل لفظ المولى على الناصر والمعنى: من كنت ناصرًا له فعلي ناصر له، أو المعنى: من كنت سيدا له فعلي سيد ،له ولا شك أن هذا اللفظ يفيد التعظيم العظيم لما أنه يفيد القطع بسلامة باطن علي عن الكفر والفسق وأنه لا يحبه إلا من أحبه الله.
As for the second face of the two faces by which they adhere to this report, its answer is: we interpret the word 'mawla' as 'al-nasir' (the supporter), and the meaning is: he whose supporter I am, Ali is his supporter. Or the meaning: he whose master (sayyid) I am, Ali is his master. And there is no doubt that this word indicates great magnification because it indicates the certainty of the soundness of Ali's inner state from disbelief and sin, and that none loves him except those whom Allah loves.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents the second face (wajh) of the refutation of the twelfth shubha. Here, Razi offers an alternative interpretation of the word 'mawla' in the hadith. Instead of insisting on the meaning 'awla' (more deserving) or using it for imamate, Razi says the word can be interpreted as 'al-nasir' (the supporter) or 'al-sayyid' (the master). The meaning then becomes: 'He whose supporter I am, Ali is his supporter' or 'He whose master I am, Ali is his master.' This interpretation does not entail political authority or succession but rather affirms Ali's high status and close relationship with the Prophet. Razi further notes that this interpretation yields great veneration (ta'zim) for Ali because it affirms the certainty (qat') of the soundness of his inner state (salamat batin) from disbelief (kufr) and sin (fisq), and that only those loved by Allah love him. Thus, the hadith becomes a statement of honor and love, not a proof of imamate. The second face complements the first by providing a plausible and orthodox interpretation that undermines the sectarian claim.

Correction: Razi demonstrates that 'mawla' can also mean 'supporter' or 'master' in a non-political sense, indicating high status and love, not political authority.

Razi's Refutation: Does 'Mawla' Mean 'First'?
Razi refutes the claim that 'mawla' means 'first' implying imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's linguistic and contextual arguments against interpreting 'mawla' as 'first' to prove Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
Linguistic flexibility does not require a word to carry its rare meaning in every context.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi dismantles a key Shi'i argument for Ali's imamate based on the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu fa Ali mawlahu'. He first questions the certainty of the claim, then challenges the linguistic assumption that 'mawla' can be equated with 'awla' (first/more deserving), and finally contextualizes the hadith to limit its scope.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opponent's claim
Razi begins by questioning whether the community accepted Ali's superiority with certainty or conjecture. This sets the stage for challenging the proof.
He denies certainty and says conjecture does not suffice for a foundational doctrine like imamate.
2
Examine the linguistic argument
Razi argues that 'mawla' does not linguistically mean 'first' (awla). He cites Quranic usage and syntactic differences to show they are not interchangeable.
Note the example: 'This is awla than that' is correct, but 'this is mawla than that' is not.
3
See the contextual argument
Even if 'mawla' could mean 'first', Razi restricts its meaning to the specific occasion of the hadith—a dispute between Zayd and Ali over being the Prophet's mawla.
The context limits the 'firstness' to love and honor, not universal authority.
4
Grasp the consequence
Interpreting 'mawla' as imamate would imply Ali was imam during the Prophet's lifetime, which is absurd and historically false.
This reductio ad absurdum strengthens Razi's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون في تفضيل علي، ومنهم من تمسك به في إمامته قلنا : تدعي أن كل الأمة قبلوه قبول القطع أو قبول الظن ؟ الأول : ممنوع، وهو نفس المطلوب، والثاني: مسلم، وهو لا ينفعكم في مطلوبكم، سلمنا صحة الحديث لكن لا نسلم أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى. والاستدلال بقوله تعالى : والنَّارُ هِيَ مَولَكُمْ ﴾ [الحديد: ١٥] بمعنى هي أولى بكم معارض بما أنه لا يجوز إقامة كل واحد من هذين اللفظين مقام الآخر، فيقال : هذا أولى من ذلك، ولا يقال : هذا مولى من ذلك، ويقال : هذا مولى فلان، ولا يقال : هذا أولى فلان. وسلمنا أن لفظ المولى يحتمل الأولى، ولكن لا نسلم أنه يجب حمل لفظ المولى في هذا الحديث على الأولى. قوله : المولى مجمل والأولى يحتمل أن يكون بيانا له فوجب حمله عليه " قلنا : هذا دليل ظني فلا يقبل في القطعيات سلمنا أنه محمول على الأولى لكن لا نسلم يجب أن يكون أولى بهم في كل شيء، بل يجوز أن يكون أولى بهم في بعض الأشياء، وهو وجوب محبته وتعظيمه والقطع على سلامة باطنه، فإنه روي أنه إنما قال هذا الكلام عند منازعة جرت بين زيد وعلي، فقال علي لزيد : أنت مولاي، فقال زيد لست مولى لك، وإنما أنا مولى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم فقال هذا الكلام عند هذه الواقعة فوجب صرف الأولوية إلى حكم هذه الواقعة، وهو أن هذه كنت أولى به في المحبة والتعظيم والقطع على سلامة الباطن فعلي أولى به في الأحكام، ثم نقول : حمل اللفظ على ما ذكرناه أولى من حمله على الإمامة، وإلا لزم كونه إماما حال حياة محمد له و نافذ الحكم، متصرفا في الأمة، ولا شك في بطلان هذا الكلام.
The thirty-ninth issue concerning the preference of Ali. Among them is one who adheres to it for his imamate. We say: Do you claim that the entire community accepted him with certainty or with conjecture? The first: denied, and it is the very thing demanded; the second: conceded, but it does not avail you in your demand. We concede the authenticity of the hadith, but we do not concede that the word 'mawla' admits the meaning of 'first'. And the argument by His statement, 'the Fire is your mawla' [Quran 57:15] meaning 'it is more deserving of you' is countered by the fact that it is not permissible to substitute one of these two words for the other: it is said 'this is more deserving (awla) than that', and it is not said 'this is mawla than that'; and it is said 'this is mawla of so-and-so', and it is not said 'this is awla of so-and-so'. And we concede that the word 'mawla' may admit the meaning of 'first', but we do not concede that it is obligatory to interpret the word 'mawla' in this hadith as 'first'. His statement: 'mawla' is ambiguous and 'first' may be an explanation of it, so it must be interpreted accordingly. We say: this is a probabilistic proof, so it is not accepted in certainties. We concede that it is interpreted as 'first', but we do not concede that it must mean he is 'first' over them in everything. Rather, it is permissible that he be 'first' over them in some matters, namely the obligation of loving him, magnifying him, and affirming the soundness of his inner state. For it is narrated that he only said this statement during a dispute that occurred between Zayd and Ali. Ali said to Zayd: 'You are my mawla', and Zayd said: 'I am not your mawla; rather, I am the mawla of the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him.' So he said this statement at that incident. Thus, the 'firstness' must be directed to the ruling of that incident, namely that he was more deserving of love, magnifying, and affirming the soundness of the inner state. So Ali is more deserving of him in [these] matters. Then we say: interpreting the word as we have mentioned is more appropriate than interpreting it as imamate; otherwise, it would necessitate that he be an imam during the lifetime of Muhammad, with enforceable authority and acting over the community, and there is no doubt about the invalidity of this statement.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that the word 'mawla' in the hadith 'man kuntu mawlahu fa Ali mawlahu' means 'first' (awla), thereby implying Ali's imamate. Razi begins by questioning the opponent's claim about the community's acceptance of Ali: if they claim it was with certainty, it is denied; if with conjecture, it does not prove imamate. Then he concedes the hadith's authenticity but argues that 'mawla' does not linguistically mean 'first'. He cites Quran 57:15 where 'mawla' is interpreted as 'more deserving', but counters that one cannot substitute 'mawla' for 'awla' in Arabic usage; e.g., one says 'this is awla than that' but not 'this is mawla than that'. Even if 'mawla' could mean 'first', Razi argues that it is not obligatory in this hadith; the opponent's proof is merely probabilistic, not certain. The hadith was occasioned by a dispute between Zayd and Ali over who was the mawla of the Prophet, so the 'firstness' must be limited to love and honor, not universal authority. Interpreting the hadith as establishing imamate would imply Ali was imam during the Prophet's lifetime, which is absurd. Thus, the linguistic and contextual arguments undermine the imamate claim.

Key Terms
مولى (mawla)
أولى (awla)
إمامة (imama)
قطعي (qat'i)
ظني (zanni)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that if 'mawla' can mean 'first' in some contexts, it must mean 'first' in the hadith, ignoring linguistic restrictions and contextual specificity.
Correction: Razi demonstrates that syntactical usage and the specific occasion of the hadith prevent a generalized interpretation of imamate.

Checkpoint

B) By showing that 'mawla' and 'awla' are not interchangeable in Arabic usage.
C) By claiming the hadith is weak.
D) By accepting the meaning but denying the implication for imamate.

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that the hadith does not prove imamate because 'mawla' does not linguistically mean 'first' and the context limits its application to love and honor.

Alternative Meaning of 'Mawla' as Helper or Master
Razi offers alternative interpretations of 'mawla' and explains their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi reinterprets the word 'mawla' to mean 'helper' or 'master' rather than 'leader', and why this still honors Ali without implying political authority.

Core Idea
Razi argues that 'mawla' means helper or master, not necessarily imam.

In this passage, Razi offers a counter-interpretation of the famous hadith that some Shi'a use to support Ali's imamate. He explains that the word 'mawla' can mean 'helper' (nasir) or 'master' (sayyid), and this still conveys great honor for Ali without conferring political authority. Let's see how he defends this interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text of Razi's alternative interpretation. Try to identify the key terms for 'helper' and 'master'.
Pay attention to the structure: Razi first presents the alternative meaning, then explains its implications.
2
Identify the Two Interpretations
Razi gives two possible meanings for 'mawla': helper (nasir) and master (sayyid). Notice how each is phrased: 'Whoever I was a helper to...' and 'Whoever I was a master to...'.
These interpretations suggest a relationship of support or mastery, not necessarily political leadership.
3
Reflect on the Exaltation
Razi argues that even with these alternative meanings, the hadith still greatly honors Ali by affirming his inner purity and love by God. Consider how this refutes the claim that the hadith must imply imamate.
The honor is spiritual and moral, not political.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني من الوجهين اللذين تمسكوا بهما من هذا الخبر فجوابه: أنا نحمل لفظ المولى على الناصر والمعنى: من كنت ناصرًا له فعلي ناصر له، أو المعنى: من كنت سيدا له فعلي سيد له ولا شك أن هذا اللفظ يفيد التعظيم العظيم لما أنه يفيد القطع بسلامة باطن علي عن الكفر والفسق وأنه لا يحبه إلا من أحبه الله ورسوله وهذا يفيد أعظم المدائح وأجل المناصب. ومما يدل قطعًا على أنه ليس المراد من هذا الخبر تقرير الإمامة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كان يخاف أحدًا في تبليغ أحكام الله تعالى كما قال تعالى: ﴿يَاتُهَا الرَّسُولُ يَلْعَ ما أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] إلى قوله تعالى : هو والله يعصمك من الناس الله المائدة : ٦٧] فلو كان غرضه تقرير كونه إمامًا لذكره بلفظ صريح معلوم يعرفه كل أحد، ولما لم يذكر ذلك اللفظ الصريح علمنا أنه ليس الغرض من هذا الخبر ذكر أمر الإمامة.
As for the second aspect of the two aspects they clung to from this report, its answer is: we interpret the word 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir), meaning: 'Whoever I was a helper to, then Ali is a helper to him'; or the meaning: 'Whoever I was a master (sayyid) to, then Ali is a master to him'. There is no doubt that this word indicates great exaltation, because it indicates certainty about the soundness of Ali's inner state from disbelief and sin, and that none loves him except those whom Allah and His Messenger love. And this indicates the greatest praises and the loftiest positions. And what indicates with certainty that the intention of this report is not to establish imamate is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not fear anyone in conveying the rulings of Allah Almighty, as He said: 'O Messenger, convey what has been revealed to you from your Lord' [Quran 5:67] to His statement: 'Allah protects you from the people' [5:67]. So if his purpose was to establish his being imam, he would have mentioned it with a clear, known wording that everyone would understand. And since he did not mention that clear wording, we know that the intention of this report is not to mention the matter of imamate.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's alternative interpretation of the hadith and a decisive argument against its use for imamate. First, Razi explains that 'mawla' can mean 'helper' or 'master', implying that Ali is a helper or master to those whom the Prophet helped or was master over. This interpretation still honors Ali greatly, affirming his inner purity and love by God, but does not confer political authority. Then Razi provides a separate argument: if the Prophet had intended to appoint Ali as imam, he would have said so explicitly, because he was not afraid to convey clear commands from Allah, as the Quran affirms. Since he did not use a clear term like 'imam' or 'khalifa', it proves that imamate was not the intended meaning. This is a strong refutation based on the principle of clarity in religious commandments.

مولى (mawla)
ناصر (nasir)
سيد (sayyid)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the hadith explicitly appoints Ali as imam solely because of the word 'mawla'.
Correction: Razi shows that 'mawla' can mean helper or master, which does not confer political authority.

Key Takeaway

Explicit Mention Argument
Razi argues that the absence of explicit wording proves imamate was not intended.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that if the Prophet intended to appoint Ali as imam, he would have explicitly said so, given that he was not afraid to convey clear commands from Allah.

Core Idea
If imamate was intended, the Prophet would have said it clearly.

Razi presents a powerful principle: the Prophet was commanded to convey Allah's message without fear, and he would not have used ambiguous language for such an important matter as imamate. Since the hadith does not contain an explicit appointment of Ali as imam, it cannot be used to support imamate. Let's examine this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Begin by reading the second part of Razi's argument. Focus on the logical structure: premise, Quranic evidence, conclusion.
The Quranic verse is quoted with OCR errors; focus on the meaning.
2
Identify the Premise and Quranic Proof
Razi states that the Prophet did not fear anyone in conveying Allah's rulings, and he cites Quran 5:67 as evidence. Understand how this verse supports the premise.
The verse emphasizes that Allah protects the Prophet, so there is no need for ambiguity.
3
Analyze the Logical Conclusion
Razi concludes: if the Prophet had intended to appoint Ali as imam, he would have used clear wording (tasrih). Since he did not, the hadith cannot be about imamate. Consider whether this reasoning is sound.
This is a classic case of argument from silence, but Razi grounds it in the Prophet's fearlessness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الوجه الثاني من الوجهين اللذين تمسكوا بهما من هذا الخبر فجوابه: أنا نحمل لفظ المولى على الناصر والمعنى: من كنت ناصرًا له فعلي ناصر له، أو المعنى: من كنت سيدا له فعلي سيد له ولا شك أن هذا اللفظ يفيد التعظيم العظيم لما أنه يفيد القطع بسلامة باطن علي عن الكفر والفسق وأنه لا يحبه إلا من أحبه الله ورسوله وهذا يفيد أعظم المدائح وأجل المناصب. ومما يدل قطعًا على أنه ليس المراد من هذا الخبر تقرير الإمامة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كان يخاف أحدًا في تبليغ أحكام الله تعالى كما قال تعالى: ﴿يَاتُهَا الرَّسُولُ يَلْعَ ما أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] إلى قوله تعالى : هو والله يعصمك من الناس الله المائدة : ٦٧] فلو كان غرضه تقرير كونه إمامًا لذكره بلفظ صريح معلوم يعرفه كل أحد، ولما لم يذكر ذلك اللفظ الصريح علمنا أنه ليس الغرض من هذا الخبر ذكر أمر الإمامة.
As for the second aspect of the two aspects they clung to from this report, its answer is: we interpret the word 'mawla' as 'helper' (nasir), meaning: 'Whoever I was a helper to, then Ali is a helper to him'; or the meaning: 'Whoever I was a master (sayyid) to, then Ali is a master to him'. There is no doubt that this word indicates great exaltation, because it indicates certainty about the soundness of Ali's inner state from disbelief and sin, and that none loves him except those whom Allah and His Messenger love. And this indicates the greatest praises and the loftiest positions. And what indicates with certainty that the intention of this report is not to establish imamate is that the Prophet, peace be upon him, did not fear anyone in conveying the rulings of Allah Almighty, as He said: 'O Messenger, convey what has been revealed to you from your Lord' [Quran 5:67] to His statement: 'Allah protects you from the people' [5:67]. So if his purpose was to establish his being imam, he would have mentioned it with a clear, known wording that everyone would understand. And since he did not mention that clear wording, we know that the intention of this report is not to mention the matter of imamate.
Source pages p. 452

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's alternative interpretation of the hadith and a decisive argument against its use for imamate. First, Razi explains that 'mawla' can mean 'helper' or 'master', implying that Ali is a helper or master to those whom the Prophet helped or was master over. This interpretation still honors Ali greatly, affirming his inner purity and love by God, but does not confer political authority. Then Razi provides a separate argument: if the Prophet had intended to appoint Ali as imam, he would have said so explicitly, because he was not afraid to convey clear commands from Allah, as the Quran affirms. Since he did not use a clear term like 'imam' or 'khalifa', it proves that imamate was not the intended meaning. This is a strong refutation based on the principle of clarity in religious commandments.

Key Terms
تصريح (tasrih)
إمامة (imama)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the hadith's ambiguity leaves room for interpretation that it might appoint Ali as imam.
Correction: Razi argues that for such an important matter, the Prophet would not have been ambiguous; therefore ambiguity itself disproves the claim.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why would the Prophet have used explicit wording if he intended to appoint Ali as imam?
A) Because the Prophet was afraid of opposition.
B) Because the Prophet was commanded to convey clearly and feared no one.
C) Because the Prophet wanted to test the people.
D) Because the Prophet was required to use poetry.

Key Takeaway
The absence of explicit wording in the hadith proves that imamate was not intended, given the Prophet's fearlessness in conveying Allah's message.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are convinced of a truth, do you state it clearly and boldly like the Prophet, or do you sometimes soften or obscure your words out of fear? This lesson shows that the Prophet feared no one when conveying Allah's message. Where in your life are you hesitating to speak the truth clearly?
?
Consider a belief you hold that is based on indirect evidence or personal interpretation rather than clear revelation. Are you willing to subject it to the same standard of explicit textual proof that Razi demands for imamate? If you find no clear wording, will you have the courage to let go of that belief?
?
The Prophet used words that everyone would understand. Are there teachings of Islam that you have made complicated or ambiguous in your own mind? How can you return to the clarity of the original sources?

Refuting the Harun Analogy for Imamate
Razi refutes the Harun-Musa analogy for imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the claim that the hadith 'You are to me as Harun to Musa' proves Ali's imamate.

Core Idea
The Harun analogy fails to prove imamate because it may be temporary and limited.

Razi tackles the thirteenth doubt, which uses the famous hadith to argue for Ali's succession. He systematically dismantles the analogy by questioning its scope and implications. This passage shows how careful reasoning can overturn seemingly strong analogical arguments in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation. Notice how Razi structures his refutation step by step.
The Arabic uses terms like 'آحاد' and 'استخلاف'.
2
Identify the doubt and Razi's first point
The doubt claims that as Harun was Musa's caliph, so Ali should be Muhammad's. Razi first notes the hadith is solitary (ahad), which weakens its certainty.
3
Follow the argument on temporary deputyship
Even if authentic, Harun's deputyship may have been for a limited time, so its end is not an insult but a return to normalcy.
Razi uses logical possibility (جائز) to challenge the necessity of permanence.
4
Analyze the analogy's scope
Razi questions whether the hadith grants all positions of Harun to Ali. He suggests exceptions and limitations, undermining the universal claim.
Note terms like 'جميع المنازل' and 'الاستثناء'.
5
Reflect on the reasoning method
Consider how Razi uses distinctions (temporary vs permanent, specific vs general) to refute. This is a model of kalam argumentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة عشرة فجوابها أن هذا الخبر من باب الآحاد - على ما مر تقريره فيما تقدم - سلمنا صحته لكن لا نسلم أن هارون كان بحيث لو بقي لكان خليفة لموسى اله. قوله : لأنه استخلفه، فلو عزله كان ذلك إهانة في حق هارون، قلنا: لا نسلم، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذلك الاستخلاف كان إلى زمان معين، فانتهى ذلك الاستخلاف بانتهاء ذلك الزمان. وبالجملة : فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدليل على لزوم النقصان عند انتهاء هذا الاستخلاف بل هذا بالعكس أولى؛ لأن من كان شريكا لإنسان في منصب، يصير نائبا له وخليفة له كان ذلك يوجب نقصان حاله فإذا أزيلت تلك الخلافة زال ذلك النقصان وعاد ذلك الكمال سلمنا أن هارون كان بحيث لو عاش لكان خليفة له بعد وفاته لكن لم قلتم : إن قوله : "أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى" جميع المنازل، ودليل الاستثناء معارض بحسن الاستفهام وحسن التقسيم، وحسن إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه.
As for the thirteenth doubt, its answer is that this report is from the category of solitary (ahad) reports—as established previously. We concede its authenticity, but we do not concede that Harun was such that if he had remained, he would have been the caliph of Musa. His statement: 'Because he appointed him as deputy, so if he removed him, that would be an insult to Harun.' We say: We do not concede that. Why is it not permissible to say that that deputyship was for a specific time, so that deputyship ended with the end of that time? In summary, they are required to provide evidence for the necessity of deficiency when this deputyship ends. Rather, the opposite is more appropriate; because one who was a partner with someone in a position, then becomes his deputy and caliph, that would cause deficiency in his state. So if that caliphate is removed, that deficiency disappears and that perfection returns. We concede that Harun was such that if he had lived, he would have been his caliph after his death, but why do you say that his statement, 'You are to me as Harun was to Musa' includes all positions? And the evidence of exception is countered by the goodness of the exception, the goodness of the division, and the goodness of entering the words 'all' and 'some' upon it.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage refutes the thirteenth doubt, which argues that just as Harun was Musa's caliph, Ali should be Muhammad's caliph by analogy. Razi first notes that the hadith is solitary (ahad), thus not definitive. Even if accepted, it does not prove that Harun would have been caliph after Musa's death; his deputyship may have been temporary. Removing a temporary deputy is not an insult but a return to the original state. Furthermore, the analogy does not grant Ali all the positions of Harun; the hadith is general, but exceptions can apply. Thus, the argument fails to establish imamate conclusively.

Key Terms
آحاد
استخلاف
خليفة
منزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Harun analogy proves Ali must be imam permanently because Harun was Musa's caliph.
Correction: Razi shows the deputyship may have been temporary, and the analogy does not cover all positions; thus, it does not establish imamate.

Checkpoint
How does Razi counter the claim that removing Ali from imamate would be an insult like removing Harun?
A) By denying the hadith is authentic.
B) By saying Harun's deputyship was temporary, so its end is not an insult.
C) By arguing that Harun was never a caliph.
D) By claiming the hadith applies only to love.

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the Harun analogy by showing the hadith is not definitive, the deputyship may be temporary, and the analogy does not cover all positions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a religious argument or hadith that you later realized was not as strong as you thought? How does Razi's willingness to concede authenticity but still refute the conclusion challenge your own approach to evidence?
?
Razi argues that being a deputy implies a deficiency, so ending it restores perfection. Is there a position, role, or reputation you hold onto because you fear losing it would diminish you? What would it mean to let go of it for the sake of greater excellence?
?
The opponent assumed the analogy covered all positions (manazil). Are there areas in your faith where you have assumed a principle applies universally, only to discover exceptions? How open are you to re-examining your assumptions based on sound reasoning?

Refutation of the Tabuk Deputyship Argument
Razi refutes the claim that Ali's deputyship at Tabuk proves his imamate after the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Tabuk deputyship does not prove Ali's imamate according to Razi's refutation.

Core Idea
Temporary deputyship does not equal permanent succession.

This passage addresses a key sectarian argument for Ali's imamate from the Tabuk expedition. Razi dismantles it by pointing out the temporary nature of the appointment and offering a comparable example from the Prophet's life. Let's see how he does it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very direct rhetorical style here.
2
Identify the two refutation points
The passage presents two distinct counterarguments. Find each one and notice how Razi builds his case.
The first point is about temporality, the second is about a counterexample.
3
Reflect on the counterexample
Consider why Razi chose Abu Bakr's appointment to lead prayer. How does this parallel the Tabuk deputyship?
This strengthens his refutation by showing consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة عشرة وهي أنه اللي استخلفه في غزاة تبوك، فنقول : لم لا يجوز ان يقال : ذلك الاستخلاف كان مقدرًا بمدة ذلك السفر، فلا ذلك الاستخلاف بانقضاء تلك المدة، وأيضًا فإنه معارض باستخلاف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أبا بكر حال مرضه في الصلاة فإن أنكروا ذلك أنكرنا ذلك.
As for the fourteenth doubt, namely that he appointed him as deputy in the expedition of Tabuk, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that that deputyship was limited to the duration of that journey, so that deputyship ended with the end of that period? Also, it is countered by the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead prayer during his illness; if they deny that, we deny that.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage refutes the fourteenth doubt, which argues that the Prophet appointed Ali as his deputy during the Tabuk expedition, implying he was the general caliph. Razi counters that this appointment was temporary, specific to that journey, and thus does not prove lifelong imamate. Moreover, he points out that the Prophet also appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer during his final illness—a similar temporary deputyship. If opponents reject this analogy, they are inconsistent. Thus, the Tabuk argument fails to establish Ali's exclusive right to imamate.

Key Terms
استخلاف (istikhlaf)
غزاة تبوك (ghazwat Tabuk)
خلافة (khilafa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Tabuk deputyship proves Ali was the universal successor.
Correction: It was a temporary appointment for the expedition, not a permanent succession.

Checkpoint

B) The appointment of Umar as judge.
C) The appointment of Ali as vizier.
D) The appointment of Zayd as commander.

Key Takeaway
The Tabuk deputyship was temporary and thus does not establish Ali's universal caliphate, especially given the Prophet's similar appointment of Abu Bakr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Proof that the Hadith is not about Imamate
Al-Razi offers a decisive proof that the hadith is not about imamate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' cannot be about imamate, based on the Prophet's fearless proclamation and the absence of explicit wording.

Core Idea
If the Prophet intended to declare imamate, he would have said so explicitly.

Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a decisive proof that one of the most debated hadiths in Islamic theology—'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses'—is not about the imamate. He argues that the Prophet was fearless in conveying Allah's commands and would have used clear, explicit language if he meant to establish imamate. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the Quranic verse quoted.
2
Understand the Quranic reference
The verse from Surah al-Ma'idah (5:67) shows the Prophet was commanded to convey openly and promised protection. This establishes he had no fear.
The verse is cut off at 'wal-lahu ya'simuka min an-nas'.
3
Grasp the logical inference
If the Prophet's aim was to declare imamate, he would have used an explicit expression known to all. Since he did not, the hadith is not about imamate.
This is a straightforward argument from absence of necessary evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل قطعًا على أنه ليس المراد من هذا الخبر تقرير الإمامة أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ما كان يخاف أحدًا في تبليغ أحكام الله تعالى كما قال تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا الرَّسُولُ بَلِّغْ مَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ مِن رَّبِّكَ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] إلى قوله تعالى: ﴿وَاللَّهُ يَعْصِمُكَ مِنَ النَّاسِ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] فلو كان غرضه تقرير كونه إمامًا لذكره بلفظ صريح معلوم يعرفه كل أحد، ولما لم يذكر ذلك اللفظ الصريح علمنا أنه ليس الغرض من هذا الخبر ذكر أمر الإمامة.
And among that which indicates decisively that the intent of this report is not to establish the imamate is that the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم did not fear anyone in conveying the rulings of Allah Almighty, as Allah Almighty said: 'O Messenger, convey what has been revealed to you from your Lord' (Al-Ma'idah 5:67) up to His saying: 'And Allah will protect you from the people' (Al-Ma'idah 5:67). So if his purpose were to establish that he is an imam, he would have mentioned it with an explicit, well-known expression that everyone would understand. And since he did not mention that explicit expression, we know that the purpose of this report is not to mention the matter of imamate.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage provides a decisive proof that the hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' is not about imamate. The argument is based on the fact that the Prophet was fearless in conveying Allah's commandments, as affirmed by the Quranic verse that commands him to convey and promises divine protection. If the Prophet intended to establish his own imamate (or that of Ali), he would have used an explicit and unambiguous expression that would leave no doubt for anyone. Since he did not use such explicit wording in this hadith, it cannot be about imamate. The reasoning follows a straightforward logical inference: if the aim was to declare imamate, explicit wording would have been necessary; its absence indicates a different purpose.

Key Terms
إمامة
خبر
دليل قطعي
تصريح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith explicitly establishes imamate.
Correction: The passage shows that the Prophet did not use explicit wording, which would be required for such a declaration.

Checkpoint

A) The hadith is weak
B) The Prophet did not use explicit wording
C) Aaron was not a successor
D) The Prophet feared people

Key Takeaway
The absence of explicit wording in the hadith shows it is not about imamate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The verse 'Allah will protect you from the people' gave the Prophet fearless confidence in conveying the message. Is there a truth you know you should speak but hesitate because of fear? What would it take for you to speak with the same conviction?
?
