Refuting the Thirteenth Objection: Deputyship and Its Limits
Al-Razi refutes the thirteenth objection by questioning the parallel with Aaron.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the claim that Aaron's deputyship implies permanent succession for Ali.

Core Idea
Deputyship can be temporary; removing it may restore, not diminish, perfection.

Al-Razi addresses a key Shia argument that the Prophet's statement 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' proves Ali's succession. He dismantles this by showing that Aaron's role was limited in time and scope, and that the analogy does not necessitate all stations. This passage demonstrates the careful reasoning in Sunni theology against extrapolating from a single hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the multi-layered refutation structure.
2
Identify the refutation points
The passage contains several distinct arguments. Find each: the solitary nature of the hadith, the temporary deputyship, the rejection of the insult claim, the idea that deputyship can be a deficiency, and the limitation of the analogy.
These points progressively weaken the original objection.
3
Reflect on the logic
Ask yourself why the temporary nature of Aaron's deputyship is such a strong counter-argument. Consider how the principle of 'goodness of inquiry' etc. supports limiting the analogy.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الثالثة عشرة فجوابها أن هذا الخبر من باب الآحاد - على ما مر تقريره فيما تقدم - سلمنا صحته لكن لا نسلم أن هارون كان بحيث لو بقي لكان خليفة لموسى عليه السلام. قوله : لأنه استخلفه، فلو عزله كان ذلك إهانة في حق هارون، قلنا: لا نسلم، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال: إن ذلك الاستخلاف كان إلى زمان معين، فانتهى ذلك الاستخلاف بانتهاء ذلك الزمان. وبالجملة : فهم مطالبون بإقامة الدليل على لزوم النقصان عند انتهاء هذا الاستخلاف بل هذا بالعكس أولى ؛ لأن من كان شريكا لإنسان في منصب، يصير نائبا له وخليفة له كان ذلك يوجب نقصان حاله فإذا أزيلت تلك الخلافة زال ذلك النقصان وعاد ذلك الكمال سلمنا أن هارون كان بحيث لو عاش لكان خليفة له بعد وفاته لكن لم قلتم : إن قوله : "أنت مني بمنزلة هارون من موسى" جميع المنازل، ودليل الاستثناء معارض بحسن الاستفهام وحسن التقسيم، وحسن إدخال لفظي الكل والبعض عليه. يتناول [؟] جرم [؟] انتهى
As for the thirteenth objection, its answer is that this report is from the category of solitary (āḥād) reports—according to what was established previously—and even if we grant its authenticity, we do not concede that Aaron was such that had he remained alive he would have been the successor (khalīfa) of Moses عليه السلام. His statement: 'Because he appointed him as deputy, and if he removed him that would be an insult to Aaron,' we say: We do not concede. Why is it not permissible to say that that appointment was for a specific time, so that appointment came to an end with the end of that time? In summary: they are required to provide proof for the necessity of deficiency at the end of this appointment. Rather, the opposite is more fitting: because one who is a partner with a person in an office and becomes his deputy and successor—that would cause deficiency in his state. So when that deputyship is removed, that deficiency is removed and perfection returns. Even if we concede that Aaron was such that had he lived he would have been his successor after his death, why do you claim that his statement 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' encompasses all stations? And the proof of exception is opposed by the goodness of inquiry, the goodness of division, and the goodness of inserting the words 'all' and 'some' upon it. It encompasses [؟] jarama [؟] end.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the thirteenth objection. The objection claimed that the hadith implies that Ali is like Aaron to Moses, and since Aaron was the successor of Moses, Ali must be the successor of the Prophet. The answer has several parts: (1) The hadith is solitary (āḥād), which may not be sufficient for such a claim. (2) Even if it is authentic, we do not concede that Aaron was destined to be Moses' successor if he outlived him. The appointment of Aaron was for a limited time, as when Moses went to Mount Sinai. So it does not prove perpetual succession. (3) The opponent's argument that removing a deputy would be an insult is rejected; it is possible for a deputyship to be temporary. (4) On the contrary, being a deputy might be a deficiency, and its removal restores perfection. (5) Finally, even if we accept that Aaron would have succeeded Moses, the hadith does not necessarily encompass all stations—the parallel may be limited. The author points to principles like the 'goodness of inquiry' etc. The passage ends with unclear text.

Key Terms
شبهة
خبر الآحاد
استخلاف
خليفة
نقصان
كمال
منازل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deputyship always implies permanent succession.
Correction: The passage shows that deputyship can be for a limited time, and its removal can restore perfection rather than cause deficiency.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the 13th objection, what is a possible interpretation of Aaron's deputyship that undermines the argument for Ali's succession?
A) It was for a limited time
B) Aaron was not actually a deputy
C) The hadith is fabricated
D) Moses outlived Aaron

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'You are to me as Aaron was to Moses' does not prove succession because Aaron's role was time-limited and the analogy may not extend to all states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever clung to a beloved tradition or saying, refusing to examine its authenticity or context, because it supported a conviction you hold dear? What would it cost you to honestly reexamine that evidence today?
?
If a cherished belief about a righteous figure was shown to be based on a time-limited role, would you accept it, or would you rationalize to preserve your emotional attachment? What does that reveal about your trust in Allah's plan versus your own preferences?
?
When you encounter a proof that challenges your deeply held assumptions, do you immediately look for counter-arguments, or do you pause and ask: 'What if I have been wrong? Am I seeking truth or vindication? What would it mean for my life if this objection is correct?'

Refutation of the Tabuk Appointment Objection
Al-Razi refutes the fourteenth objection regarding the Tabuk appointment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Razi refutes the claim that the Prophet's appointment of Ali during Tabuk proves Ali's right to the caliphate.

Core Idea
Temporary deputyship does not imply permanent succession.

This lesson examines an argument used to support a specific claim about succession. Al-Razi presents two concise but powerful rebuttals: one rooted in the limited duration of the appointment, and another that counterbalances it with an even more significant appointment of Abu Bakr. The passage demonstrates how theological reasoning uses historical evidence and logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that the Prophet's appointment of Ali as his deputy during the Tabuk expedition indicates that Ali was meant to be the successor.
This is the fourteenth of several objections al-Razi addresses.
2
First refutation: temporary appointment
Al-Razi argues that the deputyship was limited to the duration of the journey. Once the Prophet returned, the appointment ended. Therefore, it does not imply permanent succession.
3
Second refutation: counterexample
Al-Razi points out that the Prophet also appointed Abu Bakr to lead prayer during his final illness. This is a stronger indication of succession, and if the opponent accepts one appointment, they must accept the other for consistency.
This is a standard argumentative technique called mu‘āraḍa (countering).
4
Evaluate the logical consistency
Consider whether the opponent can consistently accept one appointment while rejecting the other. Al-Razi suggests inconsistency would expose their bias.
This shows the importance of internal coherence in theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الرابعة عشرة وهي أنه الذي استخلفه في غزوة تبوك، فنقول : لم لا يجوز ان يقال : ذلك الاستخلاف كان مقدرًا بمدة ذلك السفر، فلا ذلك الاستخلاف بانقضاء تلك المدة، وأيضًا فإنه معارض باستخلاف النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم أبا بكر حال مرضه في الصلاة فإن أنكروا ذلك أنكرنا ذلك.
As for the fourteenth objection, which is that he appointed him as his deputy in the expedition of Tabuk, we say: Why is it not permissible to say that that appointment was limited to the duration of that journey, so that appointment ended with the termination of that period? Moreover, it is countered by the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم appointing Abu Bakr as his deputy during his illness in prayer. If they deny that, we deny that.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This is a concise refutation of the objection that the Prophet's appointment of Ali as his deputy during the Tabuk expedition proves his right to the caliphate. The answer is two-fold: (1) That appointment was temporary, for the duration of the Prophet's absence. Thus it does not imply permanent succession. (2) This action is countered by the Prophet's appointment of Abu Bakr to lead the prayer during his final illness, which is a stronger indication of succession. If the opponent rejects the latter, they are being inconsistent, because they accept a similar appointment for Ali.

Key Terms
استخلاف
غزوة تبوك
معارضة
صلاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's appointment of Ali during Tabuk proves his right to the caliphate.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that appointment was temporary and is counterbalanced by a similar appointment of Abu Bakr, so it does not establish permanent succession.

Checkpoint
How does al-Razi counter the argument from the Prophet's appointment of Ali during the Tabuk expedition?
A) He denies it happened
B) He claims it was for a limited time and also points to Abu Bakr's appointment in prayer
C) He says Ali refused the appointment
D) He says the expedition did not happen

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's appointment of Ali in Tabuk was temporary and is countered by his appointment of Abu Bakr in prayer, showing that such deputyships do not establish permanent succession.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a historical event that seems to confirm a cherished belief, do you ever question whether your interpretation is the only plausible one? What might you lose by not questioning it?
?
Al-Razi uses a counterexample to challenge a claim. Is there a belief or attitude you hold that you have never honestly tested with a contrary example? What would it take for you to consider that counterexample seriously?
?
The temporary deputyship ended when its purpose was fulfilled. Are there roles, duties, or positions in your life that you treat as permanent when they were actually meant to be temporary? What is holding you back from releasing them?

Refutation of the Fifteenth Objection on Aspersions
Al-Razi refutes the fifteenth objection that raises aspersions against the first three caliphs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why probable criticisms cannot overturn certain proofs in kalam methodology.

Core Idea
Certain proofs always outweigh probable doubts.

Al-Razi here addresses a common sectarian objection that raises aspersions against the first three caliphs. His response is straightforward and methodologically important: the evidence for Abu Bakr's imamate is certain (yaqīniyya), while the criticisms are merely probable (muhtamala). In Islamic theology, probable claims cannot stand against certain proofs. This lesson explores that principle and its application.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Al-Razi frames the objection and then gives a concise answer.
2
Identify the key terms
Look for the terms 'دلائل يقينية' (certain proofs) and 'محتمل' (probable). Understand their meanings in context.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Break down the answer into its components: what is the basis of the objection, how does Al-Razi counter it, and why is the counter sufficient?
The guided breakdown below will help.
4
Reflect on the methodological principle
Consider why certain evidence is prioritized over probable evidence in kalam. How does this principle protect foundational beliefs?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشبهة الخامسة عشرة : وهي التمسك بالمطاعن في أبي بكر وعمر المسألة التاسعة والثلاثون ٤٥٣ وعثمان فجوابها أن ما ذكرناه من الدلائل على إمامة أبي بكر ها دلائل يقينية، وما ذكرتموه من المطاعن محتمل، والمحتمل لا يعارض اليقين والاستقصاء في تلك التفاصيل لا يليق بهذا المحتصر، وبالله التوفيق.
As for the fifteenth objection, which is holding on to the aspersions against Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman, its answer is that what we have mentioned of the proofs for the imamate of Abu Bakr are certain proofs, while what you have mentioned of aspersions are probable. And the probable does not oppose the certain. And exhaustive detail on those points is not fitting for this compendium. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage answers the objection that raises criticisms against the first three caliphs. The author asserts that the proofs for Abu Bakr's imamate are certain (yaqīniyya), while the alleged criticisms are merely probable (muhtamal). In kalam methodology, probable evidence cannot overturn certain evidence. Therefore, the objections are insufficient. The author also notes that a full discussion of these details is beyond the scope of this concise work.

Key Terms
شبهة
مطاعن
دلائل يقينية
محتمل
يقين
معارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any criticism of a companion automatically invalidates their caliphate.
Correction: The passage clarifies that probable claims do not defeat certain proofs; only decisive evidence can overturn established certainties.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the 15th objection, why do the aspersions against Abu Bakr, Umar, and Uthman not invalidate their imamate?
A) The aspersions are false
B) The proofs for Abu Bakr's imamate are certain and not opposed by probable claims
C) The companions are infallible
D) The aspersions are too many to address

Key Takeaway
In kalam methodology, certain proofs (yaqīniyya) cannot be overturned by merely probable (muhtamal) objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a vague criticism of a person you know to be righteous, do you instinctively give it weight? What would it take for you to let their established good deeds outweigh the unproven doubt?
?
The passage teaches that probable aspersions cannot shake certain truths. Are there any clear truths about Allah’s mercy or justice that you allow minor doubts to undermine? What is one doubt you must consciously set aside today?
?
If the evidence for Abu Bakr’s caliphate is certain in the sight of Ahl al-Sunna, yet your heart still hesitates, what does that reveal about your attachment to certainty? Will you submit to the weight of the proofs, or continue to entertain baseless objections?

Certainty Prevails: Certain Proofs Over Probable Objections
Razi concludes his argument by asserting the certain nature of his proofs and the probable nature of objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle that certain (yaqini) proofs outweigh probable (muhtamal) objections, as applied to the debate over Abu Bakr's caliphate.

Core Idea
Certain proofs silence probable objections.

Imam Razi brings his argument on Abu Bakr's imamate to a close by invoking a fundamental principle of reasoning: when certain proofs exist, probable objections carry no weight. In this brief concluding passage, he explains why the detailed refutation of every objection is unnecessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the two key phrases: 'دلائل يقينية' (certain proofs) and 'مطاعن محتملة' (probable objections). Understand how Razi contrasts them.
The certainty of the proofs is the foundation of his argument.
3
Understand the logical maxim
The core principle: 'المحتمل لا يعارض اليقين' — the probable does not oppose the certain. Reflect on why this is a sound rational principle.
This principle is widely used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.
4
Note the author's reason for brevity
Razi declines to address objections in detail because 'الاستقصاء في تلك التفاصيل لا يليق بهذا المختصر'. Consider the implications for scholarly writing.
This shows the author's awareness of scope and purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعثمان فجوابها أن ما ذكرناه من الدلائل على إمامة أبي بكر هي دلائل يقينية، وما ذكرتموه من المطاعن محتمل، والمحتمل لا يعارض اليقين والاستقصاء في تلك التفاصيل لا يليق بهذا المختصر، وبالله التوفيق.
And regarding Uthman, the answer to it is that what we have mentioned of proofs for the imamate of Abu Bakr are certain proofs, and what you have mentioned of objections are probable, and the probable does not oppose the certain, and detailed investigation of those details is not befitting for this compendium, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 454

Commentary
This passage is a concluding statement in an argument. It asserts that the proofs for Abu Bakr's caliphate are certain (يقينية), while the objections raised are merely probable (محتمل). The author then states a logical principle: a probable argument cannot override a certain one. He also notes that delving into all those details is inappropriate for this concise work.

Key Terms
دلائل يقينية
محتمل
يقين
المختصر
مطاعن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Razi dismisses objections arbitrarily or without consideration.
Correction: Razi's dismissal is based on a rational principle: certain knowledge takes precedence over probable speculation. He is not being dismissive but applying a foundational rule of reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the relationship between the proofs for Abu Bakr's imamate and the objections raised?
A) Both are certain.
B) The proofs are certain and the objections are probable.
C) The objections are more certain.
D) Both are probable.

Key Takeaway
Certain (yaqini) proofs cannot be overridden by probable (muhtamal) objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a clear command from Allah (such as prayer, hijab, or truthfulness) that you know with certainty is true. What probable objection or doubt have you allowed to weaken your commitment to it? Will you choose today to silence that doubt with the weight of certainty?
?
When was the last time you acted on a probable assumption rather than a certain ruling, and what was the result? What will you do differently to ensure that certain knowledge takes precedence over speculation in your daily life?
?
The author used a concise logical principle to dismiss objections. In your life, where have you entertained prolonged doubts about matters of faith that could have been resolved by simply recalling the principle that certainty is not removed by doubt? Write down one specific step you will take this week to reaffirm a certain truth over a lingering probable doubt.

The Best Person After the Prophet: Positions of Ash'aris and Shia
Chapter opening classifying positions on the best person after the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different positions on who is the best person after the Prophet, focusing on the Ash'ari and Shia views and the alignment of early and later Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
The debate over the best person after the Prophet divides Sunni and Shia perspectives.

The question of who is the best person after the Prophet Muhammad is a significant theological and historical issue. This passage presents the positions of the Ash'ari school (and early Mu'tazila) favoring Abu Bakr, and the Shia (and later Mu'tazila) favoring Ali. Understanding these positions sets the stage for the arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how it classifies the positions.
2
Identify the two main positions
Identify the Ash'ari position (Abu Bakr) and the Shia position (Ali). Also note the alignment of early Mu'tazila with Ash'aris and later Mu'tazila with Shia.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this question matters for theology and sectarian identity. The passage only states positions; the proofs are referenced but not given.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الخامس: في بيان أن أفضل الناس بعد رسول الله من هو ؟ مذهب أصحابنا : أن أفضل الناس بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هو أبو بكر قول قدماء المعتزلة. وهو مذهب الشيعة أنه علي بن أبي طالب الله له ناو وهو قول أكثر المتأخرين من المعتزلة. أما أصحابنا : فقد تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: وَسَيُجَنَّبُهَا الْأَنْقَى [الليل : ١٧] وبقوله والله ما طلعت شمس ولا غربت على أحد بعد النبيين والمرسلين أفضل من أبي بكر وكل ذلك قد مضى تقريره في الفصل المتقدم.
Chapter Five: Concerning the clarification of who the best of people after the Messenger of Allah is. The doctrine of our companions (Ash'aris) is that the best of people after the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, is Abu Bakr, the saying of the early Mu'tazila. And the doctrine of the Shia is that it is Ali ibn Abi Talib [garbled] and it is the saying of most of the later Mu'tazila. As for our companions, they have adhered to His statement, the Most High: 'And the most righteous will be kept away from it' [al-Layl: 17], and to His saying: 'By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set on anyone after the prophets and messengers better than Abu Bakr.' And all of that has already been established in the preceding chapter.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter classifying the positions on the question of the best person after the Prophet. It states the Ash'ari position (Abu Bakr is best), which is also the position of the early Mu'tazila. Then it states the Shia position (Ali is best), which is also that of most later Mu'tazila. It briefly references the Ash'ari proofs—a verse from al-Layl and a prophetic saying—that were discussed previously.

Key Terms
أفضل الناس
مذهب أصحابنا
قدماء المعتزلة
الشيعة
المتأخرين من المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agree with the Shia that Ali is the best after the Prophet.
Correction: The early Mu'tazila actually agreed with the Ash'aris that Abu Bakr is best; only later Mu'tazila sided with the Shia.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which group agreed with the Ash'aris on the superiority of Abu Bakr?
A) The Shia
B) The early Mu'tazila
C) The later Mu'tazila
D) None

Key Takeaway
The early Mu'tazila agreed with Ash'aris that Abu Bakr is best, while later Mu'tazila aligned with the Shia view of Ali's superiority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that some theological positions shifted over time (early vs. later Mu'tazila). Have you ever changed a core religious belief based on who you followed? Are you willing to re-examine your own views if evidence points otherwise, even if it costs you social approval?
?
The passage implies that the question of who is best after the Prophet is a matter of doctrinal identity. If someone held a different view, how would you treat them? Can you separate their error from their person? Would you still love them as a brother in faith?
?
Knowing that early Mu'tazila agreed with Ash'aris on Abu Bakr's superiority, does it surprise you that later Mu'tazila changed? What does this tell you about how theological schools evolve? Are you blindly following your school's later position, or have you checked the original evidence for yourself?

Shia Proof from the Mubahala Verse
Shia argument from the Mubahala verse for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shia interpret the Mubahala verse to argue for Ali's equality with Prophet Muhammad except in prophethood.

Core Idea
Equality except in prophethood makes Ali the best of creation.

The Shia employ a distinctive reading of the famous Mubahala verse. By taking 'ourselves' to refer to Ali, they build a case for his superiority over all other Companions. This lesson unpacks their logical steps and shows how they reach their conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation once to grasp the overall flow of the Shia reasoning.
2
Identify the key premise
Notice how they ground their argument in the Mubahala verse and the interpretation that 'ourselves' means Ali.
They rely on authentic reports for that identification.
3
Follow the logical deduction
Trace the reasoning from equality of selves (excluding literal identity) to equality in virtues, except prophethood.
The exception for prophethood is crucial.
4
Examine the conclusion
Understand why they conclude Ali is the best of creation: the equal of the best must be the best.
This conclusion depends on the premise that Muhammad is the best.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشيعة فقد احتجوا على أن عليّا أفضل الصحابة بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَقُلْ تَعَالَوْا نَدْعُ أَبْنَاءَنَا وَأَبْنَاءَكُمْ وَنِسَاءَنَا وَنِسَاءَكُمْ وَأَنفُسَنَا وَأَنفُسَكُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦١] وثبت بالأخبار الصحيحة أن المراد من قوله : وَأَنفُسَنَا هو علي، ومن المعلوم أنه يمتنع أن تكون نفس علي هي نفس محمد بعينه فلا بد وأن يكون المراد هو المساواة بين النفسين، وهذا يقتضي أن كل ما حصل لمحمد من الفضائل والمناقب فقد حصل مثله لعلي. ترك العمل بهذا في فضيلة النبوة، فوجب أن تحصل المساواة بينهما فيما وراء هذه الصفة، ثم لا شك أن محمدا لله كان أفضل الخلق في سائر الفضائل، ولما كان علي مساويًا في تلك الفضائل وجب أن يكون أفضل الخلق؛ لأن المساوي للأفضل يجب أن يكون أفضل.

الحجة الثانية : التمسك بخبر الطير، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «اللهم ائتني بأحب الخلق إليك يأكل هذا الطير معي والمحبة من الله تعالى عبارة عن كثرة الثواب والتعظيم.
As for the Shia, they have argued that Ali is the best of the Companions with proofs: The first proof: adherence to His statement, the Most High: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves' [Aal Imran: 61]. And it is established by authentic reports that what is meant by His saying 'and ourselves' is Ali. And it is known that it is impossible that the self of Ali is exactly the same self as Muhammad, so it must necessarily mean that there is equality between the two selves. This requires that every excellence and virtue that Muhammad obtained, the like of it obtained for Ali. The application of this is abandoned in the virtue of prophethood, so equality must hold between them in what is beyond this attribute. Then there is no doubt that Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was the best of creation in all excellences, and since Ali was equal in those excellences, he must be the best of creation; because the equal of the best must be the best.

The second proof: adherence to the hadith of the bird, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, bring to me the most beloved of creation to You so that he may eat this bird with me.' And love from Allah, the Most High, is an expression of abundant reward and honor.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage presents two Shia arguments for Ali's superiority. The first uses the Mubahala verse: since 'ourselves' refers to Ali and cannot mean literal identity, it implies equality in excellence except for prophethood. Thus, as Muhammad is the best of creation, his equal (Ali) must also be the best. The second proof cites the hadith where the Prophet prayed for the most beloved person to eat a bird with him, and Ali came. The author notes that divine love signifies abundant reward and honor, implying Ali's high rank.

Key Terms
الحجة الأولى
آية المباهلة
النفسين
المساواة
فضيلة النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the Shia claim Ali is equal to Muhammad in every respect, including prophethood.
Correction: The argument explicitly makes an exception for prophethood, so equality applies only to other excellences.

Checkpoint
According to the first Shia proof, what is the relationship between Ali and the Prophet Muhammad in terms of virtues?
A) Ali has some virtues of Muhammad.
B) Ali is equal to Muhammad in all virtues except prophethood.
C) Ali is superior to Muhammad.
D) Ali has no virtues.

Key Takeaway
The Shia use the Mubahala verse to argue that Ali must be the best of creation because he is equal to Prophet Muhammad in all virtues except prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When presented with a proof from the Quran that challenges your understanding of early Islamic history, do you courageously examine its merits or defensively reject it? Commit today to studying such arguments with an open heart seeking truth.
?
The argument makes an exception for prophethood. Is there an area in your own faith where you hold a rigid position that might need an exception in light of clear evidence? Be honest: are you willing to adjust your beliefs?
?
If you were convinced by this argument that Ali is the best of creation, would that change how you view other Companions? What does your emotional reaction reveal about your attachment to certain figures?

Shia Proof from the Hadith of the Bird
Shia proof from the hadith of the bird for Ali's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Shia cite the hadith of the bird to argue for Ali's superior rank before Allah.

Core Idea
The Prophet prayed for the most beloved to Allah, and Ali came.

The second Shia proof relies on a well-known hadith where the Prophet asks Allah to send the most beloved person to eat with him. The argument is that Ali's arrival demonstrates his being the most beloved, which implies great reward and honor. This lesson examines the hadith and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith and its interpretation
Read the Arabic text and translation. Notice the wording of the Prophet's prayer and the response.
The hadith is cited without a full chain, but the Shia consider it authentic.
2
Understand the key concept: divine love
The passage explains that Allah's love means abundant reward and honor. This links the hadith to Ali's high status.
3
Reflect on the argument's strength
Consider how this hadith, if accepted, directly supports Ali being the most beloved, hence the best after the Prophet.
Sunnis might question the authenticity or interpretation.

Source Text

As for the Shia, they have argued that Ali is the best of the Companions with proofs: The first proof: adherence to His statement, the Most High: 'Say: Come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves' [Aal Imran: 61]. And it is known that it is impossible that the self of Ali is exactly the same self as Muhammad, so it must necessarily mean that there is equality between the two selves. This requires that every excellence and virtue that Muhammad obtained, the like of it obtained for Ali. The application of this is abandoned in the virtue of prophethood, so equality must hold between them in what is beyond this attribute. Then there is no doubt that Muhammad, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, was the best of creation in all excellences, and since Ali was equal in those excellences, he must be the best of creation; because the equal of the best must be the best.

The second proof: adherence to the hadith of the bird, which is his saying, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, bring to me the most beloved of creation to You so that he may eat this bird with me.' And love from Allah, the Most High, is an expression of abundant reward and honor.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage presents two Shia arguments for Ali's superiority. The first uses the Mubahala verse: since 'ourselves' refers to Ali and cannot mean literal identity, it implies equality in excellence except for prophethood. Thus, as Muhammad is the best of creation, his equal (Ali) must also be the best. The second proof cites the hadith where the Prophet prayed for the most beloved person to eat a bird with him, and Ali came. The author notes that divine love signifies abundant reward and honor, implying Ali's high rank.

Key Terms
خبر الطير
المحبة
الثواب

Key Takeaway
The Shia use the hadith of the bird to argue that Ali is the most beloved of creation to Allah, signifying his superior rank and abundant reward.

Shi'a Proofs for Ali's Superiority: Mubahala and the Hadith of the Bird
Imam al-Razi presents two complete Shi'a proofs for Ali's superiority over Abu Bakr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first two Shi'a arguments from the Qur'an and hadith that claim Ali's superiority over Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The Shi'a argue that Ali is equal to Muhammad in all virtues except prophethood, making him the best.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents two complete Shi'a proofs for Ali's superiority. The first uses the Mubahala verse to establish equality between Ali and the Prophet, while the second relies on a hadith about a bird. Understanding these arguments is essential for appreciating the sectarian debate on the best companion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source for the first two proofs, then their translations. Focus on the logic of each argument.
The third proof is incomplete and omitted here.
2
Understand the Mubahala argument
Notice how the Shi'a interpret 'ourselves' as Ali and derive equality. Why can't it be literal identity?
They conclude Ali shares all virtues except prophethood.
3
Understand the Hadith of the bird
See how 'most beloved' is linked to superiority. What does love from Allah imply?
It implies abundant reward and honor.
4
Compare with Ash'ari position
The passage states the Ash'ari view that Abu Bakr is best. Contrast the two positions.
This sets up the sectarian debate.

الفصل الخامس: في بيان أن أفضل الناس بعد رسول الله من هو؟ مذهب أصحابنا : أن أفضل الناس بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هو أبو بكر قول قدماء المعتزلة. و مذهب الشيعة أنه علي بن أبي طالب الله له ناو وهو قول أكثر المتأخرين من المعتزلة. أما أصحابنا : فقد تمسكوا بقوله تعالى: وَسَيُجَنَّبُهَا الْأَنْقَى [الليل : ١٧] وبقوله والله ما طلعت شمس ولا غربت على أحد بعد النبيين والمرسلين أفضل من أبي بكر وكل ذلك قد مضى تقريره في الفصل المتقدم. وأما الشيعة فقد احتجوا على أن عليّا أفضل الصحابة بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : التمسك بقوله تعالى: ﴿ فَقُلْ تَعَالَوْا نَدْعُ أَبْنَاءَنَا وَأَبْنَاءَكُمْ وَنِسَاءَ نَا وَنِسَاءَكُمْ وَأَنفُسَنَا وَأَنفُسَكُمْ ﴾ [آل عمران: ٦١] وثبت بالأخبار الصحيحة أن المراد من قوله : وَأَنفُسَنَا هو علي، ومن المعلوم أنه يمتنع أن تكون نفس علي هي نفس محمد بعينه فلا بد وأن يكون المراد هو المساواة بين النفسين، وهذا يقتضي أن كل ما حصل لمحمد من الفضائل والمناقب فقد حصل مثله لعلي. ترك العمل بهذا في فضيلة النبوة، فوجب أن تحصل المساواة بينهما فيما وراء هذه الصفة، ثم لا شك أن محمدا لله كان أفضل الخلق في سائر الفضائل، ولما كان علي مساويًا في تلك الفضائل وجب أن يكون أفضل الخلق؛ لأن المساوي للأفضل يجب أن يكون أفضل. الحجة الثانية : التمسك بخبر الطير، وهو قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «اللهم ائتني بأحب الخلق إليك يأكل هذا الطير معي والمحبة من الله تعالى عبارة عن كثرة الثواب والتعظيم. الحجة الثالثة : أن عليها نوعية الهيلينا كان أعلم الصحابة، والأعلم أفضل. إنما قلنا : إنه كان أعلم الصحابة للإجمال ،والتفصيل أما الإجمال فهو أنه لا نزاع أن عليا كان في أصل الخلقة في غاية الذكاء والفطنة والاستعداد للعلم، وكان محمد الله أفضل العقلاء وأعلم العلماء، وكان على في غاية الحرص في طلب العلم، وكان محمد الله في غاية الحرص في تربية علي ووفي إرشاده إلى اكتساب الفضائل. ثم إن عليا هنا نشأ من أول صغره في حجر محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم ، وفي كبره صار ختنا له ، وكان يدخل عليه في كل الأوقات، ومن المعلوم أن التلميذ إذا كان في غاية الذكاء والحرص على النقل، وكان الأستاذ في غاية الفضل وفي غاية الحرص على التعليم ثم إن اتفق لمثل هذا التلميذ ان اتصل بخدمة هذا الأستاذ من زمان الصغر، وكان ذلك الاتصال بخدمته حاصلا في كل الأوقات فإنه يبلغ ذلك التلميذ في العلم مبلغا عظيمًا. وهذا بيان إجمالي في أن عليا كان أعلم الصحابة، وأما أبو بكر فإنه اتصل بخدمته في زمان الكبر وأيضًا ما كان يصل إلى خدمته في اليوم والليلة إلا زمانا يسيرًا، أما علي فإنه اتصل بخدمته في زمان الصغر، وقد قيل: " العلم في الصغر كالنقش في الحجر، والعلم في الكبر كالنقش في المدر" فثبت بما ذكرنا أن عليا أعلم من أبي بكر. وأما التفصيل فيدل على ذلك وجوه : الأول : قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «أقضاكم علي» والقضاء محتاج إلى جميع أنواع العلوم، فلما رجحه على الكل في القضاء لزم أنه رجحه عليهم في جميع العلوم، وأما سائر الصحابة فقد كل واحد منهم على غيره في علم واحد كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «أفرضكم رجع زيد بن ثابت، وأقرأكم أبي. والثاني: أن أكثر المفسرين سلموا أن قوله تعالى : وعيها أُذُنٌ وَعِيةٌ الحاقة : ۱۲] نزل في حق علي الها وتخصيصه بزيادة الفهم يدل على اختصاصه بمزيد العلم. الثالث : روي أن عمر ا أمر برجم امرأة ولدت لستة أشهر فنبهه علي
The fifth chapter: On explaining who is the best of people after the Messenger of Allah. The doctrine of our companions (Ash'aris) is that the best of people after the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, is Abu Bakr; the doctrine of the early Mu'tazila is [also that?]. And the doctrine of the Shia is that he is Ali ibn Abi Talib, may Allah be pleased with him, and it is the doctrine of most of the later Mu'tazila. As for our companions, they have adhered to His statement, the Exalted: 'And the most righteous shall be kept away from it' [Al-Layl:17] and to his statement: 'By Allah, the sun has not risen nor set upon anyone after the prophets and messengers better than Abu Bakr,' and all that has been discussed previously in the preceding chapter. As for the Shia, they have argued that Ali is the best of the companions through aspects: The first proof: Adherence to His statement, the Exalted: 'Say, come, let us call our sons and your sons, our women and your women, and ourselves and yourselves' [Aal Imran:61]; and it has been established through authentic reports that the intended meaning of His statement 'and ourselves' is Ali. And it is known that it is impossible that the self of Ali is the very self of Muhammad, so the intended meaning must be equality between the two selves. And this requires that every virtue and merit that Muhammad obtained, its like has been obtained for Ali. This is set aside in the virtue of prophethood, so equality between them must obtain in what is beyond this attribute. Then there is no doubt that Muhammad, peace be upon him, was the best of creation in all other virtues, and since Ali was equal to him in those virtues, he must be the best of creation; because the one equal to the best must be best. The second proof: Adherence to the hadith of the bird, which is his statement, peace be upon him: 'O Allah, bring to me the most beloved of creation to You to eat this bird with me,' and love from Allah, the Exalted, is an expression of abundant reward and glorification. The third proof: That Ali, may Allah be pleased with him, was the most knowledgeable of the companions, and the most knowledgeable is best. We say: He was the most knowledgeable of the companions in general and in detail. As for the general, it is that there is no dispute that Ali was, in his original disposition, at the utmost intelligence, acumen, and readiness for knowledge; and Muhammad, peace be upon him, was the best of the rational and the most knowledgeable of scholars; and Ali was at the utmost eagerness in seeking knowledge; and Muhammad, peace be upon him, was at the utmost eagerness in raising Ali and guiding him to acquiring virtues. Then Ali grew up from his earliest childhood in the lap of Muhammad, peace be upon him; and in his adulthood he became his son-in-law; and he used to enter upon him at all times. And it is known that if the student is at the utmost intelligence and eagerness for transmission, and the teacher is at the utmost excellence and at the utmost eagerness for teaching, then if such a student happens to be continuously in the service of this teacher from the time of childhood, and that connection with his service occurs at all times, then that student will reach a great level in knowledge. This is a general demonstration that Ali was the most knowledgeable of the companions. As for Abu Bakr, he connected to his service in the time of adulthood, and also he did not have access to his service day and night except for a little time. As for Ali, he connected to his service in the time of childhood; and it has been said: 'Knowledge in childhood is like engraving on stone, and knowledge in adulthood is like engraving on mud.' So it is established by what we mentioned that Ali is more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr. As for the detail, aspects indicate that: The first: His statement, peace be upon him: 'The most knowledgeable among you in judicial matters is Ali.' And judicial matters require all types of knowledge. So when he gave him preference over all in judicial matters, it follows that he gave him preference over them in all sciences. As for the other companions, each one of them was distinguished over others in one science, as his statement, peace be upon him: 'The most knowledgeable among you in inheritance law is Zaid ibn Thabit, and the best reciter among you is Ubayy.' The second: That most of the commentators conceded that His statement, the Exalted: 'And an ear that hears' [Al-Haqqa:12] was revealed concerning Ali, and singling him out with increased understanding indicates his special possession of more knowledge. The third: It was narrated that 'Umar ordered the stoning of a woman who gave birth after six months, and Ali alerted him.
Source pages p. 455

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the Shi'a arguments for the superiority of Ali ibn Abi Talib over Abu Bakr as-Siddiq. The author first states the positions: the Ash'aris (our companions) hold Abu Bakr as best, the early Mu'tazila also, while the Shi'a and later Mu'tazila hold Ali. Then he goes through three proofs from the Shi'a.

The first proof is from the Mubahala verse (Aal Imran 61). The Shi'a argue that 'ourselves' in the verse refers to Ali, and since it cannot mean literal identity, it means equality between Muhammad and Ali. So Ali shares all of Muhammad's virtues except prophethood, making Ali equal to Muhammad in other virtues, thus he is the best of creation after him.

The second proof is the hadith of the bird, where the Prophet asked Allah to bring the most beloved creation to eat with him, and it was Ali. Love from Allah means abundant reward, so Ali is the most beloved, hence best.

The third proof is that Ali was the most knowledgeable of the companions. The author elaborates with a general argument and detailed proofs. The general argument compares Ali's early and continuous companionship with the Prophet against Abu Bakr's later and limited companionship. The detailed proofs include: 1) The Prophet said 'The most knowledgeable among you in judicial matters is Ali' which implies superiority in all sciences because judicial knowledge requires all sciences. 2) The verse 'And an ear that hears' was revealed for Ali, indicating special understanding. 3) A reported incident where Ali corrected Umar on a legal matter. The text ends abruptly here, so the third detailed proof is incomplete.

Key Terms
آية المباهلة
خبر الطير
أفضل الصحابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mubahala verse means Ali is literally identical to Muhammad.
Correction: The Shi'a argue only for equality in virtues, not identity, as literal identity is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the first proof the Shi'a use to argue that Ali is the best of the Companions?
A) The hadith of the bird
B) The verse of Mubahala (Aal Imran: 61)
C) The argument that Ali was most knowledgeable
D) The incident with Umar about stoning

Key Takeaway
The Shi'a use the Mubahala verse and the hadith of the bird to argue that Ali is equal to Muhammad in all virtues except prophethood, making him the best companion.

Imagine standing before Allah on the Day of Judgment and He asks you: 'Did you follow the most beloved to Me?' How would you answer? Have you ever honestly pondered what it means to love those whom Allah loves, beyond your inherited labels?

The General Argument for Ali's Superior Knowledge
General argument that Ali was the most knowledgeable Companion due to early and continuous learning from the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the general argument (al-ḥujja al-ijmāliyya) that Imam al-Razi uses to claim that Ali was the most knowledgeable Companion, and how it contrasts Ali's lifelong learning with Abu Bakr's limited association.

Core Idea
Ali's continuous training from childhood with the Prophet made him the most knowledgeable Companion.

This passage presents the third proof in Imam al-Razi's series arguing for Ali's superiority in knowledge. The argument uses a general (ijmali) reasoning based on the ideal conditions for learning: an intelligent student, an excellent teacher, and lifelong continuous association. By comparing Ali's upbringing in the Prophet's household with Abu Bakr's later and limited companionship, the argument concludes that Ali's knowledge was greater.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the claim
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the initial claim that Ali was the most knowledgeable Companion.
The claim is presented as the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha).
2
Identify the structure: Ijmal and Tafsil
Notice that the author mentions both summary (ijmāl) and detail (tafsīl), but only the summary is given here.
The detailed argument is not included in this passage.
3
Unpack the three factors of the summary argument
The summary lists three factors: Ali's innate intelligence, the Prophet's excellence as teacher and his eagerness to teach, and Ali's continuous association from childhood.
These are presented as conditions for attaining great knowledge.
4
Contrast with Abu Bakr
Read the comparison: Abu Bakr associated in adulthood and for short periods, while Ali associated from childhood continuously.
The adage about learning in youth vs. adulthood reinforces the point.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes that Ali was more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr based on these general considerations.
Consider whether this is a convincing general argument.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن عليا كان أعلم الصحابة، والأعلم أفضل. إنما قلنا : إنه كان أعلم الصحابة للإجمال والتفصيل أما الإجمال فهو أنه لا نزاع أن عليا كان في أصل الخلقة في غاية الذكاء والفطنة والاستعداد للعلم، وكان محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم أفضل العقلاء وأعلم العلماء، وكان علي في غاية الحرص في طلب العلم، وكان محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم في غاية الحرص في تربية علي وفي إرشاده إلى اكتساب الفضائل. ثم إن عليا نشأ من أول صغره في حجر محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم، وفي كبره صار ختنا له، وكان يدخل عليه في كل الأوقات، ومن المعلوم أن التلميذ إذا كان في غاية الذكاء والحرص على النقل، وكان الأستاذ في غاية الفضل وفي غاية الحرص على التعليم ثم إن اتفق لمثل هذا التلميذ أن اتصل بخدمة هذا الأستاذ من زمان الصغر، وكان ذلك الاتصال بخدمته حاصلا في كل الأوقات فإنه يبلغ ذلك التلميذ في العلم مبلغا عظيما. وهذا بيان إجمالي في أن عليا كان أعلم الصحابة، وأما أبو بكر فإنه اتصل بخدمته في زمان الكبر وأيضا ما كان يصل إلى خدمته في اليوم والليلة إلا زمانا يسيرا، أما علي فإنه اتصل بخدمته في زمان الصغر، وقد قيل: "العلم في الصغر كالنقش في الحجر، والعلم في الكبر كالنقش في المدر" فثبت بما ذكرنا أن عليا أعلم من أبي بكر.
The third proof: That Ali was the most knowledgeable of the Companions, and the most knowledgeable is superior. We say that he was the most knowledgeable of the Companions in summary and in detail. As for the summary, it is that there is no dispute that Ali was in his original nature in the utmost intelligence, acumen, and readiness for knowledge, and Muhammad (peace be upon him) was the best of the intelligent and the most knowledgeable of the scholars, and Ali was in utmost eagerness in seeking knowledge, and Muhammad (peace be upon him) was in utmost eagerness in raising Ali and guiding him to acquire virtues. Then Ali grew up from his earliest childhood in the care of Muhammad (peace be upon him), and in his adulthood became his son-in-law, and he would enter upon him at all times. It is known that if a student is in utmost intelligence and eagerness for learning, and the teacher is in utmost merit and utmost eagerness for teaching, then if it happens that such a student is connected to the service of this teacher from the time of childhood, and that connection to his service is present at all times, then that student will attain a great degree of knowledge. This is a summary explanation that Ali was the most knowledgeable of the Companions. As for Abu Bakr, he connected to his service in adulthood, and also he would only reach his service during the day and night for a short time. As for Ali, he connected to his service in childhood, and it has been said: 'Knowledge in childhood is like engraving on stone, and knowledge in adulthood is like engraving on clay.' So it is established by what we have mentioned that Ali was more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the general (ijmālī) argument for the claim that ʿAlī (ra) was the most knowledgeable Companion. The author, Imām al-Rāzī, begins by stating the claim: the third proof (al-ḥujja al-thālitha) is that ʿAlī was the most knowledgeable of the Companions, and the most knowledgeable is superior. He then explains that this can be shown both summarily (ijmāl) and in detail (tafṣīl). The summary argument focuses on three factors: 1) ʿAlī's innate intelligence and readiness for knowledge, 2) the Prophet's excellence as a teacher and his great care in teaching ʿAlī, and 3) ʿAlī's lifelong continuous association with the Prophet from early childhood, including being raised in the Prophet's household and later becoming his son-in-law. The author contrasts this with Abū Bakr (ra), who only associated with the Prophet in adulthood and for limited times. He cites the adage that knowledge learned in youth is like engraving on stone, whereas knowledge learned in adulthood is like engraving on clay. The conclusion is that ʿAlī was more knowledgeable than Abū Bakr based on these general considerations.

Key Terms
أعلم الصحابة
الإجمال والتفصيل
غاية الذكاء
الحرص على التعليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument relies on mere sentimental praise for Ali.
Correction: The argument is a structured analogy based on ideal student-teacher dynamics and the known advantage of childhood learning.

Checkpoint
According to the general argument, why was Ali more knowledgeable than Abu Bakr?
A) Ali had more natural intelligence.
B) Ali studied under more teachers.
C) Ali had continuous association with the Prophet from childhood, while Abu Bakr only associated in adulthood.
D) Ali was older than Abu Bakr.

Key Takeaway
The general argument for Ali's superior knowledge rests on the combination of innate intelligence, excellent teaching, and lifelong continuous learning from childhood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you leverage your best learning years as Ali did from childhood, or have you delayed seeking deep knowledge until now? What price might you be paying for that delay, and what is one change you will make this week to prioritize learning from the best sources?
?
If you are a parent or teacher, are you as eager to instruct and raise your children/students in virtue as the Prophet was with Ali? What concrete action can you take today to create an environment of continuous, intimate learning for them?
?
This passage argues that knowledge acquired in youth is like engraving on stone, while in adulthood it is like engraving on clay. What have you allowed to be engraved on your soul in your youth—and what needs to be deeply etched now before your heart hardens? How will you ensure your pursuit of sacred knowledge is not superficial but deeply ingrained?

The Hadith 'Aqdakum Ali' as Proof of Comprehensive Superiority
First detailed proof: the hadith 'Aqdakum Ali' shows Ali's superiority in all sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's saying 'Aqdakum Ali' provides proof for Ali's comprehensive superiority over all other Companions in knowledge.

Core Idea
Ali's superiority in judgment proves his superiority in all sciences.

This passage presents the first detailed proof from a hadith. The Prophet's statement that Ali is the best judge is used to argue that Ali must be the most knowledgeable in all sciences because judgment requires comprehensive knowledge. The author contrasts this with other Companions who were praised for only one area.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: a claim, a premise, a conclusion, and a contrasting example.
2
Understand the premise
Identify the key premise: judgment (al-qaḍāʾ) requires all types of sciences. Why is that? Because a judge must know law, interpretation, and many fields.
3
See the conclusion from the hadith
The Prophet said Ali is the best judge. Since judgment requires all sciences, being the best judge implies being the best in all sciences. That is the core proof.
4
Contrast with other Companions
The author mentions that other Companions were praised for specific sciences (e.g., Zayd in inheritance, Ubayy in recitation). This highlights that Ali's praise is comprehensive while others are limited.
5
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does this argument matter? It establishes Ali's superiority in knowledge over all other Companions, which is a key point in theological discussions about his status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «أقضاكم علي» والقضاء محتاج إلى جميع أنواع العلوم، فلما رجحه على الكل في القضاء لزم أنه رجحه عليهم في جميع العلوم، وأما سائر الصحابة فقد كل واحد منهم على غيره في علم واحد كقوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : «أفرضكم زيد بن ثابت، وأقرأكم أبي».
The first: His saying, peace be upon him: 'The best judge among you is Ali.' Judgment requires all types of sciences. Since he preferred him over all in judgment, it follows that he preferred him over them in all sciences. As for the other Companions, each one of them was preferred over others in a single science, such as his saying, peace be upon him: 'The most learned in inheritance division among you is Zayd ibn Thabit, and the best reciter among you is Ubayy.'
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the first detailed (tafṣīlī) proof for ʿAlī's superior knowledge. The proof is based on the Prophet's statement: 'The best judge among you is ʿAlī' (aqḍākum ʿAlī). The author argues that judgment (qaḍāʾ) requires all types of sciences (jamīʿ anwāʿ al-ʿulūm), because a judge must know law, interpretation, and many fields. Since the Prophet explicitly preferred ʿAlī over all others in judgment, it necessarily follows that he preferred him over them in all sciences. The author contrasts this with other Companions, where the Prophet specified each as superior in a single science (e.g., Zayd in inheritance, Ubayy in recitation), implying that no other Companion was said to be superior in all sciences combined. Thus ʿAlī's superiority is comprehensive.

Key Terms
أقضاكم
القضاء
جميع أنواع العلوم
أفرضكم
أقرأكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith only refers to Ali's expertise in judicial matters, not general knowledge.
Correction: The author argues that because judgment requires all sciences, being the best in judgment implies being the best in all sciences.

Checkpoint
Why does the hadith 'Aqdakum Ali' prove that Ali is superior in all sciences?
A) Because judgment requires only a few sciences.
B) Because judgment requires all types of sciences, so preference in judgment implies preference in all.
C) Because the Prophet said it twice.
D) Because no other Companion was ever praised.

Key Takeaway
The hadith 'Aqdakum Ali' functions as a comprehensive proof of Ali's superiority because judgment inherently requires all sciences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you rely on limited knowledge or do you seek comprehensive understanding from all sources? This lesson shows that judgment requires all sciences. How will you expand your own knowledge to make better judgments in your life?
?
The Prophet praised Ali comprehensively, but praised others in only one area. Do you tend to label people based on a single skill or do you recognize that true excellence is holistic? What would it take for you to develop a more complete perspective?
?
Consider a time you were quick to judge someone or a situation. What 'sciences'—knowledge, empathy, context—were you missing? How can you cultivate the humility to admit that you don't know everything and need to learn from all fields?

Second Proof: The Verse of the Listening Ear
Second proof: verse interpretation arguing for Ali's superior knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the verse 'Wa'iyataha udhunun wa'iya' is used by Imam Razi to argue that Ali was singled out for increased knowledge.

Core Idea
A single verse's interpretation can carry weight for a theological argument.

Imam Razi begins presenting the second detailed proof for the superiority of Ali's knowledge. This proof relies on a consensus among commentators that a specific Quranic verse refers to Ali and indicates his unique understanding. Let's examine this argument closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the claim that most commentators agree on the verse's reference to Ali.
2
Identify the argument
The author says 'singled out for increased understanding' implies 'singled out for more knowledge'. Understand the logical link.
3
Reflect on methodology
Consider why this verse is considered a proof. What does it assume about the authority of tafsir?
4
Connect to broader context
This is one of several proofs; think about how it fits with the others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: أن أكثر المفسرين سلموا أن قوله تعالى: ﴿وَعِيتَهَا أُذُنٌ وَاعِيَةٌ﴾ [الحاقة: 12] نزل في حق علي، وتخصيصه بزيادة الفهم يدل على اختصاصه بمزيد العلم. الثالث: روي أن عمر أمر برجم امرأة ولدت لستة أشهر فنبهه علي وقال له: ﴿وَحَمْلُهُ وَفِصَالُهُ ثَلَاثُونَ شَهْرًا﴾ [الأحقاف: 15] مع قوله: ﴿وَالْوَالِدَاتُ يُرْضِعْنَ أَوْلَادَهُنَّ حَوْلَيْنِ كَامِلَيْنِ﴾ [البقرة: 233] على أن أقل مدة الحمل ستة أشهر، فقال عمر: "لولا علي لهلك عمر". وروي أن امرأة أقرت بالزنا، وكانت حاملا فأمر عمر برجمها، فقال علي: "إن كان لك سلطان عليها فما سلطانك على ما بطنها؟" فترك عمر رجمها وقال: "لولا علي لهلك عمر". فإن قيل: لعل عمر أمر برجمها من غير تفحص عن حالها، فظن أنها ليست بحامل، فلما نبهه علي ترك رجمها. قلنا: هذا يقتضي أن عمر ما كان يحتاط في سفك الدماء، وهذا أشر من الأول. أيضا: روي أن عمر قال يوما على المنبر: ألا لا تغالوا في مهور النساء، فمن غالى في مهر امرأة جعلته في بيت المال. فقامت عجوز وقالت: يا أمير المؤمنين أتمنع عنا ما أحله الله لنا؟ قال تعالى: ﴿وَإِنْ أَرَدْتُمُ اسْتِبْدَالَ زَوْجٍ مَكَانَ زَوْجٍ وَآتَيْتُمْ إِحْدَاهُنَّ قِنْطَارًا فَلَا تَأْخُذُوا مِنْهُ شَيْئًا﴾ [النساء: 20]. فقال كل الناس أفقه من عمر حتى المخدرات في البيوت. فهذه الوقائع وقعت لغير علي، ومثلها لم يتفق لعلي.
And the second: That most of the commentators acknowledge that the saying of the Most High: 'And a listening ear listened to it' (al-Haqqah 69:12) was revealed concerning Ali, and his being singled out for increased understanding indicates his being singled out for more knowledge. The third: It is narrated that Umar ordered the stoning of a woman who gave birth after six months, so Ali alerted him and said to him: 'And his gestation and weaning is thirty months' (al-Ahqaf 46:15) along with His saying: 'And mothers shall give suck to their children for two whole years' (al-Baqarah 2:233), indicating that the minimum period of pregnancy is six months. So Umar said: 'Were it not for Ali, Umar would have perished.' And it is narrated that a woman confessed to adultery and was pregnant, so Umar ordered her stoning. Ali said: 'If you have authority over her, what authority do you have over what is in her womb?' So Umar left off stoning her and said: 'Were it not for Ali, Umar would have perished.' If it is said: Perhaps Umar ordered her stoning without investigating her condition, thinking she was not pregnant, and when Ali alerted him, he left off stoning her. We say: This implies that Umar was not cautious in shedding blood, and this is worse than the first. Also: It is narrated that Umar one day said on the pulpit: 'Do not go to extremes in the bridal gifts of women; whoever goes to extremes in the bridal gift of a woman, I will put it in the public treasury.' An old woman stood up and said: 'O Commander of the Faithful, do you prevent us what Allah has made lawful for us? He said, the Most High: 'And if you wish to exchange one wife for another and you have given one of them a great amount, do not take anything from it' (al-Nisa 4:20). So he said: 'All people are more knowledgeable than Umar, even secluded women in their homes.' So these incidents occurred for others than Ali, and similar did not happen for Ali.
Source pages p. 456

Commentary
This passage presents the second and third detailed proofs. The second proof is based on the common tafsīr of the verse 'And a listening ear listened to it' (al-Ḥāqqa 69:12), which many commentators understood as revealed concerning ʿAlī (ra). The phrase 'listening ear' is interpreted as a metaphorical description of ʿAlī's deep comprehension, and the fact that the verse singles him out for 'increased understanding' indicates he was granted more knowledge than others. The third proof is a series of historical incidents involving ʿUmar (ra) being corrected by ʿAlī. The first incident: ʿUmar ordered the stoning of a woman who gave birth after six months, believing she had committed adultery. ʿAlī pointed out that the Qur'anic verses about gestation (thirty months) and nursing (two years) together imply that the minimum pregnancy period is six months, so the woman was innocent. ʿUmar acknowledged his error. The second incident: a pregnant woman confessed to adultery, and ʿUmar ordered stoning. ʿAlī objected: 'If you have authority over her, what authority do you have over what is in her womb?'—meaning the unborn child should not be killed. ʿUmar again withdrew. The author then addresses an objection: perhaps ʿUmar ordered stoning without careful examination. The author refutes this by saying that would imply ʿUmar was negligent in bloodshed, which is a graver charge. Then an additional story is cited: ʿUmar proposed capping bridal gifts (mahr), and an old woman corrected him by quoting the Qur'an (al-Nisā 4:20) which allows giving a great amount. ʿUmar accepted the correction. The point is that these errors occurred to others (like ʿUmar and Abū Bakr) but never to ʿAlī, highlighting his infallible knowledge.

Key Terms
وعية أذن واعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does being singled out for 'increased understanding' indicate?
A) That Ali was the only one who could hear the Quran.
B) That Ali was given more knowledge.
C) That Ali was a prophet.
D) That the verse was revealed many times.

Key Takeaway
The verse's interpretation as referring to Ali is used to argue that he was granted superior knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this verse (69:12) before, did you ever imagine it could be about a specific person? How does knowing that many scholars explain it about Ali affect your attachment to that interpretation?
?
This proof relies on the authority of 'most commentators'. Are you willing to question the opinions of the majority when your personal loyalties are involved? What would it take for you to change your mind on a matter of religion?
?
If you were to learn that this verse is not about Ali at all, would it shake your trust in the Imamate position? Is your faith anchored in verses that might have multiple interpretations, or in something more certain?
